bc-mexico - a2158
 &UR; (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mary Beth Sheridan </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Zedillo Inaugurates New Democratic Era in Mexico (Mexico City) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MEXICO CITY  &MD;  President Ernesto Zedillo inaugurated a new, more
democratic era in Mexican politics Monday with a passionate appeal to
ascendant opposition parties not to derail the country's free-market
economic revolution.
<p>
   ``For the first time in our history, we can achieve healthy,
durable economic growth, along with a full, pluralistic and harmonious
democracy,'' the president declared in his State of the Union Address.
``This is the opportunity of our generation.''
<p>
   The president's appeal reflected the unprecedented challenges to
his power as Mexico moves from an authoritarian, one-party system to
democracy.
<p>
   In midterm elections in July, the ruling party lost its control of
Congress for the first time in seven decades. While the president's
Institutional Revolutionary Party, or PRI, still maintains a
commanding presence in the legislature, the Mexican leader suddenly
must fight to preserve his policies.
<p>
   Perhaps as much as his words, the ceremony surrounding the
president's most important speech of the year reflected the historic
change in Mexico.
<p>
   For the first time, an opposition politician, leftist firebrand
Porfirio Munoz Ledo, responded to the address.
<p>
   ``From today, we hope that forevermore in Mexico no power will be
subordinated to another,'' he declared.
<p>
   A ceremony that in the past featured all the pomp of a U.S.
presidential inauguration became a simple event, without the parades
and adoring crowds of business and political leaders so common before.
<p>
   ``This is like seeing one of the most momentous presidential
symbols we had for a century just deflate,'' said Lorenzo Meyer, a
Mexican historian.
<p>
   The new Congress' independent spirit was so strong there almost
wasn't a State of the Union speech. The PRI balked last week at
demands by opposition parties for important posts in the Chamber of
Deputies, the lower house of Congress. When the opposition went ahead
and installed the lower house Saturday, the PRI boycotted
<p>
    &MD;  and threatened a walkout Monday that would have forced the
president to postpone his speech. The party backed off Sunday night.
<p>
   Before and after Zedillo's speech Monday, the opposition warned him
that the days of a pliant Congress that rubber-stamped presidential
decisions were over.
<p>
   ``We will have parliamentary battle,'' declared a gleeful Pablo
Gomez, a deputy from the left-wing Democratic Revolution Party, or
PRD, in a speech before the president arrived for his address.
<p>
   Later, Munoz Ledo warned Zedillo in strong terms that he would have
to share power.
<p>
   ``Knowing how to govern is also knowing how to listen, and knowing
how to correct mistakes,'' Munoz Ledo, who was elected president of
the lower house Saturday, infuriating the PRI, said in his response to
the president.
<p>
   ``Certainly, the democratic exercise of power means ruling while at
the same time obeying,'' Munoz Ledo said.
<p>
   He was greeted with more applause than the president received.
Zedillo sat stone-faced during the speech.
<p>
   Before Monday, a PRI member had always held the presidency of the
lower house and had responded to the president's speech in a virtual
paean to the leader. In the wake of the July elections, the four
opposition parties together hold a 261-seat majority in the 500-seat
Chamber of Deputies.
<p>
   Still, Zedillo and his party aren't about to be steamrollered.
Deeply divided on ideology  &MD;  the coalition ranges across the political
spectrum  &MD;  members of the opposition alliance could split. In
addition, most legislation must be approved by the Senate, the upper
house  &MD;  where the PRI continues to hold a majority.
<p>
   But the opposition has vowed to work together to challenge certain
aspects of economic policy, vowing to cut the 15 percent sales tax
that is a key source of government revenue and oppose the bailout of
troubled banks and construction companies.
<p>
   Even in his own party, the president could run into trouble
maintaining unpopular economic policies. The country's recession was
believed to be a major factor in the PRI's poor showing at the polls
in July.
<p>
   In his speech Monday night, Zedillo pledged to cooperate with the
new Congress, which was opening a three-year term.
<p>
   But he appealed to legislators not to return to populist economic
policies of the past. He maintained the country had to focus on
generating economic growth of at least 5 percent a year to create
badly needed jobs.
<p>
   ``Any new mirages, any new decisions that affect conditions for
(economic) growth ... will cause a great reverse,'' warned the
president, a Yale-educated economist.
<p>
   ``If we reached agreements to establish democracy, we must reach
accords about the foundation of our economic policy,'' declared
Zedillo, a driving force behind reforms that have produced Mexico's
most democratic elections ever.
<p>
   Zedillo noted that while Mexico's economy began to rebound last
year from the recession, many citizens complain that they still don't
feel improvement. He appealed to them to keep supporting tight-money,
balanced-budget policies that have reduced inflation and encouraged a
renewed flood of foreign investment.
<p>
   One of Zedillo's principal goals is to end the cyclical economic
crises that have plagued Mexico for the last 20 years. Most have
coincided with the political uncertainty surrounding the end of a
president's term. The next presidential election is in 2000.
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Good morning, PM editors.
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service budget for
afternoon papers of Tuesday, Sept. 2, 1997. All stories have moved.
For questions about Post stories, call (202) 334-7666 between 10 a.m.
and 1 a.m. EDT; for questions about Times and other Times Mirror
stories, call (213) 237-7832 between 11 a.m. and 2 a.m. EDT.
<p>
    &UR; The Death of Princess Diana:
<p>
   DIANA-TIMES  &MD;  LONDON  &MD;  Two prideful patrician families agree with
the British government to give Princess Diana a ``unique funeral''
that will combine the families' wishes for privacy with public demands
to honor a woman who touched the nation's heart. 1,315 words, by
William D. Montalbano (Times). With DIANA-POST.
<p>
   DIANA-CRASH-TIMES  &MD;  PARIS  &MD;  The man driving the limousine in which
Princess Diana was killed was legally drunk, with more than three
times the lawful level of alcohol in his blood as he raced through
central Paris at speeds of more than 110 mph, French sources say.
1,500 words, by John Thor-Dahlburg (Times). With DIANA-CRASH-POST.
<p>
   DIANA-SONS  &MD;  LONDON  &MD;  Prince William and Prince Harry are described
as brave and courageous, princes of boys who showed a stiff upper lip
just four hours after being told about their mother's death;
performing their royal routine of duty would have infuriated their
late mother. 1,200 words, by Dean E. Murphy (Times).
<p>
   DIANA-TABLOIDS  &MD;  LONDON  &MD;  Britain's raucous tabloids are unusually
quiet as reports that pursuing photographers might have contributed to
Princess Diana's death lead to talk of tough strictures on the prying
press. 1,060 words, by Eugene Robinson and Christine Spolar (Post).
<p>
   DIANA-GRIEF-TIMES  &MD;  LONDON  &MD;  Thousands of mourning Britons of all
ages and races flock to Harrods department store to share their grief
over Princess Diana and the last love of her life, Dodi Al Fayed; the
scene at the gingerbread palace offers a microcosm of a nation's
people and their raw emotions at a time of sudden loss. 1,051 words,
by Richard Boudreaux (Times).
<p>
   DIANA-FASHION  &MD;  If Princess Diana leaves any gift to fashion, it
would be her love for it  &MD;  she embraced the fashion industry in a way
that few public figures outside of Hollywood do and with more passion
than any statesman dares; although she did not live long enough to
develop an iconic style, she had enough time to discover and embrace
the power of clothes. 1,350 words, by Robin Givhan (Post).
<p>
    &UR; Other International:
<p>
   MEXICO  &MD;  MEXICO CITY &MD;  President Ernesto Zedillo inaugurates a new,
more democratic era in Mexican politics with a passionate appeal to
ascendent opposition parties to not derail the country's free-market
economic revolution. 920 words, by Mary Beth Sheridan (Times).
<p>
   ALGERIA  &MD;  CAIRO, Egypt  &MD;  Reeling from a recent spate of massacres,
Algeria's government orders the house arrest of an Islamic leader
released from prison just six weeks ago. 765 words, by John
Daniszewski (Times).
<p>
   Q&AMP;A-RAMPHELE  &MD;  Mamphela Ramphele on Nelson Mandela, Steven Biko and
the process of integrating South Africa. 1,725 words, by Scott Kraft
(Times).
<p>
    &UR; National:
<p>
   CONGRESS  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  Congress started its August recess on a
bipartisan high from the historic balanced-budget agreement, but many
members are eager to resume work Tuesday on appropriations bills and
other issues with partisan vigor, to sharpen political differences as
a new election year approaches. 1,340 words, by Edwin Chen (Times).
With CONGRESS-POST.
<p>
   ALABAMA  &MD;  TROY, Ala.  &MD;  The parents of four Jewish children in the
small Alabama town of Troy say their kids have been persecuted by
other children as well as teachers and the school system at large;
they say officials have responded to complaints about name-calling and
other abuse with, ``This is how we do things in Alabama.'' 1,670
words, by Sue Anne Pressley (Post).
<p>
   PALMETTO  &MD;  IMMOKALEE, Fla.  &MD;  Fired up by reports that it is a
sexual stimulant and antidote to enlarged prostates, the
hard-to-swallow berry of the saw palmetto has become Florida's newest
cash crop. 900 words, by Mike Palmetto (Times).
<p>
    &UR; Commentaries and news features are listed on the
NEWSFEATURES-LAT-WP budget that moves daily at noon EDT.

<p>
   (Early) FRONTINGS FOR TUESDAY, SEPT. 2, 1997 &QL; 
<p>
    &QL; 4-col lede w/foto of
flowers in front of Buckingham Palace and sidebar: SWARDSON/PARIS--The
driver of the Mercedes sedan carrying Princess Diana was legally drunk
when he crashed in a roadway tunnel here early Sunday, killing Diana,
companion Dodi Fayed and himself, the Paris prosecutor's office said
today. The announcement did not specify his blood alcohol level, but
news agencies quoted official sources as saying it was three times the
legal limit for driving in France.  &QL;  &QL; 3-col sidebar: BALZ/London--As
thousands of mourners paid
their last respects today to Diana, Princess of Wales, officials at
Buckingham Palace prepared for a funeral on Saturday that will combine
royal ritual with less formal touches designed to reflect the
extraordinary role Diana played in the life of the nation. Palace
officials described the service, which will be held at Westminster
Abbey, as ``a unique funeral for a unique person.'' The arrangement
appeared devised to accommodate Diana's complex social position as the
divorced wife of the heir to the throne, Prince Charles, as well as
her popularity among Britons and around the world. &HT;     &QL; 1-col off-led w/ foto of AIDS educator. third of five articles
in AIDS series (with apologies. this story was in sept. 1 frontings,
but, in fact, ran in sept. 2 paper. diana-mania is to blame):
Okie/Florence, S.C.--Aggressive HIV prevention efforts proposed in the
early years of the epidemic-such as widespread condom distribution,
clean-needle giveaways to drug addicts and frank sex education in
schools-triggered intense controversy and enormous political
opposition in many more conservative parts of the country. Moreover,
some public health experts argued that money spent on national media
campaigns could be more effective if focused on those at greatest risk
of contracting the virus. So three years ago, the federal government
revamped the country's prevention strategy, abandoning a single
national effort in favor of locally tailored efforts that it hoped
would be both more acceptable and more effective. &HT;     &QL; 1-col at fold: AIDS sidebar: Goldstein--differences in theme
and tone of the literature supplied by Whitman-Walker, the largest
provider of AIDS services locally, attest to the divergent
sensibilities even within the Washington area about how communities
should try to prevent the spread of the disease. Only in the District
and in Alexandria, for example, can high school students pick up
condoms at school. And only in the District do AIDS workers visit
street corners where illegal drugs are sold, offering clean needles to
intravenous drug users. In the three years since the federal
government decentralized the nation's approach to AIDS prevention, the
increased local control has created a trade-off for metropolitan
Washington: More money is being devoted to niches in the population
that are especially vulnerable to HIV, but the effectiveness of local
efforts has in some cases been questionable, according to AIDS
experts. &HT;    
<p>
    &QL; 1-col below fold: HOCKSTADER/Sarajevo, Bosnia--Hurling stones
and swinging clubs, several hundred Bosnian Serbs attacked nearly 300
heavily armed U.S. troops today in an attempt to take back control of
a key television transmitter seized last week by the Americans. The
troops fended off the assault with tear gas, neither taking nor
inflicting casualties, but they remained locked in a tense standoff
with the crowd as darkness settled over the remote hillside near the
town of Udrigova in northeastern Bosnia. &HT;     &QL; 3-col below fold w/foto of plebe and upper classman:
Argetsinger--At the U.S. Naval Academy and other military colleges,
the people responsible for indoctrinating each incoming class are
themselves relative newcomers to the rigors of military life-not
battle-hardened sergeants, but young fellow students. It's a crucial
role, especially at Annapolis, where many say the most honorable
traditions, as well as the worst habits, are passed from one class to
the next. &QL;  &QL; 3-col bottom: Allen and Hsu/Buena Vista,
Va.--Waking up a Virginia governor's race so far tranquilized by
cautious candidates with sound-alike platforms, Democrat Donald S.
Beyer Jr. charged today that Republican James S. Gilmore III sides
with extremists who would undermine public education, while Gilmore
said Beyer keeps waffling on important issues. The exchanges before a
Labor Day crowd at the foot of the Blue Ridge, where sunny pep talks
usually kick off the Old Dominion's fall campaigns, marked a change in
direction for the candidates, who have been even in the polls for
months. Each showed a steely side that is expected to be on view in a
nasty advertising war through the Nov. 4 election. &HT;     &QL; WORLD FRONT: &QL;  &QL; MOORE AND ANDERSON/Mexico City--Mexican
President Ernesto Zedillo, after a weekend of intense political
clashes between his ruling party and the opposition, appealed tonight
to the first divided Congress in modern Mexican history to establish a
``mature, constructive relationship'' with his office to battle the
double scourges of drug trafficking and corruption in Mexico. But, for
the first time in the modern Mexican Congress, the president was
forced to share the podium on opening day with an opposition leader
who delivered a forceful counterpoint to the ruling PRI, which once
dominated the actions of a rubber-stamp legislative branch. &HT;     &QL;  &QL; JORDAN/Tokyo--Many Japanese believe crime here has grown
faster than the nation's ability to cope with it. Critics say Japan's
criminal laws were written for a more peaceful time and that its
police practices are better suited for a farming village than a
modern, urban society. Many people are frustrated about what they see
as outdated laws that are too forgiving of criminals, such as the
15-year statute of limitations on murder and the laws that allow
murderers under 16 to be set free after three years in juvenile
detention. &QL;  &QL; AP/Tel Aviv--Israel eased its closure of the
West Bank and Gaza today to allow in a few thousand Palestinians-a
move Palestinian leaders dismissed as a token gesture that does little
to help their stymied economy. The United States, meanwhile,
reportedly is working on a compromise to get peace talks
restarted. &QL;  &QL; INSIDE: &QL;  &QL; ROBINSON AND SPOLAR/London--Britain's raucous
tabloid newspapers were uncharacteristically quiet today, as reports
that pursuing photographers might have contributed to Princess Diana's
death led to talk of tough new privacy laws, curbs on electronic
eavesdropping and other strictures on the prying press. Usually
blustery and bellicose, many tabloids opted for lyrical, subdued
language this morning. Perhaps more significant, however, the tabloids
had nothing to say today regarding the allegation that their hunger
for photos of Diana and boyfriend Dodi Fayed set the stage for
Sunday's tragic accident. &QL;  &QL; LONDON
NOTEBOOK--mourning at Harrods, newspapers selling out, no flag at
Buckingham Palace (queen is away) and moment of silence at land mines
meeting in Oslo. &QL;  &QL; Reuter/Beijing--tidal wave hits N.
Korea. &QL;  &QL; FOREIGN ROUNDUP:
<p>
   AP/Moscow--yeltsin says he won't run for third term.
Reuter/Moscow--Mir spacewalk set for saturday. AP/jerusalem--Netanyahu
says Israel-Egypt ties will survive dispute over trial.
REuter/Prague--Albright and members of her family visit her parents'
birthplace. Reuter/Tehran--alleged CIA spy is hanged.
<p>
   METRO: &QL;  &QL; Victoria Benning--For British citizens visiting or
living in the Washington area, Diana's death at 36 was particularly
hard felt. Many said wished they could be home grieving with their
countrymen. Instead, many found themselves glued to television-or
computer screens- trying to glean every detail about the accident that
killed Diana and her boyfriend Dodi Fayed. Had they been home in
England, they surely would be gathered in the streets offering solace
to each other, many said. Absent from their homeland, Brits joined
throngs of other grief-stricken mourners outside the embassy in the
3100 block of Massachusetts Avenue NW where the steps have been turned
into a shrine to the popular princess. &HT;     &QL; STYLE: &QL;  &QL; Givhan--If Princess Diana leaves any gift to fashion,
it would be her love for it. She embraced the fashion industry in a
way that few public figures outside of Hollywood do, and with more
passion than any statesman dares. She did not live long enough to
develop an iconic style, but she had enough time to discover and
embrace the power of clothes. &HT;     &QL; Roxanne Roberts/London--On this flawless summer day, a
continuous stream of people poured onto the walkways leading to the
red-brick palace that was Princess Diana's London residence. The
grounds on the edge of Hyde Park usually close at dusk, but officials
kept the park open until 2 a.m. this morning to accommodate the tens
of thousands of mourners. When the grounds reopened three hours later,
more than 300 people were waiting to pay their respects. They were
drawn, they said, by a need to do something, anything to show Diana
how much they cared. &QL;  &QL; FINANCIAL: &QL;  &QL; AP/London--Financial markets pause
in memory of
Diana. Commerce will come to a halt for funeral Saturday.  &QL;  &QL; News
Services/Tokyo--Japan's stock market and some other Asian markets
rebounded early today after three sessions of sharp declines stemming
from investor worries about Asia's currency crisis. &HT;     &QL; Cheers,
<p>
   Terri Shaw

 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Editorial-Page editors) &QL;

<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; False Converts &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The following editorial appeared in Tuesday's Washington Post:
<p>
   The tobacco companies are trying to create a new image of
themselves as repentant and in retreat when in fact they remain onthe
attack. Only the packaging has changed. When they settled the lawsuit
that Florida had filed against them the other day, the companies said
the deal was ``a concrete demonstration that the industry is prepared
to cooperate with government and the public health authorities to
emphasize that it doesn't want kids to smoke.'' They called the
settlement ``another step in a process to end the climate of
confrontation and litigation that has marked the national debate on
tobacco-related issues.''
<p>
   So they're pussycats, except that:
<p>
   (1) As part of the national deal they now want President Clinton to
bless, they've agreed to pay $368.5 billion over 25 years. Pretty
imposing. But the companies also got the Republican leadership of
Congress  &MD;  no less than House Speaker Newt Gingrich and Senate
Majority Leader Trent Lott  &MD;  to tuck into the budget they sent the
president in July a couple of sentences forgiving them $50 billion of
this before they were even required to pay it. The $50 billion was to
compensate them for the tobacco tax increase Congress had just voted
in part to help finance a new program of children's health insurance.
No one professes to know exactly how the forgiveness made its way into
the legislation  &MD;  who suggested it, for example. But part of the
explanation, we are willing to guess, lies in the enormous campaign
contributions the tobacco companies have made to the Republicans in
recent years. (They also have given a lot, but less, to the
Democrats.) That's what these folks mean when they say they are buying
``access.'' The $368 billion figure won't endure; they'll find ways to
knock it down. The durable part of the deal, if they have their way,
will be the limits on future lawsuits that the $368 billion is meant
to buy. Surely no one would be so crass as to suggest that Congress go
back on its word on  &UR; that &LR; .
<p>
   (2) A second aspect of the deal has to do with the power of the
Food and Drug Administration to regulate nicotine. The companies
professed to agree that it has such power, which was nice of them,
since a court had already ruled so. Then they proposed that the power
should be exercised only if the FDA could jump through a series of
impossible and irrelevant hoops whose effect would be, as intended, to
negate the power. Meanwhile, the companies continue to appeal the
court ruling which confirmed that the authority exists.
<p>
   (3) They agreed as well to back off ads calculated to appeal to
kids, and a great show was made of the withdrawal of the Joe Camel
series. But it was withdrawn only to be replaced by another aimed at
pretty much the same market. The current behavior of the companies
demonstrates the same thing that their past behavior so amply shows:
They'll game any system that's put in place to keep them from selling
cigarettes. It's a mirage to think they're preparing to go out of
business. They simply want to buy at as low a price as possible a
steady platform from which to continue.
<p>
   Here's a rule, which the president and Congress alike should keep
in mind as try in the weeks ahead to figure out what to do about the
proposed master agreement: Almost by definition, any deal the tobacco
companies find congenial is against the public interest. It's a real
temptation to sign off on a big peace pact such as has been proposed  &MD; 
a world-class photo op, and there are in fact some substantive
features of the proposal that would be gains. Our own instinct is that
the politicians should go carefully and slowly. Among other things,
they need to see what a few more of the pending state suits, the one
in Minnesota particularly, turn up by way of evidence that the
companies knowingly put the many millions of people they hooked on
their product at risk. Which politicians want to vote for a limit on
future lawsuits and other liability in advance of that?
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-travel-homer </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0664 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-travel-homer
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Travel editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Paul Richard </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Winslow Homer's Masculine Essay (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  War stamped Winslow Homer (1836-1910), and had already
done so when he decided he was ready to exhibit with the big boys in
the art world of New York.
<p>
   He was only 27. When it came to making pictures with oil paints and
brushes, he was still a green beginner, but he'd already been to
battle, at Alexandria and Yorktown  &MD;  and already he'd been seized by
that ache of male sharing and that sense of strong men dying that
would, until he died, remain central to his art.
<p>
   ``Home, Sweet Home,'' his calculated debut piece, would be
submitted to the annual exhibition of the National Academy of Design
in the middle of the Civil War in 1863. War would be his subject. His
figures would be soldiers, their bivouac his setting. His themes would
be political, religious, geometrical. He would plan that little
picture, its feelings and its forms and its forces-in-reserve, as if
he were a general designing the deployments of a cavalry campaign.
<p>
   In June, ``Home, Sweet Home,'' 21 inches high, was sold at auction
at Christie's in Manhattan for $2.6 million to the National Gallery of
Art, where it is now on view.
<p>
   Evening after rain. A tented Union camp. The brass band in the
background is playing ``Home, Sweet Home,'' a pop anthem of the time.
Two bedraggled Union infantrymen  &MD;  whose true home is their
ever-so-humble pup tent, but whose thoughts are elsewhere  &MD;  are
listening intently to that sentimental air.
<p>
   And to something else as well. Somewhere in the distance, beyond
that plaintive melody, is the echo of the tomb and the rumble of the
guns. Beneath its calibrated poignance, ``Home, Sweet Home'' is
grounded on more turbulent emotions  &MD;  on a sense of endless dread, a
danger-darkned consciousness of sacrificial flesh.
<p>
   Right from the beginning, Homer had the knack of expressing in his
pictures actual duration, as if we the viewers were immersed in the
painting, like the soldiers in their reveries, only while the music
lasts.
<p>
   ``Home, Sweet Home,'' a buddy picture almost, is an essay in
masculinist absorption. It is, to use a term in vogue now in feminist
art discourse, a deeply gendered object, but it isn't feminist at all.
Homer is convinced that life is most intense, and most intensely
beautiful, in realms of male testing, in places women aren't.
<p>
   ``Home,'' one feels, couldn't be European. What most makes Homer's
canvas seem characteristically American is the quality of its
protagonists. In a French or British war painting of the mid-19th
century the warriors in the foreground would be bold aristocrats,
officers with sabers, or the peasantlike but loyal troops in their
command, not two regular guys in ill-fitting uniforms.
<p>
   Both men know they may soon die. War, they sense, has cursed them  &MD; 
or, this being Homer's world, perhaps they have been blessed.
<p>
   That courage is redemptive is a constant theme of Homer's (and
Hemingway's, and Hollywood's). His soldiers in their reveries have
been touched by special grace. Homer, in his later work, would grow a
lot more reticent, broadening his brushwork and silencing the creaking
of his symbolic machinery, but here the signs are clear. The painting,
in its details, invites us to accept that these men are kin to Christ.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   The Homer retrospective that Nicolai Cikovsky Jr. and Franklin
Kelly organized together for the National Gallery in 1995 made Homer's
towering stature evident to all. Our most accomplished brushman, our
clearest visual thinker, he is America's prime painter. Though their
museum owns six other Homer oils, including ``Right and Left'' (1909),
his last, the curators were right to urge the purchase of ``Home,
Sweet Home.'' The canvas is on display with a group of Homer's
on-the-spot battlefield sketches that were given to the museum by
Edmund Louis Gray Zalinski II.
</TEXT>

AP-NY-09-02-97 0941EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-on-the-fridge </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0368 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-on-the-fridge
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Pig Out on Web Site on Pork &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Add this Web site address to your bookmarks: www.npcc.org. It's a
pig-out, a site for anyone who wants to learn more about pigs and pork
from the National Pork Producers Council.
<p>
   Choose Option 1: food and nutrition; recipes and cooking tips (even
a how-to on hosting a hog roast). Or, choose Option 4: food fun for
kids; farm tours, travel the world through the Magical Kitchen Pantry
and kid-friendly recipes.
<p>
   We include the following from the recipe section.
<p>
   THAI-STYLE PORK CHOPS (4 servings)
<p>
   (You can control the heat by using more or less of the spice
paste.)
<p>
   4 pork chops, each about 1¼ inches thick, well trimmed
<p>
   1 teaspoon grated lemon peel
<p>
   4 crushed garlic cloves
<p>
   ¼ cup grated fresh ginger root
<p>
   1 tablespoon chili powder
<p>
   ½ teaspoon ground coriander
<p>
   ½ teaspoon black pepper
<p>
   ½ teaspoon crushed red chilies
<p>
   Place all the ingredients except the pork chops in a blender
container with 3 tablespoons water. Blend until smooth and then spread
about 2 teaspoons of the resulting paste on each chop, covering both
sides. Cover the chops with plastic and refrigerate them for 2 to 24
hours. Prepare a medium-hot fire in a kettle-style covered grill.
Grill the chops directly over the coals for 7 to 8 minutes per side.
Serve immediately.
<p>
   Per serving: 430 calories, 51 gm protein, 4 gm carbohydrates, 23 gm
fat, 119 mg cholesterol, 8 gm saturated fat, 146 mg sodium.
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   Want to know the name of the cheese of the decade? Torta del Casar,
from the Extremadura region of Spain. ``Its flavor will wipe you
out,'' says Steven Jenkins, the author of last year's highly praised
``Cheese Primer'' (Workman, 1996).
<p>
   Jenkins discovered Spanish cheeses about 10 years ago when he was
``smug and jaded and thought I'd ferreted out everything there was to
know about cheese.'' Now he's wild about them  &MD;  at least the regional
ones made by hand by people living on the same land where the cows and
goats and sheep graze.
<p>
   Among the others he adores: Afuega'l Pitu, Can Pujol, Garrotxa, La
Serena, Mahon and Majorero.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-poultry </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1416 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-poultry
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Nancy McKeon and Stephanie Witt Sedgwick </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Smoke Gets in Your Poultry &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Yes, we're still out here by the backyard grill, even if we do slip
back into the air conditioning when it's time to eat. And yes, we're
getting a little cranky standing here, balancing a Diet
Fill-in-the-Blanks and a chicken thigh so charred that the salmonella
marched out in protest.
<p>
   It's around now, when the coals are getting ready to cool for the
season, that a little alfresco legerdemain is in order. We're not
suggesting that you work any harder than your guests do at their
outdoor fetes, or that you fix anything particularly exotic, but what
we have in mind will make yours the outdoor dinner to remember next
February, when we're looking back (fondly) on the humidity.
<p>
   Lightly smoked. Unbelievably moist. Does that sound like chicken to
you? Probably not. It doesn't sound like turkey either. Or Cornish
game hen.
<p>
   But that's exactly what they  &UR; can &LR;  be. And bronzed to almost a
mahogany veneer. Norman Rockwell poultry  &MD;  the kind that will make you
glad that a whole bird should sit for a few minutes  &MD;  on a platter on
the buffet table, maybe, you little show-off?  &MD;  before you carve and
serve it.
<p>
   The method that achieves this is no secret: It's indirect cooking.
You see, the reason chickens end up charred is a combination of hot
charcoal fires and that layer of fat beneath the bird's skin, some
chickens being more egregiously fattened than others. Over coals, the
dripping melted fat causes flare-ups and, eventually, char.
<p>
   Some of us enjoy the deep taste of skin-charred chicken, but the
health implications of that are not great. Also, such a taste is very
much a personal idiosyncrasy, on a par with burned popcorn. Yet some
who wouldn't dream of serving a shriveled bird and burned vegetables
during the rest of the year seem to think the rules of good cooking
are suspended at grillside (mea culpa).
<p>
   LIGHTLY SMOKED CHICKEN (6 to 8 servings)
<p>
   Once you get the technique down, you'll always want to cook chicken
this way. It's  &UR; that &LR;  good. The chickens cook to a beautiful mahogany
color, and the meat is lightly smoky and very moist.
<p>
   This recipe was perfected by Kate Davidson of Arlington, Va.
Although she makes her own vinaigrettes for salads, Davidson uses
bottled dressing to marinate her chickens. We agree that her method
makes chickens that are delicious and easy.
<p>
   2 fryer chickens, about 3 pounds each
<p>
   2 cups Italian-style dressing, bottled or homemade
<p>
   Salt and pepper to taste
<p>
   1½ cups pecan or hickory chips
<p>
   Rinse the chicken with cold water. Tuck the wing tips under the
bird and tie the legs together with kitchen twine. Put each chicken
into a resealable plastic bag, pour ¾ cup of the Italian dressing into
each bag and seal (keep the remaining ¼ cup of dressing refrigerated
separately). Let the chickens marinate in the refrigerator for at
least 3 hours but not more than 24.
<p>
   Prepare the grill.
<p>
   Remove the chickens from the marinade and discard the bag and the
marinade. Salt and pepper to taste. Place the chickens, breast-side
up, on the grill directly above the drip pan. Cover the grill. After
15 minutes, remove the cover and turn the chickens over so the
breast-side is down.
<p>
   Brush with the reserved dressing and cover the grill. Repeat every
15 minutes. If the coals start to die down, remove the lid, add 7 or 8
coals to each side and leave the cover off for 5 or 6 minutes to
rejuvenate the fire. After a total of 45 minutes, add the remaining 2
wood-chip packets to the coals and start checking the temperature of
the chickens with an instant-read thermometer.
<p>
   The chickens are done when the thigh meat registers 170 degrees and
the breast meat registers 180. This can take anywhere from 45 to 75
minutes, depending on the heat of the fire. When done, remove the
chickens from the grill and let rest for 10 minutes before cutting
into serving pieces.
<p>
   Per serving: 494 calories, 57 gm protein, 1 gm carbohydrates, 28 gm
fat, 169 mg cholesterol, 7 gm saturated fat, 351 mg sodium
<p>
   LEMON-ROSEMARY CORNISH GAME HENS (6 servings)
<p>
   It's easiest to serve game hens cut in half. This can be done with
a sharp knife or, better yet, with poultry shears. If you don't
already own them, consider buying some. They make cutting up chickens
and other poultry a snap.
<p>
   1 teaspoon salt
<p>
   ½ teaspoon freshly ground pepper
<p>
   1 teaspoon grated lemon zest plus 2 whole lemons, quartered
<p>
   1 tablespoon chopped rosemary, plus 3 whole sprigs rosemary
<p>
   2 tablespoons vegetable oil
<p>
   3 Cornish game hens, about 1¼ pounds each
<p>
   1½ cups wood chips
<p>
   Combine the salt, pepper, lemon zest, rosemary and 1 tablespoon of
the oil to make a lemon-rosemary paste. Set aside.
<p>
   Rinse the game hens under cold water. With your fingers, gently
loosen the skin over the breasts and legs. Using 1/3 of the
lemon-rosemary paste, spread the paste on one of the hens between the
meat and the skin over the breast and legs. Repeat with the other two
hens. Place the hens in resealable plastic bags or wrap in plastic and
refrigerate for at least 6 hours but not more than 24. Prepare the
grill.
<p>
   Stuff 2 or 3 lemon quarters and a sprig of rosemary inside the
cavity of each hen and brush the hens with the remaining 1 tablespoon
of oil. Place the hens, breast-side up, on the grill directly over the
drip pan. Cover.
<p>
   Turn the game hens over every 15 minutes. Add the remaining 2
wood-chip packets and start checking the temperature of the game hens
with an instant-read thermometer after 30 minutes. The game hens are
done when the thigh meat registers 170 degrees and the breast meat
registers 180. This can take anywhere from 30 to 45 minutes, depending
on the heat of the fire. When they are done, remove the game hens from
the grill and let rest for 10 minutes. Cut each game hen in half,
discard the lemon quarters and rosemary sprigs, and serve immediately.
<p>
   Per serving: 255 calories, 28 gm protein, trace carbohydrates, 15
gm fat, 85 mg cholesterol, 4 gm saturated fat, 404 mg sodium
<p>
   GINGER-PLUM-GLAZED CHICKEN (8 servings)
<p>
   Unless you have a very large grill, you'll probably be able to cook
only half this recipe at a time. Cook the dark meat first: It takes
the longest (up to 45 minutes). The breast quarters will be done in 25
to 30 minutes. Cover the cooked pieces with aluminum foil to keep warm
until you're ready to serve.
<p>
   You don't have to use the indirect cooking method here, but it's so
much better. The sauce beautifully glazes the lightly smoked chicken.
<p>
   3 tablespoons vegetable oil
<p>
   1 cup diced onion
<p>
   2 tablespoons chopped garlic
<p>
   2 tablespoons chopped fresh ginger root
<p>
   2 cups tomato puree
<p>
   ½ cup plum jam or preserves
<p>
   1 teaspoon hot pepper sauce, such as Tabasco
<p>
   1 teaspoon salt, plus additional to taste
<p>
   ½ teaspoon freshly ground black pepper, plus additional to taste
<p>
   8 chicken quarters (about 6 pounds), or any similar combination of
parts
<p>
   1½ cups wood chips
<p>
   Heat 1 tablespoon of the oil in a small saute pan over medium heat.
Add the onion and cook for 3 to 4 minutes, until the onion starts to
soften. Add the chopped garlic and ginger and cook for 2 minutes.
<p>
   In a blender, combine the onion mixture with the tomato puree, plum
jam, hot pepper sauce, salt and pepper and blend until smooth.
Refrigerate until ready to use. Prepare the grill.
<p>
   Salt and pepper the chicken quarters and brush with the remaining
oil. Place the chicken quarters, meat-side down, on the grill directly
over the drip pan. Cover. After 10 minutes, turn the quarters
meat-side up and, using a portion of the reserved ginger-plum barbecue
sauce, baste the chicken.
<p>
   Cover the grill and let the chicken cook, basting again and turning
the pieces every 5 minutes. After a total of 20 minutes, start
checking the temperature of the chicken with an instant-read
thermometer. Breast quarters are done at 180 degrees. Thighs and legs
at 170 degrees, or when the juices run clear. If cooking the chicken
in two batches, add more coals and the remaining wood-chip packets
between batches and give the coals about 10 minutes, uncovered, to
rejuvenate.
<p>
   Serve the chicken with the reserved ginger-plum barbecue sauce.
<p>
   Per serving: 363 calories, 30 gm protein, 22 gm carbohydrates, 17
gm fat, 84 mg cholesterol, 4 gm saturated fat, 466 mg sodium
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-poultry-smoking </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0299 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-poultry-smoking
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Stephanie Witt Sedgwick </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; When the Smoke Clears: A Question of Gas vs. Charcoal &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   This subject is guaranteed to spark debate. On the gas-grill side,
you have something that's a breeze for even a novice to use. On the
charcoal-grill side, you have a method that requires knowing how to
build a fire using charcoal, through which you get flavors that can't
be duplicated by gas heating elements.
<p>
   It comes down to just what you want and how much trouble you're
willing to go to get it. If you're cooking items that are done in 25
minutes or less, and you don't want to fuss with charcoal, choose gas.
Gas grills heat up quickly, with controls similar to those on an oven
or stove.
<p>
   But if you want foods with a truly smoky flavor, there is only one
choice  &MD;  a traditional charcoal grill. Nothing replicates the flavors
imparted by charcoal, whether you're cooking a hamburger or lightly
smoking a turkey. And for that turkey, the charcoal grill's a
necessity. In fact, anything that needs to cook for more than 25
minutes should be cooked using indirect heat (otherwise you risk the
food's becoming charred on the outside long before it's done on the
inside).
<p>
   With the indirect method, coals are banked on opposite sides of the
grill's interior cavity, and food is placed in the middle of the grill
over a drip pan set between the two banks of charcoal. Soaked wood
chips are placed on top of the coals, and the grill is covered. Such
cooking produces items that have a light smoky flavor, moist meat and
a beautiful even color.
<p>
   You will find instructions for simulating indirect cooking in the
booklet that came with your gas grill, but, unfortunately, the results
are just not the same.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 0943EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-poultry-grill </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0157 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-poultry-grill
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Stephanie Witt Sedgwick </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Preparing the Grill for Indirect Cooking &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   At least 30 minutes before cooking the poultry, soak the wood chips
in water; set aside.
<p>
   Pile at least 20 pieces of charcoal or briquettes on one side of
the grill and an equal number on the opposite side, leaving space in
the middle for a drip pan to catch any juices or fat. When the coals
are covered with light gray ash, the fire is ready.
<p>
   Drain the wood chips. Using aluminum foil, form 6 packets of wood
chips. With the tip of a knife, make three 1-inch gashes in each
packet so the smoke can escape. Place 2 packets on each side of the
grill, on top of the coals (the rest will be added later). Place the
rack on the grill with the handles positioned over the coals (this
will allow you to add coals to the fire through the handle openings
without removing the rack).
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-ornamentals </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0648 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-ornamentals
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Charles Fenyvesi </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Putting Hostas Where They'll Do Best (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Not all the setbacks in the garden this year can be
laid at the foot of the drought.
<p>
   If your well-watered hostas and ferns have leaves curling up and
withering around the edges, they overdosed on sunlight.
<p>
   But if your lilies and daylilies, yuccas and yarrows, cannas and
black-eyed Susans offered puny blooms or only a few of them, they did
not receive enough sun. Transplanting may be in order; a switch of
locations seems to be called for, and you can prepare for the move
now.
<p>
   Getting shade-light requirements correct the first time is an
elusive skill even for practiced hands. That's because some shade
plants do best bathed in sunlight early or late in the day. Similarly,
plants listed for full sun may crave every sunbeam in Connecticut but
elsewhere they are happier if shielded from the hottest sunshine of
the day.
<p>
   Some hosta plants, those hardworking perennials for the shade
garden, look better when they get a few hours of sun. This applies
especially to those with gold or deep-green leaves, such as the
cultivars Sum and Substance, Green Fountain and Green Edge.
<p>
   Frequently, the malaise facing a new plant rests not with sun or
shade but age. The showiest hostas with the popular flaring leaves and
others with the much-admired wide and irregular margins in contrasting
colors take several years to give their best performance. And they may
never look precisely the same as catalog photographs because of
critical divergences in soil conditions and rainfall, nutrients and
temperature, as well as the quality of the light.
<p>
   When it comes to hosta clumps, remember the slogan of Paul Aden,
eminent hosta breeder and writer: ``The older the better.''
<p>
   But the extended periods of intense heat may be too much for many
of them, and those that look the most battered should be transplanted
where there is more shade. The same applies to ferns and astilbes,
Virginia bluebells and forget-me-nots.
<p>
   Once the heat breaks  &MD;  and relief is expected to last  &MD;  you can
transplant sunburnt shade-lovers into locations offering more shade.
For sun-lovers needing a new place out of the shade, wait until the
garden has cooled markedly in late September. The principal rule of
successful transplanting is that the new location should offer better
soil than the old.
<p>
   Putting plants in a small mound amended with compost or other humus
is pampering that pays dividends in better performance for years to
come. The transplants should be dug with as much soil around their
roots as possible and then moved carefully to the new location so the
soil does not break up. Do not lift the plant by its stem.
<p>
   After the rootball is embedded in the new, high-quality, porous
soil, a thorough soak is required: Keep the soil moist to touch for
about a week. A blanket of mulch is mandatory. In case of sun-lovers,
be extra careful; covering the plant with a white cloth for a week
provides the additional protection from the sun that the plant may
need.
<p>
   Bold spirits may decide to use the opportunity of a transplant to
break up clumps that have gotten too large. Hostas, astilbes and
daylilies in particular are famous for root systems that become too
dense and eventually compete for nutrients in such a way that leaf and
bloom production declines significantly.
<p>
   A razor-sharp straight-edged spade is the right tool for whacking
the roots in two, four or even eight equal parts. The cut should be
strong and decisive: brought down on the plant without any hesitation.
(A good saw or serrated kitchen knife also may work.)
<p>
   Some roots inevitably will be damaged and some tubers sliced, but
enough tissue will remain to start new lives in a new place, to spread
out and to spread joy.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 0944EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-gardening </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0711 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-gardening
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Garden, Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Adrienne Cook </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Sweet Potatoes That Live Up to Their Name (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Long forgotten in the home garden, sweet potatoes are
an ideal addition to the domestic plot: A few go a long way. With
sweet potatoes, a patch of 12 to 25 plants will produce enough for a
dozen meals, with a few extra for storing or planting out again next
year.
<p>
   There is a myth that these slow-to-mature, sun-loving plants must
have the 10-month growing season and heavy heat of the deep South to
produce their delicious tubers. The same is said of growing okra.
<p>
   In fact, both grow eagerly at more northerly latitudes, and not
just in the Washington area: Some, like Georgia Jet, thrive as far
north as Vermont.
<p>
   Garden-grown sweets are sweeter and more flavorful than those found
in supermarkets. To find out just how good they can be, sample some
first from a farmer's market. The first harvest will be coming in
shortly.
<p>
   When lifted and steamed lightly the same day, the young potatoes
reveal a gentle but unmistakable sweetness, undertones of buttery
nuttiness that recall hazelnut, and a texture that is flaky but never
doughy.
<p>
   When used immediately after harvest, the sugars have not yet turned
starchy, the texture is still very crunchy and moist. Freshly
harvested sweet potatoes always should be served alone  &MD;  don't even
think about candying these. Adding raisins or nuts or other
traditional sweet-potato accompaniments simply detracts from the pure
and delicate flavor. Later, in full maturity or after they have been
stored, sweet potatoes will take rougher treatment in the kitchen. But
with home-grown sweets, even the larger, mature tubers have greater
complexity of flavor than the store-bought versions and should be
served as ungarnished as possible.
<p>
   Sweet potatoes, though they may resemble their white Irish or Idaho
counterparts, are not related to the more-traditional tubers. Potatoes
are members of the nightshade family, which includes tomatoes,
eggplant and tobacco.
<p>
   Sweet potatoes actually are tropical morning glories. As such, they
are beautiful to grow. Their foliage is heart-shaped, ranging from
blue-green to burgundy and purple; flowers are trumpet-shaped and
generally pale pink or lilac.
<p>
   Sweet potatoes make a great ground cover. Growers with small
gardens should try bush-type varieties: Look for Vardaman and Bush
Porto Rico, which take three square feet per plant. They would look
attractive rolling down a hill or filling wide spaces between newly
planted perennials. I grew Centennial one year and loved the tubers
but found the plants to be as riotous as pumpkins.
<p>
   With hot summers, sweet potatoes grow luxuriantly, with few pests.
Japanese beetles enjoy them, but there's not much that Japanese
beetles won't go after. Spray foliage with a pyrethrum insecticide to
quell the beetles, but avoid spraying the flowers.
<p>
   Apart from these beetles, I never have had any other pest problems.
I grow sweets in organic-rich soil, almost pure leaf compost, to which
I add wood ash in the spring. They should not get too much nitrogen
but benefit from potassium and potash for root development.
<p>
   ``Slips,'' small sprouts taken from mature potatoes, are available
from garden centers in the spring and through mail-order catalogs.
They should be planted singly in warm ground  &MD;  mid-May in my rural
Virginia garden  &MD;  and spaced two feet apart.
<p>
   Although plants will thrive even with scant water, tubers will be
skimpy and the flesh less full-flavored if they are deprived of water.
This summer, I watered my sweets every 10 days and kept them heavily
mulched in between. The mulching is especially important because the
thick-stemmed vines, as they trail along the ground, send out small
rootlets to gather more moisture and nutrients.
<p>
   Tubers can be lifted 90 to 100 days after the slips went in. They
must be removed completely before the first frost. Frost can ruin the
flavor and texture of delicate sweets.
<p>
   Use nicked tubers first; store the rest in a dry place at room
temperature. Next year, make room for twice as many: You will want a
good deal more once you have tried them fresh from the garden.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 0944EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<STORYID cat=l pri=r sel=tm--a> X3183 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-gardening-tip </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0065 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-gardening-tip
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Garden editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Tip of the Week &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Leaf blight on English ivy can be controlled by thinning overgrown
vines, removing dead leaves and spraying with a basic copper spray.
<p>
   Die-back in vinca is probably caused by phomopsis blight, says
Maryland Home and Garden Information Center's David Clement. Remove
dead growth.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-tv-missamerica </SLUG>
<HEADER> adv0607   09-02 0664 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-tv-missamerica adv0607
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: TV editors) &QL; 
 &UR; Adv wknd sept 6-7 &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Michael E. Hill </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Check local listings for time and channel &QL; 
 &UR; One Piece Or Two? Other Questions Await `Miss America' &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Questions. There are so many questions. Really? Like what?
<p>
   Seldom in the 77-year history of the Miss America pageant have
questions loomed as such a large part of the proceedings.
<p>
   When the producers of Saturday's pageant (on ABC) looked for ways
to distinguish this year's ceremonies from those of the past, they
decided to give contestants a swimsuit-style option (one piece or
two?) and to have the interviews of the five finalists conducted by a
journalist rather than the hosts.
<p>
   Suddenly, many questions were in order.
<p>
   First of all, who would ask the questions?
<p>
   Pageant officials decided on Nancy Glass. Well, why Nancy Glass?
<p>
   ``They didn't say why they'd picked me,'' said Glass. ``They just
called and asked.''
<p>
   And she said yes, resolving one question. Then came another: What
should she ask? She gets to ask two questions of each of the final
five.
<p>
   To answer that question, Glass launched a world-wide search for
good questions.
<p>
   Naturally, that involved a Web site. And an old-fashioned radio
show.
<p>
   ``I asked for suggestions on my radio program,'' said Glass.
<p>
   So, what was the worst suggestion called in by a radio listener?
<p>
   ``Somebody suggested that I ask them whether they believe in
UFOs,'' said Glass. ``The radio-caller believed in them and said that
if a contestant didn't believe in them, she shouldn't be Miss
America.''
<p>
   So she won't be asking the fab five about UFOs. What will she be
asking?
<p>
   That's not a question. That's a secret.
<p>
   ``I have had some good suggestions,'' said Glass. ``I've heard some
I may use.''
<p>
   By the way, who is Nancy Glass?
<p>
   Until the syndicated ``American Journal'' went to a brother-sister
host team (Charles Perez and Michele Dabney-Perez), she had hosted the
program and been its senior correspondent since 1993. Before that, she
was the weekend anchor and senior correspondent for ``Weekend
Edition.''
<p>
   Her background in TV magazine shows also has included stints as
writer-host for ``This Evening,'' co-host of ``Attitudes'' on Lifetime
cable and host of PBS' ``Sneak Previews.''
<p>
   Her radio call-in program, ``The Nancy Glass Show,'' is broadcast
from Philadelphia on Saturday mornings.
<p>
   She and her husband, periodontist Mark Snyder, have two children,
Max and Sloane. In her spare time, Glass likes to scull on the
Schuylkill River. She is 39.
<p>
   In the course of her career, Glass has interviewed a ton of
celebrities and the likes of Marife Torres Nichols, wife of Oklahoma
City bombing defendant Terry Nichols, and serial killer Jeffrey
Dahmer.
<p>
   So, interviewing pageant finalists should be no problem. Glass said
she's already ruled out certain kinds of questions.
<p>
   Like what?
<p>
   ``I won't ask about a specific issue,'' she said. ``They are all
trained and have memorized answers when it comes to issues.''
<p>
   Glass said she's thinking in terms of one fun question and one
serious one.
<p>
   ``I have to ask hardball-softball questions designed to reveal who
they are rather than what they've managed to memorize,'' she said.
``That's hard. And I have to do it within a finite amount of time. And
the questions have to be equally hard for each contestant.''
<p>
   She will have two hours to zero in on the five finalists after
husband-wife co-hosts John Callahan and Eva LaRue Callahan of ``All My
Children'' have announced the cutdown from 10.
<p>
   Glass has other questions on her mind, too  &MD;  such as, what to wear
to Convention Hall on the biggest night in Atlantic City.
<p>
   ``No gown, no tiara,'' said Glass. ``They want me to dress in a
professional manner, not like a contestant.''
<p>
   Meanwhile, anyone with question suggestions can drop them by the
Web site, www.abc.com/missamerica
 &UR; End adv wknd sept 6-7 &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-tv-sisterwendy </SLUG>
<HEADER> adv0607   09-02 1078 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-tv-sisterwendy adv0607
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: TV editors) &QL; 
 &UR; Adv wknd sept 6-7 &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Harriet Winslow </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Check local listings for time and channel &QL; 
 &UR; In `Story of Painting,' Sister Wendy Explains It All for You &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Sister Wendy Beckett prefers to be alone. The now-familiar ``art
nun'' who lives in a trailer on the grounds of a Carmelite monastery
in Norfolk, England, wakes at 3 in the morning, prays seven hours a
day, then turns her attention to art for two more.
<p>
   She has combined her devotion to God with her passion for art since
1980, after a decade translating medieval Latin in her limited free
time. Before that, as a Sister of Notre Dame, she taught until
epilepsy forced her to stop.
<p>
   But Sister Wendy's routine has been interrupted more and more,
culminating in Sunday's ``Story of Painting,'' a five-part series on
PBS.
<p>
   In it, the South African-born, Oxford-educated 67 year old reveals
her passion for Western painting, from prehistoric cave art to Andy
Warhol, to encourage people to respond to art.
<p>
   The effort took more than 100 days of shooting on locations that
spanned 30,000 flying miles in 12 countries.
<p>
   But how does a nun go from writing about art in a trailer to
hosting her own globe-trotting television series?
<p>
   Well, first Sister Wendy landed a book contract on contemporary
women artists (the first of 15 she's written). She also became a
contributor to British art magazines, reviewing shows from her trailer
using postcard reproductions.
<p>
   BBC television producer Nicholas Rossiter took note, and in 1991
invited her to do a documentary on Britain's National Gallery, which
he thought would be fascinating considering she'd be gazing at
masterpieces she'd never seen  &MD;  but long studied.
<p>
   That led to ``Sister Wendy's Odyssey,'' six 10-minute films on her
rendezvous with art treasures around Britain  &MD;  which lucky American
viewers would catch if a PBS show, such as ``House of Eliott,'' ended
10 minutes before the hour. These ``Odyssey'' snippets attracted a lot
of attention, despite the fact that their short length prevented them
from being put on television schedules. They air still.
<p>
   Thankfully, another series, ``Sister Wendy's Grand Tour,'' and a
special, ``Pains of Glass,'' both aired here in late 1995, making her
easier to find. These took her to museums and churches all over
Europe. And now Sunday night is the first episode of the sweeping
``Sister Wendy's Story of Painting,'' told with her characteristic
insight, wit and total lack of shyness even when discussing nudes.
<p>
   A self-taught art scholar, Sister Wendy often brings an irreverent
attitude toward her subject. When she saw the sphinxes at the Temple
of Luxor in Egypt, she said, ``They look like garden gnomes.'' And at
Monet's garden at Giverny, France, she said, ``Much like any other
garden. We could have filmed this at Kew.''
<p>
   Sister Wendy reduces art theory to language as simple as her life.
Unwilling to use a telephone for an interview, she instead typed
answers to a reporter's questions, which were faxed to America.
<p>
   For example, on why humans express themselves through art: ``For
the artist, I suspect it is a need to create, to make visible a
vision, whatever the consequences. For the viewer, it is a sharing in
that vision which illuminates one's own truth,'' she wrote. ``But that
is a by-product, not the goal. I never think motives matter very much,
because we are so easily deceived by what we think they are. What
matters is the result, which can be judged.''
<p>
   Sister Wendy encourages her audience to take a good look before
judging. Even better, to grasp a context  &MD;  for which we have her
``Story of Painting.'' Each of the five episodes contains two
segments:
<p>
   Episode 1 begins with the roots of Western painting at prehistoric
caves in France, then stops at Pompeii and Iona, an island off the
coast of Scotland where the Book of Kells was discovered. Then she
travels to Italy to examine Giotto's earth-shaking works, done around
1300.
<p>
   Episode 2 covers the Renaissance through the 16th century.
<p>
   Episode 3 examines the Baroque movement throughout Europe in the
17th century.
<p>
   Episode 4 begins with how the French Revolution and the Industrial
Revolution influenced artists of the time; and continues with the
Impressionists in Paris and their descendants.
<p>
   Episode 5 follows modernism, which ``dared to be ugly,'' says
Sister Wendy, with a look at Cezanne and Picasso and Matisse. The
series wraps up with a trip to the United States to look at Pop Art
and Minimalism, then back to London to check out England's
contemporaries.
<p>
   Asked if the current attempts at expression could be considered
part of a low or a high period, Sister Wendy was cautious.
<p>
   ``The contemporaries of the Impressionists thought that they were
in a low period creatively. It took time to make it clear that this
was, on the contrary, a high. So it is too soon to be pessimistic
about the present artistic scene, I think. It may be better than it
seems!''
<p>
   Sister Wendy wants more people to have a brush with art, no matter
where they begin learning.
<p>
   ``I am a great lover of the chronological, and would always like to
begin at the beginning, which for painting, effectively, is Giotto,''
she said. ``But the timorous newcomer might feel more at home with the
Renaissance, and work backwards and forwards from it, or even the
Impressionists, which everybody responds happily to.''
<p>
   Her zest for explaining paintings seems endless, despite her
preference to live and work alone. She also shrinks from watching
herself on camera. ``When I've been forced to, because the producers
really wanted input, then I have watched and offered my opinion,'' she
said.
<p>
   But before she can return to her quiet life, Sister Wendy has more
art to explain.
<p>
   ``Paradoxically, the more painting I saw the more I yearned to do
something on sculpture, so yes: if I do `another project' (I am old
and tired, remember) it would be sculpture.''
<p>
   Until then, she'll live the quiet life.
<p>
   ``I came to solitude to live wholly for God. If I am now asked to
spend brief periods in non-solitude, it would be selfish to resist on
the grounds that I do not want to! So it fits in well with the ethos
of being a nun: not taking much notice of one's personal desire. Yet
since my deepest personal desire is to love God, this little sacrifice
fits in well!''
 &UR; End adv wknd sept 6-7 &QL; 
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Patricia Brennan </BYLINE>

</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Check local listings for time and channel &QL; 
 &UR; On PBS, `Truman,' `Lewis &AMP; Clark' and a British Nun on Art &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   PBS viewers get three documentaries by Ken Burns this season, one
of them tracing the journey of explorers Meriwether Lewis and William
Clark; a three-hour biography of Harry S. Truman; a search for
improved ways to protect the environment; and the return of veteran
series such as ``Masterpiece Theatre,'' ``Mystery,'' ``American
Masters,'' ``Frontline'' and ``Nova.''
<p>
   Aside from all that, PBS steps out with an unusual choice, ``Sister
Wendy's Story of Painting'' (Sunday) featuring the bespectacled,
Oxford-educated nun from Norfolk, England, who had rarely seen
television before the BBC sent her and a TV crew 30,000 miles through
12 countries to visit more than 40 galleries, churches, studios and
collections of paintings. She'll tell you all about them over five
weeks.
<p>
    &MD;  On Monday, three-hour ``Planet Neighborhood'' looks at ideas for
a better environment. William McDonough hosts.
<p>
    &MD;  On Sept. 15 is Michael Palin's third travel series, ``Full
Circle,'' a 10-parter visiting the countries of the Pacific Rim.
<p>
    &MD;  On Sept. 17, ``American Masters'' begins with a biography of poet
Allen Ginsberg (``Howl,'' ``Kaddish'').
<p>
    &MD;  On Sept. 22, ``The American Experience'' begins profiling
20th-century presidents, reprising ``TR, the Story of Theodore
Roosevelt,'' Sept. 22 and 23; ``FDR'' on Sept. 24 and 25; ``Ike''
Sept. 29, and offering a new ``Truman'' on Oct. 5 and 6.
<p>
    &MD;  ``Mystery!'' begins its 18th season Sept. 28 with ``Into the
Blue,'' starring John Thaw as Harry Barnett, a character created by
novelist Robert Goddard. He'll be followed by four stories featuring
Patricia Routledge as investigator Hetty Wainthropp.
<p>
    &MD;  ``Masterpiece Theatre'' returns Oct. 12 with ``The Mill on the
Floss,'' a two-hour story starring Emily Watson, and follows with Ann
Bronte's ``The Tenant of Wildfell Hall'' (Oct. 19 and 26) and starring
Tara Fitzgerald and Rupert Graves.
<p>
    &MD;  Ken Burns' ``Divided Highways: The Interstates and Transformation
of American Life'' on Oct. 22, traces the ideals, motives and methods
of the Interstate Highway system.
<p>
    &MD;  Burns' ``Lewis &AMP; Clark: The Journey of the Corps of Discovery,''
airing Nov. 4 and 5 and based on Stephen Ambrose's best-selling
``Undaunted Courage.'' Over four years, Burns and company covered the
same route that Meriwether Lewis, Thomas Jefferson's personal
secretary, and William Clark took when they left Virginia on May 14,
1804, to map and explore Jefferson's Louisiana Purchase.
<p>
    &MD;  Donna Murphy, Roger Rees, Campbell Scott and others star in
``Liberty! The American Revolution,'' a six-hour documentary beginning
Nov. 23.
<p>
    &MD;  In early 1998: Burns' film on architect Frank Lloyd Wright; the
four-part series ``Cancer Wars''; and ``The Face of Russia,'' a
digital production hosted by James H. Billington, the U.S. Librarian
of Congress, about art and architecture.

 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL;

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Labor Pains &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Cumulative trauma disorders, also known as repeated trauma
disorders or repetitive stress illnesses, have grown dramatically as a
cause of workers' compensation claims and absenteeism in the last
decade, according to the Bureau of Labor Statistics.
<p>
   These disorders, which range from carpal tunnel syndrome and
tendinitis to lower-back strain, are ``the biggest workplace health
problem today,'' according to the Occupational Safety and Health
Administration.
<p>
   Alan Couturier of the American College of Occupational and
Environmental Medicine says these injuries can be reduced by making
workstations more flexible, allowing workers appropriately timed rest
breaks and rotating tasks. Workers can help by getting proper
nutrition, exercise and sleep.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-health-vacation </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0185 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-vacation
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Sally Squires </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Good Feeling Fades Fast After Vacation, &QL; 
 &UR; Researchers Find &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   How long does it take for vacation afterglow to fade?
<p>
   Researchers from Tel Aviv University in Israel examined that
question in a study of 76 clerks at an electronics firm in central
Israel. Stress levels and feelings of job burnout were measured by
questionnaires twice before a two-week company shutdown for vacation,
once during the vacation and twice after the vacation.
<p>
   Results from the study, which appeared in the August issue of the
Journal of Applied Psychology, suggest that a vacation's good feeling
was already beginning to fade just three days after the study
participants returned to work. By three weeks after, stress levels and
feelings of burnout had returned to pre-vacation levels for all
participants.
<p>
   Women benefited most from vacations, the study found. And the
beneficial effects of a vacation faded more gradually for people who
reported enjoying their vacation the most.
<p>
   There may be a lesson in that.
</TEXT>

AP-NY-09-02-97 1050EDT &QL;

<SLUG> bc-health-pierce </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0320 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-pierce
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Susan Okie </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Mouth Piercing Can Lead to Problems, Dentists Say &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   With the growing popularity of body-piercing, dentists are becoming
concerned about complications that can occur when people have had
jewelry or metal devices placed in the tongue and other parts of the
mouth.
<p>
   Such piercing is done by unlicensed, self-trained operators,
without anesthesia. Pain and swelling are frequent, but other side
effects can include prolonged bleeding, chipped teeth, scarring and
infections, according to an article in the July issue of the Journal
of the American Dental Association.
<p>
   Shelia S. Price and Maurice W. Lewis of the West Virginia
University School of Dentistry described the case of a 20-year-old
patient, treated in their clinic for an unrelated wisdom-tooth
problem, who had pierced his tongue, lip and uvula (the pink tag of
tissue that hangs down in the back of the throat).
<p>
   He subsequently swallowed the uvula ornament when it accidentally
came out, but he still wore the barbell-shaped tongue ornament. Price
warned that such jewelry can fall out and obstruct the patient's
airway, can damage teeth during chewing, and can prevent dentists from
getting a good look at the teeth on dental X-rays. It may also impede
speech and increase the flow of saliva. Ornaments in the tongue (the
most common site for piercing in the mouth) can collect food debris
and other foreign material, increasing the risk of infection.
<p>
   Although there are no reliable statistics on the prevalence of
mouth-piercing, Price said dentists at the Morgantown clinic are
seeing an increasing number of patients with piercing of the tongue,
lips or cheeks, and wanted to alert colleagues to the risks, ``just in
the event that a patient who may be contemplating having piercing done
comes to seek the guidance of his or her dentist.''
</TEXT>

AP-NY-09-02-97 1051EDT &QL;

<SLUG> bc-health-trikes </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0395 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-trikes
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Don Colburn </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Even Toddlers on Trikes Benefit from Helmets, Study Suggests &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Preschool cyclists, even those on tricycles, benefit from wearing
helmets as much as older children, a national study suggests.
<p>
   ``Even young children who do not ride cycles in the street sustain
severe injuries and need protection from head injury,'' researchers
concluded. Although children under age 5 account for a small
percentage of bike-riding injuries, the new study found, their
injuries are comparable in severity to those of older children.
<p>
   The study is based on a nationwide database of pediatric trauma
cases, including 4,041 children hospitalized for bicycle-related
injuries. About 5 percent of the injured children  &MD;  219 in all  &MD;  were
under age 5; the rest were 5 to 14 years old. Tricycle-related
injuries were counted as bicycle injuries in the database.
<p>
   Almost none of the injured children  &MD;  less than 3 percent of the
preschoolers and about 3 percent of those aged 5 to 14  &MD;  wore a helmet
at the time of their injury. Head trauma was the most common serious
injury among both older and younger children hospitalized after
bicycling mishaps.
<p>
   While most injury-prevention efforts are aimed at school-age
children and adolescents, researchers said the wear-a-helmet message
should target preschoolers as well. An estimated 200,000 to 300,000
bicycles for preschool-age children are sold each year in the United
States.
<p>
   ``Helmet use by young children would likely prevent most head
injuries (in cyclists) and might help form habits that would result in
improved helmet use rates as these young cyclists grow older,''
researchers concluded.
<p>
   The study was conducted by a team from Children's Memorial Hospital
and Northwestern University Medical School in Chicago and Tufts-New
England Medical Center in Boston. The results were published in this
month's Annals of Emergency Medicine.
<p>
   Aside from the severity, which was similar, researchers found a
slightly different pattern of injuries in very young cyclists,
compared with older ones. Preschool riders are more likely than older
ones to be injured in the driveway or yard, and less likely to be hurt
in the street. They were also less likely than older riders to be
involved in crashes with motor vehicles.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-health-vaccine </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0309 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-vaccine
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Susan Okie </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Vaccine for Poultry Takes Aim at Salmonella &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   A new vaccine for poultry may soon be able to prevent many of the
more than 45,000 reported cases of food poisoning caused annually by
salmonella bacteria.
<p>
   Chickens are frequent carriers of  &UR; Salmonella enteritidis &LR; , a type
of bacteria that doesn't make the birds sick but can cause cramps,
fever and diarrhea in people. Raw or undercooked eggs (often used in
mayonnaise or salad dressing) are the most frequent source of
infection.
<p>
   Contaminated chicken or turkey also can produce the illness, which
is particularly dangerous for children, the elderly or people with
weakened immune systems. Because most cases of salmonella food
poisoning are undiagnosed, the number of Americans who suffer from it
annually may be as many as 4 million.
<p>
   Roy Curtiss III, a professor of biology at Washington University at
St. Louis, developed an oral vaccine for poultry that makes use of a
weakened strain of a related bacterium,  &UR; Salmonella typhimurium &LR; . The
vaccine strain is missing two genes that the bacteria need in order to
cause serious illness in birds.
<p>
   When given the vaccine, adult chickens develop lifelong immunity to
salmonella (including the strains that cause food poisoning in humans)
and pass on this immunity to their eggs and to their chicks.
<p>
   Curtiss presented results of initial trials of the vaccine in July
at the International Veterinary Vaccines and Diagnostics Conference in
Madison, Wis. Scientists at Megan Health Inc., a St. Louis company
that will produce the vaccine, are awaiting approval from the
Department of Agriculture to conduct larger field trials, involving
thousands of birds. If those studies are successful, the USDA could
license the vaccine next year.
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; KIDS' TV: What's Hot for Younger Viewers &QL;

Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles in ``Ninja Turtles: The Next Mutation''
