bc-tv-daytime adv07 a0801
 &UR; (ATTN: Entertainment editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
 &UR; Adv sun sep 7 &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Susan King </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; A Kinder, Gentler Daytime (Hollywood) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   HOLLYWOOD  &MD;  Nazi biker chicks and senior citizens married to
Generation Xers will not be appearing on any of the four syndicated
daytime series premiering Monday  &MD;  unless, perhaps, they're sharing
cooking tips or doing a comedy routine.
<p>
   Thanks to the enormous success of ``The Rosie O'Donnell Show,''
dysfunctional daytime TV seems to be out and good clean fun,
entertainment and information are in.
<p>
   ``Rosie has clearly shown that there is room for clean TV,'' says
Gayle King, Emmy Award-winning news anchor and Oprah Winfrey's good
friend. She appears on the daytime scene as host of ``The Gayle King
Show.''
<p>
   ``Variety and talk are very appealing,'' says Los Angeles sports
anchor Fred Roggin, who hosts the talk-variety series ``The Arthel &AMP;
Fred Show'' with Arthel Neville, late of ``Extra.''
<p>
   ``People who run TV stations want to have a certain image,'' Roggin
says. ``It's a kinder, gentler time, and the people who program the TV
stations want to have a good feeling about their TV stations. I think
that's why these shows are coming back.''
<p>
   Joining ``Arthel &AMP; Fred'' and ``The Gayle King Show'' this week are
``The Home Team With Terry Bradshaw'' and ``Martha Stewart Living.''
(Two other new daytime shows already have a jump-start. Last month,
ABC added Barbara Walters' talk series ``View'' to its lineup, and
Fox's hip but struggling ``Fox After Breakfast'' morning show was
rechristened ``The Vicki Lawrence Show.'')
<p>
   Having fun is the main objective of ``Arthel &AMP; Fred.'' The hourlong
series will feature a live band, celebrity guests and field pieces.
<p>
   ``Because we are both broadcasters, we are of the mind you have to
get out there with people,'' Neville explains. ``We work well with
people.''
<p>
   ``We really want our guests to have fun,'' offers Roggin, who is
continuing his sports duties on NBC's L.A. affiliate, KNBC. ``We are
two people who enjoy having fun. We want people to be relaxed and want
them to be part of the party.''
<p>
   Martha Stewart, queen of ``how-to'' TV and publishing, has adapted
her former weekend series to weekdays. The daily version ``is truly
alternative television,'' she says. ``It's an alternative to talk.
It's an alternative to junk. That's my objective.''
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   ``Martha Stewart Living'' will be similar to her old series but
will make some adaptations on the theory that the available viewing
audience will be much more dominated by women. The daily version,
Stewart says, will feature such new segments as tool of the week,
cookie of the week and cake of the month. She also is introducing an
interactive Web site on the program, so viewers can print out recipes
and information while watching the show.
<p>
   ``I'm trying to fill every single minute with information that is
valuable and desirable,'' says Stewart, who built a state-of-the-art
studio in Connecticut to produce the series. ``The motto of the show
is, `You learn something new every day.' ''
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   ``The Gayle King Show'' will be following Stewart in most markets .
``The thinking is that Martha does stuff for your house and Gayle does
stuff for the people in your house,'' says King, who will continue to
anchor the 5:30 p.m. newscast at WFSB-TV in Hartford, Conn.
<p>
   Unlike ``Arthel &AMP; Fred,'' King's talk show is not celebrity-driven.
``I'm convinced you can do a show about normal people,'' says King,
who tapes the program in Hartford. ``There aren't any new topics.
Every damn thing has been done, so our challenge is presenting it in a
way that people will not mind seeing it again.''
<p>
   King will explore a new topic each day. ``I'm fascinated by
relationships and shows that are oriented toward kids,'' says the
divorced mother of two children. ``What I hope is that you will feel
comfortable watching it.''
<p>
   Terry Bradshaw, famed Pittsburgh Steeler quarterback and Fox ``NFL
Sunday'' broadcaster, describes his new series as ``viewer-friendly.
We're noncombative with our guests. Hopefully, when the show is over,
the viewer at home has a smile on their face.''
<p>
   The hourlong ``Home Team'' combines celebrity interviews with
informational segments on cooking, health, fitness, home repair and
stories of human interest. ``Home Team'' members include ``Ms. Fix
It'' Kim Gordon and roving reporter Eric Tunney. Bradshaw will also
chat, a la Art Linkletter, with kids.
<p>
   Bradshaw, who acknowledges he's still trying to find a
``comfortable range'' as host, says he could never have done a tabloid
daytime show. ``I see life through the eyes of a child,'' he says. ``I
see nothing but the fun of life.''
<p>
    &UR; End adv sun sep 7 &QL; 
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Editors: Check local listings for program times, channels. &QL; 
 &UR; SELECTED TELEVISION PROGRAMS FOR TUESDAY, Sept. 9 &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   ``Dalziel and Pascoe''  &MD;  A string of stranglings are accompanied by

Southern mansion. TLC

   ``Caroline in the City''  &MD;  Repeat: The final three episodes from
last season bring viewers up to speed on the cartoonist (Lea Thompson)
and her love life. In the cliffhanger airing, Caroline admits her true
feelings for Richard (Malcolm Gets), whose relationship with a former
flame (Sofia Milos) has heated up. NBC
<p>
   ``Grace Under Fire''  &MD;  Repeat: Jimmy feels left out when Rob's
bonding with Grace and the kids grows stronger. ABC
<p>
   ``Cries From the Heart''  &MD;  In this 1994 TV movie based on a true
story, a teacher (Patty Duke) and the mother (Melissa Gilbert) of an
autistic student (Bradley Pierce) disagree over his care and
development. In queston: the use of an experimental technique
facilitating communication through a computer. CBS
<p>
   SPORTS:
<p>
   Beach Volleyball: Women's Pro League, ESPN
<p>
   Baseball: Marlins at Padres, FX
<p>
   MOVIES:
<p>
   ``The Long, Hot Summer'' AMC
<p>
   ``A Family Thing'' TMC
<p>
   PROFILE:
<p>
   ``Wallace Stegner: A Writer's Life'' PBS

 &UR; Editors: Check local listings for program times, channels. &QL; 
 &UR; SELECTED TELEVISION PROGRAMS FOR MONDAY, Sept. 8 &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   ``The Arthel &AMP; Fred Show''  &MD;  Former ``Extra'' anchor Arthel Neville
and sportscaster Fred Roggin co-host a new weekday show featuring
celebrity interviews and audience interaction. Syndicated
<p>
   ``Martha Stewart Living''  &MD;  The weekly series becomes a daily
fixture, with Stewart offering tips and ideas on cooking, gardening
and entertaining. Syndicated
<p>
   ``The Gayle King Show''  &MD;  The New England TV personality examines
family, relationships, parenting, health and fitness in another new
weekday entry. Syndicted
<p>
   ``Monday Night Football''  &MD;  The players and coaches change, but the
rivalry continues as Kansas City travels to Oakland for a date game
with the rowdy Raiders. ABC
<p>
   ``Body Doubles: The Twin Experience''  &MD;  No need to adjust your
sets. This documentary reports on a variety of identical twins, from
those together their entire lives to others who spent decades apart.
It also raises questions about the interaction of environment and the
genetic process. HBO
<p>
   ``Melrose Place''  &MD;  With Grant Show, Courtney Thorne-Smith and
Laura Leighton out of the complex, what will that mean in terms of
storylines for those left behind? Find out in the sixth season
premiere. Fox
<p>
   ``Planet Neighborhood''  &MD;  A three-hour program in which
eco-architect William McDonough travels across country  &MD;  from
Burlington, Vt. to Sacramento  &MD;  in search of innovative environmental
technologies at home, the workplace and in the community. ``All of
us,'' says McDonough, ``are inevitably connected.'' PBS
<p>
   SPORTS:
<p>

<p>
   ``Religion &AMP; Ethics Newsweekly''  &MD;  Veteran journalist Bob Abernethy
hosts a new PBS series providing religious and ethical perspectives on
domestic and foreign events and the arts. PBS
<p>
   ``Men Are From Mars, Women Are From Venus ... ''  &MD;  Think of this
two-hour special as ``The Real World'' for married couples. ABC News
puts the theories of author John Gray to the test as it follows six
couples over eight months to determine if they can improve their
relationships. ABC
<p>
   ``Sister Wendy's Story of Painting''  &MD;  Sister Wendy Beckett, the
67-year-old British nun and art maven (whom the media has called
``both a consecrated virgin and pop star''), guides viewers through
great eras of Western art: the cave pintings of Lascaux; Goya's
revolutionary Spain; Monet's Giverny and Picasso's Paris and Andy
Warhol's New York. PBS
<p>
   ``The Italians''  &MD;  A look at the writers, photographers, fashion
designers and luxury cars (we're talking Ferrari here) of Italy.
Actress Linda Hunt narrates. Discovery Channel
<p>
   ``Soap Opera Update Awards''  &MD;  Emma Samms and Kristoff St. John
co-host the eighth annual special honoring actors and actresses from
each of the daytime dramas. Marcy Walker, Kathleen Noone and Peter
Bergman are among the scheduled presenters. Lifetime
<p>
   ``This Week in Business''  &MD;  A new series featuring in-depth
reporting and analysis of the people and forces behind business
developments with host Sheilah Kast, a former ABC News correspondent.
PBS
<p>
   SPORTS:
<p>
   Football: Packers at Eagles, FOX
<p>
   Tennis: U.S. Open, CBS
<p>
   Baseball: White Sox at Indians, ESPN

<p>
   ``The Parent 'Hood'' WB

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Good morning, Feature, Entertainment, Travel, Book editors. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service feature budget
for Tuesday, Sept. 2, 1997. The feature editor is in Los Angeles at
(800) 283-NEWS, ext. 77832, or (213) 237-7832. Other key phone numbers
for repeats or general questions, are listed at the bottom of this
advisory.
<p>
    &UR; Category codes of stories are in parentheses. &QL; 
<p>
   Food:
<p>
    &UR; (All category d.) &LR; 
<p>
   FOOD-BURMESE  &MD;  Welcome to a potluck dinner party featuring the
cuisine of Myanmar. 505 words, by Barbara Hansen (Times) (d). With
FOOD-BURMESE-RECIPES. Three photos (TPN2-4).
<p>
   FOOD-COD  &MD;  Poor cod has been misunderstood, misjudged and maligned,
but champions of the great North Atlantic white fish range from Julia
Child to Mark Bittman, author of the encyclopedic ``Fish.'' 625 words,
by Deborah Hornblow (Courant). With FOOD-COD-RECIPES. One photo
(TPN5).
<p>
   FOOD-TAYLOR  &MD;  For Steve Taylor, with 24 years of cooking
experience, it's quality over quantity. 645 words, by Camille Whelan
(Sun). One photo (TPN6).
<p>
   FOOD-SCHOOLLUNCH  &MD;  Health again is the byword for children's school
lunches, but parents can still be creative with this meal. 935 words,
by Maria Hiaasen (Sun special). With FOOD-SCHOOLLUNCH-RECIPE.
<p>
   POULTRY  &MD;  It's around now, when the coals are getting ready to cool
for the season, that a little alfresco magic is in order; smoke gets
in your poultry; tips and recipes. 1,375 words, by Nancy McKeon and
Stephanie Witt Sedgwick (Post). With POULTRY-SMOKING and
POULTRY-GRILL, all moved (d).
<p>
   FORKLORE  &MD;  French lesson: It's batter up for the pancake. By
Charles Perry (Times).
<p>
   FOOD-BITES  &MD;  Tidbits from the food industry (Times).
<p>
   RECIPE-FINDER  &MD;  Pear dishes are light, delicious. By Ellen Hawks
(Sun).
<p>
   RUSH-HOUR-REMEDY  &MD;  Campbell's restaurant soup a pricey wannabe. By
Maria Hiaasen (Sun special).
<p>
   ON-THE-FRIDGE  &MD;  Pig out on web site on pork; food notes (Post) (d).
Moved.
<p>
   VINTAGE-POINT  &MD;  Hungary's dessert nectar making a comeback. By
Michael Dresser (Sun).
<p>
   WINE-OF-THE-WEEK  &MD;  1995 Domaine de Pierredon Cotes du Rhone.By
Michael Dresser (Sun).
<p>
   Travel:
<p>
   TRAVEL-WALES  &MD;  SWANSEA, Wales  &MD;  In Dylan Thomas Square in the heart
of the marina, the apple-cheeked children of weekend sailors clamber
merrily over the poet's statue; this once-grimy port has become
Swinging Swansea, the heart of the Welsh Riviera. 1,465 words, by
Barry Zwick (Times) (t). With TRAVEL-WALES-INFO. Three photos
(TPN8-10).
<p>
   TRAVEL-WESTERNFRONT  &MD;  Popular interest in World War I sites, and
the conflict that largely shaped this century, appears to be on the
rise; superintendents of World War I American military cemeteries
report an increasing number of visitors, including Americans. 1,445
words, by Dave Drury (Courant) (t). With TRAVEL-WESTERNFRONT-INFO. Two
photos (TPN11,12).
<p>
   TRAVEL-HOMER  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  Winslow Homer's debut piece, ``Home,
Sweet Home,'' an essay in American male values, is on exhibit at
Washington's National Gallery through Sept. 28. 680 words, by Paul
Richard (Post). With TRAVEL-HOMER-BOX, both moved (t).
<p>
   TOURS&AMP;CRUISES  &MD;  Smell the roses, stop to see New Zealand. By Karin
Dominello (Times) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-TIPS  &MD;  Timely tips for today's traveler. By Jane Engle
(Times) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-ECONOMY  &MD;  More bang for the buck. By Barbara Shea (Newsday)
(t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-NOTES  &MD;  Take bug precautions if going to Disney World. By
Barbara Shea (Newsday) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-PLANNER  &MD;  Commemorate Black History Month in Africa. By
Martin Hollander (Newsday) (t).
<p>
   FEARLESS-TRAVELER  &MD;  Travel Q&AMP;A. By James T. Yenckel (Post) (t).
<p>
   WORLDWISE  &MD;  Travel notes from all over (Post special) (t).
<p>
   There is no CRUISE feature (Post) this week.
<p>
   Entertainment and the arts:
<p>
   MUSIC-CRYSTALMETHOD  &MD;  The duo behind the Crystal Method is
releasing this week its highly anticipated debut album ``Vegas,''
which is being billed by some as America's answer to British
electronic pop, with its West Coast ``break beat'' sound. 990 words,
by D. James Romero (Times) (e). One photo (TPN7).
<p>
   TV-DAYTIME  &MD;  HOLLYWOOD  &MD;  Thanks to the enormous success of ``The
Rosie O'Donnell Show,'' dysfunctional daytime TV seems to be out and
good clean fun, entertainment and information are in. 850 words, by
Susan King (Times) (e). Sunday advance.
<p>
   TV-SISTERWENDY  &MD;  Sister Wendy's routine has been interrupted more
and more, culminating in Sunday's ``Story of Painting,'' a five-part
series on PBS; in it, Sister Wendy reveals her passion for Western
painting, from prehistoric cave art to Andy Warhol, to encourage
people to respond to art. 1,000 words, by Harriet Winslow (Post) (e).
Weekend advance, moved Tuesday.
<p>
   TV-PBS-PREVIEW  &MD;  PBS viewers get three documentaries by Ken Burns
this season, one of them tracing the journey of explorers Meriwether
Lewis and William Clark; a three-hour biography of Harry S. Truman; a
search for improved ways to protect the environment; and the return of
veteran series such as ``Masterpiece Theatre,'' ``Mystery,''
``American Masters,'' ``Frontline'' and ``Nova.'' 500 words, by
Patricia Brennan (Post) (e). Weekend advance, moved Tuesday.
<p>
   TV-MISSAMERICA  &MD;  Seldom in the 77-year history of the Miss America
pageant have questions loomed as such a large part of the proceedings.
600 words, by Michael E. Hill (Post) (e). Weekend advance, moved
Tuesday.
<p>
   TV-CABLE  &MD;  Paul Newman, who combines an abundance of talent with
amazing good looks, made even better movies; this week, American Movie
Classic features five of the memorable ones. 600 words, by Martie Zad
(Post) (e). Weekend advance, moved Thursday.
<p>
   TV-VIDEOS  &MD;  An unusual entry into the made-for-video market debuts
Tuesday, ``Casper, A Spirited Beginning''; the film combines
live-action and a cast of familiar faces with animation for a mix that
produces an extra helping of that lovable Casper flavor. 500 words, by
Martie Zad (Post) (e). Weekend advance, moved Tuesday. With TV-PICKS
(e), weekend advance, moved Tuesday.
<p>
   ROSENBERG  &MD;  This time, `Defenders' will hold court on showtime. By
Howard Rosenberg (Times) (e).
<p>
   TV-CARMODY  &MD;  John Carmody on TV (Post) (e).
<p>
   TV-KIDS  &MD;  What's hot for younger viewers. Lee Harris (Times) (e).
Sunday advance.
<p>
   TV-TIPS  &MD;  Best bets for Wednesday (Times) (e). Sunday advance,
moved.
<p>
   MAGAZINES  &MD;  Current magazine articles and industry trends. (Post)
(e).
<p>
   Lifestyle and people:
<p>
   ROESSNER  &MD;  The final words of William S. Burroughs. By Barbara T.
Roessner (Courant) (c).
<p>
   SOURCE  &MD;  The Reliable Source: Notes on newsmakers. By Annie Groer
and Ann Gerhart (Post) (w).
<p>
   There is no MULTICULTURAL-MANNERS (Times special) feature this
week. It moves every other Tuesday.
<p>
   Home and garden:
<p>
   HOMEWORK  &MD;  Wood may be the problem in caulking cedar siding. By
Karol V. Menzie and Randy Johnson (Sun) (l).
<p>
   GARDENING  &MD;  Sweet potatoes that live up to their name. By Adrienne
Cook (Post special). With GARDENING-TIP, both moved (l).
<p>
   ORNAMENTALS  &MD;  Putting hostas where they'll do best. By Charles
Fenyvesi (Post special) (l). Moved.
<p>
   Books:
<p>
    &UR; (All category e.) &LR; 
<p>
   BOOKS-JUNGER  &MD;  The long and winding road for Sebastian Junger, 35,
has led him to a national tour promoting ``The Perfect Storm,'' his
finely written, terrifying tale of an awesomely powerful storm that
struck the Northeast in October 1991 and took the lives of six men
aboard a Gloucester, Mass., swordfishing boat that sank without a
signal, leaving only some deck gear to float to shore. 1,295 words, by
Anthony Day (Times) (e). One photo (TPN1).
<p>
   BOOKS-ANGELOU  &MD;  ``Even the Stars Look Lonesome,'' by Maya Angelou.
Reviewed by Glenn McNatt (Sun).
<p>
   BOOKS-MENGIVING  &MD;  ``Men Giving Money, Women Yelling,'' by Alice
Mattison. Reviewed by Carole Goldberg (Courant).
<p>
   BOOKS-CUCKOOLAND  &MD;  ``Last Days in Cloud Cuckooland,'' by Graham
Boynton. Reviewed by Gilbert A. Lewthwaite (Sun).
<p>
   BOOKS-RESENTMENT  &MD;  ``Resentment,'' by Gary Indiana. Reviewed by Ben
Neihart (Sun Special).
<p>
   BOOKS-MIRROR  &MD;  ``The Mirror,'' by Lynn Freed. Reviewed by Heller
McAlpin (Newsday special).
<p>
   BOOKS-EDER  &MD;  James Salter's memoir matches his lofty style. Richard
Eder (Times).
<p>
   BOOKS-MCCLURG  &MD;  `Cold Mountain' a hot commodity. By Jocelyn McClurg
(Courant).

   For questions about stories from:

<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-health-diet </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1051 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-diet
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Sally Squires </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Dieters' Persistence Can Pay Off, Study Finds &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Yo-yo dieters take heart. Just when it seemed that successful
weight loss could be as elusive as an icy day in summer, a team of
University of Pittsburgh researchers has found evidence suggesting
otherwise.
<p>
   In an attempt to better understand successful long-term weight
loss, the team has studied nearly 800 people who shed a minimum of 30
pounds and kept the weight off for nearly six years.
<p>
   These were not simply lucky first-time dieters. The participants in
the study were hard-core, weight-gaining recidivists who had lost an
average total of 270 pounds each during their lives. Nearly half of
the participants, whose average age was 45, had been overweight since
early childhood, and another 25 percent had put on unwanted pounds
from age 12 to 18.
<p>
   Through the years ``they had done a lot of weight cycling,'' said
Mary Lou Klem, senior research fellow at the University of Pittsburgh
School of Medicine and lead author of the ongoing study, which was
reported in the August issue of the American Journal of Clinical
Nutrition.
<p>
   Unlike the majority of dieters who usually regain their lost pounds
and often add more, the study found that these people ``have tried a
lot of things to lose weight and finally found a combination that
worked for them,'' Klem said.
<p>
   Some weight-loss experts said the findings parallel work from other
addiction fields, such as smoking cessation.
<p>
   ``Most people who quit smoking successfully do it on their own, and
we have learned that it takes four or five tries,'' said psychiatrist
Albert Stunkard, a weight-loss researcher at the University of
Pennsylvania. The weight registry study ``shows that persistence pays
off (in dieting),'' Stunkard said. ``It's a wonderful, optimistic
message and would be very nice to counter the terrible pessimistic
message that people can't lose weight.''
<p>
   ``This is a superb study,'' Stunkard said. ``It confirms earlier
smaller studies in a very dramatic way with this large sample size.''
<p>
   People in the study were recruited from national advertisements in
newspapers and magazines for people who had successfully lost weight
and kept it off. The researchers then confirmed the weight loss in a
variety of ways, including documentation from participants'
physicians, interviews with family members or friends and photographs.
<p>
   The majority of participants in the survey came from families that
had a tendency to be overweight, a fact that placed them at higher
risk of becoming heavy themselves.
<p>
   All the people in the study were veteran dieters who had attempted
nearly everything to shed unwanted pounds.
<p>
   ``Let me count the ways I've tried to lose weight,'' said Johari
Rashad, who works at the Department of Labor and has successfully
trimmed more than 67 pounds from her 5-foot-3-inch frame. She tried a
commercial weight-loss program that helped at first but then lost its
effect. ``I've done a modified fast that didn't work ... and in one of
my really crazy days, I took liquid protein that really didn't work
and was dangerous besides.''
<p>
   Yet it was often these failures to control their weight that helped
participants in the registry manage to figure out how to successfully
shed pounds and keep them off.
<p>
   Nearly all participants  &MD;  some 77 percent  &MD;  said they had a strong
impetus for launching their weight-reduction course. For about a third
of the participants, the trigger was a relatively minor health
problem, such as varicose veins, sleep apnea, low-back pain, aching
legs or simply fatigue caused by their extra pounds.
<p>
   Dieters often search for the perfect way to lose weight and keep it
off. But the study found that there was no one successful route to
shedding pounds. ``It's further condemnation of the faddish diet
books,'' said Kelly Brownell, a professor of psychology who studies
weight loss at Yale University.
<p>
   Each of their failed attempts, however, appeared to teach valuable
lessons about past diet mistakes and to familiarize the dieters with
weight-loss techniques that ultimately worked more effectively. No one
found any ``miracle method'' to lose weight.
<p>
   ``This may go back to the theory that when you're trying to change
behavior, you will try and fail, but if you try long enough you will
ultimately be successful,'' Klem said, noting that for this group ``it
took them until their 40s (and older) to get successful.''
<p>
   Nearly half of those in the registry lost weight on their own.
Slightly more than half the participants said they either consulted a
physician for help or joined organized weight-loss programs such as
Overeaters Anonymous, a 12-step program for compulsive eaters
patterned after Alcoholics Anonymous, and Weight Watchers, the
well-known commercial weight-loss system.
<p>
   Most simply did what diet experts have been advising for years:
They reduced the amount of calories that they ate and increased their
physical activity. Nearly all restricted their intake of certain types
or classes of foods, such as high-fat, high-calorie meals.
<p>
   Counting calories was important to about 40 percent of those who
succeeded in losing weight. So was keeping track of fat grams, a
technique employed by about 25 percent of those in the registry. About
20 percent used liquid diets to help curb their weight, but only 4
percent relied on any diet drugs, most of which were not available
when participants lost their unwanted pounds. The prescription weight
loss drug Redux, for example, was only approved for use last year by
the Food and Drug Administration.
<p>
   There was also a strong commitment to regular exercise, something
that other studies have found is key to successful weight loss as
well. People in the registry reported doing one to two activities
nearly every day and also made lifestyle changes such as walking
stairs rather than taking the elevator.
<p>
   One of the surprising results of the study was that a large
minority of the people in the registry found that maintaining weight
loss was easier than researchers expected. Slightly more than 40
percent of people in the weight registry said keeping off the pounds
was easier than actually shedding weight.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-health-snore </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0159 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-snore
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Nicole Lewis </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Snore-Free Sleeping &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Physicians from the American Academy of Otolaryngology-Head and
Neck Surgery, warn that snoring is not a condition to joke about.
Twenty-five percent of adults snore on a regular basis; 45 percent of
adults snore off and on.
<p>
   These noisy sounds signal that the sleeper may suffer from
excessive bulkiness of throat tissue or obstructed nasal passages, a
problem that could be life-threatening if not treated. For the light
snorer, physicians recommend sleeping on the side, avoiding alcohol
and heavy food for a few hours before bedtime and maintaining regular
sleep patterns.
<p>
   For a copy of the free brochure ``Doctor, Can You Cure My
Snoring?'' send a self-addressed, stamped, business-size envelope to
Snoring c/o American Academy of Otolaryngology-Head and Neck Surgery,
One Prince Street, Alexandria, Va. 22314-3357.
</TEXT>

people declared innocent but awaiting prosecutor appeals; his success
has made him a hero at home and a model for reform in other countries.
1,175 words, by Sebastian Rotella (Times) (i). Upcoming.

well-documented but little-scrutinized sterilization program are going
public with their anger and grief; the program was abandoned only in
the 1970s after 62,888 state-sponsored procedures. 1,530 words, by
Dean E. Murphy (Times) (i). With SWEDEN-STERILE-VICTIM. Upcoming.

diplomatic clout into the effort seems to have dispelled the gloom and
inertia that prevailed through much of last year. 825 words, by Thomas
W. Lippman (Post) (w).

<SLUG> bc-food-cod-recipes </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0843 09-02 1425 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-food-cod-recipes - a0843
 &UR; (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Hartford Courant
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Pity the Poor, Misunderstood Cod: The Recipes (Hartford) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   HARTFORD, Conn.  &MD;  Chef Billy Grant recently ran a cod special at
his Restaurant Bricco in West Hartford. He recommends buying
Prosciutto di Parma at specialty stores.
<p>
    &UR; RESTAURANT BRICCO'S COD FISH WRAPPED WITH PROSCIUTTO di PARMA
BEANS
<p>
   1½ cups flageolet or white beans
<p>
   Water
<p>
   3 tablespoons olive oil
<p>
   1 clove garlic, smashed
<p>
   1 onion, finely diced
<p>
   2 medium carrots, diced
<p>
   1 rib celery, diced
<p>
   1 leek, trimmed, washed and sliced thin
<p>
   Fresh thyme
<p>
   1 cup Chardonnay
<p>
   Extra virgin olive oil (if needed)
<p>
   6 plum tomatoes, seeded and diced
<p>
   6 basil leaves, finely sliced
<p>
   2 tablespoons unsalted butter
<p>
   FISH
<p>
   6 8-ounce cod fish fillets (center cut, from the thickest part of
the
<p>
   fillet, or ``captain's cut'' are best)
<p>
   Freshly cracked black pepper
<p>
   Kosher salt
<p>
   Fresh thyme
<p>
   Fresh sage
<p>
   About 9 paper-thin slices of Prosciutto di Parma
<p>
   Olive oil
<p>
   SAUCE:
<p>
   1 shallot, diced
<p>
   1½ cups Chardonnay
<p>
   ¼ cup bean broth
<p>
   ¼ cup reserved bean broth
<p>
   2 tablespoons unsalted butter
<p>
   Rinse and wash beans; remove any stones. Soak beans overnight in a
large pot with water to cover by 1 inch, changing water once or twice.
<p>
   The next day, drain the beans. In a large saute pan, heat 3
tablespoons of olive oil over medium heat. Saute the garlic and onion
until onion is almost translucent. Add carrots, celery, leeks and
about 1 tablespoon of fresh thyme (leaves only). Saute until
vegetables are slightly softened, about 5 minutes. Add Chardonnay,
beans and enough water to cover all. Simmer the mixture until beans
are cooked through. (They should not be ``al dente,'' but quite tender
to the bite.) Drain the bean and vegetable mix, reserving the bean
broth. (This may be done 1 day ahead of serving. Store the beans and
liquid separately in the refrigerator overnight.
<p>
   Drizzle the beans with some olive oil so they won't dry out. Next
day, reheat beans on low heat before serving, and thin with some bean
broth if they look dry.)
<p>
   To serve the beans: Heat the bean mixture with ½ cup of reserved
bean broth. Add tomatoes and fresh basil, and cook over low heat until
broth is evaporated or absorbed. Add butter and stir to combine.
<p>
   To prepare the fish: Preheat the oven to 400 degrees F. Season each
fillet with salt and pepper, and sprinkle with fresh thyme and sage.
Wrap each cod fillet in prosciutto, wrapping it tightly to keep it
secure.
<p>
   Set a nonstick skillet over medium high heat. Add 2 tablespoons of
olive oil. When oil is hot but not smoking, add cod fillets, sealed
side down, and brown, about 2 minutes on each side. (Do not crowd them
or they will not brown. Cook in batches if necessary.) Transfer
fillets to an oven-proof pan, and place pan in oven. Roast until cod
is just cooked through, 5 to 10 minutes, depending on the thickness of
the fillets.
<p>
   While fish is roasting, make the sauce: Drain oil from the skillet
and add diced shallot and cook 1 minute. Deglaze the pan by adding 1½
cups Chardonnay. Reduce the liquid to ¼ cup. Add ¼ cup of reserved
bean broth, and reduce a bit. Whisk in butter, and season with salt
and pepper.
<p>
   To serve: Spoon a mound of bean stew on each of 6 plates. Set a cod
fillet atop each one, and drizzle with Chardonnay sauce. Serve
immediately. Serves 6.
<p>
   Chef Patty Queen at the Cottage Restaurant in Plainville provided
this recipe. ``Classic New England chowder is not thickened. You can
leave out the flour and butter or add more depending on your personal
tastes,'' she says.
<p>
    &UR; THE COTTAGE'S COD &AMP; CORN CHOWDER
<p>
   1/4 pound bacon or salt pork, diced small
<p>
   2 medium onions, cut into ½-inch dice
<p>
   2 bay leaves
<p>
   1 tablespoon chopped fresh thyme
<p>
   ½ cup butter
<p>
   1/3 cup flour
<p>
   3 cups clam broth or juice, or chicken or fish stock
<p>
   5 potatoes, cut into ½-inch cubes
<p>
   6 ears fresh corn, kernels removed
<p>
   2 cups heavy cream
<p>
   2 pounds cod fillet, cut into 2-inch pieces
<p>
   Salt
<p>
   Freshly ground pepper
<p>
   2 tablespoons chopped fresh parsley
<p>
   In a large soup kettle, saute the bacon or salt pork over medium
heat until slightly crisp and fat is rendered. Do not brown.
<p>
   Add the onion, bay leaves, thyme and butter and saute until onion
is translucent. Add flour, stirring constantly, and cook until flour
is well incorporated, about 3 minutes.
<p>
   Add broth or stock slowly, stirring constantly to prevent lumps
from forming. Add potatoes and corn and simmer until potatoes are
tender, about 12 minutes.
<p>
   Add the cod pieces and heavy cream, simmering until cod flakes
apart. Season with salt and pepper. Ladle into bowls and sprinkle with
parsley. Serves 6.
<p>
   Apricots chef Jeff Gantkin took a fusion approach with delicious
results.
<p>
    &UR; APRICOTS PAN-FRIED COD WITH LIGHT EGG BATTER AND SOY-GINGER
<p>
   DIPPING SAUCE
<p>
   1 pound cod, cut into 2 fillets
<p>
   1 egg
<p>
   ¼ cup flour
<p>
   1 tablespoon canola oil
<p>
   SAUCE:
<p>
   1 tablespoon ginger, cut into matchstick pieces
<p>
   1 teaspoon chopped garlic
<p>
   1 tablespoon sugar
<p>
   1 tablespoon white wine
<p>
   2 scallions, chopped
<p>
   ¼ cup good quality soy sauce
<p>
   ¼ cup rice wine vinegar
<p>
   ¼ cup water
<p>
   Pinch crushed red pepper flakes
<p>
   Combine the sauce ingredients, stirring until sugar is dissolved.
Set aside.
<p>
   Dredge fish fillets in flour, shaking off excess. Dip in egg.
<p>
   Set a skillet over medium heat and add canola oil. When oil is hot
but not smoking, add fillets and cook until browned on both sides.
Cook until fish is done to your liking. Serve cod with stir-fried
vegetables and rice. Serves 2.
<p>
   David E. Curtis, chef at Pika Tapas Cafe in New Haven, Conn., uses
salt cod to create the restaurant's codfish croquettes and
``piquillo'' peppers stuffed with cod.
<p>
    &UR; PIKA TAPAS CAFE'S CODFISH CROQUETTES WITH ALIOLI
<p>
   (CROQUETAS DE BACALAO CON ALIOLI)
<p>
   1 cup mayonnaise
<p>
   4 cloves garlic
<p>
   Juice of ½ lemon
<p>
   Pinch salt
<p>
   ¼ teaspoon Tabasco
<p>
   4 sprigs parsley, washed and coarsely chopped
<p>
   CROQUETTES
<p>
   3 pounds salt cod
<p>
   ½ pound butter
<p>
   ¾ pound flour
<p>
   2 tablespoons olive oil
<p>
   1 onion, diced
<p>
   6 cloves garlic, sliced
<p>
   ½ cup white wine
<p>
   1 quart milk
<p>
   Salt and white pepper, to taste
<p>
   1 teaspoon chopped fresh thyme (leaves only)
<p>
   1 tablespoon chopped fresh parsley
<p>
   FOR FRYING
<p>
   Flour, for dredging
<p>
   3 eggs, beaten
<p>
   Bread crumbs
<p>
   Canola or vegetable oil, for frying
<p>
   Making the alioli:
<p>
   Place all ingredients in a food processor and pulse to combine
until garlic and parsley are finely pureed. Keep refrigerated until
ready to use.
<p>
   Soak cod in cool water for 24 to 36 hours, changing the water 3 or
4 times. Remove cod and drain. Remove skin and bones, and cut into
small strips. Set aside.
<p>
   In a saute pan, melt butter over medium-low heat. Add flour,
stirring to avoid lumps. Cook, stirring constantly with a wooden spoon
until mixture is light brown. Remove from heat and let cool.
<p>
   Set a saucepan over medium heat, and add olive oil, onion and
garlic. Cook until onions are translucent. Do not brown. Add wine, and
cook until volume is reduced by three-quarters. Add milk and bring
mixture to a boil. Reduce heat to a simmer (if the milk separates,
don't be concerned). Whisk in the butter-and-flour mixture, a heaping
spoonful at a time, until you have a very thick paste. Simmer for 10
to 15 minutes.
<p>
   Fold in the codfish strips, and remove mixture from heat. Transfer
mixture in batches to the bowl of a food processor or an electric
mixer, and process until somewhat smooth (do not puree it entirely).
Season mixture with salt and white pepper to taste. Add herbs, and mix
to combine.
<p>
   Transfer mixture to a cookie sheet and let cool in the
refrigerator. When mixture is cool enough to handle, roll it into
1-inch balls.
<p>
   When ready to cook, dredge croquettes in flour, dip in beaten eggs
and roll in bread crumbs. Set a skillet or deep fryer over medium heat
and add enough olive oil to make it about 1-inch deep. When oil is
hot, fry the croquettes until golden on all sides. (Do this in
batches, and do not crowd the croquettes or they will not brown.)
Drain on paper towels, and serve with alioli.
<p>
   Makes about 50 pieces. Serve 8 pieces with a dollop of alioli as an
appetizer serving.
 &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-food-cod </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0844 09-02 0580 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-food-cod - a0844
 &UR; (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Deborah Hornblow </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Hartford Courant
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; One photo (TPN5) &QL; 
 &UR; Pity the Poor, Misunderstood Cod (Hartford) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   HARTFORD, Conn.  &MD;  Mention cod and grown-ups clamp their lips shut
like little children.
<p>
   Their mental movie theaters flashes a montage of horrors: cod liver
oil, smelly slabs of dry salt cod, a mother standing with a fork
poised before tightly pursed lips.
<p>
   Poor cod has been misunderstood, misjudged and needlessly maligned.
<p>
   Perhaps if it weren't so inexpensive and common, cod would be
elevated to higher stature.
<p>
   Champions of the great North Atlantic white fish range from Julia
Child to Connecticut's Mark Bittman, author of the encyclopedic
``Fish,'' as well as many of the area's top chefs.
<p>
   ``If cod were rare, it would be a luxury item, treasured for its
snow white, delicate flesh and subtle but far from characterless
flavor,'' writes Bittman, whose book includes 16 recipes for cod
dishes.
<p>
   ``It's not that cod is a humble fish, it's just been neglected,''
opined Child in a recent TV Food Network appearance.
<p>
   Cod is the largest of a family of fish that includes pollock,
haddock, whiting and hake. It is a round fish with a tan- or
khaki-colored back, small yellow-brown spots, a white belly and a
telltale barbel under its chin. It can grow to a length of 3 to 4 feet
and weigh up to 100 pounds.
<p>
   The modern aversion to cod is a remarkable display of mankind's
disregard for history. The fish played a crucial role in the survival
of early settlers, and the cod fishing industry spawned economic
development in the nations bordering the Atlantic.
<p>
   But the truth is in the taste, and here, the lowly cod deserves the
highest heraldry.
<p>
   ``It's a really versatile fish,'' says David E. Curtis, chef at New
Haven's Pika Tapas Cafe. ``It's not an oily fish by any stretch, it's
not got a really strong fishy flavor on it. It's a basic fish so you
don't have to get real elaborate and try to overpower it.''
<p>
   Bittman calls cod ``the pig of the sea,'' noting that almost every
part of it  &MD;  from the pristine fillets to the air bladders, cheeks and
tongues  &MD;  can be consumed (and are consumed in less finicky cultures).
<p>
   Cod is equally delicious fried, sauteed, poached, baked, stuffed or
roasted (grilling is a bit tricky as the fish tends to flake apart).
In England, it is synonymous with the national dish of fish and chips;
in France, it is the backbone of ``brandade,'' that comforting mound
of fish and mashed potatoes; in Spain, salt cod is used to make
``croquetas'' or it is pureed and stuffed inside tangy piquillo
peppers; in Portugal, cod is served with fried potatoes, or it is
scrambled with eggs and onions.
<p>
   When buying fresh cod for home cooking, most chefs recommend
starting at a good fish market. From there, freshness can be assessed
by the appearance of the fish or fillet. If you see a whole fish, ``it
should have really clear eyes and red gills, says Curtis. ``Any touch
of brownness or spottiness shouldn't be acceptable.'' If you are
buying fillets, the fish should be glistening white with no grayness
or dryness at the edges. Buyers also should avoid dull, watery looking
cuts. ``If you can press the meat and there's a lot of water, it means
they've soaked it to get the smell off,'' Curtis warns.
<p>
   Salt cod is available in some ethnic stores, but generally is
harder to come by. ``The good thing is it lasts forever,'' Curtis
jokes.
</TEXT>

<PREAMBLE>
bc-food-burmese-recipes - a0844
 &UR; (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997 Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; A Burmese Garden Potluck: The Recipes &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
    &UR; LEILEI THEIN'S CHICKEN CURRY &QL; 
<p>
   SPICE MIXTURE
<p>
   3 tablespoons cumin
<p>
   1 teaspoon coriander
<p>
   1 teaspoon ground bay leaves
<p>
   1 teaspoon ground cardamom
<p>
   ½ teaspoon cinnamon
<p>
   ½ teaspoon cloves
<p>
   ½ teaspoon nutmeg
<p>
   ½ teaspoon pepper
<p>
   ½ teaspoon ground fenugreek
<p>
   CHICKEN
<p>
   3 pounds boneless skinless chicken breasts
<p>
   1 teaspoon salt
<p>
   5 tablespoons oil
<p>
   2 cups ground onions
<p>
   1 (2-inch) piece ginger root, ground or finely grated
<p>
   1 clove garlic, ground or finely minced
<p>
   ¼ teaspoon turmeric
<p>
   1 to 1½ teaspoons medium hot ground red chile
<p>
   1 cup nonfat plain yogurt
<p>
   1 tablespoon flour
<p>
   SPICE MIXTURE
<p>
   Combine cumin, coriander, bay leaves, cardamom, cinnamon, cloves,
<p>
   nutmeg, pepper and fenugreek. Set aside.
<p>
   CHICKEN
<p>
   Wash chicken breasts, pat dry and cut into medium-size pieces.
Place in baking dish and sprinkle with salt and half of curry spices
and marinate 30 minutes.
<p>
   Heat oil in medium saucepan. When hot, add onions and saute 1
minute. Add ginger, garlic and turmeric and saute 2 minutes. Add
ground chile and saute 1 minute. Add chicken and stir slowly until all
pieces are covered with onion mixture. Reduce heat to medium. Cover
and cook 30 minutes.
<p>
   Mix yogurt and flour. Add to chicken with remaining curry spices
and cook until chicken is done, about 10 minutes more.
<p>
   Four to 6 servings. Each serving: 519 calories; 814 mg sodium; 149
mg cholesterol; 22 grams fat; 15 grams carbohydrates; 64 grams
protein; 1.27 grams fiber.
<p>
    &UR; THE WIECHECS' BAGAN-STYLE NOODLE SALAD
<p>
   MANGO-COCONUT DRESSING
<p>
   3 cloves garlic
<p>
   Juice and pulp of 1 large, very ripe peeled mango
<p>
   2 tablespoons sesame oil
<p>
   1 (17½-ounce) can young coconut juice
<p>
   Juice of 3 lemons
<p>
   1 tablespoon crystallized ginger, minced
<p>
   1 tablespoon cilantro, minced
<p>
   2 teaspoons salt, or to taste
<p>
   SALAD
<p>
   10 ounces flat, dried rice noodles
<p>
   Water
<p>
   Ice
<p>
   ½ pound broccoli florets
<p>
   ½ pound cauliflower florets
<p>
   ¼ pound green beans
<p>
   2/3 cup sliced green onions
<p>
   1 cup sliced red bell pepper
<p>
   1 cup sliced yellow bell pepper
<p>
   Romaine lettuce leaves
<p>
   MANGO-COCONUT DRESSING
<p>
   Wrap garlic cloves in foil and bake at 400 degrees 30 minutes.
Mince. There should be 1½ teaspoons.
<p>
   Puree garlic, mango and juice, oil, coconut juice, lemon juice,
ginger, cilantro and salt in blender at high speed. Makes about 4
cups.
<p>
   SALAD
<p>
   Soak noodles in cold water 20 minutes.
<p>
   Prepare large bowl ice water for cooling vegetables.
<p>
   Blanch broccoli in boiling water 4 minutes, remove from pot with
slotted spoon, place in ice water until cooled; remove and set aside.
In same fashion, blanch cauliflower 4 minutes and cool; blanch green
beans 5 to 6 minutes and cool. (Note: There should be about 2 cups
broccoli, 2 cups cauliflower and 1 cup beans.)
<p>
   Drain noodles. Bring large pot water to boil. Place drained noodles
in metal strainer and dip in boiling water 10 to 15 seconds. Remove
from pot. Work in batches if necessary. Noodles will not be cooked
fully but will soften further when combined with dressing.
<p>
   Turn noodles into large bowl. Add 3 cups Mango-Coconut Dressing,
mix gently and let cool.
<p>
   Add broccoli, cauliflower, green beans, green onions and red and
yellow bell peppers and toss gently with noodles. Line salad bowl with
romaine leaves. Place noodles and vegetables in center. Serve
remaining dressing on side or refrigerate for another use.
<p>
   Six to 8 servings. Each serving with ½ cup dressing: 290 calories;
898 mg sodium; 0 cholesterol; 5 grams fat; 60 grams carbohydrates; 4
grams protein; 1.50 grams fiber.
<p>
    &UR; NILAR BELLAS' COCONUT RICE
<p>
   2½ cups jasmine rice
<p>
   1 (14-ounce) can coconut milk
<p>
   2 cups hot water
<p>
   2 teaspoons salt
<p>
   2 teaspoons sugar
<p>
   ½ tablespoon oil or butter
<p>
   2 bay leaves
<p>
   1 stick cinnamon
<p>
   ¼ teaspoon cumin
<p>
   ¼ teaspoon curry powder
<p>
   1 or 2 very small onions, peeled
<p>
   Wash rice and drain. Repeat and drain well.
<p>
   Place rice in Dutch oven. Add coconut milk, hot water, salt, sugar,
oil, bay leaves, cinnamon stick, cumin and curry powder and stir with
wooden spoon. Cook, covered, over medium heat until boiling, about 5
minutes.
<p>
   Stir with wooden spoon. Bury whole onions in rice. Reduce heat to
low. Cover and cook until rice is tender, 18 to 20 minutes.
<p>
   Eight to 10 servings. Each servings: 340 calories; 600 mg sodium; 0
cholesterol; 13 grams fat; 51 grams carbohydrates; 5 grams protein;
1.54 grams fiber.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Shopping List
<p>
   3 pounds boneless skinless chicken breasts
<p>
   1 (8-ounce) container plain yogurt
<p>
   1 (2-inch) piece ginger root
<p>
   1 small package fenugreek seeds
<p>
   1 large mango
<p>
   1 (17½-ounce) can young coconut juice
<p>
   3 lemons
<p>
   1 jar crystallized ginger
<p>
   1 bunch cilantro
<p>
   ½ pound broccoli florets
<p>
   1 small cauliflower
<p>
   ¼ pound green beans
<p>
   1 small bunch romaine
<p>
   1 (1-pound) package dried flat rice noodles
<p>
   1 bunch green onions
<p>
   1 red bell pepper
<p>
   1 yellow bell pepper
<p>
   1 pound jasmine rice
<p>
   1 (14-ounce) can coconut milk
<p>
   1 or 2 very small onions
<p>
   Staples:
<p>
   Salt
<p>
   Oil
<p>
   Garlic
<p>
   Turmeric
<p>
   Ground red chile
<p>
   Flour
<p>
   Cumin
<p>
   Coriander
<p>
   Bay leaves
<p>
   Cardamom
<p>
   Ground cinnamon
<p>
   Cloves
<p>
   Nutmeg
<p>
   Pepper
<p>
   Sugar
<p>
   Cinnamon sticks
<p>
   Curry powder
<p>
   Timetable:
<p>
   Morning of party: Combine curry spices for chicken.
<p>
   1½ hours before: Marinate chicken with spices.
<p>
   1 hour before: Start cooking chicken. Make salad dressing. Blanch
and cool salad vegetables. Soak rice noodles. Cook rice noodles and
combine with dressing.
<p>
   45 minutes before: Wash and begin cooking jasmine rice.
<p>
   20 minutes before: Add salad vegetables to noodles and dressing.
Line salad bowl with romaine and add noodle mixture.
<p>
   After dinner: Brew tea. Serve dessert.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-food-burmese </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0845 09-02 0498 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-food-burmese - a0845
 &UR; (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Barbara Hansen </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997 Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Three photos (TPN2-4) &QL; 
 &UR; A Burmese Garden Potluck &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   It wasn't your everyday picnic. Instead of shorts and jeans, some
of the women wore sarongs. Tables were covered with exotic Asian
fabrics. And the food was even more exotic  &MD;  chicken smothered in
spices, noodle salad with mango dressing, jasmine rice flavored with
coconut and served in an antique silver bowl from northern Thailand.
<p>
   Although the look and the flavors were tropical, the location was
Southern California, the home of Molly Kellogg and her husband, Mark
Brownstein.
<p>
   This was a Myanmar party, a potluck dinner staged by the newly
formed Myanmar Arts Council of the Pacific Asia Museum in Pasadena.
The music, most of the guests and most of the food were Burmese.
<p>
   Betty Wiechec and her husband Don, who regularly travel to Myanmar
to take photographs for a book on the country. They brought the
mango-dressed noodle salad. ``We had something similar in Bagan at a
wonderful place named River View that overlooks the Irrawady (now
Ayeyarwaddy River),'' says Betty Wiechec. ``We ate there every day and
every night that we were in Bagan.''
<p>
   Leilei Thein, who was about to leave for Myanmar, prepared chicken
curry in the style of Mawlamyine, (formerly Moulmein), where she grew
up. The chicken was juicy with ground onion and yogurt. ``In Burma,
chickens are not soft, like here,'' she says. ``Marinating with yogurt
makes the chicken tastier and more tender.''
<p>
   A raft of spices, including an awesome amount of cumin, goes into
this dish. Nevertheless, the flavor is mellow, not dominated by
spices, and the yogurt adds a pleasing tang. Thein brought tea from
Shan state for after dinner.
<p>
   Nilar Bellas, who performed a folk dance from her home city,
Yangon, brought rice seasoned with cinnamon sticks, cinnamon tree
leaves (similar to bay leaves) and barely detectable amounts of curry
powder, cumin and sugar. The effect was subtle, perfumed, intriguing.
<p>
   Such rice is a festival dish in Myanmar, she says, and is presented
as an offering to monks. Bellas prepares it in typical Asian fashion,
measuring liquid levels with a finger joint and adding sprinkles of
water until the rice attains proper consistency.
<p>
   Other contributions included chicken red with hot spices and a
salad that combined homemade fish cake with cabbage, fried onions,
fried garlic and chiles. There were a few interlopers, such as Thai
fish cakes and a Japanese red bean dessert. But Malaysian noodles were
pure Myanmar, having been acquired long ago from Malays living on the
southern coast around Mergui (now called Bak) and incorporated into
Burmese cuisine.
<p>
   An expert at cooking as well as gardening, Brownstein provided
desserts: a spiced apple cranberry crumble, a crust-free lemon-lime
pie and coconut pudding. Were these Burmese? Of course not, but nobody
seemed to mind as they wiped the dishes clean.
</TEXT>

<HEADER> 09-02 0297 </HEADER>

bc-food-schoollunch-recipe
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Some No Fuss No Muss Recipes for Kids' Lunches &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Here are easy recipes kids should like. The first two are from the
American Heart Association.
<p>
    &UR; PEANUT BUTTER KISSES &QL; 
<p>
   1/3 cup peanut butter
<p>
   1/3 cup honey
<p>
   1 cup nonfat dry milk powder
<p>
   Mix ingredients and roll into one-inch balls. If too sticky,
increase powdered milk. Serves 12.
<p>
    &UR; STRAWBERRY-BANANA SMOOTHIE &QL; 
<p>
   1 medium banana, peeled and frozen
<p>
   1 cup strawberries, fresh or frozen
<p>
   1 cup low-fat vanilla yogurt
<p>
   Blend ingredients on high speed in blender of food processor. Keep
chilled. Serves one.
<p>
    &UR; EASY CHEESY ROLL-UPS &QL; 
<p>
   2 tablespoons reduced fat cream cheese
<p>
   1 whole wheat tortilla
<p>
   1½ tablespoons crushed pineapple
<p>
   1½ tablespoons chopped walnuts, almonds or cashews
<p>
   leaf lettuce
<p>
   4 slices thinly sliced honey ham
<p>
   Spread cream cheese on tortilla. Sprinkle with pineapple and nuts.
Layer with lettuce and ham. Roll up tortilla. Cut in half or into
bite-sized pieces. Serves one.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Prepare and freeze meat and cheese sandwiches ahead of time. Remove
from your freezer as necessary. They'll thaw by lunchtime.
<p>
   Have breakfast for lunch. Fortified cereal bars, like Kellogg's
NutriGrain, and mini-boxes of cereal, or cereal in a sandwich bag,
pair nicely with milk in a kid's lunch box.
<p>
   Keep small pop top cans of fruit on hand to toss into lunches when
you run out of fresh fruits.
<p>
   If you have the energy, pack lunch while you're making dinner.
You're already in the kitchen.
<p>
   Stow juice boxes in your freezer. As the juice box thaws, it'll
keep perishables chilled all morning. (Thawing time is roughly four
hours.)
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-food-schoollunch </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0846 09-02 0903 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-food-schoollunch - a0846
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Maria Hiaasen </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Kids' Lunches Can Be Appealing and Healthy &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The moment I spotted that first back-to-school lunch box display in
July, my dread became palpable. What on earth would I pack in my kids'
lunch boxes this year? I averted my eyes and hurried on to the
sale-priced sunscreen.
<p>
   Now that my three have boarded the school bus again, I'm faced with
a second-grader who hates sandwiches and a third-grader who rejects
pretzels and wants Pringles. (``All my friends get to have them,'' he
argues.) Only my kindergartner accepts the contents of her new
Winnie-the-Pooh lunch box without complaints, and I predict her
attitude won't last past the first teacher workday.
<p>
   Help. I need new lunch ideas. No cutesy foods, like mini-pizzas
sporting zucchini noses and bell pepper smiles, and no sandwiches
shaped like footballs or gingerbread men. Give me kid appeal, but make
it something healthy that I can put together without calamity before
our 8:15 a.m. bolt to the bus stop.
<p>
   For inspiration, I turned to some licensed dietitians in the area,
the Internet, school-aged kids on my block and a family magazine.
<p>
   From the dietitians, I culled some healthy ground rules.
<p>
   Lunch ought to include a protein, a couple of servings of
carbohydrate, some fruit, and a dairy product, says Kelly O'Connor,
chief clinical dietitian at Greater Baltimore Medical Center.
Together, they'll quell hunger, boost energy levels and keep minds
alert through the afternoon.
<p>
   Yes, a peanut butter sandwich, a box of raisins and a carton of
milk fill the bill, she says, recalling the lunch her mom packed
incessantly when she was a kid. But to prevent boredom, try a
snacklike meal of air-popped popcorn, cheese cubes, ready-peeled baby
carrots, and fruit juice. Or try pairing milk with a soft pretzel and
fruit or trail mix made with cereal, pretzel sticks, peanuts, dried
fruit and a few M&AMP;Ms.
<p>
   It's smart to include some treats with the healthy stuff, says
Denise Schuffman, a dietitian. She doesn't forbid any food outright.
In fact, she says things like potato chips or chocolate chip cookies  &MD; 
in moderation  &MD;  belong in a child's lunch.
<p>
   ``If kids don't get them in their own lunch box, they'll get them
from someone else's or at a friend's house after school,'' Schuffman
says. ``If you provide them, your child won't feel deprived or want to
sneak things.''
<p>
   Susan Hunter Schulze, a consulting dietitian at GBMC who also
teaches nutrition at Towson University, is blessed with children
who'll eat strips of green pepper and carrots at lunch. She's too busy
for cookie cutter sandwiches or lunches with a theme, but she
confesses to preparing and freezing homebaked blueberry muffins for
her son.
<p>
   ``It sends that he-knows-that-I-care message,'' she says.
<p>
   If Hunter Schulze has a mantra, it's this: Know the nutrition
content of what you pack. For example, she says, there isn't any
vitamin C in Capri Sun, there's no milk in a YooHoo, and Oscar Mayer
Lunchables are loaded with fat. Those are treats and should be
limited, she says. For the majority of your kids' lunches, stick with
100 percent fruit juice, make your own chocolate milk, and assemble
your own lunch kit. You'll control fat, sugar and preservatives.
<p>
   Kids get too much of that stuff, says Roxanne Moore, the community
dietitian at St. Joseph's Medical Center. They need more whole-grained
foods, she says, so build lunches around different breads, muffins,
low-sugar cereals, rice cakes, baked tortillas, pretzels, fig bars,
graham crackers and animal crackers. Wraps are in, she notes, so fill
a baked tortilla with meat, cheese or a dab of peanut butter and
raisins. Then, roll your child a healthy lunch.
<p>
   My on-line search netted similar ideas. Using key words ``lunch box
ideas kids,'' I reached a Seattle hospital's newsletter, which
recommended packing small servings of different types of foods. That
way, a kid is less likely to toss or trade away a whole food group.
<p>
   An American Dietetic Association's ``Hot Topics'' page suggested
that parents get their kids involved in lunch food choices. That's
supposed to encourage them to eat a variety of foods. Use a grocery
expedition to teach your children about the food pyramid, the page
suggests. For more nutrition information from the ADA, call
800-366-1655.
<p>
   Finally, from the neighborhood kids, I gleaned what I consider the
fundamentals of good lunch box packing: Know what they like, and stick
with it.
<p>
   Joy, the second-grader across the street, definitely wants that
same old mustard and American cheese sandwich with a container of
applesauce every day until winter break. Come Valentine's Day, she'll
be ready to switch to a ham sandwich. Rebecca, the seasoned
fourth-grader next door, is open to a variety of healthy selections,
so long as they don't include a turkey sandwich. Her advice to those
stuffing lunch bags this morning?
<p>
   ``Ask your kids what they want. Maybe break it down into ... fruit,
dessert and sandwiches.''
<p>
   That's precisely one of the suggestions from Family Fun Magazine.
In a spread featuring readers' suggestions, the September issue
showcases a chart from Liz Ruff of West Chester, Pa. It's displayed
prominently in her kitchen and is written on a chalkboard so it can be
easily changed to suit a child's fickle tastes.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-food-taylor </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0847 09-02 0610 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-food-taylor - a0847
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Camille Whelan </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; One photo (TPN6) &QL; 
 &UR; Things Were Cooking at This Summer Camp &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   When Steve Taylor remembers summer camp, he doesn't recall poison
ivy, archery or square knots. He thinks about making challah bread and
blueberry pie for 300 people.
<p>
   As a 13-year-old at the Episcopal Church camp his father directed
in Charlton Depot, Mass., Taylor passed up canoeing lessons and took
to hanging around in the camp's kitchen, where as an ``underchef'' he
first learned that quality is better than quantity.
<p>
   He's been dedicated to serving outstanding food ever since.
<p>
   ``Quality and consistency are the hallmark of excellence,'' he says
firmly.
<p>
   Now, with 24 years of cooking experience under his belt, including
classical training at the Culinary Institute of America and chef's
positions at various four-star restaurants around the country, this
Fallston, Md.-based basement waterproofing entrepreneur and father of
four knows what it takes to cook something delicious.
<p>
   That's why his renovated farmhouse kitchen has a six-burner gas
range, double ovens and tons of counter space. He saves his commercial
kitchen, which he built into one of his barns, for parties, weddings
and major events.
<p>
   ``I like to cook dinner,'' says Taylor, who describes his style as
``Continental with a Swiss flair.'' He admits that he uses a lot of
butter in his sauces, but doesn't seem to care. This chef, attentive
to every detail and nuance of flavor, won't serve anything that
doesn't taste good  &MD;  healthful or not  &MD;  although he refrains from
making cream sauces too often. What does he hate? He quotes a former
mentor: ``a steak that is battleship gray.''
<p>
   He likes to prepare salmon or fresh Maine lobster dishes with
vegetables and has concocted a wedding cake or two. His favorite
wedding-cake recipe is a filled chocolate sauvignon cake.
<p>
   His children, 8, 7, 5, and 3 years old, gobble up his
fresh-vegetable pasta, which he developed as a ``refrigerator
cleaner.'' He makes it by topping off bow-tie pasta with carrots, corn
and broccoli, which he disguises in a tasty beef sauce. Here is
Taylor's recipe:
<p>
    &UR; PASTA RENWICK &QL; 
<p>
   1 pound package bow-tie pasta
<p>
   1 cup diced carrots
<p>
   1 cup broccoli florets
<p>
   12-ounce can of corn
<p>
   4 ounces diced or shredded cooked chicken
<p>
   4 ounces crab meat or shrimp
<p>
   2 garlic cloves, mashed
<p>
   2 tablespoons maple syrup
<p>
   ½ cup beef bouillon or beef stock
<p>
   a pinch of leaf thyme
<p>
   a pinch of leaf marjoram
<p>
   Cook pasta in boiling salted water, drain and rinse, dry well and
coat with 3-4 tablespoons olive oil, then set aside.
<p>
   Blanch carrots and broccoli (separately) in salted water, then
saute briefly (separately) in 1-2 tablespoons of butter.
<p>
   Add maple syrup, thyme and marjoram to carrots, and toss. Steam
broccoli with beef stock, and season to taste with salt, pepper and
sugar, then set aside.
<p>
   Saute the crab meat or shrimp in 1-2 tablespoons of butter over
medium heat, being careful not to brown. Season with salt and white
pepper and set aside.
<p>
   Heat corn in a small pan, season with white pepper and set aside.
Be sure to keep the pans hot and the food moving.
<p>
   Heat large saute pan with 2-4 tablespoons of olive oil and saute
pasta until hot, seasoning to taste with salt and pepper.
<p>
   Add fresh garlic and toss, and then add the carrots, broccoli,
corn, chicken and seafood (strain all liquid, except the carrots,
before adding).
<p>
   Saute until hot. Season sparingly, to taste.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-food-bites </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0847 09-02 0331 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-food-bites - a0847
 &UR; (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Charles Perry </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; FOOD BITES: Where the ``Joys'' Are &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Eighteen cartons of ``Joy of Cooking'' documents  &MD;  diaries,
genealogies, family photos, certificates, correspondence and, of
course, recipes  &MD;  have been donated to the Schlesinger Library at
Radcliffe College by the estate of Irma S. Rombauer and Marion R.
Becker, the mother-daughter team that produced the most popular
cookbook of the last two generations.
<p>
   They'll have good company. Other collections residing at the
scholarly library include those of Julia Child, food historian Sophie
Coe and the American Institute of Wine and Food.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   GOT HEARTBURN? The California Milk Processors Board, the ``Got
Milk?'' people, claim there's scientific evidence that casein, the
principal protein in milk, removes the hot chemical in chiles from the
mouth, thus providing immediate relief. Hmm, since one touch of that
hot chemical (actually, it's five related chemicals known as
capsaicinoids) causes pain nerves to send distress signals for six
minutes, this is a little hard to believe. Still, with a mouth full of
habaneros, you have to believe in something.
<p>
   Meanwhile, Baylor College of Medicine reports that drinking milk to
soothe heartburn might provide temporary relief but can actually
worsen the problem. Heartburn is an upwelling of acidic gastric juices
from the stomach into the esophagus, and yes, milk will wash the acid
back down into the stomach. But down there it will cause more acid,
and you'll get another heartburn.
<p>
   Not wanting to seem to hard on milk, the college also warns that
peppermints are no cure for heartburn. They can neutralize the acid in
the esophagus and stimulate salivation, which also helps clear acid
from the esophagus, but it turns out that peppermints weaken the
valve-like sphincter muscle at the base of the esophagus that serves
as a lid on the stomach. Then, oops: heartburn.
</TEXT>

 &UR; WINE OF THE WEEK: 1995 Domaine de Pierredon Cotes du Rhone &QL;

<p>
    &UR; 1995 Domaine de Pierredon Cotes du Rhone ($10). &LR; 
<p>
   This robust, full-bodied red wine shows all the depth and
complexity of a well-made Chateauneuf-du-Pape at half the price. With

estimate.

<SLUG> bc-vintage-point </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0848 09-02 0751 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-vintage-point - a0848
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Michael Dresser </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; VINTAGE POINT: Hungary's Dessert Nectar Making a Comeback &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   For more than 40 years, Tokaji Aszu was a great wine in chains.
When Hungary came under Communist rule after World War II, the
individual wine estates that produced one of the world's classic
dessert wines were absorbed into a vast state monopoly.
<p>
   Competition ceased, and standards plunged. Winemaking decisions
were made by bureaucrats rather than artisans. Tokaji Aszu  &MD;  known in
English-speaking countries as Tokay  &MD;  all but disappeared from the
world's finest tables.
<p>
   But the 1989 collapse of the Berlin Wall sent ripples through every
wine cask in the ancient wine-growing region of Tokaji. The
post-Communist government has moved boldly to dismantle the monopoly
and open its wine industry to foreign investment and ideas.
<p>
   Now, to taste Tokay is to taste freedom itself. Seldom has a type
of wine changed so dramatically for the better in the space of a few
vintages.
<p>
   In its decades under Communist rule, Tokay had taken on a highly
unusual character. A high level of oxidation  &MD;  comparable to some
sherries  &MD;  had become part of its identity. The wines were rough and
harsh, but still oddly fascinating.
<p>
   The new dessert wines of Tokay are noticeably different, even
before the cork is pulled. Gone is the deep copper color of the state
monopoly's Tokays. In its place is a deep golden nectar. Traces of
oxidation remain, but the dominant flavors come from the fresh,
honeyed fruit.
<p>
   Tokaji Aszu is a white dessert wine produced primarily from two
traditional Hungarian grapes, the furmint and the harslevelu. Like
Sauternes and the top dessert wines of Germany, its sweetness and
flavors are intensified by the use of grapes shriveled by the ``noble
rot'' botrytis.
<p>
   Dessert tokay is graded by its sweetness  &MD;  from three to six
puttonyos (an ancient measure of how many nobly rotten grapes are
included in the blend). There is also a supersweet version called
Tokaji Aszu Escenzia, a great rarity, and an even sweeter nectar
called Essence  &MD;  more legend than commercial reality.
<p>
   The prospect of participating in the renaissance of one of the
world's legendary wines has attracted wine-industry stars, including
Michael Rolland, a celebrated Bordeaux enologist, at Chateau Megyer,
and Jean-Michel Cazes, owner of Chateau Lynch-Bages, at Disznoko.
<p>
   The challenge is daunting: to bring Tokay back into the mainstream
of the world's great wines without sacrificing its unique character.
Judging by the results at Megyer and Disznoko, they are succeeding
admirably.
<p>
   The difference is dramatic when one tastes an old-style Tokay
beside one of the new versions.
<p>
   The 1984 Tokaji Aszu 4 Puttonyos from the old monopoly producer
Hungarovin ($17) displays aggressive burnt-caramel flavors that
dominate the nuances of orange, orange peel and spice. There's still
something compelling about the wine, though the finish is thin and the
remaining sweetness isn't enough to balance out the screeching
acidity.
<p>
   By contrast, the 1992 Disznoko Tokaji Aszu 4 Puttonyos ($21) offers
a fresh and lively blend of orange, peach, apricot, honey and spice
flavors, with just the merest hint of oxidation. The
characteristically high acidity is balanced admirably by medium-sweet
fruit. (Tokay, old and new, is generally sold in 500-milliliter
bottles, two-thirds the size of the standard wine bottle.)
<p>
   Less sweet but a shade more complex is the 1990 Chateau Megyer
Tokaji Aszu 3 Puttonyos ($16). The flavors are quite similar to the
Disznoko, but are more vibrant and persistent  &MD;  perhaps a result of
the extra bottle age.
<p>
   The 1988 Chateau Megyer Tokaji Aszu 5 Puttonyos ($23) shows a fiery
quality that is quite distinctive. Apricot and honey flavors blend
with lemon and tropical spice to achieve an impressive complexity.
<p>
   The 1992 Disznoko 5 Puttonyos ($27) is brilliantly structured but
will need a decade in the bottle to let its flavors come together.
<p>
   The crowning glory of the tasting was the 1993 Disznoko 6 Puttonyos
($32). This luscious, honeyed wine is jampacked with flavors of
apricot, peach, spices, honey and caramel. The other new-style Tokays
are encouraging signs, but this is a complex, layered, fully realized
nectar that proves post-Communist Hungary is on its way back as a
wine-producing nation.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 1153EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-recipe-finder </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0849 09-02 0635 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-recipe-finder - a0849
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Ellen Hawks </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; RECIPE FINDER: Pear Dishes Are Light, Delicious &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   You'll want to put poached pears in your ``light, lovely, delicious
and easy-to-make'' file.
<p>
   Jean Beecher of Walla Walla, Wash., wrote that she has been looking
for a poached-pear recipe for some time now, and she would surely
appreciate help.
<p>
   Chef Kent Rigby chose recipes from Virginia S. Hodges and Jo Ann M.
Nuetzel, both of Baltimore.
<p>
    &UR; HODGES' PEARS IN RED WINE &QL; 
<p>
   8 to 9 Bosc pears
<p>
   2 cups each Burgundy wine and water
<p>
   1 cup sugar
<p>
   8 or 9 whole cloves
<p>
   Peel pears, leaving whole with stems intact. Press a whole clove in
each pear bottom. Simmer wine, water and sugar in a saucepan, stirring
until combined. Stand pears in liquid and poach covered for about 30
minutes or until tender. Chill pears in liquid, drain and serve with
zabaglione sauce.
<p>
    &UR; Zabaglione sauce &QL; 
<p>
   4 egg whites
<p>
   4 tablespoons sugar
<p>
   ½ cup white wine
<p>
   Beat egg yolks and sugar in a pan over hot water until thick. Add
wine, slowly beating until fluffy. Chill.
<p>
   Hodges notes that the Bosc pears, ``which I get at the Giant  &MD;  they
are possibly available at most grocers  &MD;  are best for poaching because
they are firmer than the Bartlett pear. Also, I sometimes slice the
pears in half and poach cut side down in the liquid.''
<p>
    &UR; NUETZEL'S POACHED PEARS IN RED WINE &QL; 
<p>
   6 small pears, pared, cored and halved
<p>
   juice of 1 lemon
<p>
   2 cups dry red wine
<p>
   1½ cups sugar
<p>
   2-inch cinnamon stick
<p>
   Dip pears into lemon juice. Heat wine, sugar and cinnamon stick
together in a 10-inch skillet, stirring constantly until sugar is
dissolved and mixture boils. Reduce heat and add pears. Simmer
uncovered, about 15 minutes, until pears are soft but not mushy when
pierced with a sharp knife. Cool pears in syrup until lukewarm;
discard cinnamon stick.
<p>
   Place pears in individual dessert dishes. Spoon syrup over pears
and serve warm, or refrigerate and serve cool.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Recipe requests
<p>
   Shirley McCoy of Crystal Lake, Ill., writes that she wants a
homemade soft caramel candy recipe. ``A man from our church made big
batches of them to sell at our church fair. You could chew them
without losing a tooth, and they had the greatest caramel, buttery
flavor, and he wrapped each piece in wax paper. He recently passed
away, and I'd like to carry on for him here but cannot find a recipe.
Would surely appreciate your help.''
<p>
   V. Wilcox of Lacey, Wash., wants help with a crumb base for a
dessert. ``In the '60s, Betty Crocker had a boxed date-bar mix which
this dessert called for, and it is no longer available. As I recall,
we baked the cookie base and crumbled it for the bottom and top of
this dessert and added some whipped cream and instant coffee for the
filling. It was layered and could be frozen. I'm stumped. Do you think
anyone would have a recipe for a dessert like this and the crumb
base?''
<p>
   Ann S. Bish of Fallston, Md. writes that she'd like to find a good
recipe for making green tomato pickle, ``or even a store which sells
them already processed.''
<p>
   T. Bailey if Baltimore wants a crab/spinach dip like that from
Phillips in White Marsh.
<p>
   Jack Cohen of St. Augustine, Fla., writes: ``Years ago I had a
recipe for halvah, a very sweet dessert made with vegetable shortening
and sesame seeds. Since moving I have misplaced it. Please see if you
can locate a new one for me.''
<p>
    &UR; Send requests or answers to requests on a sheet of paper
containing &QL; 
 &UR; your name, address and phone number to Ellen Hawks, Recipe Finder, &QL; 
 &UR; The Baltimore Sun, 501 N. Calvert St., Baltimore MD 21278. &QL; 
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC;

<BYLINE> By Maria Hiaasen </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; RUSH HOUR REMEDY: Campbell's Restaurant Soup a Pricey Wannabe &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Item: Campbell's Restaurant Soup
<p>
   What you get: 7 to 8 servings or 28 to 32 ounces, depending on
variety
<p>
   Cost: $6.49
<p>
   Time to prepare: 20-30 minutes stovetop or 12 to 20 minutes
microwave (depending on variety of soup)
<p>
   Review: I think Campbell's ought to rethink this one. The display
in my grocer's frozen food section promised restaurant taste from
flavors like New England clam chowder and tomato pasta Florentine. But
I found these frozen soups on a par with condensed soup from a can,
and I certainly didn't want to buy seven or eight servings at once.
Avoid this pricey and inconvenient wannabe (12 to 20 minutes, even in
the microwave?), and reach for a can of Campbell's canned chunky soup
when you need a break from tomato or chicken noodle.

<HEADER> - a0850 09-02 0334 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-forklore - a0850
 &UR; (ATTN: Food editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Charles Perry </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; FORKLORE: French Lesson: It's Batter Up for the Pancake &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Fifteen years ago, a French tourist guidebook expressed shock at
the American idea of breakfast: ``Their crepes are over a centimeter
thick!'' This was almost more horrifying to the writer than what we
put on our ``crepes,'' a syrup made from tree sap.
<p>
   The French writer should really get out more. Many of the world's
pancakes are thicker and, well, more cake-like than a French crepe.
Crepe-like pancakes are mostly used for wrapping stuff in; pancakes
eaten by themselves tend to be thick.
<p>
   Pancakes are made from batter, rather than from a kneaded dough
with a good gluten structure, so they often have eggs mixed in to keep
them from falling apart as they fry. Possibly because eggs didn't
often come their way, medieval Central Asian nomads made a pancake
called chalpak by kneading dough and then laboriously mixing in more
water to get a sort of glutinous batter.
<p>
   Thicker pancakes have usually been made with yeasted batter. The
Gold Rush miners who lived on bacon, coffee and sourdough flapjacks
were following an old tradition.
<p>
   However, Americans always have been impatient, and about 200 years
ago we invented baking soda for instant leavening. Hence the
buttermilk pancake; buttermilk provided the acidity to make the baking
soda work.
<p>
   But even that was too inconvenient, so we invented baking powder.
The first flour sold with baking powder already mixed in appeared in
1889 under the brand name Aunt Jemima. A woman named Nancy Green was
hired to be Aunt Jemima (making her possibly the world's first
spokesmodel) and served more than a million pancakes to visitors to
the Chicago World's Fair. She toured the country for the next 30
years.
<p>
   By the way, the reason foreign pancakes never taste quite right to
us is that we Americans are the only people who always put a dash of
corn meal in our pancake batter. &QL; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 1154EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>
