<SLUG> bc-rosenberg </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0850 09-02 0986 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-rosenberg - a0850
 &UR; (ATTN: Entertainment editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Howard Rosenberg </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; ROSENBERG ON TV: This Time, `Defenders' Will Hold Court on Showtime &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   From ``Marty,'' ``Days of Wine and Roses'' and ``Dragnet'' to
``Mission: Impossible'' and the just-released ``Leave It to Beaver,''
theatrical movies have been doing television for decades.
<p>
   So it's only appropriate that TV should copy TV too.
<p>
   In a sense, it's done just that, cannibalizing itself through the
years like no other entertainment medium. ``The Fugitive,'' starring
Harrison Ford, for example, was a successful feature-film remake of
Quinn Martin's ABC hit of the 1960s that itself became a prototype for
scores of man-on-the-lam series.
<p>
   Now it's Showtime, in association with Paramount, making some of us
happy by again drawing on the past with a series of movies reinventing
that scintillating old CBS legal series, ``The Defenders''  &MD;  with E.G.
Marshall, now 87, reprising the idealistic attorney he introduced in
1961. This time, though, no love interest.
<p>
   ``The Fugitive'' gave life to a concept that will surely resonate
on TV into the next millennium. How curious that Reginald Rose's ``The
Defenders,'' which arrived as a regular series just two years before
``The Fugitive,'' resonated so fleetingly. And no one quite knows why.
<p>
   Although widely celebrated, and enormously bold and cutting-edge on
legal and moral issues for its time, ``The Defenders'' seems to have
quietly vanished from the U.S. airwaves after ending its prime-time
run in 1965.
<p>
   ``These 132 hours of television were sitting in the vaults back
East some place,'' said Stan Rogow, executive producer for the
Showtime ``Defenders'' movies  &MD;  the first of which airs Oct. 12. A
second one has also been shot, Rogow said, and scripts are being
written for two more.
<p>
   An attorney himself, Rogow said that ``The Defenders'' is ``the
show that made me go to law school.'' He had been asking for years,
``Why can't we make `The Defenders?''' only to be told there was a
``rights problem.'' As it turned out, Paramount, where Rogow had a TV
development deal, held three-sevenths of the TV rights, he said, with
Rose, the show's creator, retaining the balance.
<p>
   Rose, who lives in Connecticut, is no stranger to Showtime: A
successful new version of his durable play, ``12 Angry Men,'' ran on
the pay-cable network two weeks ago. He is listed as a co-writer and
consultant for Showtime's ``The Defenders.''
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Although Showtime liked the notion of ``The Defenders'' as a sort
of TV-movie franchise, Rogow said he was turned down by the major
networks when he pitched it to them as a new series. Still in their
minds, apparently, were the O.J. Simpson case and lingering public
attitudes about the law and defense attorneys.
<p>
   ``No one was interested because it was about a defense lawyer,''
said Rogow. ``It was the networks' perception that the public wanted
to see shows about people being put behind bars.''
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   What has the United States been missing all these years? When it
comes to ``The Defenders'' series, something special.
<p>
   It began as a Rose-written play on ``Studio One'' in 1957, and,
when turned into a series four years later, still retained an aura
recalling TV anthologies of that era. ``The writers and the actors
were all people in New York at that time doing `Studio One' and
`Playhouse 90,''' said Rogow. ``It had a stature and tone to it, and
nothing like that is done anymore.''
<p>
   Not quite like that, anyway, even though today's prime time has no
shortage of quality legal series, led by NBC's ``Law &AMP; Order.''
<p>
   ``The Defenders,'' though, was about a small law firm consisting of
the widowed veteran Lawrence Preston (Marshall) and his son, Kenneth,
a recent law school graduate, played by Robert Reed. With Reed now
dead, Beau Bridges assumes second chair in the Showtime movies as
Preston's younger son, and Martha Plimpton is the firm's third member,
playing Kenneth's daughter, a former prosecutor.
<p>
   They have a lot to live up to. There were no shiny suits in the
original. Instead, both Prestons wore their ethics proudly and had
high principles.
<p>
   The casts often included actors who went on to become big stars,
and the scripts were personalized and intimate. In addition, what
distinguished ``The Defenders'' was its courage in embracing a range
of controversial topics  &MD;  from abortion, mercy killing and political
blacklisting  &MD;  rarely addressed by the timid TV entertainment of that
age. What's more, their cases sometimes ended as ambiguously as issues
do in life. And unlike Perry Mason, the Prestons were fallible in the
courtroom, losing on more than one occasion.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   ``I remember one episode,'' said Rogow, ``that started with Dustin
Hoffman throwing a ball against a wall. There was a racket upstairs,
and the camera pans up there, where Ruth Roman has gotten into an
argument with her husband. She shoots him, and the Prestons defend
her. They tried the abuse excuse  &MD;  this was only 1961  &MD;  and they lost.
At the end of the show, she goes to the gas chamber.''
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Controversy typifies the Showtime movies, too. ``The Defenders:
Payback'' finds the Prestons defending a father, played by John
Larroquette, who shoots down a man who assaulted his daughter. The
second movie has a cop killing a suspect, raising the question of
whether the shooting was police brutality or self-defense.
<p>
   In addition to substance, ``The Defenders'' still has Rose, who
reads every script, Rogow said, and knows better than anyone what the
Prestons are about. Rogow recalls hearing from Rose about a line of
dialogue that he didn't like. ``He said, `Lawrence Preston wouldn't
say that.''' How did Rogow respond? ``I said, `OK.'''
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 1154EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<STORYID cat=k pri=r sel=tm--a> X3233 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-india-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0851 09-02 1307 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-india-comment - a0851
 &UR; (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Paula R. Newberg </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to the Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Power of the Market Raises India's Star &QL; 
 &UR; Newberg, has lived and worked in South Asia, and is a senior &QL; 
 &UR; associate at Georgetown University. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   If symbols define politics, then India's Independence Day
celebrations in Washington on Aug. 15 spoke volumes about the changing
profile of U.S.-lndian relations. Flanked by Secretary of State
Madeleine K. Albright and Commerce Secretary William M. Daley, first
lady Hillary Rodham Clinton announced to about 1,300 assembled Indian
Americans that President Clinton soon would visit the subcontinent.
For a region notably absent from official travel schedules, the
president's trip  &MD;  and, separately, those of Albright, Daley and a
raft of deputies  &MD;  suggests renewed interest in a country whose
relations with the United States have often been testy.
<p>
   India's rising estimate in the United States probably illustrates
more about America than about India. Mercantilism now tints U.S.
diplomacy, and the bigger the trading market, the brighter our
diplomatic colors. Even if current figures are exaggerated  &MD;  India
claims its growing middle class is close to 200 million people  &MD; 
consumer potential is enormous. Moreover, educated, prosperous Indians
have become significant business partners: Since serious economic
reform began 10 years ago, foreign partnerships have extended and
diversified across the business community. India not only trades but
also produces  &MD;  for the West, as well as Asia and Africa.
<p>
   To Indians, this is no surprise: The increasing world interest
simply vindicates their long-term (if occasionally changeable)
policies and plans. Since independence 50 years ago, India has worked
doggedly to create a place for itself in the world. Its leaders,
regardless of political stripe, consistently have sought to anchor
India's economy and security in an international setting. Its
ambitions are no less than a great power's: a seat at the table when
the world's affairs  &MD;  money, development, war, weapons  &MD;  are
discussed.
<p>
   But these aspirations accompany serious liabilities, for India, its
interlocutors and its partners. India represents bundles of
contradictions, as most travelers quickly note: half a billion poor
living alongside the nascent rich; an agricultural economy that can
feed most of its population but no longer supports its farmers; far
more trained job-seekers than jobs, creating a labor market that feeds
exasperation and political extremism; a cumbersome bureaucracy that
often stifles the same entrepreneurs whose efforts should ground
economic prosperity, and a highly militarized state whose value is, in
an era of prospective peace, contestable.
<p>
   The old India  &MD;  impoverished, famine stricken, sharply assertive  &MD; 
slowly is being replaced by a new India with more rounded edges. The
distinction is never fully clear, however; no development process is
completed overnight. It is a changing India  &MD;  neither new India nor
old, neither black not white, but the India of contradictions and
many, many gray areas  &MD;  that compels our attention. For in its
contradictions lies the success or failure of renewed U.S. engagement.
<p>
   Washington has not really been listening to the complexities that
compose India, and its deafness is dangerously selective. When the
United States responds to India's bywords of democratic secularism, it
willingly ignores resolute class problems that pervade its society and
economy. When the United States gleefully embraces businessmen who
speak the language of Wall Street, it assumes the old socialism is
dead and the detritus of the centralized state soon will wither away.
Not so. Centralism has played a critical role in India's development,
and until the state's constituencies can be satisfied differently,
India's unique mixture of money and militarism almost undoubtedly will
continue.
<p>
   First, the ideology of the old India was socialism, and with it
came a large state and concentrated central power. This structure
outlived its usefulness and created industrial behemoths that still
resist change as much as India's bureaucracy. But ideology doesn't die
with the first breaths of reform. Indeed, the enduring legacy of old
India is not the fact of socialism, but its promise of equity.
<p>
   For the vast army of the poor whose sheer numbers can sink future
profit centers, this promise is sacrosanct  &MD;  even if they have given
up on corrupt politicians who rarely deliver on familiar slogans.
Disputatious politicians and dominating bureaucrats still know the
pulse of the poor, who understandably fear the indifference of
unfettered capitalism. Until this unevenness is leveled, old
ideologies will endure, the promise of profit will be limited and the
bureaucratic state will surely survive.
<p>
   Second, India's large, modern and assumedly nuclearized military,
whose primacy rarely is contested, draws its authority from two
separate impulses: insecurity about national borders and a sense of
regional, if not international, destiny. Border insecurity gradually
is diminishing: Fears about China seem quelled for the moment;
relations with Bangladesh and Nepal, achieved by diminishing old
hegemonic impulses, seem smoother as well. The Pakistan border and the
festering sore of Kashmir, however, continue to impede regional
stability and domestic peace.
<p>
   These are the places where internal and external politics meet:
with hundreds of thousands of security forces along the Kashmir and
Pakistan borders; regular armed incidents between the Pakistani and
Indian armies, and police actions against insurgents in Kashmir.
India's profile along its northwest frontier is militaristic and, in
Kashmir, far from democratic.
<p>
   Destiny is more difficult to qualify, particularly when nuclear
weapons are involved. India's nuclear arabesque with the West
highlights fundamental incompatibilities between U.S.
nonproliferationists and India's nuclear establishment. India refuses
to curtail its program until the rest of the world, including the
United States, does too. It considers itself a member of the nuclear
club  &MD;  not a supplicant to its arbitrary rules. Washington has been
unable to resolve this stalemate  &MD;  and equally unable to arbitrate
disputes between its in-house nonproliferation advocates and its India
promoters, who envision shared profits and, perhaps in the distant
future, some form of nuclear control in the subcontinent.
<p>
   The lure of Mammon may settle this wrongheaded dispute  &MD;  Washington
is far more interested in India's economy than in repetitive nuclear
debates. But this choice entails significant cost. India is right to
dispute the rules of the nuclear game, but only if it will subject
itself to new ones  &MD;  a debatable proposition. India's future role in
Asian security will depend on establishing new trust with its
neighbors and the West, and this, too, depends on keeping its nuclear
word.
<p>
   But India's demand for greater voice  &MD;  backed by increasing
economic muscle  &MD;  nonetheless echoes our own discussions about the
responsibilities of power today. India's policies and practices remind
us that superpower politics may be evanescent and, more important,
that the club of global decision-makers must be expanded if peace is
to prevail in the next century.
<p>
   Politics remains the art of the possible  &MD;  for India's President
Kocheril Raman Narayanan as much as for Clinton. If anything has
changed in U.S.-Indian relations, it is, at least for the moment, a
new sense of possibility. But for possibility to be realized, both
sides will have to think more clearly about their long-term interests
 &MD;  by replacing lectures with more acute listening, and the simplicity
of bilateralism with nuanced internationalism. Only then can
partnership begin.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 1155EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>
</DOC>

<DOCID> latwp970902.0058 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=k pri=r sel=tm--a> X3234 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-easteurope-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0852 09-02 1606 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-easteurope-comment - a0852
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Hal Piper </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Welcome to the Western World &QL; 
 &UR; Piper, The Baltimore Sun's Opinion/Commentary editor, reported from &QL; 
 &UR; Eastern Europe before the fall of communism. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Time was when urban grime, shabby clothes and empty shop shelves
told you that you were behind the Iron Curtain. No longer. Young women
in platform sandals and tattoos flirt with young men with nose rings.
On sidewalks and in cars, businessmen and teen-agers are glued to
their cell phones. Buskers and beggars patrol crowded sidewalk cafes.
One Hungarian family's various members summered this year in Cancun,
Mexico; Corfu and Malta.
<p>
   Eight years after the marvelous revolutions of 1989, life in
Eastern Europe seems remarkably unremarkable  &MD;  downright normal.
<p>
   ``Well, it may appear that way to you,'' chides Andras Farago,
media assistant to the president of Hungary, ``but our
balance-of-payments crisis has mandated a wage freeze. In conjunction
with 13 percent inflation, that has meant a fall in living standards
over the last two years.''
<p>
   One really ought not to laugh at adversity, but Farago has proved
the point.
<p>
   Balance-of-payments crises and falling living standards are the
most normal of afflictions for normal countries.
<p>
   Hungarians used to worry  &MD;  has Farago forgotten?  &MD;  about their
neighbors informing on them to the secret police. About the necessity
of whispering to their children truths that contradicted the
children's school lessons. About their ``brotherly'' next-door
neighbor Russia, which claimed the right to oversee developments in
Hungary  &MD;  and backed it up with military force.
<p>
   Now Hungarians worry about living standards, unemployment and the
balance of payments. Welcome to the capitalist world, Hungary! And
Poland, East Germany, the Czech Republic.
<p>
   There is real pain in all these countries. Inflation has eroded
savings and destroyed pensions. Medical care has become unaffordable
for many. Unemployment is high in all the former Soviet satellites,
and nowhere higher (estimates range up to 30 percent) than in eastern
Germany.
<p>
   There are so many obsolete skills. Who, after all, would hire a
professor of Marxist science? Or a lathe operator, now that computers
run lathes? Or a former mid-level secret-police technician? Or the 18
waiters and waitresses who used to work in a restaurant now replaced
by a McDonald's with five employees?
<p>
   The transition from communism to capitalism has brought hardship to
many who deserved better. To others it has brought undeserved wealth.
Ridiculously often in all the old Soviet satellite countries, old
Communists effortlessly have transformed themselves into new
capitalists and carried on manipulating the system to their personal
benefit. ``In a hundred years,'' says Christopher Bobinski, a
journalist who has studied the ``privatization'' sell-offs of
state-owned property in Poland, ``you will find that most great Polish
fortunes were founded by friends or operatives of the secret police.''
<p>
   So there is plenty to complain about these sun-kissed late-summer
days  &MD;  in Eastern Europe, as everywhere else. Unless we think back to
our nightmares of just eight years ago.
<p>
   As the Communist regimes toppled like so many dominoes in the
miracle year of 1989, the world held its breath, thinking of all the
things that could go wrong:
<p>
   War might break out, either because the Soviet Union refused to
countenance the liberation of its satellites, or because the
Communists in the satellites refused to go quietly. But there was no
war, and except in Romania, almost no bloodshed at all.
<p>
   With the collapse of Soviet-style, command economies, heat,
electricity and food deliveries might fail. But they didn't. Nobody
starved or froze.
<p>
   Without the suffocating blanket of Soviet control, bitter
nationalist enmities might rekindle. But that didn't happen. Slovak
nationalists peacefully dissolved their artificial union with the
Czech lands. Elsewhere, nationalist rabble-rousers  &MD;  such as Russia's
Vladimir Zhirinovsky  &MD;  came to prominence, then faded. Nowhere in
Eastern Europe is there a rightist movement as potentially powerful as
France's National Front.
<p>
   Reunited Germany might turn its back on Europe and assert a sphere
of dominance in Eastern Europe. But it didn't. Germany is indeed the
largest investor in Eastern Europe  &MD;  directly or indirectly, it
controls up to 40 percent of the Hungarian economy, according to
Farago  &MD;  but it remains committed to the integration of Europe through
the European Union.
<p>
   And then  &MD;  in Lithuania, Poland, Romania and Hungary  &MD;  old
Communist parties, perfumed and renamed, began to return to power
through the ballot box. Would this mean a roll-back of the revolutions
of 1989? It didn't. The former Communists' success signaled the
healthiest thing that can possibly happen to emerging democracies  &MD; 
the normal, peaceful alternation of political groupings from
government to opposition and back. Life is normal again in Eastern
Europe.
<p>
   The great exception, of course, is Yugoslavia, which has dominated
the front pages with carnage, suffering and crimes against humanity.
But Yugoslavia is an exception  &MD;  a tragedy indeed, but one that should
not overshadow the extraordinary success of the rest of the formerly
Communist states in shedding their old regimes and peacefully
rejoining Europe.
<p>
   Best of all, the people who live there are starting to believe in
Eastern Europe's recovery. Economies seem to be on the upswing in
Poland and Russia, and Moscow is even reporting a modest baby boom,
after years of falling birthrates.
<p>
   Jan Zelenka, a Czech book editor, says that beyond the reflexive
pessimism endemic to Central Europe, his countrymen had to learn new
expectations.
<p>
   ``The government always took care of everybody  &MD;  badly, but it took
care of them,'' he says. Hence, when a business venture fails, people
have difficulty understanding that ``freedom to fail'' is the norm in
a free and dynamic economy  &MD;  and that there will be second, third and
fourth chances.
<p>
   Bobinski, an Anglo-Polish journalist in Warsaw, is even more
enthusiastic.
<p>
   ``This is probably the best time in this whole century to be young
and Polish,'' he says. ``The opportunities are limitless.'' He does
not make such judgments lightly, for his own two children are
teen-agers and will make their futures in Poland - or not, because
Poles can travel now.
<p>
   The younger generation, Bobinski says, knows nothing about life
before 1989, when Communist power collapsed.
<p>
   ``They have no baggage. They grew up without the double messages of
what was taught in school and what was said at home. They expect a
normal life. They can't imagine a living in a country that forbids
travel. They can't imagine a secret police. Or fearing a Russian
invasion.''
<p>
   These young people, and others like them in the rest of Central
Europe, are the future. The world may commiserate with retired people
whose pensions lost their value, or with 40-year-olds who can no
longer find work in post-Communist society, but such people represent
a shrinking reality.
<p>
   Even Hungary's Farago, an economist by training, admits: ``I am
very optimistic about things five years from now.''
<p>
   By then, he hopes that Hungary will be on its way to membership in
the European Union. Hungary, Poland, the Czech Republic, Romania and
Slovenia (one of the former constituents of Yugoslavia) have been put
on the fast track for admission to the continental grouping.
<p>
   Negotiations will not be easy. Poland and Hungary, in particular,
are low-cost agricultural producers that could swamp Western Europe
with cheap meat and grain products  &MD;  good news for the consumers, but
not the farmers, of France and Germany. Still, all the European
governments, east and west, seem committed to making European
integration happen.
<p>
   And the former Communists who control the Polish and Hungarian
governments are the biggest boosters of all.
<p>
   ``They've gotten more than anyone else out of the switch to
capitalism,'' explains Bobinski. ``They're the last ones who would
want to go back.''
<p>
   In its last years, it seems, communism recruited opportunists, not
ideologues. So apolitical former Communists now have the most
experience and talent for running things, whether factories or
political meetings. Their recapture of the country's wealth and power
positions is ``an aesthetic problem,'' Bobinski acknowledges, but
possibly the new regimes' best guarantee of stability. He tells a
story that has become famous in Poland.
<p>
   Poland's most famous dissident, the poet Adam Michnik, and its
current president, Aleksander Kwasniewski, a former Communist, became
warm friends during the months of arduous negotiations leading to the
eventual Communist surrender of power. In recent years, they have
appeared together often at forums and seminars in foreign countries,
where politicians and academics discuss and ponder the extraordinary
peaceful collapse of communism.
<p>
   After one such session, Michnik and Kwasniewski were enjoying a
nightcap in a hotel bar when the bill was presented. Michnik, ever and
still the poet-intellectual, had no money. Kwasniewski, the former
Communist reputed now to be a millionaire many times over, expansively
picked up the check and flourished his credit card.
<p>
   ``And, Adam, my dear friend,'' Kwasniewski is quoted as saying,
``may I thank you for making it possible for us Communists to travel
with credit cards.''
<p>
   Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-justice-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0852 09-02 0866 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-justice-comment - a0852
 &UR; (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By T. Markus Funk </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to the Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; JUVENILE JUSTICE: The Price We Pay for Cleaning the Slate &QL; 
 &UR; Funk is an Oxford University Ph.D. candidate in law. This article is &QL; 
 &UR; based on a piece written with Professor Dan Polsby of Northwestern. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   If there is anything approaching a truism in criminal law, it is
that violent men most likely were violent teens. This fact alone
should at least temper the contemporary optimism concerning decreases
in adult crime rates. Consider, for example, that the most recent U.S.
Justice Department statistics estimate that the juvenile arrest rate
for murder has jumped 128 percent between 1983 and 1992, and that the
juvenile arrest rate for offenses such as forcible rape and robbery
has increased by 57 percent during the same time period. As today's
teens reach majority, then, there inevitably will be a corresponding
dramatic increase in overall victimization levels.
<p>
   While some states try to grapple with this troubling reality, most
still cling to ``expungement'' statutes that allow or even mandate the
destruction or sealing of juvenile criminal records  &MD;  often including
even rape and murder convictions. In so doing, these jurisdictions
prevent the appropriate sentencing of young adult offenders, impede
effective law enforcement and hurt private business.
<p>
   An obvious implication from the unprecedented increases in juvenile
crime is that we, as a society, have a stronger interest in
determining which recidivistic and violent teens will become
tomorrow's violent and predatory adult offenders and that we therefore
must treat them differently from their peers who have merely committed
one-time acts of juvenile indiscretion. And while few would argue that
a nonviolent juvenile mistake should forever haunt a reformed adult,
expungement statutes often destroy the extensive and violent criminal
histories of chronic juvenile offenders for the sole reason that they
reach age 18, when the level of their involvement in criminal activity
is still very much on the rise.
<p>
   In addition, many states give judges unbridled discretion as to
when and what to expunge. Typically, a judge is instructed simply to
determine what is in the ``best interest of the child.'' But it is
society  &MD;  not the 17-year-old ``child''  &MD;  that may be victimized in
the future. Therefore, we all have an interest in keeping tabs on an
individual who has evidenced a willingness to break the law and to
disrespect other people's rights to property or safety. What is more,
the extremely broad and unguided discretion accorded many state courts
raises the troubling possibility of some juveniles having their
records expunged on the basis of their race or socioeconomic class
rather than on the basis of their rehabilitative potential as
evidenced by extensive crime-free periods. Because juveniles in these
jurisdictions must ``petition'' the court for expungement, those with
the financial wherewithal and legal sophistication to know that this
option is available and how to exercise it will have an advantage.
<p>
   Most state expungement schemes were drafted in the 1960s, when it
was widely believed that violent juveniles would outgrow their
delinquent tendencies.
<p>
   While this surely does happen in individual instances, most
research shows that such a metamorphosis from chronic predator to
law-abiding citizen is more an aberration than the norm. In the 1987
book ``From Boy to Man, From Delinquency to Crime,'' University of
Pennsylvania criminologist Marvin E. Wolfgang and his co-authors found
a strong correlation between juvenile delinquency and adult crime and
concluded that juvenile delinquency is the ``best predictor of adult
criminality.''
<p>
   A 1994 report on juvenile crime prepared for Gov. Mario M. Cuomo
found the recidivism rate among New York juvenile offenders to be
approaching 90 percent. Similarly, a 1991 study by researchers John C.
Steiger and Cary Dizon tracked 926 males released from the Washington
state division of juvenile rehabilitation in 1982; of these, 68
percent were reconvicted within two years and 53 percent committed at
least one violent offense within that time. In short, juvenile
delinquents often turn into adult criminals, and adult criminals
almost never were crime-free as juveniles. But expungement can make it
impossible for employers to identify job applicants with histories of
theft or violence. It also can impede law enforcement efforts to
identify repeat offenders by their patterns of criminal conduct.
<p>
   Expungement merits re-examination. The monumental increases in
juvenile crime, both in numbers and severity, already have caused
states such as Pennsylvania to limit the circumstances under which it
is permitted by, for example, prohibiting the expungement of a minor's
record unless he or she has been crime-free for at least five years.
While it is appealing to view expungement statutes as benign attempts
to reduce the penalty that public opinion places on former offenders
by allowing them to enter adulthood with a clean (or cleaned) slate,
we must understand that such societal beneficence comes at a price.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-affirm-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0853 09-02 0842 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-affirm-comment - a0853
 &UR; (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Herma Hill Kay and M. Michael Sharlot </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to the Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Higher Education Affirmative Action Was a Success &QL; 
 &UR; Hill Kay is dean of the UC Berkeley School of Law (Boalt Hall). &QL; 
 &UR; Sharlot is dean of the University of Texas School of Law. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   These are challenging times for public law schools in California
and Texas. In the past year, our affirmative action programs for
attracting and educating minority students have been dismantled. These
programs had enabled the University of Texas Law School to overcome a
tragic legacy that, until 1950, intentionally excluded African
Americans. Although no such discrimination had existed at the
University of California, few minority students applied or were
admitted to the University of California, Berkeley, before the late
1960s.
<p>
   In the past 30 years, affirmative action programs have enabled us
to recruit diverse student bodies that brought intellectual depth to
our educational programs and produced leaders in the legal profession.
<p>
   This year, we were forced to change our educational approach to
conform to race-neutral requirements imposed in Texas by the 5th U.S.
Circuit Court of Appeals' Hopwood decision in 1996, and in California
by a 1995 UC Regents' resolution and subsequently by a 1996 statewide
initiative banning affirmative action. Both schools have had to adopt
race-neutral admissions policies, thus minimizing the opportunities
for all students to benefit from the perspectives of society's
traditionally marginalized groups.
<p>
   The substitution of race-neutral selection factors, such as
socioeconomic class and disadvantage, produces a different student
mix, one that is less reflective of racial conflicts in society that
pose special challenges to the administration of justice. And as one
law professor observed, ``How can you teach Brown vs. Board of
Education with no African American students in the classroom?''
<p>
   In our first year of race-neutral admissions, both schools
experienced a drop of 66 percent in the number of offers of admission
to applicants from traditionally underrepresented groups. The
successful applicants are excellent students also eagerly recruited by
private law schools with enormous financial aid resources. As a
result, of the 11 African Americans admitted at Texas, no more than
four are expected to attend; at Berkeley, all 14 African Americans are
going elsewhere, all but one to prestigious private schools.
<p>
   Some argue that the numbers show that we have been admitting
unqualified minority students. We strongly disagree. Law schools do
not admit students only by their undergraduate grade point average and
Law School Admissions Test scores. Because law students participate
actively in their educational process, law schools seek an
intellectually diverse class of students from different backgrounds
who can challenge each other's ideas. To achieve this mix, we and
other law schools have looked beyond the numbers to an applicant's
background, educational and life experience, initiative,
self-discipline, creativity, success in overcoming obstacles and
future promise as a lawyer.
<p>
   Our admissions policies utilizing affirmative action were
strikingly successful. The experiences and perspectives of these
talented minority students improved our schools. Many are now partners
in law firms, directors of public interest firms' law professors,
judges and deans.
<p>
   Some argue that it does not matter if these students are not
admitted to Texas and Berkeley since they can go elsewhere. But public
law schools have in the past served as gateways to the legal
profession for students of merit drawn from all parts of our
incredibly heterogeneous society. It is vital that major public law
schools in other states continue to play this role in providing a
first-rate legal education to a student body reflective of the society
whose laws they will administer.
<p>
   What can be done at our schools to continue to preserve diversity
while complying with the new admissions policies? We are reviewing our
admissions processes to see whether improvements are possible. Since
we can no longer take race or ethnicity into account, we cannot in the
short run hope to achieve the diversity that was once our hallmark. We
can and will call upon the private sector for help. Private
foundations have raised and awarded scholarships and financial aid to
public law school students, and these programs must be pursued more
aggressively.
<p>
   For the future, we will work on outreach efforts designed to
prepare traditionally underrepresented minority students to attend the
university and aspire to become lawyers.
<p>
   Ultimately, the issues we face are simply a reflection of the
crossroads at which America now stands. This country's past efforts to
address racial inequalities have been discarded in the public
institutions of our two states and face serious challenges nationwide.
Americans must think carefully about the long-term implications of
such change.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-reno </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0854 09-02 0828 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-reno - a0854
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Editorial Page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Robert Reno </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; RENO AT LARGE: Heavy Toll for Personal Freedom &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   I confidently predict  &MD;  and I hope to heaven I'm wrong  &MD;  that
within 10 years, E-ZPass toll devices, or some more advanced version
of them, will be mandatory on all vehicles.
<p>
   Soon, the monthly E-ZPass statement of most motorists will exceed
their telephone or electric bills.
<p>
   Let me explain why I'm not nuts.
<p>
   The E-ZPass is, of course, that small object people attach to their
windshield that allows them to cruise through busy toll plazas and be
billed later. The urban toll plaza is a late 20th-century horror.
Labor intensive, vulnerable to robbery, they increase pollution, cause
accidents, add to commuters' travel time and reduce the productivity
of commercial vehicles. They defeat the purpose of building
billion-dollar bridges, tunnels and turnpikes to speed traffic flow
because they do more to impede tra ffic flow than any other obstacle.
Their operating costs suck up much of the toll money that's supposed
to be used for maintenance and to retire bonds.
<p>
   So the E-ZPass is a technological blessing, the more so because it
will allow the tolling authorities to use imaginative pricing,
lowering tolls during off hours and raising them in rush hours in ways
that will reduce congestion.
<p>
   But the E-ZPass, much as it is a great convenience, is also one of
the most efficient revenue generators ever invented. It will most
surely become an inducement for municipalities to slap tolls on
less-used roads and crossings where manual toll collection is simply
not feasible. If unmanned toll points are vulnerable to evasion, well,
technology has already given us the hidden cameras that are used to
give out silent tickets to people who run red lights. And they can
always install menacing spikes tha t rise out of the pavement and
threaten to flatten the tires of toll beaters who try to hide their
E-ZPasses.
<p>
   With so many of the nation's bridges in a dangerous condition,
states and municipalities will be under tremendous pressure to finance
repairs and replacement with new toll systems that place costs on the
user but do not have the drawback of delaying traffic or requiring
24-hour manning. About 14 percent of urban expressways are officially
classified in poor to mediocre condition and more than a third of the
interstate highway system is in similarly rotten repair. Easily
collected tolls  &MD;  ``user fees''
<p>
   is the euphemism  &MD;  become the alternative of choice for politicians
who want to brag they never voted to raise ``taxes.''
<p>
   The cumbersome and sometimes dangerous process of hauling over
speeding motorists could be phased out just as soon as police agencies
discover computers mounted on roadside poles can identify vehicles,
spew out 1,000 tickets an hour and automatically mail them to
offenders.
<p>
   The national and state park systems, under pressure to increase
user fees, will find automatic toll systems irresistible, both as a
cheap way to collect revenues and a means of spreading park usage more
evenly during weekdays and the off-season. The National Park Service,
operating on a budget that in inflation-adjusted terms is half what it
was 20 years ago, and faced with an unfunded bill for $5 billion in
deferred maintenance, will find automatic toll collection the obvious
choice both at its most popular attractions and at less visited sites
that aren't normally manned.
<p>
   And, of course, it can't be long before the private sector
discovers the glories of automatic tolls. Congested shopping malls
will find it a neat way to finance double decking of parking lots, new
access roads or simply a way to supplement rental income. Private
tourist attractions, parking garages, automatic car washes, even I
guess, drive-through fast-food restaurants, will find this cashless
system attractive not just as a cost cutter but as a profit maximizer.
<p>
   After all, the automated teller machine, now ubiquitous in the
banking system, was developed as a way to cut labor costs. But banks
have since discovered their ATMs can be profit centers. They slap on
extra ATM fees even as they get rid of human tellers who have to be
paid wages and provided with benefits and pensions. Surely, automatic
toll collection can similarly be squeezed as a new profit source.
<p>
   But if you want to know what's really scary about automatic toll
collection, stop to think of the endless possibilities it opens up for
surveillance of private citizens. The time can't be far off when the
detailed travel records of every motorist in America are neatly stored
in data banks, available to be rummaged through by the police, the
CIA, the FBI and any hacker or snoop who's interested, subpoenaed by
congressional committees or generally misused in ways the technology
geniuses haven't even thought of yet.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<p>
   The Morris Arboretum of the University of Pennsylvania is offering
a tour of New Zealand gardens in January when flowers are in full
summer bloom. The three-week tour begins Jan. 10. Called Gardens, Wine
and Wilderness, the tour will be led by a New Zealand horticulturist
and landscape architect. The small group will visit private and public
gardens, nature preserves and wilderness areas. Vineyard tours and
wine tastings are included. Travel is by motor coach, boat and
internal flights.
<p>
   Cost: $6,400 per person, double occupancy, including one night in a
hotel in Los Angeles, where the group will visit a local garden;

transportation; lodging; most meals; and guides.

    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   FISHING NEW ZEALAND: If garden flowers aren't exciting enough, then
try fly-fishing in New Zealand's lakes and rivers. The tour is from
March 19 to 29. Guests will land in Auckland and travel to the
Tongariro Lodge on the shores of Lake Taupo and the banks of the
Tongariro River, one of New Zealand's most popular fly-fishing spots.
<p>
   Then the group will fly to the South Island to stay at the Lake
Rotoroa Lodge on the northern part of South Island. It is a
Victorian-style fishing lodge built in the 1920s. The group will spend
three days there fishing for brown trout.

Los Angeles, accommodations, meals, guides, domestic flights and

    &UR; -0- &QC;

are the sights on a trans-Canada rail tour Feb. 22 to 28. The train
passes through Winnipeg, Saskatoon, Edmonton and Jasper. The tour
begins with two nights in Toronto with a day excursion to Niagara
Falls with lunch at the Skylon Tower.
<p>
   During three days and nights riding across Canada from Toronto to
Vancouver, guests view scenery from day window seats but have sleeping
accommodations at night.
<p>
   Following the rail portion, guests stay overnight in Vancouver and
take a sightseeing tour of the city and surrounding area, followed by
a farewell dinner and an evening theater performance.
<p>
   Cost: $1,775 per person, double occupancy, including Via Rail
sleeping accommodations, round-trip air fare to Toronto and return
home from Vancouver, hotels, all meals except one dinner in Toronto,
sightseeing tours and admissions, transfers, tour escort and local
guides.

Pasadena, Calif. 91107; (626) 578-1161 or (800) 344-7090.

combines historic sites with scenery. Guests will explore the hills of
Donegal and Kerry, see the Burren (unusual rocky terrain in County
Clare) and the Aran Islands. The group will also get a chance to kiss
the fabled Blarney Stone, tour medieval castles and listen to Irish
ballads.
<p>
   Cost: $2,900 per person, double occupancy, including round-trip air
fare, hotels, ground transportation, daily breakfast, some meals,
entrance fees and porterage.
<p>
   Contact: Betty Leonard at Shamrock Tours, 213-C Avenida Majorca,
Laguna Hills, Calif. 92653; (714) 470-0301.

<SLUG> bc-homework </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0855 09-02 0465 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-homework - a0855
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Karol V. Menzie and Randy Johnson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; HOME WORK: Wood May Be the Problem With Caulking Cedar Siding &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Just when we thought there wasn't anything more we could say about
caulk, we get a letter from a reader in Queenstown, Md., with a
caulking question we can't answer.
<p>
   ``I have cedar siding on my home,'' he writes. ``In caulking the
drip edge, the caulk does not adhere to the cedar. It pulls away after
30 to 60 days. The siding is stained with a Cabot opaque acrylic stain
and I used DAP Acrylic Caulk Plus Silicone. Is there a particular type
or make of caulk to use that is compatible with the properties in
cedar and the stain we have applied to it?''
<p>
   Since it's taking some time for the caulk to lose its adherence,
it's probably a problem with the wood, surmised Larry Horton, vice
president and general manager of Budeke's Paint Co. of Baltimore. Any
exterior wood will expand and contract with the temperature  &MD;  cedar
especially, he said. He suggested using an elastomeric caulk, which is
designed to give a little.
<p>
   ``With silicone, the caulk will stretch only so far, then it lets
go,'' Horton said. ``You need caulking material with a little more
elasticity.''
<p>
   The only problem with the elastomeric caulks is that color
selection may be limited, he said.
<p>
   Horton also had an answer for a reader in Woodbine who has a
problem with paint adhering to a surface. ``What is the best way to
paint pressure-treated wood white? I have tried oil and water-based
paints. None works for very long.''
<p>
   The first step in getting paint to adhere to any surface is proper
preparation  &MD;  it needs to be clean, dry, patched, sanded and, in most
cases, primed.
<p>
   Horton said pressure-treated trim, such as rails and balusters,
needs a good exterior-rated oil-based primer under a good exterior
acrylic finish paint. That gives a ``breathable'' finish, he said, one
that won't crack. ``The problem with any exterior wood is that there
are so many places where moisture can get in - there are so many
cuts'' in the lumber, Horton said.
<p>
   One mistake people make is to try to paint too soon:
Pressure-treated wood needs to season for six to 12 weeks before it
can be covered, he said.
<p>
   He said customers have been happy with the look of a new
solid-color deck stain from Cabot. ``It's formulated a little
differently, but it looks like paint,'' and gives a matte finish to
the wood. However, he said, the product is so new that ``the jury is
still out on long-term performance.''
 &UR; Send tips or experiences about working on houses, to Home Work, &QL; 
 &UR; The Baltimore Sun, 501 N. Calvert St. Baltimore, 21278. &QL; 
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

AP-NY-09-02-97 1158EDT &QL;

<SLUG> bc-roessner </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0855 09-02 0696 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-roessner - a0855
 &UR; (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Barbara T. Roessner </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Hartford Courant
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; ROESSNER COMMENTARY: The Final Words of William S. Burroughs &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Famous last words of the 19th century tended to be something short
of inspiring.
<p>
   ``Is it the Fourth?'' asked Thomas Jefferson in his final utterance
on July 3, 1826. (He died the next day.)
<p>
   ``Strike the tent,'' said Robert E. Lee when he kicked the bucket
in 1870.
<p>
   By the middle of this century, things had improved considerably.
Dylan Thomas, after a lethal bout of drinking in a New York bar in
1953, gasped this goodbye to the universe: ``Seventeen whiskeys. A
record, I think.''
<p>
   And in 1977, Gary Gilmore told his firing squad: ``Let's do it!''
<p>
   Today, as dusk falls on the millennium, an entirely new standard
for the ultimate farewell appears to have been set. With his death
last month at the age of 83, William S. Burroughs, grandfather of Beat
and author of the infamously obscene, infamously nonlinear novel
``Naked Lunch,'' has done to the convention of Last Words what his
life's work did to contemporary American prose  &MD;  clawed it raw and
left it oozing with hilarity and pathos and rage.
<p>
   The current issue of The New Yorker excerpts Burroughs' journal
entries (his only recent writings) from May through the eve of his
death Aug. 2. And if last words are a distillation of a person's short
stint on Earth, Burroughs' was, very simply, one blazing blow against
banality, especially that perpetrated upon the masses by politicians.
<p>
   On May 25, he begins an entry: ``All governments are built on lies.
All organizations are built on lies.''
<p>
   Less than a week later, he elaborates: ``That vile salamander
Gingrich, squeaker of the House, is slobbering about a drug-free
America by the year 2001. What a dreary prospect! Of course this does
not include alcohol and tobacco, of which the consumption will soar.
How can a drug-free state be achieved? Simple. An operation can remove
the drug receptors from the brain. Those who refuse the operation will
be deprived of all rights.''
<p>
   And after a lifetime love affair with heroin, methadone and
marijuana, Burroughs had these departing musings on cannabis and its
effect on his art: ``A few drags on the green tit and I can see
multiple ways out and beyond. So why all this heat on this harmless
and rewarding substance?''
<p>
   Burroughs isn't the first Beat to go out with a whole new concept
in deathbed profundity. When Burroughs' cohort Allen Ginsberg died in
April, Ginsberg's own last words to Burroughs were: ``I thought I
would be terrified, but I am exhilarated!''
<p>
   Timothy Leary, with whom both Ginsberg and Burroughs experimented
extensively with LSD, bid his goodbye in May 1996 with a disyllabic
synopsis of his beliefs, his religion, his personality, his politics
and his attitude toward the great unknown awaiting him: ``Why not?''
<p>
   As the baby boom lurches through the passages of middle age, and
the Xers somnambulate through their first bouts with adulthood, these
old rebels, in their dying words, say a great deal not only about
confronting the ultimate passage, but about the living that precedes
it.
<p>
   In 1994, Ginsberg was asked during a student lecture in Colorado
why the Beats were suddenly inspiring a new and expanded audience.
Listen: ``Because of the sincerity of the works of art, the passion,
the feeling of self-empowerment independent of government, media and
social conditioning, the breaking out of the plastic mass into human
flesh and blood, vulnerability and tenderness''  &MD;  all of which, he
correctly pointed out, stand in raving contrast to ``20 years of the
Reagan-Bush-Nixonian ugly spirit.''
<p>
   Conformity is a sin in the Beat bible. Wrote Burroughs on May 31:
``How good will it be to have total conformity? What will be left of
singularity? And personality? And you and me?''
<p>
   But the greatest sin, perhaps, is disinterest. A numbing of the
spirit, the psyche, the mind. The loss of the ability to feel.
<p>
   The last of Burroughs' last words, penned in a quavering scrawl, is
this: ``LOVE.''
<p>
   Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-music-crystalmethod </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0856 09-02 0965 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-music-crystalmethod - a0856
 &UR; (ATTN: Entertainment editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By D. James Romero </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997 Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; One photo (TPN7) &QL; 
 &UR; The Crystal Method Hopes to Make Mark for U.S. Techno (Los Angeles) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LOS ANGELES  &MD;  With their album ``Vegas,'' the Los Angeles-based duo
behind The Crystal Method has a real chance to put the West Coast
``break beat'' sound on the map.
<p>
   They seem like unlikely techno heroes. Ken Jordan and Scott
Kirkland grew up in working-class Las Vegas and as teen-agers put on
their own dance parties wherever they could, including the local
tavern.
<p>
   ``The only way you could get people to come dance in Las Vegas was
if you sold beer,'' says Kirkland, 26.
<p>
   Beer bashes were a long way from the designer drug-fueled world of
all-night raving that entranced England and the U.S. West Coast by the
turn of the decade. But in 1990, Jordan and Kirkland had moved to the
rave epicenter of Los Angeles to chase what was then a long-shot
dream: To become a techno band.
<p>
   Under the name The Crystal Method, the duo is releasing its debut
album ``Vegas'' this week. It's the most anticipated domestic techno
album of the year  &MD;  what some are billing as the American answer to
the Chemical Brothers and all that other hard-rocking, hip-hopping
U.K. electronic noise that is wooing the world. The group also
represents the best chance of the long overlooked West Coast ``break
beat'' sound going overground.
<p>
   Using synthesizers, drum machines and live guitar licks, the duo is
masterful at appropriating soul-wrenching samples (Jesse Jackson's
angry ``Keep hope alive'') and matching them with appropriately
energetic hip hop beats (also known as break beats or just ``breaks'')
and acidic synths.
<p>
   What gives The Crystal Method an edge in a music scene fast
becoming cluttered with electronic next-big-things is its ability to
play live  &MD;  and play live well. Using a minimum of backing tracks, the
group actually improvises during live shows  &MD;  all the while sweating,
jumping, even abusing its instruments in classic rock style.
<p>
   ``The music,'' says Jordan, 29, ``has traditionally been presented
as an afterthought to the rave culture.''
<p>
   Jordan and Kirkland first bonded over their love of drum machines
and samplers around 1989. Kirkland grew up on Led Zeppelin and Pink
Floyd, but found a new edge in dance music as a deejay at UNLV's
student-run radio station.
<p>
   Jordan was exposed to disco and soul as a kid and took that
heritage to the turntables as a teen. Both became music studio
engineers, which supported their act. In fact, the duo came upon the
name of their group in 1992 when they told a rapper they were assigned
to record that they were going to speed up the process and edit his
album overnight. ``Ah,'' said the emcee, referring to the slang term
for methamphetamine, ``you're going to use the crystal method.''
<p>
   These days the duo still favors beer over the exotic, sometimes
dangerous drug world that surrounds the scene. They seem down to earth
 &MD;  sporting skateboard shoes and short sideburns. They have
responsibilities now.
<p>
   Having just wrapped up the album, the group paired up with
industrial act Filter to remix ``Vegas''' first track, ``Trip Like I
Do,'' for the ``Spawn'' movie soundtrack. And now the two are on the
road with the Electric Highway Tour, an electronic answer to
Lollapalooza.
<p>
   And, in an affirmation of where their heads are at, Jordan and
Kirkland say they are just happy to be able to make and perform music
full time. ``This music out here wasn't really viable financially,''
says Kirkland.
<p>
   ``It was totally underground.''
<p>
   The West Coast sound of break beat techno has been a strong but
quiet force in the electronic pop revolution. The Chemical Brothers,
for one, admit being influenced by San Francisco's Dub Tribe, which
helped pioneer the California style in 1994 with the searing
underground single ``Mother Earth.''
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Today, other California groups are poised to follow The Crystal
Method into national prominence. San Francisco's Hardkiss collective,
which produced one of America's best techno records ever with 1995's
``Delusions of Grandeur,'' recently inked a deal with Columbia
Records: It will see the release of several albums from this
three-member deejay crew under such monikers as Hawke and God Within.
<p>
   The origins of the West Coast sound are mysterious, though there
are definite shades of hip hop's early-80s break-dancing genre, known
as ``electro'' (as in Afrika Bambaataa's ``Planet Rock''). While
electro was influencing a group of Detroit musicians who would later
coin the term ``techno,'' electro's funky rhythms and synthesized
melodies also took hold in California with rap acts such as the L.A.
Dream Team (home of a young Dr. Dre), Egyptian Lover and DJ Unknown.
<p>
   Where the West Coast had Egyptian Lover, the Southeast also had 2
Live Crew, a Florida group with ties to California that produced a
similarly electrified, break beat-driven sound known as ``Miami
bass.'' Today, the West Coast sound still has a Florida counterpart
under the skillful hands of such Orlando rave acts as DJ Icey and
Rabbit in the Moon.
<p>
   Until recently, the domestic break beat sound was the domain of
underground deejays such as Icey, Los Angeles's Eric Davenport and San
Francisco's DJ Dan.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   The Crystal Method have no qualms about taking the sound to an
international level. They've prepared nearly eight years to become
stage stars and they don't plan on looking back.
<p>
   ``We make a conscious effort not to deejay because we want people
to take us seriously as a band,'' Jordan says. But he stops himself
and adds: ``But we'll deejay for beer money.''
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; TRAVEL DEALS: More Bang for the Buck &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The U.S. dollar has been on a better roll this summer than anytime
in the past six years against European currencies, including the
franc, mark and peseta.
<p>
   This means that Americans in Europe have been enjoying  &MD;  in the
words of the U.S. chairman of the 27-nation European Travel Commission
 &MD;  ``more champagne, sauerbraten and tapas for the buck.''
<p>
   The U.S. chairman, Jeff Hamblin  &MD;  who's also the British Tourist
Authority's executive vice president for the Americas  &MD;  continued that
``there is full expectation that the dollar will remain quite robust
for the remainder of the year.'' The dollar has even managed to avoid
losing significant ground vs. the British pound, Europe's steadiest
currency.
<p>
   With this incentive, the commission expects that 1997 American
departures to Europe  &MD;  an anticipated 9.5 million  &MD;  will set a record
for the fifth year in a row.
<p>
   If the dollar holds tough, transatlantic travelers the rest of this
year can expect greater than the usual off-season value. ``In addition
to the dollar's added buying power, fall and winter visitors will
benefit from considerably lower off-peak airfares and other seasonal
discounts,'' Hamblin said.
<p>
   Among new values are discount passes good for museums in London
($32 for a three-day individual card to $105 for a seven-day family
card), Paris ($20 for a one-day pass to $50 for a five-day pass),
Amsterdam ($25), Switzerland ($25 adult, $30 adult and children) and
the French Riviera ($15 for three days, $30 for seven days) available
from a company called Tickets To ..., which is a division of New
York-based Marketing Challenges International. The company also sells
Paris dining passes, ranging in price from $129 for three meals at
different restaurants to $299 for six meals; single restaurant deals
range from $36 to $65 for one meal. Discount transport passes include
one for Vienna at $19.50. Call (212) 529-9069.
<p>
   Rail Europe has reduced the price for travelers with Eurailpasses
and Europasses on the year-old Artesia high-speed train between France
and Italy. The Paris-Lyon-Torino-Milan route is now $11 one way for
any segment or the whole trip, whether traveled in first or second
class. For information, call (800) 4-EURAIL.
<p>
   Travelers who book fall and winter travel by various deadlines
should also find some extra deals. Norvista has taken $100 off
air-inclusive packages to Eastern Europe, Scandinavia and Russia if
booked by Sept. 30. The discounted rates start at $499 for five nights
in Prague with breakfast daily, airport transfers and city tour; with
first-class hotels, the discounted rate starts at $699. A ``Two City
Special''  &MD;  a night each in Helsinki and Stockholm and a night on a
luxury Silja line ship between the two  &MD;  starts at $599 with the
discount. Departures are Oct. 1 through March. Call (800) 526-4927.
<p>
   British Airways has a Sept. 15 deadline for three-night, shoestring
getaways to London ($399 to $509), Paris ($429 to $529) or Rome ($559
to $719). All include round-trip airfare from New York and continental
breakfast daily. Prices are per person, based on double occupancy, and
vary depending on date of travel. They're available Nov. 1 through
Dec. 12. Call (800) 359-8722.

   Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; TRAVEL NOTES: Take Bug Precautions If Going to Disney World &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Travelers packing for an imminent trip to Disney World should throw
in long-sleeve shirts, long pants and plenty of bug spray.
<p>
   The Orlando resort  &MD;  and reportedly others in central Florida  &MD;  has
also curtailed some evening activities in view of a recent alert
issued by the public health unit of Orange County, Fla., concerning a
mosquito-borne virus called St. Louis encephalitis.
<p>
   ``We're stressing it's not a reason to panic, it's just a reason to
take precautions,'' said Disney World spokeswoman Diane Ledder. She
said no human cases of the disease have been reported this year in the
county.
<p>
   Mosquitos are particularly active from one hour before sundown
until an hour after sunrise, so until further notice, Disney's three
water parks and the pools at its 15 resorts are closing at 7 p.m. The
water parks usually close at 8; some hotel pools might normally remain
open as late as 11, Ledder said. Campfire programs and night hayrides
have been canceled at Disney World's heavily wooded Fort Wilderness
campground.
<p>
   But it's business as usual, Ledder said, elsewhere on the
30,000-acre Disney property  &MD;  which is treated on a nightly basis,
according to a letter being distributed to guests.
<p>
   The letter also passes along the advice from Orange County health
officials to use insect repellent containing DEET, with about 30
percent active ingredient; it continues that because in rare instances
DEET has been reported to have been linked to seizures in children, it
is prudent to use lower concentrations of 10 percent or less on them
and wash treated skin when mosquito exposure has ended.
<p>
   At week's end, Disney World didn't know how long its evening
curtailments would continue. The county ``usually requires several
weeks of clear data before lifting an alert,'' Ledder said.
<p>
   For more information, travelers can call Disney World at (407)
W-DISNEY or the Orange County Health Department at (407) 836-2664.
<p>
   -0-
<p>
   FLEXIBLE SKIES: United Airlines will soon offer members of its
frequent-flier program spur-of-the-moment getaways requiring less than
the usual minimum of stockpiled miles.
<p>
   Starting Oct. 1, a member of United's Mileage Plus program and a
guest will be able to travel to destinations within 750 miles and back
for 15,000 miles per person (instead of the usual 25,000). Trips are
subject to a Saturday-night stayover, blackout dates and other
restrictions but won't have to be booked in advance. The offer will be
available through March. Call United at (800) 241-6522.
<p>
   -0-
<p>
   ZOO NEWS: The latest attraction at the 2,200-acre San Diego Wild
Animal Park in Escondido, Calif., is a 30-acre Heart of Africa
walking-safari area that immerses visitors in varied natural habitats
where a variety of birds and animals roam free.
<p>
   As you make your way along a three-quarter-mile trail, bridges,
hidden moats, natural barriers and disguised fences keep the animals
beyond arm's length  &MD;  but not much farther. In other areas of the
wildlife park, where animals roam free within vast enclosures,
visitors tour via a five-mile monorail. The park is operated by the
non-profit Zoological Society of San Diego, which also runs the
acclaimed San Diego Zoo (where animals also roam free but species are
separated from one another).
<p>
   -0-

by credit card, including postage) from de Cordova  &MD;  who runs a

http://www.hostels.com/guides/jbb/.

<SLUG> bc-travel-westernfront </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0858 09-02 1444 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-travel-westernfront - a0858
 &UR; (ATTN: Travel editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Dave Drury </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Hartford Courant
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Two photos (TPN11,12) &QL; 
 &UR; Visitors to WWI Sites on the Rise &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   It is quiet along much of the former Western Front.
<p>
   From the lowlands of Flanders to the Swiss border in the
mountainous Vosges, Belgium and northern France offer panoramas of
tranquil, postcard-come-to-life scenes.
<p>
   Robust cattle graze in rich, green pastures in the Pays du Nord.
<p>
   Vineyards fill the Champagne region.
<p>
   Picturesque wooded hills and sparkling streams enrich the
Meuse-Argonne area.
<p>
   Indeed, it's hard to imagine this part of Europe gripped by the
first world war, as massed armies reduced the countryside to mud and
desolation and shellfire blasted historic towns to rubble.
<p>
   Hard to imagine, that is, unless the visitor takes a closer look.
<p>
   Along the battle lines of 1914-1918, in the many memorials and
burying grounds large and small, the Great War remains very much part
of the landscape.
<p>
   Consider the Somme field of Beaumont-Hamel, the high ridge known as
Vimy near Arras or the wooded ridges north of Verdun.
<p>
   The ground in those places, and others like them, tells a story. It
is haunting, still dangerous ground, scarred by craters, crumbling
concrete emplacements and trenches. Rusted barbed-wire posts still
protrude from the earth.
<p>
   Popular interest in those places, and the conflict that largely
shaped this century, appears to be on the rise. Superintendents of
World War I American military cemeteries report an increasing number
of visitors, including Americans.
<p>
   ``There is ... a new, renewed interest in World War I now by a lot
of people. They are coming because grandparents or great grandparents
are dying, and (survivors) are finding the old papers in the attic,''
said Phillip De La Mater, superintendent of the U.S. Military
Aisne-Marne Cemetery at the base of Belleau Wood.
<p>
   An acclaimed public television series, ``The Great War and the
Shaping of the 20th Century,'' was broadcast last November. Its Web
site is http://www.pbs.org/greatwar.
<p>
   Ypres, Belgium, just across the French border and some 20 miles
from the English Channel, is a good place to start a Western Front
tour.
<p>
   This charming town of 35,000, with its historic cathedral and famed
Cloth Hall, was devastated by major battles in 1914, 1915 and 1917.
<p>
   Nearly every building was demolished.
<p>
   The town, once a thriving medieval marketplace, was at the point of
a salient  &MD;  or bulge  &MD;  in the British lines, and British and German
forces took turns hurling masses of infantry at one another's
entrenched positions. Some 55,000 British Commonwealth troops died
defending the city, and their names are inscribed on the memorial
Menin Gate, a must-see stop for tourists, as is the rebuilt cathedral.
<p>
   An excellent museum, in the rebuilt Market Square, is devoted to
the fighting in and around the town. Photographs taken before and
after the battles provide a startling contrast to the life in the
square today.
<p>
   A few kilometers outside of Ypres is the rebuilt village of
Passchendaele, ground zero during the third and most tragic of the
Ypres battles.
<p>
   The 1917 battle  &MD;  which left a half-million British and German dead
 &MD;  became known in Britain as the ``Battle of the Mud'' because British
Cmdr. Gen. Douglas Haig ordered repeated attacks, though the mud made
it difficult to move.
<p>
   Passchendaele is the site of Tyne Cot Cemetery, the British
Commonwealth's largest military cemetery, with 12,000 graves and a
memorial containing the names of another 35,000 soldiers whose remains
were never recovered.
<p>
   Like all British cemeteries  &MD;  there are more than 2,000 British
World War I cemeteries in France alone  &MD;  it is well maintained by the
Commonwealth War Graves Commission. Individual grave sites have live
plantings, and the purple irises blooming beside stark white crosses
are memorable.
<p>
   Traveling south, crossing the French border, near the town of
Arras, is Vimy Ridge.
<p>
   Atop the ridge, dominating the countryside, towers the Canadian
National Vimy Memorial. The massive limestone monument, with its twin
white pylons and eloquent sculpted figures, commemorates the site of a
costly Canadian victory in April 1917.
<p>
   Canadian students are guides at the memorial, which includes a park
with restored trenches. Much of the area has been reforested to
prevent further erosion of the massive shell holes and craters, but
hiking in much of the park is discouraged. Bright red signs warn of
still-unexploded ordnance beneath the surface.
<p>
   The signs are no joke. It's not by chance that much of the terrain
of the former Western Front remains pastureland. Cultivation can still
be deadly and souvenir-hunting hazardous.
<p>
   South of the Arras, some 70 miles or so northeast of Paris, lies
the valley of the Somme, scene of the epic British offensive in 1916.
<p>
   It was here that British idealism died, as newly conscripted
regiments of youths were chewed up by German machine guns and
shellfire.
<p>
   On the first day of the battle, 20,000 British soldiers died,
making July 1, 1916 the costliest single day in the history of the
British Army. The battle continued for months.
<p>
   Visitors should stop by the Thiepval memorial, where British
schoolchildren today make day trips via the English Channel Tunnel to
decorate gravesites of school alumni and read the names of the dead.
<p>
   At Beaumont Hamel, a majestic caribou memorial is dedicated to a
Newfoundland regiment whose green troops charged from their trenches
that first day. More than 90 percent died.
<p>
   The trench lines here still run deep, and the grazing cattle appear
oblivious to the rusted barbed-wire posts that poke through the green
grass. And, as always, there is a cemetery.
<p>
   The Somme River marked the end of the British sector of the Western
Front. Below the Somme, running south to Compiegne and then eastward,
the trenches were manned by the French, later joined by the Americans.
<p>
   It is here, in the Marne Valley, that the German offensive failed
to win the war in August 1914. It is also the site of the decisive
campaigns of 1918.
<p>
   At Chateau-Thierry, near the highwater mark of the German 1918
summer offensive, American troops were thrown into action in a
desperate bid to stem the tide.
<p>
   The Aisne-Marne Cemetery, one of six World War I American
cemeteries in France, lies at the base of the Belleau Wood, where U.S.
Marines fought valiantly that summer. Nearly 2,300 Americans are
buried here.
<p>
   A beautiful memorial chapel contains the names of another 1,060
Americans listed as missing in action.
<p>
   Visitors are encouraged to hike through the still rugged woods to
view scenes from the conflict.
<p>
   Another point of interest is the double colonnaded Chateau-Thierry
Monument, some 4.5 miles southeast of the cemetery overlooking the
village. The vantage point provides a spectacular view of the Marne
Valley. A map on the east facade of the memorial provides
easy-to-follow orientation to World War I U.S. military operations in
the region.
<p>
   But the valley has more than cemeteries and memorials. The Marne
countryside provides mile after mile of visual delights.
<p>
   This is France's famed Champagne region, and grapes for the world's
most famous bubbly are abundant. The industry is headquartered in the
town of Epernay, where visitors are invited to sip champagne and tour
the Moet &AMP; Chandon cellars, where bottles of Dom Perignon and less
costly wines are produced in the miles of moist, cool darkness.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Other points of interest are the Verdun Memorial Museum, Fort Vaux,
the chapel at Fleury  &MD;  erected on the spot of one of nine villages
destroyed in the fighting  &MD;  and the peaceful, wooded Bois de Caurus,
site of the initial German onslaught.
<p>
   Less than 30 miles northwest of Verdun is the largest U.S. military
cemetery in Europe, where 14,246 Americans  &MD;  including 376 Connecticut
veterans  &MD;  are buried. The Meuse-Argonne Cemetery is the last resting
place of many Americans who died in the September 1918 offensive that
finally cracked the German defenses on the Western Front.
<p>
   It is a beautiful place. A recently remodeled visitors' center
overlooks an oval-shaped pool. Beyond the pool, long rows of crosses
rise toward a memorial chapel. Four Medal of Honor recipients are
buried here, including famed air ace Frank Luke Jr. and U.S. Army Cpl.
Freddie Stowers (honored by President Bush), who became the only
African American in the war so decorated.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Check the 'Net for Tips on Historic Tours &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Tours of World War I sites are not the type of trip you'll find in
glossy brochures at most travel agencies.
<p>
   But groups that specialize in historic tours (particularly to
places associated with the two world wars) are out there, and the
World Wide Web is a good place to look. Most offer package deals, with
ground transportation, accommodations, dining and admissions included
in the base price.
<p>
    &MD; Galaxy Tours, based in Wayne, Pa., has been organizing battlefield
tours for 36 years. The business began as a way to return World War II
veterans to places such as Normandy and the Ardennes.
<p>
   In the past few years, however, combination tours of World War I
and World War II sites are becoming more and more popular, said Galaxy
Tours general manager Sue Scott.
<p>
   ``Typically, most of our tours are custom-designed for specific
groups, whether units or veterans or students. We also do a standard
tour anyone can buy into,'' she explained.
<p>
   Groups range in size from 25 to 200, with land transportation
provided in Europe by motorcoach. Galaxy is accepting reservations now
for a European World War I tour planned for April 1998. Call (800)
523-7287 or e-mail ww1 at galaxytours.com
<p>
    &MD; Holts' Tours in England, one of the premier military and historic
tour agencies in the world, offers a varied listing of tours of Great
War locales, including three- and four-day tours each month to the
British battlefields on the Western Front.
<p>
   ``That's our most popular tour,'' explained Sue Butler, Holts'
operation manager. ``We take ages 9 to 90, male or female, from all
walks of life.''
<p>
   Each Holts' tour is led by a team of guides, often a husband and
wife, who have expertise in the particular subject area. A maximum
group size is about 45.
<p>
   A four-day tour of the U.S. Great War battlefields, titled
``Doughboys in France,'' is being planned for September 1998. Holts'
can be contacted by telephone in England at 011-44-1304-612248 or
e-mail at info at holts.co.uk.
<p>
    &MD; Sal Compagno's Bell Travel in Berkeley, Calif., offered a two-week
Western Front tour in May and will have a group touring the First
World War's Italian Front sites next month.
<p>
   Compagno, who is vice president of the Great War Society, said
interest among students of history and relatives and descendants of
World War I veterans is definitely on the rise, ``largely because
World War I defined this century, and in many ways it is still doing
so.'' Compagno added that the Balkans, which featured so prominently
in the outbreak of the Great War, are again in turmoil as the century
draws to a close.
<p>
   Another Western Front tour is being planned for the spring, and a
tour of the war's Eastern Front is also under consideration.
<p>
   Bell Travel can be reached at (800) 966-1216 or e-mail at sal at
ccnet.com.
<p>

</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Tips on How to Enhance Your Wales Experience &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Getting there: American, Delta, Virgin Atlantic, British Airways or
United offer nonstop flights from Los Angeles International Airport to
London (advance purchase, round-trip fares currently start at about
$860), from where travelers can get to Wales by rental car, train or
bus. We used BritRail's first-class Flexipasses, good for eight days'
travel in a one-month period (cost: $439 per person, $375 for those 60
and over; for second class, $305 per adult, $245 for those 25 and
under; telephone Rail Pass Express, (800) 722-7151). There is also
regular National Express bus service from London; tel. (540) 298-1395
for information. Where to stay: Swansea Marriott, Maritime Quarter,
Swansea, West Glamorgan, South Wales SA1 355; in August high season
last year, we paid $114 for our double room with king bed and view of
the marina; tel. (800) 228-9290, 011-44-1792-642020, fax
011-44-1792-642020.
<p>
   Where to eat: La Braseria (28 Wind St.; local tel. 469683)
specializes in meat and fish grilled to order; dinner for two about
$40 with wine; closed Sundays. Eleo's Brasserie (32/33 Kingsway, tel.
648609) is a bodega much like La Braseria, but less crowded and open
Sundays; dinner for two, $50 with wine. Rendezvous (Princess Way near
St. David's Shopping Centre; tel. 467113) offers French and Chinese
cuisine in a beautiful room, with a piano player; dinner for two, $50
with wine.
<p>
   For more information: British Tourist Authority, 551 Fifth Ave.,
New York, NY 10176; tel. (800) 462-2748 or (212) 986-2200, fax (212)
986-1188.
