<SLUG> bc-monument </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0908 09-02 0786 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-monument - a0908
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Eric Lekus </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Scaffolding in Capital Skyline to Mark Monument Overhaul (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  It is the most recognizable structure in Washington, a
555-foot high obelisk almost devoid of decoration.
<p>
   The Washington Monument is about to become even more easily
distinguished: Beginning sometime this winter, it will become a
555-foot high obelisk surrounded by scaffolding.
<p>
   Winter is when the National Park Service is scheduled to begin a
three-year, $5 million repair effort that will be the most
comprehensive overhaul of the monument since it opened to the public
in 1888.
<p>
   And think of it not as the Washington Monument, but the Washington
Monument-sponsored-by-Target, the department store chain. Target is
contributing $1 million, and it has helped raise the other $4 million.
This makes Target the ``corporate partner'' of the National Park
Service and the National Park Foundation  &MD;  which are determined to
avoid the term ``sponsorship.'' Target raised the money so quickly
that the company was ready to announce the project before the Park
Service was ready to award contracts for the work.
<p>
   It's an intimidating task, the repairing of a monument, an object
meant to symbolize memory and endurance. It seems reasonable that the
pyramids of Egypt might need maintenance, or the Pantheon in Rome. But
Americans are always being told that their country, and thus its
monuments, are young.
<p>
   For the first three to four months of renovation, the obelisk will
be closed to visitors, to allow workers to replace the elevator that
travels through the shaft.
<p>
   But the largest problems are posed by aesthetics. Since the
monument dominates Washington's skyline, the skyline will be
significantly changed by the scaffolding.
<p>
   ``It is a central focus for everyone in the region. If you go
downtown in any direction, you see it first,'' says Jane Freundel
Levey, editor of the Magazine of the Historical Society of Washington.
When the scaffolding goes up, ``It is going to be a bit of a shock.''
<p>
   To minimize the visual impact, the Park Service and Target have
hired architect Michael Graves, the designer of Cincinnati's Riverbend
Music Center and Denver's new Central Library and the winner of many
awards. He has the task of making the scaffolding somehow interesting.
And of making sure that people can still glimpse the monument through
the construction maze.
<p>
   ``Most scaffolding we've done in the past has been strictly
functional,'' says Stephen Lorenzetti, the Park Service's regional
resources manager. ``The thinking at Target has been that since the
monument was going to be covered up, they wanted it to be a little
interesting, a little more than a cultural icon hidden from view.''
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Americans began debating whether to build a monument to honor
George Washington  &MD;  and what form it should take  &MD;  even before
Washington's death. Congress considered the issue for 40 years without
reaching a decision. In 1833, prominent citizens of the capital
finally took charge by organizing the ``Washington National Monument
Society'' and soliciting money.
<p>
   Fifteen years later, the society had raised $87,000. President Polk
attended the ceremony for the laying of the cornerstone on July 4,
1848, and construction continued until the money ran out, in 1853.
<p>
   And there it stood for nearly 25 years  &MD;  an unfinished shaft, the
marble slowly weathering, the site perhaps reverting back to wild
grasses. In 1876, President Grant ended the embarrassment, agreeing to
have the government to take over the work. The Army Corps of Engineers
completed construction at the end of 1884.
<p>
   Total cost? $1.2 million.
<p>
   Repairs began even before the obelisk was finished. Fearful that
strong winds or an earthquake might topple the structure, engineers
decided to reinforce the base. Workers installed bars over the window
and the elevator shaft after a series of suicides in the early 1900s,
repointed the mortar in the 1930s and cleaned and polished the stone
in the 1960s.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   The stone work will begin after work on the elevator and the air
conditioning system is done. Park Service officials hope to complete
the project by 2000.
<p>
   ``These rehabilitations are larger than any one repair to go in and
fix a short circuit,'' says Earle Kittleman, a Park Service spokesman.
``It is a recognition that structures age and deteriorate with time,
and we at the Park Service are the stewards of these shrines of
American democracy.''
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-donate-gore </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0909 09-02 1481 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-donate-gore - a0909
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Ronald Brownstein and Alan C. Miller </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Campaign Finance Panel Sets Its Sights on Gore (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  It is a political confrontation of a unique and
unusually intimate sort.
<p>
   This week, hearings on campaign fund raising, chaired by Sen. Fred
Thompson, R-Tenn., are scheduled to turn the spotlight on the man
whose former Senate seat Thompson now holds: Vice President Al Gore.
<p>
   When the Senate Governmental Affairs Committee reconvenes, it will
focus on a fund-raiser Gore attended last April at a Buddhist temple
outside Los Angeles  &MD;  a site Democrats later acknowledged was
painfully inappropriate. It is unlikely Gore will be called to testify
himself, but photographs of the vice president at the temple and
questions about his role will be prominent.
<p>
   With Gore considered the front-runner for the Democratic
presidential nomination three years from now, and Thompson a potential
contender for the GOP nod, the week's proceedings may someday be seen
as a crucial early skirmish in the 2000 race for the White House. They
also mark the first collision of two ambitious and skilled Tennessee
politicians who have managed to stay almost entirely out of each
other's way throughout their careers.
<p>
   ``They never were adversaries,'' said one Gore intimate, ``because
they never had to be.''
<p>
   That all will change this week, as Thompson summons a series of
witnesses from the Hsi Lai Temple for what could prove the most
colorful, and widely televised, testimony of the hearings so far. And
though the temple affair is likely the most dramatic, it is only one
of the committee's lines of inquiry that could lead to Gore. Others
range from questions about the vice president's role in the overall
Democratic campaign fund-raising efforts, particularly solicitation
calls he made from his office, and the role of his former Senate chief
of staff in lobbying for major Democratic donors who did business with
the administration.
<p>
   ``There is substantial exposure left'' for the vice president in
the hearings, said Sen. Sam Brownback, R-Kan., a member of the
investigating committee. Gore's role in the controversy, he added,
will face ``substantial questioning.''
<p>
   With Republicans pointed down that track, the investigation poses a
direct political risk for Gore. But the proceedings are proving to
contain a surprising degree of political risk as well for Thompson,
who appeared with other potential GOP presidential aspirants last
month at a party gathering in Indianapolis.
<p>
   Widely heralded as a rising GOP star after his election in 1994,
Thompson has faced second-guessing from Republicans unhappy with his
willingness to look into alleged GOP fund-raising abuses. Democrats
question his allegations that there is an ongoing Chinese plot to
``subvert'' American elections. While the hearings in July advanced
the story of the 1996 fund-raising abuses on many fronts, they have
not yet produced a blockbuster revelation to rivet the public's
attention.
<p>
   ``Presumably the hearings offered Thompson an opportunity to
catapult onto the national stage,'' said Guy Molyneux, a Democratic
pollster. ``But that opportunity is slipping away from him.''
<p>
   Thompson has done his best to discourage such analysis. On NBC-TV's
``Meet the Press'' earlier this summer, he forcefully pointed out that
he has not ``actively considered or ... done anything'' to prepare for
a possible presidential bid. And he disputes the notion that the
inquiry has taken particular aim at Gore, as many Democrats allege.
<p>
   ``I've not talked about any individual, whether it be the president
or the vice president or anybody else in terms of where they stand in
the order of things,'' Thompson said. ``I'm not going to do that, and
I don't think it would be fair.''
<p>
   Yet Thompson appears keenly aware of the drama inherent in the
implicit showdown between Tennessee's two best-known politicians:
``Never occurred to me ...,'' Thompson said  &MD;  before breaking into a
broad grin.
<p>
   Administration officials maintain that, in fact, Thompson is
gunning for Gore.
<p>
   ``The Republicans are focusing their attacks on the vice president
every chance they get, and I think it has everything to do with 2000
politics,'' said a White House official.
<p>
   While the Senate inquiry marks the first direct clash of interests
between the two men, they long have worked at cross purposes. In 1970,
for example, Thompson  &MD;  then a young attorney  &MD;  worked in the Senate
campaign of Republican Bill Brock, who unseated Gore's father, Al Gore
Sr. And in 1994, Gore campaigned for Democrat Jim Cooper, who Thompson
swept past to capture the seat Gore vacated when he was elected vice
president.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   In personal style, the two men offer a study in contrasts. Gore,
50, has always been seen as a straight arrow  &MD;  a buttoned-down
senator's son so methodical and cautious that he has jokingly compared
himself to a mechanical robot. Thompson, 54, has a shambling, even
roguish, charm that hints of a man whose idea of long-term planning is
to buy his wine by the case instead of the bottle.
<p>
   In each instance, the picture can be overly simplified: Off camera,
Gore can be bitingly funny, and beneath his easygoing manner, Thompson
has shown an eye for the brass ring in his steady rise from the tiny
Tennessee town of Lawrenceburg.
<p>
   Yet the basic portrait remains accurate: Many who know him say
Thompson isn't as single-mindedly focused on winning the presidency as
Gore  &MD;  or for that matter, the other nationally prominent politician
in Tennessee, former Republican governor and 1996 presidential
candidate Lamar Alexander. Characteristically, Alexander is already
assembling support for a second bid in 2000, which could prove a major
hurdle for Thompson should he decide to run because the two men's
bases of financial support largely overlap.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Given their personalities, it's almost ironic that it is the
circumspect vice president who finds himself accused of cutting
corners in the search for campaign cash. The hearings already have
produced one new revelation about Gore: He attended a private
Democratic National Committee dinner in 1993 whose other participants
included John Huang, who was then still at the Indonesia-based Lippo
Group, and the head of a Chinese government-owned company that is a
Lippo business partner.
<p>
   Gore finds himself in the committee's sights initially over his
appearance at the April 29, 1996, lunch at the Hsi Lai Temple in
Hacienda Heights, Calif., which raised $140,000. Democrats later
acknowledged that it was inappropriate to hold the event  &MD;  organized
by Huang and Los Angeles immigration counselor Maria L. Hsia  &MD;  in a
Buddhist temple. Eventually the party was forced to return nearly half
the money after it could not verify that the donors were legal
residents or that the individuals who made the donations were the
actual source of the funds.
<p>
   Temple officials have told reporters and investigators that the
temple reimbursed about a dozen nuns and temple devotees for their
contributions. Federal law prohibits such a reimbursement and requires
the disclosure of the true source of funds.
<p>
   When the temple fund-raiser was first disclosed, Gore said he
believed it was only a ``community event.'' Later, his staff
acknowledged that he knew the session was ``finance-related.'' But he
has continued to insist that he did not believe the event was a
fund-raiser.
<p>
   Committee Republicans have released some documents attempting to
challenge that portrayal. But in the hearings' first week, former
Democratic National Committee finance director Richard Sullivan
supported Gore's account, saying Huang had assured both he and the
vice president's staff that no one was required to make a contribution
to attend the lunch. Sullivan also testified that Gore had been
unaware money was collected at the lunch. Those attending included
people who had already donated to the DNC, potential future donors and
those who brought checks to the event itself.
<p>
   The temple event had sparked some of the most intense backstage
squabbling of the Senate investigation. Committee Democrats initially
resisted the GOP's efforts to provide immunity for several key
witnesses involved in the incident before joining the majority in
voting to do so. Republican staffers had maintained that the Democrats
were attempting to protect Gore.
<p>
   Still unresolved is whether the committee will seek testimony from
Gore  &MD;  on either the temple event or his broader role in the Clinton
financial effort, particularly the more than 75 telephone calls Gore
made from the White House to solicit campaign funds. Committee
investigators say Thompson would not take such a step lightly.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-immig-sweep </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0910 09-02 0850 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-immig-sweep - a0910
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Louis Sahagun </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Immigration Sweep Stirs Cloud of Controversy (Chandler, Ariz.) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   CHANDLER, Ariz.  &MD;  On a recent morning, while driving home from the
store, Catalina Veloz was pulled over by a Chandler police officer who
had a question. In Spanish, he asked, ``Got your papers?''
<p>
   The query puzzled the 35-year-old security guard and lifelong
Arizona resident.
<p>
   ``I said, `I've got toilet paper, writing paper and newspaper  &MD; 
what kind do you want?' '' she recalled. ``He said, `No, your
immigration papers.' ''
<p>
   The officer and his companion, a Border Patrol agent, threatened to
deport Veloz until she convinced them, in forceful English, that ``I
was born here!''
<p>
   Later the same day, a Border Patrol agent on a bicycle followed her
into a parking lot near her home and asked, ``Where's your papers?''
<p>
   ``What the hell did you ask me?'' she responded indignantly. ``I
was born here! I went to school here! I raised four kids here! Do I
have to tattoo the words `U.S. Citizen' on my forehead?''
<p>
   Veloz is among 16 Chandler residents who filed a $35 million
class-action lawsuit accusing the city of civil rights violations
during a five-day roundup of 432 undocumented workers in late July.
Adding to the outrage, local authorities conceded that the crackdown
targeted a downtown redevelopment area anchored by small businesses
catering to Latinos.
<p>
   Arizona Attorney General Grant Woods has launched an investigation
of the sweep, the largest ever in this boomtown of 165,000 people  &MD;  19
percent of whom are Latino  &MD;  just south of Phoenix. The probe aims to
determine whether some residents were harassed or treated unfairly on
the basis of their national origin, ethnicity or skin color.
<p>
   ``We have to get to the bottom of what went on there and make sure
that if people were stopped because of the color of their skin, it
never happens again in Arizona or the United States,'' Woods said.
``In my state ... to be Hispanic is to be part of our state's
heritage.''
<p>
   Still, complaints are increasing now that federal authorities are
teaming up with local police and sheriff's deputies in immigration
sweeps. Recent operations have been conducted in Denver; Tucson,
Ariz.; Flagstaff, Ariz., and Little Rock, Ark.
<p>
   Last September, police officers and federal agents in Jackson Hole,
Wyo., rounded up 150 legal and illegal Latino workers, inked numbers
on their arms and hauled them off to jail in patrol cars and a horse
trailer soiled with manure. In January, a similar sweep in Crescent
City, Fla., spawned a lawsuit alleging violations of
search-and-seizure laws.
<p>
   In most cases, these sweeps were conducted after local police had
received almost daily complaints from residents who accused
undocumented workers of public drunkenness, loitering and burglary.
<p>
   The Chandler operation was different, critics say. They contend
that the city wants to drive Latinos, whether they are legal or
illegal residents, out of the downtown redevelopment area. Officials
hope the area will one day compete with upscale civic centers in Mesa,
Tempe and Scottsdale.
<p>
   Chandler Assistant City Manager Patrick McDermott said ``the
objective of the operation between the Chandler Police Department and
the Border Patrol was to address the problems related to illegal
immigrants in the redevelopment area.''
<p>
   But Stephen Montoya, the Phoenix civil rights lawyer who filed the
lawsuit, said, ``We're suing them for racism because their primary
criterion for stopping people was skin color, a practice that is
against the law.''
<p>
   Jose Garcia, a Vietnam combat veteran and retired Mesa Police
Department lieutenant, agreed.
<p>
   Standing in front of his small video rental business in the shadow
of Chandler City Hall, Garcia said: ``The police were way out of
bounds. One of our customers was stopped four times within a single
block while on her way home on foot.
<p>
   ``They were looking for dark-skinned workers speaking Spanish,'' he
said, shaking his head. ``And they thought we'd just take it. They
were very wrong.''
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Given that the city is facing a lawsuit, officials are reluctant to
discuss the sweep, let alone charges of civil rights violations.
However, Chandler Mayor Jay Tibshraeny recently told a gathering of
200 Latinos that the city will never again engage in such an
operation.
<p>
   Chandler Police Department spokesman Sgt. Ken Phillips would not go
that far. But Phillips, who denied that anyone was stopped without
probable cause, acknowledged that the operation was flawed.
<p>
   ``We concede that we could have done things to soften the
impression  &MD;  so we've learned too,'' he said. ``We are now considering
suggestions, including that we notify the Mexican consulate and
community leaders before we implement another operation.''
<p>
   Those concessions are not sufficient for Veloz.
<p>
   ``I feel degraded, like people are looking at me. I feel like the
stuff you scrape off the bottom of your shoes,'' she said.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-helpwanted </SLUG>

<PREAMBLE>
bc-helpwanted - a0910
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Financial editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Carrie Mason-Draffen </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; HELP WANTED: No Overtime for a Nurse With Beeper &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Dear Help Wanted: I am a registered nurse at a hospital and am paid
an hourly wage. I am also required to carry a beeper and be on call to
handle emergencies 24 hours days for one- to two-week periods. I don't
usually have to go to the hospital. But I often answer time-consuming
phone calls on managerial matters such as how to rearrange schedules
or find someone to assist in surgery. Yet I am not paid any overtime.
Is it legal to expect me to be on call without being compensated?
 &MD; Feeling Underpaid
<p>
   Dear Feeling: This sounds like split-personality management. Your
company treats you like you're on the clock when it pays you an hourly
wage, but it then treats you like a manager when it refuses to pay you
for all the time you worked. Your employer can't have it both ways. I
asked the feds and New York state to weigh in on this question.
<p>
   In general, registered nurses are exempt from overtime payments
because they are considered professionals, said Irv Miljoner, who
heads the U.S. Labor Department's Long Island, N.Y., office. State and
federal laws generally define workers in this overtime-exempt category
as those who fall in the professional, administrative, executive and
outside-sales categories.
<p>
   ``However, this exemption is contingent on their being salaried,''
Miljoner said. ``If they're being paid an hourly wage, it pretty much
defeats the exemption.''
<p>
   Since you earn an hourly wage, you should be paid for the time you
work, Miljoner said. And you must be paid overtime  &MD;  1½ times your
regular hourly rate  &MD;  for every hour over 40 you work in a week.
<p>
   When a beeper is involved, it's not always clear whether a worker
is entitled to overtime pay. I would check on your status. The
exempt/nonexempt categories aren't arbitrary and they have important
implications, such as overtime pay for those who are not exempt.
<p>
   For more information, contact the U.S. Labor Department.
<p>
   Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 1219EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-water </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0911 09-02 1378 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-water - a0911
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Financial editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Harry Berkowitz </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; As Water Sales Soar, Bottlers Try to Distinguish Their Products &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The sketch on the ocean-blue labels wrapped around bottles of
Aquafina drinking water looks just like a snowcapped mountain range.
<p>
   But the water, which is bottled by Pepsi-Cola Co. and has rocketed
up the sales charts of an industry whose overall sales are exploding,
does not come from a mountain spring at all. It's not even from a
country well or a flowing stream.
<p>
   It's filtered tap water from 11 cities across the country  &MD; 
including Houston, Wichita, Kan., and Fresno, Calif.  &MD;  whose supplies
may not spring to mind as the most pristine or refreshing.
<p>
   As the public's passion for bottled water continues to heat up, a
debate over the sources of the more than 3 million gallons sold last
year in the United States has taken center stage.
<p>
   The water merchants, ranging from Pepsi-Cola and Perrier to a
Georgia landowner who adds clove and licorice flavoring, look for ways
to make the water seem special and increase sales at the same time.
<p>
   They argue about the value and popularity of minerals, the merits
of reverse osmosis and ozonation, the advantages of having one water
source or several, and the challenges of rising production.
<p>
   Competitors complain that Pepsi-Cola is misleading consumers with
the mountain sketch on bottles of ``purified'' Aquafina. The leading
brands are also keeping a close eye on the fast growth of newcomer
Dannon, which plays up its brand name (same as the yogurt) rather than
its sources.
<p>
   Neighbors of a Great Bear spring in Pennsylvania charge that
Perrier, which owns that regional brand and several others, including
Poland Spring and Deer Park, plans to expand production so much that
their trout streams and wells could dry up.
<p>
   ``People here see this bottled water as a luxury for yuppies vs.
something they need to flush their toilets and take a shower,'' said
Jerry Centofanti, regional program manager for water supply management
at the Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Protection.
<p>
   Some bottlers say many of the ``natural spring waters'' are not all
that natural because the water is trucked around and then shot with
ozone and ultraviolet light to kill bacteria before it is bottled.
<p>
   The trick for bottlers is to sell their brands in a wide area  &MD; 
possibly the whole nation  &MD;  without drowning in high transportation
expenses that can far exceed the cost of the water itself, often as
little as a few cents per bottle.
<p>
   U.S. sales of bottled water, including water cooler jugs, shot up 8
percent last year to 3.1 million gallons, or 11.7 gallons a person,
according to the Beverage Marketing Corp. of New York. That includes a
nearly 25 percent jump for plastic bottles holding 1.5 liters and
less.
<p>
   ``It fits in with today's healthier lifestyles,'' said Gary
Hemphill, vice president of Beverage Marketing, adding that bottled
water has increasingly become an alternative not just to tap water but
also to soft drinks.
<p>
   The sales and per capita consumption levels for bottled water have
more than doubled over the past 10 years  &MD;  faster growth than any
other beverage category except iced teas and sports drinks. The
industry even managed to overcome the discovery in 1990 that traces of
cancer-causing benzene had been found in some bottles of Perrier
sparkling water. Perrier temporarily halted bottling at a plant in
France and launched a worldwide recall.
<p>
   Much of the growth in bottled-water sales over the past two decades
has been in non-sparkling, or non-carbonated, water, which represents
more than 85 percent of U.S. sales.
<p>
   In fact, Perrier, long identified with its bubbly water in green
bottles, has gobbled up regional brands of non-sparkling water across
the country in recent years, from Zephyrhills in Florida to Arrowhead
in California. Perrier Group of America, whose Paris-based parent
company is controlled by Nestle of Switzerland, now grabs more than 26
percent of U.S. bottled-water sales.
<p>
   But even Perrier is frustrated that it can't afford to distribute
its very popular and fast-growing Poland Spring brand much beyond the
Northeast. The brand, founded in 1845 in Poland, Maine, focuses its
marketing on its sole use of wells tapping into an underground
reservoir amid 400 wooded acres.
<p>
   ``I would love to be selling Poland Spring in California, but I
would have to sell it at a price that's higher than all of the other
domestic waters because I am bringing it 3,000 miles across the
country,'' said Jeff Caso, vice president of marketing at the Perrier
Group in Greenwich, Conn.
<p>
   In contrast, the Deer Park brand has spread from its original
source in Deer Park, Md., to springs in New Tripoli and Valley View,
Pa.; Freedom, N.H.; and Montverde, Fla.
<p>
   Critics say using more than one source is cheating.
<p>
   ``Water is a living thing, so if you are using more than one
source, it's not the same water each time,'' said Bill Mimnaugh, U.S.
managing director for Spa Monopole Corp. in Greenwich, which imports
bottled water from the town of Spa in Belgium. Spa has been exporting
water for more than 400 years.
<p>
   After complaints that images of glaciers and waterfalls on labels
were misleading consumers, the Food and Drug Administration tightened
labeling rules for bottled water last year and more clearly defined
each type, including artesian well, mineral, purified and spring
water. The FDA also expanded the list of contaminants that bottlers
must test for, to 83.
<p>
   Executives at bottling companies, who realize that image still
counts for a lot, are extremely sensitive when it comes to questions
about sources.
<p>
   A U.S. spokesman for Danone Group, the Paris-based company that
markets Evian, double-checked with executives before revealing that
another brand the company owns, Dannon, uses two sources: a spring in
Piedmont, Canada, and AquaPenn Springs in Graysville, Pa., which is
owned by an outside bottling company.
<p>
   Unlike its strategy with Evian, marketed as untreated, unfiltered
water from a legendary spring in France, Danone is depending on the
reputation of Dannon yogurts to market Dannon water.
<p>
   Meanwhile, Perrier is rolling out a brand called Oasis that will
use several spring sources across the country, and wants to make it a
national brand. Aquafina, by Pepsi-Cola, is also going national. And
it doesn't bother with springs at all.
<p>
   Bottlers say American tastes contrast sharply with those of
Europeans, who prefer bottled water with much higher levels of
minerals such as magnesium and calcium despite the stronger taste. In
Europe, Vittel, for instance, has 20 times the mineral content as
Poland Spring. The version of Vittel imported to the United States has
half the levels of the European one, though.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Spring water bottlers point out that their supplies usually come
from protected underground reservoirs, unlike the usually chlorinated
surface supplies of municipalities. Still, water bottlers have run
into some resistance as they expand.
<p>
   Perrier Group has met opposition to increased production in
Pennsylvania and Florida. Perrier recently decided to dig a well next
to its Great Bear springs to supply its bottling plant in Allentown,
Pa. The well would enable Perrier to pump out 95,000 gallons a day,
which neighbors fear could damage nearby Pigeon Creek during dry
spells.
<p>
   Similarly, some residents of Crystal Springs, Fla., said they are
worried about Perrier's plans to start drawing 10 times more spring
water for its Zephyrhills brand.
<p>
   ``We have more vested in keeping the resources plentiful than even
local folks do,'' Perrier's Caso said.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Bottled-water mania has taken some unusual twists.
<p>
   After Nancy Panoz bought 73 acres in Blairsville, Ga., she found
that the underground aquifer was a good source and opened a bottling
plant. Her twist: tiny additions of lemon, lime, tangerine, ginger,
clove and anisette flavors.
<p>
   ``It's just enough to leave a hint of taste in your mouth,'' she
said.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-diana-media-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0912 09-02 0682 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-media-comment - a0912
 &UR; (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By James Lull </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to the Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Democracy Needs a Free Press  &MD;  Paparazzi Included &QL; 
 &UR; Lull is a professor of communication studies at San Jose State &QL; 
University and co-editor of ``Media Scandals,'' to be published &QL; 
in October by Columbia University Press. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The death of Diana, Princess of Wales, and the other passengers was
an accident caused by bad decisions and bad driving, not by the
celebrity photographers following her and not by her fans who eagerly
consume the images they produce.
<p>
   Reports Monday that the limousine driver was under the influence of
alcohol only make it more clear that it was persons inside the car who
ultimately were responsible for what happened.
<p>
   The emotional blaming of the media not only fails to recognize the
role alcohol apparently played, but also does not account for the
class-based relationships among persons in the car, which I believe
contributed to the high-speed tragedy. Beyond this, holding the media
responsible for this highly symbolic yet isolated incident
misunderstands the absolute necessity for vigorous journalism  &MD;  even
media scandals  &MD;  in democracies such as ours.
<p>
   Imagine the pressure felt by the driver of the Mercedes as he
attempted to whisk across town ``the most recognizable person in the
world'' and  &MD;  even more important  &MD;  Dodi Fayed, whose father owns the
Ritz Hotel, employer of the man behind the wheel. Though the
photographers indeed may have been chasing more than following the
limousine, some person or persons inside the car decided to take
evasive action from the annoying entourage in the rear-view mirror.
<p>
   We may never learn what really happened. But what's clear is that
whoever made the decision to race that Mercedes into the tunnel was
not a photographer. What's more, no one has alleged that the
photographers or anyone else were pursuing the princess and her
boyfriend with anything more dangerous than a 35-millimeter camera.
<p>
   Let's hope that the hysteria about the media's role in the accident
fades in the discussions that will follow. What must never be
forgotten is the indisputable requirement of a free and vigorous press
in the Western world's delicate system of checks and balances on
social power.
<p>
   The princess of Wales detested the intrusion of the media into her
private life, but at the same time she cultivated that attention and
benefited from it in many ways. So did her various humanitarian
efforts, particularly the recent attempts to get the world to notice
the harm caused by land mines in Angola, Bosnia and Cambodia.
<p>
   Putting the unglamorous land mine issue on the world's moral agenda
could only have been accomplished by combining the appeal of a
celebrity spokesperson with the technological reach and impact of mass
media.
<p>
   The underlying factor in all of this is visibility, wanted and
unwanted. When a princess or a president wants media attention, he or
she gets it. But if we allow media celebrities  &MD;  political figures,
sports heroes, movie stars, billionaire business people, pop musicians
and yes, members of the royal family  &MD;  to limit the contexts in which
they are viewed and pondered, then we would miss out on lots of
important history. We'd have a very different view, for instance, of
the late former President Nixon, O.J. Simpson, Jim Bakker, Carlos
Salinas de Gortari, even Hillary Rodham Clinton, among many others.
<p>
   Media visibility holds accountable those who influence us by means
of their political, economic or cultural power, which itself often is
won through media exposure. The tragedy last Saturday night in Paris,
emotionally wrenching as it is, must finally be understood in a much
wider and more significant sense. While some revision of the rules
regulating invasion of celebrities' privacy may be appropriate, the
visibility and accountability that the news media guarantee  &MD; 
sometimes in quite unpleasant ways  &MD;  is fundamental to democracy and
must survive.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 1220EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<STORYID cat=k pri=r sel=tm--a> X3272 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-diana-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0913 09-02 0549 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-comment - a0913
 &UR; (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Christopher Hitchens </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to the Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Mother Teresa or Mrs. Simpson: Which Was the Real Diana? &QL; 
 &UR; Hitchens is author of ``The Monarchy: A Critique.'' He wrote about &QL; 
 &UR; Princess Diana in his column in this month's Vanity Fair. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   To say that Diana, Princess of Wales, was killed by those seeking
publicity is as absurd as it is to say that she was killed by seeking
it herself. The speed limits are the same for everybody; there are
some dangers from which a bodyguard cannot protect you; if you wish to
dine incognito, then it is probably best to avoid the Paris Ritz.
<p>
   These are the few, paltry ``lessons'' of her death. It is human and
natural to attempt to invest accidents with meaning, but the urge to
do so is not always controlled by the rational faculty. Look at the
simple members of the British public, carpeting the approach to
Buckingham Palace with flowers. After all this drama and strife, they
have not grasped the salient fact that Diana no longer was a member of
the royal family; that, indeed, that family had cast her out and now,
grotesquely, becomes the proprietor of her children.
<p>
   We ought to beware of too easy a surrender to the vicarious
identification that makes us address people whom we have never met
(``Di,'' ``O.J.,'' ``Ollie'') by their first names. This synthetic
informality may mask hysterical symptoms. With one hand, we deplore
the cult of ``celebrity'' and the celebration of the nonevent; with
the other, we forward both these phenomena by our very language. This
was not, as some oafish spokesman from Buckingham Palace apparently
told CNN, ``an accident waiting to happen.'' It was just an accident,
of the sort that happens to people whether they symbolize ``life in
the fast lane'' or otherwise.
<p>
   Princess Diana had only been free of her miserable marriage for a
year and a day when she was killed, so we will never know what kind of
adult she might have become. In one incarnation, the angel of mercy in
Angola and Bosnia, she not only looked fetching but actually had the
power to alter policies and opinions. (One of her last acts was to
break with protocol and issue a stern attack on the former British
government for its blinkered policy on land mines.) In the other
incarnation, she was drawn to jet-setting, disco-minded, super-rich
sybaritism. It is a safe bet that, if she had carried on her
relationship with Dodi Fayed, son of billionaire Mohammed Fayed, she
would have been swallowed up by the world of the brittle and the
spoiled and might even have ended up in the Paris chateau--now owned
by the uncontrollably ambitious Fayed senior--in which the Duke of
Windsor and Mrs. Simpson ended their ill-spent days. Now we will never
know which aspect of her character would have emerged as the dominant
one.
<p>
   These and other speculations are enough to dispense with the stupid
call for more laws to protect the ``privacy'' of the rich and famous.
In the greater part of her public life, Princess Diana was a voluntary
member of a controversial hereditary monarchy. In pursuit of a
personal solution to an unhappy private life, she became an assiduous
leaker to the press. The first thing deserved public scrutiny and the
second one invited it.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 1221EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>
</DOC>

<DOCID> latwp970902.0093 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=k pri=r sel=tm--a> X3273 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-diana-memory-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0913 09-02 1205 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-memory-comment - a0913
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Editorial Page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Alice Steinbach </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Diana's Death Cuts Short the Promise &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   She entered the room and brought with her a halo of light: a tall,
coltish, impossibly elegant young woman; her cheeks flushed, her skin
like English cream; a nose like that of a Greek statue; eyes the color
of sapphires and hair like a crown of shining gold.
<p>
   Dressed in a lipstick-red suit with a white shawl collar and
wearing stiletto-heeled shoes that made her long, suntanned legs  &MD;  she
wore no stockings  &MD;  seem even longer, Diana, Princess of Wales, had
the effect she always had: She stopped traffic.
<p>
   It has been a dozen years since I watched Diana, accompanied by
Prince Charles, enter the drawing room of the British Embassy
residence in Washington to have tea with a small group of selected
members of the press.
<p>
   Billed as ``an informal social occasion'' in honor of Diana's first
visit to Washington, the assembled reporters were served assorted hors
d'oeuvres on silver trays and beverages by tuxedoed embassy staffers.
<p>
   But the food and ambience were not what we'd come to see. What we
had all come to see was Diana. Reporters were just as fascinated with
her as the crowds who lined the Washington streets to cheer Diana!
Diana! Diana!
<p>
   Charming and without affectation, Diana did not disappoint as she
chatted informally about the weather  &MD;  ``Lovely!''  &MD;  and her lack of
sleep  &MD;  ``No one seems to believe that even a young person can get
very tired on such a grueling trip as this one.''
<p>
   She laughed, shyly, as she told reporters she was a fan of the
American TV show ``Dynasty'' and the cartoon strip ``Peanuts.''
Reporters laughed, too.
<p>
   But beneath the laughter, even then, there was a sadness about her.
In repose, her face often seemed shadowed with a weariness beyond her
years. And, of course, there was the way she looked at you: sideways
or with eyes downcast or half-closed.
<p>
   Perhaps such a way of seeing protected her from being overwhelmed
by the attention that followed her everywhere.
<p>
   Or perhaps it was her way of not seeing the cameras constantly
thrust into her face and the screaming headlines that stripped away
almost every vestige of a private life.
<p>
   From the first pre-marriage pictures of an unsuspecting Diana
snapped in a dress made diaphanous by sunlight to the recent tabloid
photos of her Mediterranean tryst with 41-year-old Dodi Fayed
photographers tracked her down.
<p>
   Nothing about Diana, it seems, was off-limits.
<p>
   On Saturday night, her public life ended the way it began: with
Diana running from the paparazzi.
<p>
   Now the world's most photographed woman has passed into legend. But
not before giving us a tantalizing glimpse of the woman she might have
become.
<p>
   A recent Vanity Fair cover shows a new Diana. Gone is the
bulimic-looking, overdressed and coiffed Diana. In her place we see a
healthy, toned-and-sleek Diana with minimal makeup and a wash-and-wear
hairstyle.
<p>
   Gone, too, is the downcast look. This Diana gazes openly into the
camera. She looks like a woman ready to get on with the next part of
her life.
<p>
   ``There is a kind of serenity about her,'' Gianni Versace told
Vanity Fair at the time the photograph was shot. ``It is a moment in
her life, I think, when she's found herself  &MD;  the way she wants to
live.''
<p>
   But how did Diana want to live? What could she draw on to propel
herself from the old life to a new and better one?
<p>
   After all, she had gone from a painful childhood bereft of parental
love  &MD;  her mother left the family when Diana was 6  &MD;  to a painful,
loveless marriage during which she was publicly rejected by her
husband.
<p>
   She had gone from blushing ingenue to fairy-tale princess and
mother; from a woman scorned to a single, divorced parent. Along the
way, she had starved herself and made some half-hearted suicide
attempts.
<p>
   Little help came from the Royal Family  &MD;  or ``The Firm'' as they
like to call themselves. Bred to be stiff-upper-lip and rigid in their
monarchic lifestyle, the Royals didn't seem to understand Diana.
<p>
   What were they to make of a young woman who could cry openly at the
sight of a child dying from AIDS?
<p>
   In all her life, it seems, Diana had no one to turn to.
<p>
   And yet, Diana herself was to become known and loved as a warm and
compassionate woman. Against all odds she was, at her deepest core, a
woman of strong maternal feeling.
<p>
   Battered women, AIDS patients, lepers, sick and handicapped
children, the homeless. To much of society, they were the
``untouchables.''
<p>
   Not to Diana: She was always there, touching them, stroking their
hair or holding a hand. And she used her news value to see that
photographers were there to call attention to the plight of such
people.
<p>
   In the last year of her life, things seemed to be coming together
for Diana. It appeared she had shaken off her sadness about the
breakup of her marriage and was carving out a life in which charity
projects figured prominently. In Britain she was deeply admired for
the seriousness of such efforts, particularly her recent campaign to
ban anti-personnel land mines.
<p>
   But her deepest devotion was reserved for her two young sons,
Prince William and Prince Harry. She was determined, she said, to
counteract the stiffness and rigidity of the monarchy by instilling in
her sons a sense of compassion and sympathy.
<p>
   She was determined to make them feel loved, too. She was going to
give them ``plenty of kisses and comforting, too.''
<p>
   Diana always seemed to be comforting someone. From a small girl who
lost her legs to a land mine to Elton John at the funeral of Gianni
Versace, Diana was not afraid to reach out.
<p>
   But she was still searching, it seems, for a satisfying personal
life.
<p>
   To an observer, her choice in men seemed precarious. She seemed to
be looking for a man who would offer her unconditional love and a safe
haven from an unstable world, much as Jackie Kennedy Onassis did. It
is the kind of love a child seeks from a parent; the kind of love that
if you had as a child, you don't need as an adult.
<p>
   Still, it seems clear that Diana was trying to move forward in her
life. Some say she was on the threshold of a new and better life.
<p>
   Now we will never know.
<p>
   Instead we are left to wonder about the road that Diana, Princess
of Wales, might have taken had she lived; about the influence she
might have had on Prince William, the future king of England; about
the personal happiness  &MD;  or sadness  &MD;  she might have known.
<p>
   Now that Diana is a part of history, how will we remember her?
<p>
   Some say it is her beauty and grace we will remember. Others say
her legacy lies in the compassion and love she brought to all she met.
<p>
   But perhaps we should also remember Diana, Princess of Wales, as we
remember a lost child.
<p>
   She is like the child who dies on the cusp of coming into her own;
the child whose promise is cut short, leaving us to grieve what might
have been.
<p>
   Now Diana is gone, leaving a small darkness where her light once
shone.
<p>
   How strange it feels without her.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-travel-tips </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0934 09-02 1047 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-travel-tips - a0934
 &UR; (ATTN: Travel editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Jane Engle </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997 Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; TRAVEL TIPS: Moscow to Mark 850th Birthday &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MOSCOW  &MD;  Fresh gold leaf gleams from onion domes, the
``wedding-cake'' Stalinscrapers are now yellow instead of gray, and
the notorious potholes of the main thoroughfares have disappeared
under new asphalt.
<p>
   As Moscow spruces up for the Sept. 5 to 7 celebration of its 850th
anniversary, this most sinister of European capitals has benefited
from a face-lift. But in keeping with the contrived nature of the
birthday bash, the key events and civic improvements are mostly
illusion.
<p>
   External reconstruction of Christ the Savior Cathedral will be
deemed completed on Wednesday, but the cathedral will remain
off-limits to the public until the interior is finished early next
year.
<p>
   Europe's largest shopping mall, being built at the Kremlin's
doorstep on Manezh Square, will open unfinished and largely
unoccupied, if it opens at all in time for the birthday bash. Even the
highest rollers have balked at paying more than $3,000 per square yard
a year for floor space.
<p>
   Most disappointing for those who might occasion a trip to Moscow
for the three-day festivities is that seating for most celebrations  &MD; 
Red Square shows, Kremlin concerts, a laser projection of Moscow's
visual history onto the skies  &MD;  is by invitation only.
<p>
   City fathers who have organized the party as a thinly veiled
kickoff of Moscow Mayor Yuri M. Luzhkov's undeclared quest for the
presidency in 2000 console the hoi polloi with the fact that key
events will be televised. Opera star Luciano Pavarotti heads a short
list of international luminaries.
<p>
   The 10 million residents of Moscow and tourists without invitations
will have to content themselves with the fireworks display. For more
information, contact the Moscow City Tourist Office in New Jersey;
telephone (888) 966-7269, fax (973) 884-1711.
<p>
    &MD; CAROL J. WILLIAMS
<p>
   Coming soon to a city near (some) of you: the world's tallest sand
sculpture. At least, that's what Gerry Kirk of Solana Beach, co-holder
of the current Guinness record, and other sculptors from 18 countries
hope to construct on Fiesta Island in San Diego's Mission Bay.
<p>
   The castle, begun Aug. 14 and titled ``Lost City of Atlantis,'' is
due to be finished Thursday as the centerpiece of a nearly monthlong
first annual San Diego International SandFest. There will be food
courts, crafts booths, lessons in sandcastle building and
entertainment. The site will be open daily 8 a.m. to 10 p.m. through
Sept. 30, spokeswoman Jackie Evers said.
<p>
   One-day tickets to the site will cost $8 for adults, $7 for seniors
and the military, and be free for children 6 and under. They can be
bought on site or ordered in advance through Ticketmaster (tel. (619)
220-TIXS). Information: tel. toll-free (888) 310-7263.
<p>
   Kirk and crew hope to build a castle more than 60 feet tall and 480
feet wide  &MD;  breaking the height record of 56 feet, 2 inches set in
1989 in Kaseda, Japan.
<p>
   The Civil War's bloodiest battle will be reenacted Sept. 12 to 14
in Maryland in what organizers say is one of the largest Civil War
restagings ever.
<p>
   As of last week about 9,400 people had signed up to recreate the
Battle of Antietam, which raged nearly 135 years ago on Sept. 17,
1862. The restaging will be near Hagerstown on the Artz Farm, about 10
miles north of the original site. Hagerstown-area hotels are mostly
booked for the event, organizers say, but the site can be visited as a
day trip from Baltimore or Washington, D.C. Daily tickets ($9 adults,
$6 students) can be bought on site. Call toll-free (888) 248-4597.
kern,2
<p>
   The main battle reenactments are tentatively scheduled to begin at
3 p.m. Sept. 13 and at 6 a.m. and 1:30 p.m. Sept. 14. The site will be
open 9 a.m. to 6:30 p.m. Sept. 12 and 13 and 3:30 a.m. to 6:30 p.m.
Sept. 14.
<p>
   ``New Art in China, Post-1989'' opens Saturday at the San Jose
Museum of Art and runs through Nov. 2. It is the only California venue
for the traveling exhibit of about 80 paintings, prints, sculpture and
other works that opened  &MD;  unsanctioned by China's Ministry of Culture
 &MD;  in 1995 in Vancouver, Canada.... The Automobile Club of Southern
California has been issuing a new ``Guide to ... '' series of travel
planners for 10 California regions. Instead of having to pick up a map
plus a separate guidebook, with information on lodging and points of
interest, members can get one 4-inch-by-9-inch map that contains both.
They are free to members, $3.95 for nonmembers. ... Attention,
sky-high rollers: Air France is offering ``cut-rate'' trips on the
Concorde. Passengers traveling together can buy two round-trip tickets
for $11,772 total  &MD;  a savings of more than $5,000 off the regular
price, the airline says. There are some restrictions.
<p>
   Deal of the Week: The 93-room, AAA four-diamond-rated Hotel Jerome
in Aspen, Colo., is dropping rates on some of its rooms by half or
more starting Monday through Nov. 26, during fall foliage season. The
peak of fall color in Aspen is usually the third or fourth week of
September, a spokeswoman said. Under the deal, deluxe king rooms (525
square feet) are $102.79, including taxes; grand deluxe rooms (650
square feet) are $135.25; and one-bedroom suites are $243.45. The
usual brochure rates at summer high season are $275.91, $362.47 and
$551.82, respectively. Some rooms have two double beds. The deal is
subject to availability, which can be scarce on weekends; the best
times are generally after Oct. 1. Request the off-season rate.
Reservations: (800) 331-7213.
<p>
   Free for the Asking: What's in Louisiana for children to do? ``A
Kid's Guide to Louisiana'' lists everything from A (for amusement
parks) to Z (for zoos). In between there are fairs, festivals,
museums, outdoor adventures, sports events and tours, all organized by
region and city. Among the more unusual offerings: a tour of an
alligator and turtle farm in Hammond and the Krewe of Kids Parade in
Marksville on Feb. 22, 1998. The 19-page pamphlet also lists phone
numbers for local tourist commissions. To order, call (800) 947-7014
or write the Louisiana Office of Tourism, P.O. Box 94291, Department
5698, Baton Rouge, LA 70804-5527.

<SLUG> bc-health-aids </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1964 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-aids
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Susan Okie </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Health Officials Launch Campaign to Determine How Widespread &QL; 
 &UR; AIDS Virus Is &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   How many Americans are infected with the human immunodeficiency
virus? Where is it spreading most rapidly? How many new infections
occur each year?
<p>
   The answer is: Nobody knows.
<p>
   Precise information about such questions would seem necessary to
plot the spreading ripples of the AIDS epidemic. But federal health
officials can only guess at how many people are infected with HIV,
because most of the available sources of national data track cases of
AIDS  &MD;  the full-blown disease  &MD;  and not cases of new or recent
infection with the virus that causes it.
<p>
   As a result, officials can't be certain whether the current decline
in AIDS deaths is matched by a similar slowing of the epidemic as a
whole.
<p>
   The most recent estimate of the number of Americans infected  &MD; 
750,000  &MD;  is only half the total that government officials used to
cite a decade ago, at a time when experts believed that as many as 1.5
million people carried the virus. (They later revised that figure,
saying that in the mid-1980s only about 450,000 people were infected.)
The official estimate of how many Americans become infected with HIV
each year  &MD;  40,000  &MD;  hasn't budged since 1991.
<p>
   Such uncertainty persists because, without some way of counting new
infections, AIDS experts have very little to go on. ``There has never
been a broad-based national ... system'' for tracking the spread of
HIV through blood testing, said Rob Janssen, acting director of the
CDC's Division of HIV/AIDS Prevention-Surveillance and Epidemiology.
``It's very hard to do and extremely expensive.''
<p>
   Since the epidemic began, the count of AIDS cases has served as an
indirect measure of the number of people infected. To arrive at the
latter figure, health officials assumed that the majority of people
who contracted the virus would develop the disease within a certain
interval  &MD;  say, 10 years. They therefore employed a technique called
``back calculation,'' using figures on AIDS cases and deaths to
estimate how many Americans were infected at any point in time.
<p>
   Such calculations, combined with periodic surveys of various
samples of the population, are the source of current government
estimates of infection rates. For instance, the CDC estimates six of
every 1,000 adult or adolescent males and one of every 1,000 adult or
adolescent females in the United States is infected with the virus.
<p>
   But with the advent of new drugs that slow the multiplication of
the virus and help keep infected people healthy, the time from
infection until the onset of illness has become much less constant and
predictable. In order to keep on top of new developments in the
epidemic  &MD;  including watching for the emergence of drug-resistant
strains of HIV  &MD;  health officials say they now need better information
on where and when new cases of infection are occurring.
<p>
   ``We've always known that AIDS (cases) did not represent the full
number of people in (medical) care, much less the number just living
with HIV,'' said John Ward, chief of the HIV/AIDS surveillance branch
at the federal Centers for Disease Control and Prevention.
<p>
   ``We need to do a better job of conducting surveillance of the
whole HIV epidemic now, because AIDS (cases) will probably be
increasingly less representative of that total population, and more
representative of people who have either entered care very late or who
have failed therapy,'' he added.
<p>
   AIDS advocacy organizations concur with the need for a better way
of tracking cases of HIV infection, but say such a system should also
study how the new treatments are changing the total picture of the
epidemic.
<p>
   ``All of us recognize that the disease is different today,'' said
A. Cornelius Baker, executive director of the National Association of
People With AIDS. ``For us to understand where new infections are
occurring, what is the impact of these treatments, what is now the
natural history of the disease once treatment has been entered into  &MD; 
all of that requires us to have a monitoring system of some sort.''
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   Tracking new infections nationally would require doing regular
blood tests on a sample of people chosen to be representative of the
entire U.S. population, Janssen said. The closest approximation to
such a sample was a national survey that anonymously tested newborn
infants in order to measure infection rates in the nation's
childbearing women.
<p>
   Federal funding for that program was suspended in May 1995 after
opponents argued that because effective treatment for AIDS was
available, it was unethical to perform such tests without giving
patients the results.
<p>
   The CDC stopped monitoring the frequency of HIV infection in
hospitalized patients last year. The agency still tracks HIV infection
rates in treatment centers for drug abusers and in clinics that treat
sexually transmitted diseases. On a national scale, those rates have
remained relatively stable during the past three years, said Janssen.
<p>
   But he added that these stable figures mask ``a very dynamic
situation'' at the local level, with the virus spreading at varying
rates in different regions of the country and among various population
groups.
<p>
   To get a more-detailed and up-to-date picture of the movement of
the virus, ``we're beginning to focus more on new infections'' rather
than simply measuring what proportion of drug users or clinic patients
test positive for HIV at any particular time, Janssen said. CDC
researchers are trying to develop blood tests that could be used in
people who test positive to help determine how recently they became
infected with the virus.
<p>
   Focusing on new infections makes sense for several reasons.
<p>
   For patients, research increasingly suggests that people who begin
treatment with antiviral drugs soon after becoming infected with HIV
have a better chance of checking the reproduction of the virus and
preserving the health of their immune systems.
<p>
   For public-health officials, tracking new infections provides a
more-accurate picture of which groups within a community are at
greatest risk of contracting HIV, so that prevention programs can be
targeted to those groups.
<p>
   Newly infected individuals who don't know they're carrying the
virus may fail to take precautions against transmitting it. Research
suggests they may be a powerful force fueling the epidemic.
<p>
   ``It's thought that very early in the infection, people have very
high viral titers,'' said James Curran, the former director of the
CDC's Division of HIV/AIDS Prevention who is now dean of the Rollins
School of Public Health at Emory University in Atlanta. That means
they have large numbers of virus particles in their bloodstream and
body fluids, making them more likely to infect others.
<p>
   Tracking cases and studying patterns of transmission have been
vital to understanding AIDS. Early in the epidemic, even before the
human immunodeficiency virus was identified, epidemiological
surveillance suggested an infectious cause and identified all of the
major routes of transmission  &MD;  by contaminated blood products or
needles, by sexual intercourse, by a pregnant woman to her infant.
Epidemiological studies also provided evidence that AIDS wasn't
transmitted by casual contact or by mosquitoes.
<p>
   If something were to happen now to alter the current dynamics of
the disease  &MD;  for example, the emergence somewhere in the country of a
strain of HIV resistant to multiple drugs  &MD;  health officials would
probably notice the change sooner if they had a means of monitoring
new cases of infection, CDC officials say.
<p>
   ``We're interested in major shifts. I think surveillance (of
infection rates) gives you information about where the epidemic is and
where it may be heading,'' said Ward.
<p>
   Because of the mounting evidence that earlier treatment may offer
an advantage, AIDS activist organizations have begun urging people at
risk to be tested to find out whether they are infected.
<p>
   CDC officials are planning a national media campaign to encourage
more widespread HIV testing for people at risk, and have begun a study
to find out what kind of approach would be most effective.
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   AIDS organizations such as NAPWA acknowledge the need for a
national HIV reporting system, but would prefer one such as
Maryland's, which doesn't link results to names, in order to guard
against breaches of confidentiality, Baker said. He added that with
more widespread HIV testing, thousands more Americans will learn they
are infected with the virus but may not be able to afford the
expensive antiviral drugs that can help keep them healthy.
<p>
   ``It would be an extreme frustration if we institute this system
where we now know that we have this magnitude of HIV cases, and we
haven't figured out a way to help people,'' he said.
<p>
   Some have suggested that HIV infection should be made reportable
under federal law. Rep. Tom A. Coburn, R-Okla., a physician, has
introduced a bill that would require states to report cases of HIV
infection, would require notification of sexual partners of infected
individuals and would allow health-care workers to refuse to treat
anyone who refuses an HIV test. States would also have to report the
names of HIV-infected people to a federal government data base. The
bill's supporters include the American Medical Association.
<p>
   But AIDS groups and some other health organizations oppose the
legislation, pointing out that federal law does not require the
reporting of any other disease and that the measure would penalize
states that fail to comply with the law without providing any new
funds to help them carry out its requirements. States are already
required to perform partner notification as a condition of receiving
federal AIDS funds, said Julie Scofield of the National Alliance of
State and Territorial AIDS Directors.
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   One of the most complex aspects of tracking the epidemic is the
question of how to monitor for the emergence of drug-resistant strains
of HIV. Many AIDS experts fear such strains may evolve as a
consequence of broader use of new drugs to treat the infection, much
as antibiotic-resistant strains of bacteria have spread within the
U.S. population.
<p>
   ``The concern about resistance is that certain communities may
begin having higher rates'' of infection  &MD;  for instance, drug addicts
being treated for HIV infection, who may take their medicines
inconsistently, Ward said.
<p>
   He said CDC researchers started a pilot project last year to
collect blood samples from patients to search for drug-resistant
strains. But the detailed viral analysis required for such monitoring
is expensive, and the task is complex because the environment is
constantly changing, as doctors continue to try new drugs and drug
combinations, and as scientists develop new tests for monitoring
strains of the virus.
<p>
   Federal funding for the CDC's AIDS surveillance activities was
about $58 million this year, according to Janssen. About $50 million
of the total went to states to support their surveillance programs.
Funding increased slightly this year but has otherwise been flat
during the 1990s.
<p>
   Curran recently headed a federal panel that he said will recommend
an intensified focus on tracking HIV infections in high-risk portions
of the population, including young male homosexuals, intravenous drug
users and sexually active teen-agers.
<p>
   ``How are we going to make HIV more preventable, and life safer for
our adolescent kids?'' he asked. ``Our kids are the first generation
to enter a world with AIDS, probably the first of all future
generations.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-health-aids-tests </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0720 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-aids-tests
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Who Should Be Tested for HIV? &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   For a number of reasons, health officials are urging more Americans
to find out their HIV status by being tested.
<p>
   Testing has gotten easier. One new test uses a swab placed in the
mouth, making a needle stick unnecessary. Some clinics can provide
results in as little as two days. There is even a test you can give
yourself at home, with the results provided over the telephone.
<p>
   People who discover they're infected can take new drug combinations
that slow the multiplication of the AIDS virus and reduce or postpone
damage to the immune system. Equally important, they can also take
special care to avoid transmitting the virus to others.
<p>
   ``We would say that all sexually active people, particularly
persons who are sexually active with multiple partners  &MD;  whether gay
or straight  &MD;  should definitely know'' whether they're infected with
the virus, said Ronald O. Valdiserri, deputy director of the National
Center for HIV, STD and TB Prevention at the federal Centers for
Disease Control and Prevention. ``They should definitely have antibody
testing.''
<p>
   HIV testing is most strongly recommended for people whose behavior
places them in high-risk categories, Valdiserri added. That includes
anyone who has recently had a sexually transmitted disease, including
such infections as gonorrhea, chlamydia, herpes or syphilis, and
anyone who has injected drugs using equipment that's been shared with
another person.
<p>
   Women who are pregnant or contemplating pregnancy should definitely
consider having an HIV test, Valdiserri said. Giving an infected woman
drugs during pregnancy to treat HIV reduces the chance that the virus
will be passed to her infant.
<p>
   The risk of acquiring the virus through sex depends on what
practices a couple engages in and whether they take precautions. The
highest-risk behavior is anal intercourse without the protection of a
condom. Unprotected vaginal intercourse also poses a risk of infection
for both partners, as does oral sex, said Troy Knighton, director of
HIV counseling and testing at the Whitman-Walker Clinic in Washington,
D.C.
<p>
   Your chances of infection also depend on your sexual partner's past
history  &MD;  and his or her resultant likelihood of carrying the virus.
People who frequently have new partners  &MD;  a common pattern among some
teen-agers and young adults  &MD;  are at significant risk of HIV
infection, even if they sleep with just one boyfriend or girlfriend at
a time.
<p>
   Couples who've been monogamous for a long time have less reason to
worry. However, Valdiserri said, ``as a general rule of thumb,
sexually active people definitely need to know their serostatus
(whether they test positive for HIV infection) and need to know their
partner's.''
<p>
   Standard HIV tests look for antibodies against the virus. These are
chemicals that the immune system of an infected person manufactures
and releases into the bloodstream and other body fluids. The
antibodies can sometimes be detected as soon as two weeks after a
person becomes infected, but they may take up to six months to appear.
The widely used blood tests and the new oral test (which uses fluid
from cheek and gum cells, not saliva) work by measuring the same kind
of antibodies.
<p>
   Most AIDS experts emphasize that everyone should be counseled
before undergoing the test and again after obtaining the result. For
this reason, many discourage using the home test, since negative
results are often communicated only via a telephone recording.
<p>
   When choosing where to be tested, you may want to ask about cost,
whether the oral test as well as the blood test is available, whether
pre-test counseling is done individually or in a group, and how long
it will take to get the results. You can also inquire whether the
results are confidential (in which a record is kept of your name and
the result) or anonymous (in which results are coded by number only).
<p>
   For more information:
<p>
   National AIDS Hotline: 1-800-342-2437. National toll-free number,
operates 24 hours a day. Answers to questions about HIV and AIDS,
counseling, HIV testing sites, free publications, referrals to
hospitals or organizations that can help infected people find medical
care.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-health-aids-states </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0242 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-aids-states
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Susan Okie </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; 27 States Require Reporting of Positive HIV Tests &QL; 
 &UR; For Adults, Adolescents to Health Departments &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Twenty-seven states now require doctors and clinics to report the
results of positive HIV tests for adults and adolescents to health
departments, including the names of infected individuals. New Mexico
plans to institute such a requirement starting next year.
<p>
   Three states (Texas, Connecticut and Oregon) require the reporting
of positive results with names only for infected children. Maryland
(and Texas, for infected adults and adolescents) require reporting of
positive HIV tests but identify the results by number rather than
requiring that infected individuals be reported by name. The states
pass on their data on positive HIV tests to the federal Centers for
Disease Control and Prevention, but not the names of infected
individuals.
<p>
   Deciding which diseases should be legally reportable is each
state's prerogative, but CDC officials say they hope additional states
will pass laws making HIV infection a reportable condition.
<p>
   ``I think that it's likely that we will see declining numbers of
AIDS cases,'' said Rob Janssen, acting director of the CDC's Division
of HIV/AIDS Prevention-Surveillance and Epidemiology. ``As that
happens, states that don't have HIV reporting or HIV surveillance now
are likely to reconsider.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-glassman </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0850 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-glassman
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By James Glassman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Time and Chance Vs. Rhyme and Reason (Washn) &QL; 
 &UR; The writer is a fellow at the American Enterprise Institute. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  We can plan for the future, predict it, guess it, but
the truth is that it's unknowable and often irrational  &MD;  as the world
learned again this weekend with the sudden death of Diana, princess of
Wales.
<p>
   Time and chance often trump rhyme and reason. That's why I'm amused
and often irritated at stock market prognostications. ``The bull
market isn't over yet,'' wrote the chief economist for NationsBank
last week. How does he know?
<p>
   Obviously, we can't live our lives under a belief that the world is
chaotic and senseless, and, indeed, most of the time the world isn't.
We have to plan  &MD;  in our work and in our personal lives  &MD;  and we can't
survive without faith that there is some order to the future.
<p>
   But especially in the public sphere, we also need to put
forecasting and planning in their proper place  &MD;  way down the list.
Not only can they be destructive of personal liberty, they don't work.
<p>
   In a fine article in ASAP, the Forbes high-tech magazine, Virginia
Postrel, editor of Reason, resurrects a wonderful dichotomy first
posed in 1988 by the late political scientist Aaron Wildavsky. He laid
out two alternatives for dealing with risk, with the uncertainties of
the world.
<p>
   One choice, writes Postrel, is ``anticipation , the static planning
that aspires to perfect foresight.'' The other is ``resilience , the
dynamic response that relies on having many margins of adjustment.''
One alternative tries to predict the future, so we can adapt to it in
advance; the other lets it happens and tries to take advantage of its
benefits and bounce back from its adversities.
<p>
   In her piece, Postrel argues that Silicon Valley's success is built
on the paradigm of resilience: ``People do the best they can at the
moment, deal with problems as they arise and develop networks to help
them out.'' They ship software without debugging it perfectly.
<p>
   Resilience is no guarantee that companies won't fail, she writes,
``but that resources  &MD;  including human resources  &MD;  are more likely to
move to better uses more quickly, with less trauma. Indeed, the
willingness to abandon a losing project is fundamental.''
<p>
   By contrast, New England, cradle of the Harvard Business School,
``has encouraged strategies of anticipation. People try to imagine
everything that might go wrong and fix it in advance.'' And New
England trails Silicon Valley badly.
<p>
   There's no doubt where Washington stands  &MD;  on the side of
anticipation and out of tune with a world in which time and chance are
moving at frenetic speed. Look at our five-year budgets, horrendously
complicated tax code, Medicare, the Advanced Technology Program,
regulation in general. And when was the last time government abandoned
a losing project (Amtrak, the Energy Department, the public school
monopoly)?
<p>
   Anticipation is not a style that works when the world changes
rapidly. ``It encourages,'' Postrel writes, ``two types of error:
hubristic central planning and overcaution.''
<p>
   Exactly. Those are the two great diseases of our public life: (1)
the belief, held by both Democrats and Republicans, that, given enough
data and power, government can make the future secure and blissful;
and (2) the fear of entrusting that future to the imagination of
individuals and to free competition among businesses.
<p>
   Wildavsky wrote that ``incessant search by many minds . . .
produces more (and more valuable) knowledge than the attempt to
program the paths to discovery by a single one. . . . Not only markets
rely on spontaneity; science and democracy do as well.''
<p>
   But to celebrate spontaneity is not to denigrate ideals.
<p>
   On the contrary, a world in which planning is revealed as futile is
a world in which principle is elevated to its proper place. Because
the numbers inevitably will turn out to be wrong, we should feel
comfortable holding resolutely to the ideals we think are right.
That's good advice for members of Congress returning this week.
<p>
   But in concrete terms, what is public policy that looks clear-eyed
at the truth about time and chance?
<p>
   It would favor an open, risk-spreading system, like Silicon
Valley's. Conscious of the limits of planning, it would devolve
decisions to lower levels of government, leaving law enforcement,
education and welfare (to name a few) to localities. It would abandon
attempts to plan, using absurd actuarial projections, for the
retirement of tens of millions and instead allow individuals to
provide for their own. It would limit the function of the tax code to
revenue-raising. It would stop trying to regulate the daily detail of
American lives.
<p>
   In short, a public policy that understands the nature of time and
chance would respect people enough to give them back their own futures
 &MD;  and let them develop the resilience to make those futures happy and
productive, no matter what's around the next corner.
</TEXT>

 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL;

<BYLINE> By Rob Pegoraro </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Improve Your Move &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   I hate moving  &MD;  the only smart thing I've done in my last six moves
was hire a moving company this spring, saving me from my own
incompetence at stuffing my belongings into U-Hauls.
<p>
   This is a subject where I can use any help I can get. Enter
MoveQuest (http://www.movequest.com), a spinoff of the MapQuest site
(http://www.mapquest.com). It combines MapQuest's geographical data,
sometimes klutzily, with services offered by other companies at other
Web sites.
<p>
   For instance, you get moving-expense estimates and cost-of-living
comparisons from the Homebuyer's Fair site
(http://www.homefair.com/home), insurance help from Allstate
(http://www.allstate.com) and financing information from Countrywide
Home Loans (http://www.countrywide.com).
<p>
   MoveQuest can also generate a calendar of to-do reminders, starting
2½ months before moving day. (Sample event: ``Arrange ISP service.'')
When I gave it a moving date of Oct. 1, it scolded me in user-manual
English: ``Your schedule is (sic) little behind. You better hurry
up!!''
<p>
   Rob Pegoraro can be reached at rob(at)twp.com
</TEXT>
