<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Good afternoon, AM editors. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service Budget for
morning papers of Wednesday, Sept. 3, 1997. The editors are Kate
Carlisle at The Washington Post and Carlos Selva at the Los Angeles
Times. For questions about Post stories, call (202) 334-7666; for
questions about Times and other Times Mirror stories, call (213)
237-7832.
<p>
    &UR; The Death of Princess Diana:
<p>
   DIANA  &MD;  PARIS  &MD;  A French judge places four photographers under
formal investigation for involuntary homicide and failing to come to
the aid of Princess Diana and three others as they lay trapped in the
wreckage after a car crash. Developing.
<p>
   DIANA-CHARLES  &MD;  LONDON  &MD;  How will the death of Diana affect the
future of her former husband, Charles?; many angles on his reaction to
her death are being put about by the tabloid columnists, Buckingham
Palace sources, and royal chroniclers, not to mention the mourning
public, who appear to be in a singular fury with the heir to the
throne. Developing, by Dan Balz and Chris Spolar (Post).
<p>
   DIANA-ENGLAND  &MD;  LONDON  &MD;  Across England, in country lanes and at
the ancient palace where she lies, an astonishing national lament
swells for Diana, princess of Wales, stunning planners who now think
more than a million people may jam into a few blocks of London for her
funeral Saturday. Developing, by William D. Montalbano (Times).
<p>
   DIANA-CHARITY  &MD;  LONDON  &MD;  The people she touched most directly  &MD;  the
homeless, the sick, the dying, and the people who took care of them  &MD; 
remember Diana as a compassionate and caring princess who was even
more informal and engaging than her public persona. Developing, by
Richard Boudreaux (Times).
<p>
    &UR; Other international:
<p>
   BALKANS  &MD;  SARAJEVO, Bosnia-Herzegovina  &MD;  Surrounded by
stick-wielding Bosnian Serbs, American troops agree to relinquish a
television transmitter they control to forces answering to war-crimes
suspect Radovan Karadzic. Developing, by Tracy Wilkinson (Times).
<p>
   MIR  &MD;  MOSCOW  &MD;  Russian Space Agency officials cast blame on two
weary cosmonauts for the disastrous June 25 accident on board the Mir
space station and hint they will be fined for a mistake that has cost
the Russian space program millions of dollars. Developing.
<p>
   BOLIVIA-JUSTICE  &MD;  LA PAZ, Bolivia  &MD;  When Rene Blattmann became
Bolivia's first justice minister in 1994, he started dismantling an
ancient system that allowed no bail and kept prisons filled with
people declared innocent but awaiting prosecutor appeals; his success
has made him a hero at home and a model for reform in other countries.
1,175 words, by Sebastian Rotella (Times). Moved.
<p>
   SWEDEN-STERILE  &MD;  GAVLE, Sweden  &MD;  Victims of Sweden's
well-documented but little-scrutinized sterilization program are going
public with their anger and grief; the program was abandoned only in
the 1970s after 62,888 state-sponsored procedures. 1,530 words, by
Dean E. Murphy (Times). With SWEDEN-STERILE-VICTIM. Both moved.
<p>
    &UR; National:
<p>
   DONATE-GORE  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  With Vice President Al Gore considered
the front-runner for the Democratic presidential nomination three
years from now, and Sen. Fred Thompson a potential contender for the
GOP nod, this week's campaign finance proceedings, in which Gore will
be the focus, may someday be seen as a crucial early skirmish in the
2000 race for the White House. 1,465 words, by Ronald Brownstein and
Alan C. Miller (Times). Moved.
<p>
   IMMIG-SWEEP  &MD;  CHANDLER, Ariz.  &MD;  Residents sue an Arizona town,
saying its crackdown on illegal workers led to harassment of U.S.
citizens. 850 words, by Louis Sahagun (Times). Moved.
<p>
   MONUMENT  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  The Washington Monument is about to become
even more easily distinguished: Beginning sometime this winter, it
will become a 555-foot high obelisk surrounded by scaffolding, as a
3-year, privately funded $5 million overhaul begins. 775 words, by
Eric Lekus (Sun). Moved.
<p>
   REAGANBUILDING  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  The Ronald Reagan Building and
International Trade Center is the biggest federal edifice in D.C. next
to the Pentagon, and at $738 million, one of the costliest, upsetting
some lawmakers in town. 550 words, by Kasper Zeuthen (Times). Moved.
<p>
    &UR; Financial:
<p>
   WATER  &MD;  As the public's passion for bottled water continues to heat
up, a debate over the sources of the more than 3 million gallons sold
last year in the United States has taken center stage. 1,350 words, by
Harry Berkowitz (Newsday). Moved.

bc-magazines
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Entertainment editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Peter Carlson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Fashions From Another Dimension &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Vogue's annual ``Fall Fashion Blockbuster'' issue is out and it is,
as always, a delight for fashion victims, people with too much money
and aficionados of the absurd. But you wouldn't want to be a mail
carrier on a route with a lot of Vogue subscribers: The magazine is a
fat, fragrant slab of slick paper  &MD;  with 734 thick, glossy pages, it
weighs more than many of the models pictured in it.
<p>
   The theme is ``The Thrill Is Back!,'' although nobody can remember
Vogue reporting that the alleged thrill of fashion had departed.
<p>
   But you've got to suspend that kind of disbelief if you want to
enjoy Vogue. You've got to get in the properly bubble-headed frame of
mind that permits you to accept the idea that, for example, women are
actually going to paint their eyebrows with thick slashes of red
lipstick that make them look like Groucho Marx in a movie colorized by
an acid-head.
<p>
   Several women in Vogue have adopted this look, including one
wearing a $2,500 black Gucci outfit on a page where the magazine
proclaims, quite candidly, that ``fashion is more wonderfully insane
than ever.''
<p>
   There's plenty of that wonderful insanity on view in Vogue. The
pictures are like postcards from some strange planet where almost
everyone is young and female and sort of morose-looking  &MD;  probably,
judging from their emaciation, because there's not much to eat there.
<p>
   On planet Vogue, a woman advertising a cloth pocketbook has strange
thorns growing out of her naked back. On planet Vogue, a model wearing
a red see-through dress and tattoos of George Washington and Jesus
Christ poses with a model wearing a ``jewel-of-the-Nile dress made
entirely out of safety pins.'' On planet Vogue, a woman in an elegant
black gown writhes sensuously against a picture of clouds while two
disheveled men lie slumped on the floor.
<p>
   In an ad for ``gai mattiolo''  &MD;  whatever that is  &MD;  a big-haired
blonde wearing too much makeup and nothing under her plaid sport
jacket stands in a doorway marked ``Governor Suite.'' It's the kind of
photo Kenneth Starr has been hoping to find all these years.
<p>
   The ads in Vogue are wonderfully silly but the stories are even
sillier. One of them, called ``Cult of the Expert,'' proclaims
``beauty is a religion that requires devotion, sacrifice and several
guiding lights.'' The guiding lights turn out to be Art Luna, ``hair
colorist to Hollywood''; Steven Miller,``facialist to famous faces'';
Rene Diaz, ``the best blow-dryer in Manhattan''; Janea Padilha, ``the
ultimate body waxer''; and Laurie Polis, ``down-to-earth dermatologist
to the below-Fourteenth Street beautiful people.'' Polis, described as
``Earth Mother-ish,'' attracts a fanatical following: ``Peel junkies
beg for a weekly fix to keep the glow going. Pimple-phobes pop in to
get tiny imperfections obliterated.''
<p>
   The magazine isn't afraid to cover the wars in Beirut, Belfast and
Bosnia  &MD;  from its own unique perspective. In ``Armed and Glamorous,''
Vogue explains how women living in these war zones ``find strength in
stilettos, designer dresses and midnight-blue nail polish.'' Under a
photo of a Lebanese woman having her eyebrows plucked the caption
reads: ``In Lebanon, chaos reigns but brows stay neatly arched.''
<p>
   You've got to love a magazine that runs a feature on inexpensive
clothes  &MD;  ``Paint the town red without spending too much green''  &MD; 
then illustrates it with photos of a $598 dress and $965 shoes. It's a
magazine that raises important questions, like: Isn't it time to raise
taxes on the rich?
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   Let's not forget the men's mags. They, too, have annual fall
fashion features, which are at least as absurd as Vogue's.
<p>
   Esquire has a six-page feature on ``The New Fall Suit,'' which
reveals that it is a lot like the old fall suit except that: 1) it's
light gray instead of charcoal gray and 2) the corduroy cords are
horizontal instead of vertical.
<p>
   GQ gets a little more creative in its 34-page fashion package, but
the results are even more ridiculous. The magazine invents a
fictitious Italian American family that includes two brothers, Stefano
and Giulio, who have just returned from ``the old country'' with lots
of expensive new Italian clothes. In one picture, they get into a
fistfight  &MD;  Stefano in his $1,870 leather sport jacket, Giulio in his
$530 turtleneck. In another shot, Stefano stands in his $1,970 Giorgio
Armani suit talking to his father and lamenting the fact that Dad is
wearing sandals with white socks. &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-fearless-traveler </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0976 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-fearless-traveler
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Travel editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By James T. Yenckel </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Travel Q&AMP;A &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Are there any companies that arrange tours for families? Everything
we've looked at so far seems designed for adults. We're especially
interested in Europe for next year.
<p>
    &MD; Patrick K. Carroll, Gaithersburg Md.
<p>
   At least two Washington area travel firms are organizing
sightseeing tours in Europe for families next year. Roots &AMP; Wings
Excursions (1-800-722-9005 and 703-834-7244) of Herndon, Va., is
offering 10-day tours of Great Britain and Italy, and Grandtravel
(1-800-247-7651 and 301-986-0790) of Chevy Chase has put together
tours of Ireland, Great Britain, Holland/Belgium, London/Paris,
France, Italy, Austria and Spain. Roots &AMP; Wings hasn't yet set a price
on its tours; a 10-day Grandtravel tour in Europe is about $4,300 for
adults (not including air fare), although prices vary.
<p>
   On a family trip, the day-to-day pacing is slowed to give families
plenty of free time to explore on their own, says Carol Goldsmith,
president of Roots &AMP; Wings. And usually special dinners for children
only are arranged, so the adults can have an evening on their own.
Lodgings with ample space for a family also are a priority.
<p>
   How can I check on whether an ad or promotion for a discount tour
package is legitimate or some kind of scam?
<p>
    &MD; Sharon Richards, Annapolis, Md.
<p>
   If an unknown travel firm is offering you a bargain that seems too
good to be true, be wary. It could be a scam. You will want to check
out any unusual offer carefully before snapping it up. And don't be
taken in by any threat that the offer will disappear if you fail to
act immediately.
<p>
   To determine if a travel firm has been the target of consumer
complaints, contact 1) the Better Business Bureau in the community in
which the firm is headquartered, 2) your local city, county or state
consumer protection office and 3) the American Society of Travel
Agents (1101 King St., Suite 200, Alexandria, Va. 22314,
703-739-8739).
<p>
   Still unsure? Check out the components of the package. Do
guidebooks cite the hotel as a dump? Can you get a better air fare on
your own? Does the package give you flexibility to travel when you
want? Also, you can ask a travel agent to help you determine the
offer's validity. An agent may even be able to get you a better deal
to the same destination.
<p>
   I understand one can fly cheaply as a courier. What can you tell me
about courier travel?
<p>
    &MD; Virginia Berton, Alexandria, Va.
<p>
   Flying as a courier can be the cheapest route to Europe and,
sometimes, to the Far East and Australia. As a courier, your duties
are minimal. In exchange for a reduced-price, round-trip ticket issued
in your name, the courier firm uses your standard luggage allotment to
send packets of mail and other documents abroad for overnight
delivery. As checked baggage, the packets pass quickly through
immigration and customs; if shipped as cargo, they might be delayed
for hours or days. You are allowed only one piece of carry-on luggage,
and usually you are responsible for a receipt or manifest for the
checked items. You hand it over at the arrival airport to a
representative of the courier firm who meets the plane. Most trips are
for seven to 10 days.
<p>
   In business for 14 years, Now Voyager (212-431-1616) in Manhattan
serves as a clearinghouse for courier flights. For a $50 annual fee,
you are offered a choice of flights year-round; not surprisingly,
winter is the easiest time to book. Flights can be booked up to two
months in advance. This summer, the firm has been offering round-trip
flights out of New York City and Newark to London for $359; Brussels,
Copenhagen and Dublin, $399; Paris, Madrid, Milan and Rome, $499.
Flights to the Far East depart from Detroit; to Australia, from Los
Angeles. You pay the cost of getting to the departure airport. Call
Now Voyager for a detailed recording of the firm's courier policies.
<p>
   My husband and I want to go to Sydney for the Summer Olympics in
2000. What can you tell us about tickets, air fares to Australia and
lodging?
<p>
    &MD; Stefanie Hutchins, Hyattsville, Md.
<p>
   The Sydney 2000 Olympics, scheduled for Sept. 15 to Oct. 1, are
still three years away, so you are a little premature in your
planning.
<p>
   Event tickets will not go on sale until 1999. At that time, about
5.5 million tickets will become available, with 80 percent of them
reserved for purchase in Australia and the rest apportioned to other
participating countries. Cartan Tours (1-800-818-1998) of Manhattan
Beach, Calif., has been designated by the U.S. Olympic Committee as
the U.S. ticket distributor. Cartan, which also will offer Sydney
lodging packages, is compiling a list of people wanting ticket and
tour order forms.
<p>
   Sydney expects about 250,000 spectators during the Games and is
taking steps to assure sufficient accommodations for everyone. Already
in the works is a Homestay/Homehost Program, which will provide rental
lodgings in private homes  &MD;  either with or without a resident host.
This year, rates at the Sydney Hilton begin at about $165 a night for
a room for two, and budget hotels go for $35 to $50 a night.
Meanwhile, Qantas and United, airlines serving Sydney, say their
computers accept reservations only up to a year in advance  &MD;  so it's
too early to book flights. Qantas is quoting a round-trip fare from
Washington via Los Angeles of $1,594 per person, with restrictions.
<p>
   To stay abreast of Olympic Games developments, keep an eye on the
Web site set up by the Sydney Organizing Committee for the Olympic
Games (www.sydney.olympic.org) or call the Australian Tourist
Commission's USA Aussie Help Line (847-296-4900). For a packet of
sightseeing information, call the commission's toll-free number
(1-800-333-0262).
</TEXT>

</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Guy Gugliotta </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Senate's Lonely Laptop Lobby (Washn) &QL; 
 &UR; Gugliotta is a national reporter for The Washington Post. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Shortly after he was sworn in, freshman Sen. Mike
Enzi, R-Wyo., asked the Senate parliamentarian whether he could bring
his laptop computer onto the Senate floor. Ask the sergeant at arms,
the parliamentarian said.
<p>
   It turned out that Senate Rule IV authorizes ``mechanical devices''
in the chamber when the sergeant at arms deems them ``necessary and
proper'' to conduct official Senate business, and as long as they ``do
not in any way distract, interrupt or inconvenience'' others.
<p>
   Anyway, Sergeant at Arms Gregory S. Casey explained to Enzi that he
couldn't deem anything without guidance from the Senate, so the answer
was ``no.' In May, Enzi, a gentle-spoken former shoe store owner and
the Senate's only professional accountant, asked the Rules Committee
for permission.
<p>
   Rules asked Casey to write a report, and Casey presented it to the
committee on July 31, 1997, which might live in history as the date
the U.S. Senate saw the future and gave it a raspberry.
<p>
   The committee's ranking minority member, Wendell H. Ford, D-Ky.,
72, didn't want to be ``an old fogy,'' and said he was hip enough to
notice some of the younger senators treated laptops like legal pads.
<p>
   But if Ford was trying to encourage shy younger colleagues, he
needn't have bothered. Sen. Kay Bailey Hutchison, R-Tex., 54 and a
senator since 1993, had already weighed in, and she didn't like it. At
all.
<p>
   ``There's something to be said for tradition,'' she said. If
senators want to carry laptops around, put them in the cloakroom.
Laptops in the chamber would be distracting and ugly.
<p>
   Sen. Dianne Feinstein, D-Calif., 64 and a senator since 1992, was
less charitable. ``It seems to me when we go to the floor, we're
supposed to know what we're saying.'' And she didn't fancy giving a
speech in front of a bunch of senators with their heads bent over.
<p>
   Next was Sen. Robert G. Torricelli, D-N.J., 46 and also a freshman.
He would like to see the Senate ``remain different,'' he said. Laptops
would take the freewheeling fun out of the debate, and everything
would be ``scripted.''
<p>
   Rules Committee Chairman John W. Warner, R-Va., 70 and a cagey
four-term veteran, ordered copies of the Casey report to be given to
all senators, after which the committee would meet again. Stay tuned.
<p>
   Enzi, who is 53 and so green he spent part of the August recess
unabashedly enjoying Monticello and other regional tourist
attractions, studied computers in college and has used them most of
his adult life. He has found it ``kind of a strain'' to work without
one.
<p>
   He was lucky last winter when he had to lead the debate on
accounting matters related to the balanced budget amendment, he said,
because ``there weren't any rocket science questions.'' Math in the
Senate, as he will soon discover, is whatever gets you to your
favorite number.
<p>
   Enzi said he had no plans to hook his computer (Compaq, 32
megabytes internal memory, 1.2 gigabytes hard drive) into a network,
and simply wanted to take notes, prepare speeches and keep track of
legislation  &MD;  ``to do the best job for my constituents.''
<p>
   Casey's report did not take a position, but essentially it agreed
with Enzi. As long as networking was not allowed, there ``would be no
need to wire the Senate floor,'' drill holes into 200-year-old desks
or worry about security.
<p>
   ``Large screens, obnoxious beeps or buzzers'' would be prohibited,
the report continued, so there would be no modems or ``Duke Nuke'M.''
And as far as ``necessary and proper,'' the report said, the device
must be ``used to further the legislative and constitutional function
of the Senate as a whole.''
<p>
   Which is what Enzi is trying to do.
<p>
   Sen. Robert C. Byrd, D-W.Va., 79, who has spent 38 years in the
Senate, who has compiled a three-volume history of the Senate, and who
lectures newcomers of both parties on everything they need to know
about ``necessary and proper'' in the Senate, was not impressed.
<p>
   ``I will read your report eagerly,'' he told Casey. ``Then I will
cast my vote against it.''
</TEXT>

AP-NY-09-02-97 1546EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-worldwise </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0937 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-worldwise
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
 &UR; (Washn) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<TEXT>
<p>
   So, which state has the most tornadoes? If you guessed Texas,
you're in good company: Fully half the respondents to last week's
impromptu trivia quiz voted for the Lone Star state, and rightly so.
Dorothy might have been uprooted in Kansas, but Texas has far more
twisters. From 1962 through 1991, 4,174 tornadoes touched down there.
The runner-up was  &MD;  surprise  &MD;  not Oklahoma or Kansas, as most of the
rest of our respondents guessed, but Florida, with a mere 1,590.
Oklahoma came in third, and Kansas a lowly fourth.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Serious Picasso aficionados may be interested to know that the
Boston Museum of Fine Arts' presentation of ``Picasso: The Early
Years''  &MD;  which ran at the National Gallery of Art from March 30 until
July 27  &MD;  will feature 32 pictures not seen in the Washington version
of the show. Among the works, all from the painter's experimental
period: ``Blue Roofs,'' depicting the view from Picasso's studio in
Paris; ``Seated Woman and Child,'' a Blue Period image of maternity;
and ``Woman in Stockings.'' These three date from 1901.
<p>
   ``Some private collectors didn't want to lend to two venues,'' says
George Shackelford, the MFA's head of European painting, by way of
explaining the modified catalogue in Boston. ``And some pieces that
were available in Washington were prohibited from traveling.''
<p>
   With autumn air fares to Boston hovering near $100 one-way (not to
mention Southwest Airlines' $39 one-way fare from BWI to Providence,
R.I., less than an hour from Boston), the timing may be right for
those who can't quite get enough of the master's youth  &MD;  or who simply
missed the show in Washington.
<p>
   The Boston appearance of ``Picasso: The Early Years'' runs from
Sept. 10 to Jan. 4 at the Museum of Fine Arts, 465 Huntington Ave.
Admission is by ticket only. Tickets are available at the box office
or through Ticketmaster, 617-931-2632; the reserved-ticket price for
non-members is $15. For more information, call 617-PICASSO
(617-742-2776). Or check out the museum's Web site at
http://www.mfa.org.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   ``When traveling in India, one generally will visit Islamic
mosques, Hindu temples, the Taj Mahal and Buddhist shrines,'' writes
tipster Papan Devnani of Arlington. ``At all these locations one must
leave shoes outside. Reeboks, Nikes and their ilk get stolen if left
unattended. Of course, there are attendants who will watch your shoes
for a fee. But this adds to the cost. So I carry a small handbag or
shoulder bag and put my shoes inside and walk into the place. It is an
acceptable practice and is not considered offensive.''
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Your tan is fading, you're wearing shoes and socks again and most
folks are planning to spend their autumn free time observing foliage
decay and harvesting fruit grown by others. But you needn't capitulate
to the season, at least not yet. You can head to North Florida, where
summer weather lingers an extra month or two  &MD;  and great seasonal
deals make it all the easier.
<p>
   Beginning this month, the state's beaches are emptying and the Gulf
of Mexico is bathwater-warm. For travelers who want to get away for a
couple of days but not part with a lot of cash, here are two spots
worth considering.
<p>
   AMELIA ISLAND: Tucked off Florida's northeastern-most Atlantic
coast, Amelia Island has 13 miles of beaches, top-notch sports
facilities, a historic district comprising 50 blocks and, should you
care about tenure in such matters, the state's oldest saloon. The
island's most interesting claim to fame is that it's the only spot in
the United States to have flown under eight different flags.
<p>
   The deal: As part of its Secret Season promotion, beginning
tomorrow, many hotels are offering visitors the third night free when
they pay for the first two. The catch: Most offer the savings only
during midweek. Information: 1-800-226-542,
<p>
   Amelia Island is about 35 minutes from downtown Jacksonville, 25
minutes from the airport.
<p>
   DESTIN-FORT WALTON AREA: Located on the state's panhandle, Destin
and environs offer plenty: 24 miles of beach, 545 holes of golf,
fishing in the ``World's Luckiest Fishing Village'' (a k a Destin) and
10 amusement areas offer everything from bumper boats to batting
cages.
<p>
   The deal: Indian Summer '97 promises discounts of up to 40 percent
on many of the 11,000 beachfront rooms on the Emerald Coast, which
includes Okaloosa Island. During the peak summer season, condo rentals
average $1,190 a week, hotels $97 per night. In the fall, rates bottom
out at $735 for condos, $57 for hotels. Information: 1-800-322-3319.
<p>
   The Destin-Fort Walton area is served by Okaloosa County Regional
Airport in Fort Walton, about 15 minutes from Destin.
<p>
   GETTING THERE: US Airways is quoting a round-trip fare of about
$170 to Jacksonville, with restrictions, from the three area airports.
Its fare to Fort Walton is about $200 from National and Dulles, though
the price drops to $162 if you fly from Dulles to Pensacola, about 45
minutes away. Car rentals vary widely; Hertz is quoting a rate of $30
per day, plus tax and airport fees.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Novotel New York is offering a new 10-page ``Discount Shopping
Guide,'' authored by a pair of credentialed NYC bargain-hunters, to
guests of its renovated Times Square property. A swell idea  &MD;  until
you learn that the lowest-priced accommodations in the 474-room hotel
start at $249 per night, not quite in the range of the crowd shopping
at Loehmann's, Daffy's and Syms.
</TEXT>

<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-capital-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0714 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-capital-comment
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National, Editorial Page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Guy Gugliotta </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Senate's Lonely Laptop Lobby (Washn) &QL; 
 &UR; Gugliotta is a national reporter for The Washington Post. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Shortly after he was sworn in, freshman Sen. Mike
Enzi, R-Wyo., asked the Senate parliamentarian whether he could bring
his laptop computer onto the Senate floor. Ask the sergeant at arms,
the parliamentarian said.
<p>
   It turned out that Senate Rule IV authorizes ``mechanical devices''
in the chamber when the sergeant at arms deems them ``necessary and
proper'' to conduct official Senate business, and as long as they ``do
not in any way distract, interrupt or inconvenience'' others.
<p>
   Anyway, Sergeant at Arms Gregory S. Casey explained to Enzi that he
couldn't deem anything without guidance from the Senate, so the answer
was ``no.' In May, Enzi, a gentle-spoken former shoe store owner and
the Senate's only professional accountant, asked the Rules Committee
for permission.
<p>
   Rules asked Casey to write a report, and Casey presented it to the
committee on July 31, 1997, which might live in history as the date
the U.S. Senate saw the future and gave it a raspberry.
<p>
   The committee's ranking minority member, Wendell H. Ford, D-Ky.,
72, didn't want to be ``an old fogy,'' and said he was hip enough to
notice some of the younger senators treated laptops like legal pads.
<p>
   But if Ford was trying to encourage shy younger colleagues, he
needn't have bothered. Sen. Kay Bailey Hutchison, R-Tex., 54 and a
senator since 1993, had already weighed in, and she didn't like it. At
all.
<p>
   ``There's something to be said for tradition,'' she said. If
senators want to carry laptops around, put them in the cloakroom.
Laptops in the chamber would be distracting and ugly.
<p>
   Sen. Dianne Feinstein, D-Calif., 64 and a senator since 1992, was
less charitable. ``It seems to me when we go to the floor, we're
supposed to know what we're saying.'' And she didn't fancy giving a
speech in front of a bunch of senators with their heads bent over.
<p>
   Next was Sen. Robert G. Torricelli, D-N.J., 46 and also a freshman.
He would like to see the Senate ``remain different,'' he said. Laptops
would take the freewheeling fun out of the debate, and everything
would be ``scripted.''
<p>
   Rules Committee Chairman John W. Warner, R-Va., 70 and a cagey
four-term veteran, ordered copies of the Casey report to be given to
all senators, after which the committee would meet again. Stay tuned.
<p>
   Enzi, who is 53 and so green he spent part of the August recess
unabashedly enjoying Monticello and other regional tourist
attractions, studied computers in college and has used them most of
his adult life. He has found it ``kind of a strain'' to work without
one.
<p>
   He was lucky last winter when he had to lead the debate on
accounting matters related to the balanced budget amendment, he said,
because ``there weren't any rocket science questions.'' Math in the
Senate, as he will soon discover, is whatever gets you to your
favorite number.
<p>
   Enzi said he had no plans to hook his computer (Compaq, 32
megabytes internal memory, 1.2 gigabytes hard drive) into a network,
and simply wanted to take notes, prepare speeches and keep track of
legislation  &MD;  ``to do the best job for my constituents.''
<p>
   Casey's report did not take a position, but essentially it agreed
with Enzi. As long as networking was not allowed, there ``would be no
need to wire the Senate floor,'' drill holes into 200-year-old desks
or worry about security.
<p>
   ``Large screens, obnoxious beeps or buzzers'' would be prohibited,
the report continued, so there would be no modems or ``Duke Nuke'M.''
And as far as ``necessary and proper,'' the report said, the device
must be ``used to further the legislative and constitutional function
of the Senate as a whole.''
<p>
   Which is what Enzi is trying to do.
<p>
   Sen. Robert C. Byrd, D-W.Va., 79, who has spent 38 years in the
Senate, who has compiled a three-volume history of the Senate, and who
lectures newcomers of both parties on everything they need to know
about ``necessary and proper'' in the Senate, was not impressed.
<p>
   ``I will read your report eagerly,'' he told Casey. ``Then I will
cast my vote against it.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-japan-police </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0975 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-japan-police
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mary Jordan  </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; High-Profile Crimes Spark Criticism of Japan's Police &QL; 
 &UR; (Tokyo) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   TOKYO  &MD;  Murder suspect Kazuko Fukuda spent 15 years on the run,
toying with Japanese police. She had so many plastic surgeries that
police called her ``The Woman With Seven Faces.'' She spent lavishly,
gambled conspicuously and drank heavily  &MD;  once sharing a drink with a
policeman who told her she resembled the famous fugitive Kazuko
Fukuda. Pounding her fist on the table, she dared the officer to take
her fingerprints. He didn't.
<p>
   Fukuda's luck finally ran out in July, when police arrested her in
a barroom in western Japan. She was arraigned 11 hours before the
15-year statute of limitations expired on the 1982 murder of which she
is suspected.
<p>
   Fukuda's case, along with other recent high-profile crimes, has
raised disturbing questions for many Japanese.
<p>
   Many people are frustrated about what they see as outdated laws
that are too forgiving of criminals, such as the 15-year statute of
limitations on murder and laws that allow murderers under 16 to be set
free after three years in juvenile detention. Those juvenile laws,
written in 1948, caused an uproar this year when a 14-year-old boy was
arrested on charges of beheading an 11-year-old boy and leaving the
severed head at the gate of a school.
<p>
   Certainly, Japan remains one of the world's safest societies, with
enviously low rates of violent crime. The United States has twice as
many people as Japan but 18 times as many murders.
<p>
   But there has been a recent increase in violent crimes, especially
those involving guns and young people. Japan's police, many believe,
are not equipped to respond.
<p>
   Japanese are proud of their neighborhood  &UR; kobans &LR; , or police boxes,
from which unarmed police officers patrol assigned areas on bicycles.
But now people are saying sophisticated criminals  &MD;  and suspects such
as Fukuda, who hopscotched around the country and easily changed
identities  &MD;  show the need for a better trained and equipped national
police force.
<p>
   ``The police system is outdated and doesn't fit current
realities,'' said Kenjiro Mishima, a former National Police Agency
official who is now a security consultant.
<p>
   Japan has no equivalent of the FBI. The National Police Agency is
largely a bureaucratic organization that collects statistics and
information on organized crime and violent right-wing groups. With the
exception of the Imperial Police, a branch of the agency that protects
the royal family, the National Police Agency has virtually no direct
power of investigation and arrest.
<p>
   ``The NPA is not an investigative force,'' Mishima said. ``The
country needs something like the FBI, a nerve center to collect
intelligence, analyze it. Japan needs a national force to deal with
serious crime.''
<p>
   When a terrorist group killed a dozen people and injured 6,000 more
in a poison-gas attack on the Tokyo subway system two years ago, the
Tokyo metropolitan police led the investigation. Several of the key
suspects are still at large.
<p>
   Critics say Japan's organized crime families, and, increasingly,
Chinese crime syndicates that smuggle illegal immigrants into the
country, are better armed and better organized than the authorities
responsible for dealing with them. The chief of the National Police
Agency was shot three times in broad daylight on a Tokyo street two
years ago. The crime remains unsolved.
<p>
   Japan recently marked the second anniversary of a triple murder in
a suburban Tokyo grocery store. Three store employees, two of them
high school students, were gunned down in the robbery. Police have
made no arrests in the crime.
<p>
   Some TV commentators note that crime was worse in the aftermath of
World War II than now, and the current rash of random violence might
pass, too. But in the meantime, robbers are thriving in a place where
the idea of a jewelry store having an electronic door buzzer, or of
clerks checking signatures on credit cards, is alien.
<p>
   All year, armed robberies have been front-page news in a country
where ordinary people routinely carry hundreds or thousands of dollars
in cash. Thirteen times this year, thieves have robbed armored trucks
carrying vast sums of cash  &MD;  one with $2 million. The trucks were
protected by guards who carried neither guns nor radios.
<p>
   ``Most companies are not ready for robbers with guns because they
think that kind of crime is out of the question,'' Mishima said.
``Maybe mom-and-pop shops don't have to worry about armed robbers, but
banks should.''
<p>
   Police had searched for 15 years for Fukuda, who was suspected of
strangling a 31-year-old colleague to steal her cash, furniture and
clothing. When traditional police work turned up nothing, they turned
to a practice common in other countries but unheard of here: They
offered a reward. So did a Tokyo hospital where Fukuda had undergone
plastic surgery. The president of Jujin hospital, Fumihiko Umezawa,
said in an interview that he was upset he had unwittingly helped a
fugitive and he offered a $34,000 reward on top of the $8,500 offered
by police.
<p>
   ``Anyone who did a bad thing should have to pay for it,'' Umezawa
said, showing pictures of Fukuda before and after her surgery. ``I
don't like that after a certain period of time you can get away with
it.''
<p>
   The police, too, were unhappy about the statute of limitations. For
detectives working on the case, ``15 years flies by like an arrow,''
said Kimiaki Hiraoka, spokesman for the police in Ehime Prefecture in
southern Japan, where the crime was committed.
<p>
   Of 5,000 tips police received about Fukuda over the past 15 years,
4,000 came this year  &MD;  after the reward offer. Two people who led
police to Fukuda already have come forward to claim their money. &QL; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-largent </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1331 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-largent
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Lois Romano  </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; A Serious GOP Player, Largent Isn't Always on Team &QL; 
 &UR; (Broken Arrow) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   BROKEN ARROW, Okla.  &MD;  Steve Largent's constituents in this small
suburb of Tulsa are spitting bullets. But not about the fierce battle
for the soul of the Republican Party that consumed Largent in July.
This sticky August morning, the subject is mailboxes.
<p>
   The U.S. Postal Service has installed curbside boxes at the homes
of some senior citizens and they're demanding their porch service
back. The community library is packed and virtually everyone is
venting at the former football star-turned-Republican congressman.
Newt Gingrich is never mentioned.
<p>
   ``Now congressman, we hired you to solve our problems,'' pleads
Virginia Johnson. ``And we have a big problem. . . . We expect
something from you.''
<p>
   ``Well, that's a lot of pressure,'' Largent responds with a wide
smile, breaking the tension. ``I'm not going to promise results, but I
will promise 100 percent effort. Just let me get up to speed on this
and I will get answers for you.''
<p>
   Mastering the game has always been one part instinct, three parts
tireless effort for Steve Largent. A former wide receiver for the
Seattle Seahawks, he was considered too slight and too slow to succeed
in the National Football League. Friends from his football days recall
Largent spending hours studying the opposition's playbooks. He became
one of the game's greatest receiverse, a Hall of Famer who held six
NFL records when he retired from football in 1989.
<p>
   He has been just as full of surprises in his fledgling political
career.
<p>
   When he arrived in the House as a political novice in 1994, he was
privately touted by Gingrich as the future of the Republican Party, a
superstar with name recognition who could generate support for the
party agenda. He was given a coveted seat on the Budget Committee. To
Democrats, Largent was merely a celebrity right-wing beneficiary of
the 1994 GOP landslide.
<p>
   He has proven both sides wrong. In his first term, Largent was one
of handful of Republicans who embarrassed Gingrich, R-Ga., by voting
against the speaker on budget matters. This summer, he played a
leading role in the botched attempt to overthrow Gingrich. And as
Congress returns to Washington this week, Largent shows no signs of
letting up.
<p>
   ``This was not about policy issues  &MD;  it was about integrity and
leadership,'' he said the other day as he cruised from breakfast
meeting to factory tour in his district. ``We've been portrayed as the
radical right who believed we were not getting what we wanted, and
that was never the case. This is about being told that certain things
were so and they weren't.''
<p>
   Largent said when the dust settled and Gingrich survived, he asked
the speaker what he thought of him. ``He said he didn't think I was a
team player. Which I thought was a curious statement for him to make
about me. I just didn't want to play on the team he had built.''
<p>
   Largent is viewed as a serious player within the Republican Party,
someone whose fierce independence and earnest articulation of
conservative values have made him a threat to the leadership of his
party, and caused the Democrats to take notice. Largent, who turns 43
this month, also happens to be strikingly telegenic. (He was a
featured page on a gag ``Hunks of the House Calendar'' members
presented to Republican Rep. Susan Molinari of New York upon her
resignation.)
<p>
   Republicans say he has significant marquee value on the
fund-raising circuit, and willingly criss-crossed the country last
year for colleagues in more than 50 districts. It seems reasonable to
assume he could do that for himself one day.
<p>
   Many Democrats believe Largent's hard right ideology on social
issues  &MD;  he's antiabortion, anti-entitlements, and believes
homosexuality is a ``choice''  &MD;  will preclude him from generating
broad-based national support. But many also regard him as
hard-working, prepared and fair-minded. ``I don't believe we agree on
any of those (social) issues, but the ability to forge alliances
matters and he understands that,'' said Rep. Jane Harman, D-Calif.
``He's much more than an aging jock.''
<p>
   So, where is Largent heading? The lawmaker wouldn't take the bait
one recent rainy morning in Tulsa.
<p>
   ``I tell people that I think I'm the freest member of Congress
because I'm not wedded to this job,'' he said. ``There's no hidden
agenda. . . . I feel that I can vote my conscience and let the chips
fall where they may.''
<p>
   During a half-dozen recent constituent events, all filled to
capacity, Largent received one question on Gingrich, but dozens on
Social Security (he favors privatization of the system) and taxes
(he's against them.) At virtually every event, at least one person
stood up and profusely thanked him for representing the district,
which encompasses most of Tulsa, a corporate city in the heart of the
Southwest's Bible belt, and home to Oral Roberts's ministry.
<p>
   ``The seat is his as long as he wants it,'' said one prominent
local Democrat.
<p>
   A Christian conservative, Largent was initially elected to fill the
House seat vacated by Republican Sen. James M. Inhofe. ``I didn't even
know what GOP stood for until after I was elected,'' he said. He
obviously got the hang of it: He received 68 percent of the vote in
1996 against token Democratic opposition.
<p>
   Largent was raised poor in a middle-class suburb of Oklahoma City.
His father abandoned the family when Largent was a boy, and his mother
remarried an abusive alcoholic.
<p>
   Largent said he embraced Christianity as a teenager to survive.
``You know my dad taking off created a vacuum, an insecurity,'' he
explained. ``That void was filled through the development of my walk
with Christ.''
<p>
   Largent's mother, Sue, tried to attend all his high school football
and baseball games, while juggling menial jobs to make ends meet for
her four children. In high school, he met his future wife, Terry,
whose family offered Largent a stable home life. They married 22 years
ago while Largent was attending the University of Tulsa on a football
scholarship; they have four children, 11 to 18, one of whom was born
with spina bifida.
<p>
   What makes Largent a potentially formidable opponent, say Democrats
and Republicans alike, is his ability to advance the right's social
policies without sounding strident. ``He conveys credibility and
conviction . . . and he's a workhorse,'' said Rep. Bill Paxon, N.Y., a
casualty of the recent GOP House wars. ``He is not lusting after
position. He leads because of what he believes.''
<p>
   When a group of 11 angry Republicans derailed a spending bill
earlier this year, Gingrich yanked all Republicans into a closed-door
meeting. He then demanded that the 11 explain themselves. Largent
jumped up first. ``You can't intimidate me,'' he told Gingrich. ``I've
had linebackers who wanted to kill me.''
<p>
   But allies and opponents alike agree that these uncompromising
principles could also be his undoing.
<p>
   Largent says he understands the process requires him to accept less
than he wants as long as ``you keep trying to move the ball up the
court.''
<p>
   ``But this idea that you better not be too vocal about the abortion
issue, or you don't want to talk about entitlement reform, and you
sure don't want to talk about Social Security reform  &MD;  I don't buy
that because it's wrong,'' he said. ``And the pro-life issue is the
issue of my heart. It's who I am.''
<p>
   Largent has said publicly that he won't stay in the House longer
than 12 years. He has not moved his family to Washington, and instead
shares a house with Rep. Zach Wamp, R-Tenn., and Rep. Tom Coburn,
R-Okla.
<p>
   He insists he has given little thought to running for a leadership
post or higher office, but adds: ``I don't rule anything out. . . .
I'm confident that I'm where God wants me to be right now. But I don't
know about tomorrow or next year.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-solar </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1158 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-solar
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Science, News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Curt Suplee </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Solar Probe Offers New, Closer Look at Everything Under the Sun &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   You might think that the weather forecast for the surface of the
sun would be a pretty dreary affair.  &UR; Hot and dry. High 10,000
Fahrenheit.
 &UR; Outlook: continued sunny with intermittent spots and flares for the
next 4
 &UR; billion years.
<p>
   But recently scientists have learned that our local star is beset
by its own kind of incendiary tempests, seasonal squalls and rogue
currents of fire. And now new instruments that can peer beneath the
outer luminous layers have revealed an utterly unexpected
meteorological feature in the form of titanic ``jet streams'' of
electrically charged gases that circle the poles, churning the
atmosphere where it was thought to be most calm.
<p>
   They also detected several mysterious ``trade wind'' bands of
high-speed plasma 40,000 miles wide that ring the solar perimeter like
the belts on Jupiter, as well as a global pattern of upper-level
current flow north and south from the equator. Many of these effects,
said Jesper Schou of Stanford University, exhibit ``motion similar to
weather patterns in the Earth's atmosphere.''
<p>
   The findings, reported last week by Schou, Craig DeForest and other
Stanford researchers using the Solar and Heliospheric Observatory
spacecraft, are ``absolutely fascinating,'' said Juri Toomre, a
University of Colorado astrophysicist not involved in the
announcement. They ``herald a new era of solar meteorology,'' said
Douglas Gough of Cambridge University in England.
<p>
   Researchers are hoping that the SOHO data will help explain such
persistent enigmas as the origin and life cycle of sunspots, which
influence the torrents of solar emissions that blast toward Earth,
deranging radio communications.
<p>
   ``One of our ultimate goals,'' said John W. Harvey of the
ground-based National Solar Observatory in Tucson, ``is to learn
enough about the sun to predict activity'' accurately enough to warn
Earthlings days in advance that a solar storm is on the way. ``Any
sort of insight,'' DeForest said, ``could give us more lead time.''
<p>
   But in the short term, most solar scientists will settle for
anything that makes clearer the nature of our familiar, but
often-inscrutable, star.
<p>
   The sun, 98 percent of which is hydrogen and helium, is a perfectly
ordinary middle-aged representative of the 100 billion stars in our
galaxy. It can thus serve as ``a Rosetta stone'' for understanding
others large and small, said George Withbroe of NASA's solar-research
program.
<p>
   But first scientists need to understand its baffling quirks.
<p>
   For one, it swells and contracts at five-minute intervals, driven
by sound waves that bounce around its innards. For another, it rotates
at different speeds at different latitudes: At the equator, one
revolution takes about 25 days; at the poles, about 33. The difference
in speed between adjacent regions causes a sliding or shear force on
the intervening gas. (Imagine two boats cruising side by side. If one
is going 10 mph and the other 15, the water between them will be
jerked into turbulence.)
<p>
   Within that global pattern, the Stanford team observed half a dozen
horizontal bands in both hemispheres of the sun that are traveling
faster than adjoining areas. Solar surface activity is suspiciously
high alongside them.
<p>
   That makes sense: At the edges of those belts, where they rub
against slower-moving gas, there should be considerable shear stress.
That presumably causes kinks to form in the plasma-gas that has been
heated so much by fusion at the solar core that electrons have
detached themselves from their atoms, creating a seething incandescent
soup of negative electrons and positive nuclei.
<p>
   The plasma rises and falls in the same patterns you see in a pot of
boiling water. Hotter, less-dense material rises until it reaches the
surface, then cools and descends. This occurs in the outermost 30
percent of the sun, which is called the convection zone. Its top
surface, the photosphere, generates the sunlight we see daily.
<p>
   Because both the electrons and nuclei are electrically charged, any
motion in the plasma will produce a magnetic field, just as a moving
electric current in a wire does. When magnetic fields are induced by
the rope-like kinks, they sometimes form weird loops that protrude all
the way up through the outer surface. The magnetic field retards the
upward convective flow of hot plasma, and so the photosphere looks
darker where the fields poke through. These dark regions  &MD;  typically
twice the Earth's diameter  &MD;  are sunspots.
<p>
   Sunspots follow a strange but highly predictable pattern. Every 11
years, new batches start to form at high solar latitudes in both the
northern and southern hemispheres, and gradually move toward the
equator. Each spot in a cycle has the opposite magnetic orientation of
the spots in the previous cycle, probably because the sun itself
reverses its magnetic poles every 11 years: North becomes south and
vice versa.
<p>
   To better understand those and other solar idiosyncrasies, NASA and
the European Space Agency launched SOHO in December 1995. The two-ton,
12-by-12-foot spacecraft, positioned 930,000 miles sunward from Earth,
carries many instruments, some of which detect regional speed
differences in sound-wave pulses that propagate through the solar
gases. Such regions reveal their locations and relative velocities via
the Doppler effect: Waves moving toward an observer appear closer
together (that is, seem to have higher frequency and shorter
wavelength); those moving away appear just the opposite.
<p>
   That's how the Stanford group found the polar jet stream (invisible
on the surface) and the fast-track horizontal bands. The bands'
existence had been postulated in the 1980s, but the new data show they
extend at least 12,000 miles deep.
<p>
   Scientists also had long suspected that there was a general flow in
both hemispheres from the equator to the poles. SOHO confirmed that
the upper layer of the solar surface (down to a depth of at least
15,000 miles) drifts poleward at 50 mph. At that speed, it takes about
a year to make the trip. Apparently when it reaches the higher
latitudes, it descends and ``we assume that the flows will return at
some depth,'' Schou said, ``though we haven't observed that yet.''
<p>
   That means surface currents are heading toward the poles at the
same time that sunspot clusters are moving toward the equator.
According to present theory, those seemingly contradictory events are
possible because the big magnetic kinks that cause sunspots are
thought to originate at the bottommost boundary of the convection
layer, where currents are probably flowing toward the equator.
<p>
   ``Do we really understand'' all the patterns, Gough said at a NASA
briefing last week. ``The answer is no. But we will. That is the faith
of the scientist.''
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-clintons-social </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0775 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-clintons-social
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Peter Baker </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Clintons' Social Life Ripens (Martha's Vineyard) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MARTHA'S VINEYARD, Mass.  &MD;  The nightclub was hopping. The musicians
were playing a Patsy Cline number. And just off stage, just behind the
curtain, President Clinton stood and swayed gently, one arm draped
affectionately around singer Carly Simon and the other around her
daughter, Sally Taylor.
<p>
   It was just another night on the Vineyard vacation trail for
Clinton, another night soaking up the company of the famous, the
fetching and the fabulously wealthy. As the president schmoozed with
Simon, milling around in the crowd were television mogul Barry Diller,
author-humorist Fran Lebowitz and Miramax Films co-chairman Harvey
Weinstein.
<p>
   By the fifth year of his presidency, this has become something of a
familiar scene, the hobnobbing commander in chief clearly entranced
with the glitz and glamour of a social strata that must have seemed
beyond him during his coming-of-age days in Arkansas. But after two
years of trying to keep such images under wraps for fear of a
political backlash, Clinton has enjoyed something of a postelection
coming-out this summer, openly and unabashedly spending virtually
every evening partying with the likes of Ted Danson, Sylvester
Stallone and Norman Lear, then playing golf with tycoons, investment
bankers and executives from corporations such as General Electric and
Sony.
<p>
   Many of them are people he has known a relatively short time, such
as Richard Friedman, the Boston developer whose island home Clinton
first borrowed three years ago, and again this year. Called ``the
usual cast'' by a White House aide, many in the group travel in
different circles and don't socialize with each other apart from the
Clintons. Few of the Friends of Bill  &MD;  friends who go back as far as
college or Arkansas politics and with whom he networked throughout his
career  &MD;  are in evidence.
<p>
   To some extent, every president socializes with the rich and
famous. But few of Clinton's recent predecessors have made it such a
dominant element of their vacations.
<p>
   Clinton chose a place that, while long a bastion of quiet New
England money, has in recent years become a haven for newly minted
millionaires and baby boomers at the top of their game. Much like
himself.
<p>
   ``Maybe he has more of a need, not being from that crowd,'' George
Edwards, director of the Center for Presidential Studies in Texas,
said of Clinton. ``In Arkansas, being smart and rich is a little
different from the rest of the country. . . . Maybe he's making up for
lost time.''
<p>
   Clinton, a voracious reader and equally avid film buff, can command
the attention of those who write the books and make the movies, and
some who know him say it's the intellectual stimulation he relishes.
<p>
   ``That's fun for them,'' said Carolyn Staley, a Clinton friend
since childhood. She said she sees nothing pompous or posturing about
the Clintons' interest in celebrities. Instead, she said, ``It's like,
`Wow!' They sit back and they are fans (mingling) with people who also
look back at them and say, `Wow!' ''
<p>
   One day he's sailing with the Kennedys and dining at the old
Jacqueline Onassis estate. The next he's whiling away the hours at
author William Styron's house. Then it's off to the country club for a
soiree including Walter Cronkite, Katharine Graham, Diane Sawyer and
her husband Mike Nichols, the director whose latest project is the
movie version of ``Primary Colors,'' the barely fictionalized account
of Clinton's 1992 campaign.
<p>
   Simon, Jimmy Buffett and Carole King have crooned for him.
Weinstein flew Stallone in to perform a scene from ``Copland,'' along
with Clinton pal Vernon E. Jordan Jr.
<p>
   ``I think he really is almost captivated by the Hollywood set,''
said one prominent Vineyarder who has partied with Clinton during this
three-week vacation and did not want to be named.
<p>
   Clinton came to this resort island 12 miles off the Massachusetts
coast for his first two summer vacations as president, before
political adviser Dick Morris convinced him ``photos of him on a yacht
with Jacqueline Onassis did not help his populist image one bit,'' as
the consultant put it in his book, ``Behind the Oval Office.''
<p>
   After polling by Morris, Clinton spent the Augusts of 1995 and 1996
in Jackson Hole, Wyo. Morris failed, however, to prevent the Clintons
from attending the Vineyard wedding of Danson and actress Mary
Steenburgen, an old Arkansas friend. ``There was no way to talk
Hillary out of it,'' he wrote. ``I tried and got burned in the
process.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-fashion-robinson </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0706 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-fashion-robinson
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Fashion editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Robin Givhan </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Patrick Robinson's 1st Collection: No Gimmicks &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Designer Patrick Robinson has come to savor the sweet succinctness
of the phrase ``fix it.'' Those two little words can quickly end
unruly debates about production glitches, cut through red tape about
fabric mishaps. The phrase is like aspirin for the owner of a fashion
company that is not yet one year old. It makes the headaches go away.
<p>
   Robinson, who will celebrate his 31st birthday on Sept. 8, already
is a veteran of the garment business. He has lived in the world's
three fashion capitals, worked for a Wunderkind designer, a powerhouse
fashion house and a struggling one. And now he is heading up his first
signature collection.
<p>
   His collection for fall '97, his first, has no high-concept theme.
It is not  &UR; about &LR;  spiritualism, athleticism, power or anything else.
He simply has created beautiful garments in brushed alpaca, nubuck,
python and pony. His blazers, which fall to thigh length, have a bit
of spandex in them for added comfort. The clothes are simply tailored
without tricks or gimmicks. And while they are expensive  &MD;  $800 for a
jacket  &MD;  shoppers at least are assured that they are not investing in
a fly-by-night trend. The clothes have shelf life and relevance.
<p>
   Robinson's enterprise is self-financed at a time when large
companies dominate the business. He struck a deal with three Italian
garment manufacturers. In return for a break on production costs, he
has promised to remain loyal to their factories as his business grows.
It is a partnership based on optimism, business sense and his strong
resume.
<p>
   ``I really enjoy the business part. I enjoy it as much as I enjoy
the designing,'' he says. ``Everyone keeps saying that I understand
the first rule of business: Keep the overhead down.''
<p>
   So Robinson is working and living out of a single downtown New York
loft. It is not some dark, musty old space shared by struggling
artists. It is a beautiful environment full of warm, polished wood and
flooded with sunlight.
<p>
   Robinson was born in Memphis and grew up in Orange County, Calif.
He graduated from Parsons School of Design and then headed to Paris to
work as designer Patrick Kelly's first assistant. ``He was a pretty
amazing person,'' Robinson says. ``After talking with him for a few
minutes, you'd be willing to sweep the floor for him.''
<p>
   In the '90s, Robinson was responsible for many of the Giorgio
Armani power suits that female big shots have relied upon when
dealmaking and strong-arming. He designed Armani's Collezione
womenswear line  &MD;  or the ``white label,'' as it is casually called.
``I think at Armani, it was such a well-defined place to be. I was
successful there because I was able to capture what he wanted.''
<p>
   And then about two years ago, he was named head designer for the
Anne Klein Collection. He was brought in after Richard Tyler had tried
and failed to turn the struggling line around. Robinson designed three
collections for the house but only two made it into the stores before
the plug was pulled.
<p>
   ``At Anne Klein, I may have enjoyed it more if I had expressed more
of myself,'' Robinson says. ``It was a tough ship to try and lead back
to the right path.''
<p>
   For all of the stress at Anne Klein and the experience gained with
Armani and Kelly, Robinson emerged with a strong stomach for the
vagaries and demands of the fashion industry.
<p>
   ``I have no fear about business and production and all of that,''
he says. Working in so many cities ``made me more international. It
made me look at everything I do as not being a product made in one
country.''
<p>
   The task before him now is to give his clothes an identity in the
minds of consumers.
<p>
   ``If you can say that Ralph Lauren is about an old English
lifestyle, Calvin (Klein) is sexy, Donna (Karan), when she's really
on, is power wear; Patrick Robinson should be something about
adventure, something with lots of cultures mixed in. I'm looking not
only to America for inspiration, but the world.''
<p>
   ``I'm a black man and I love being that,'' he says. ``I love being
different than other people. That's part of it, too.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-japan-rocket </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0683 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-japan-rocket
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mary Jordan and Kevin Sullivan </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Chicken Being Scared to Death Halts Rocket Test (Tokyo) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   TOKYO  &MD;  The moon is the goal of a $250 million space project Japan
announced last week, but right now the biggest challenge to the
nation's space agency might be 500 dead chickens.
<p>
   Last May, when the space agency was conducting safety tests
involving its new rockets, a blast using 110 pounds of explosives
startled 500 chickens on the northern island of Hokkaido, apparently
causing them to stampede and crush themselves against a hen-house
wall.
<p>
   After months of investigation, an apologetic National Space
Development Agency told local chicken farmers and horse breeders it
will move the tests from the village of Tomakomai to another site,
possibly overseas, despite the effort and expense involved.
<p>
   ``Before conducting the tests, we couldn't imagine that this would
hurt chickens,'' agency spokesman Takashi Endo said. ``This is a very
serious problem for us. . . . We have made trouble for the people in
the chicken business.''
<p>
   In the United States, suspending multimillion-dollar space
exploration experiments over fatally panicked chickens would probably
be fodder for late night television monologues. In Japan, it's no
laughing matter. The chickens-and-space-rockets incident illustrates
both the problems of a crowded country and the lengths the Japanese
government takes to avoid being perceived as a bully.
<p>
   Japan has 126 million people living in a nation the size of
Montana. On average, that means 850 people live in every square mile
here, compared to 70 people per square mile in the United States.
<p>
   ``Americans have all this space. They have Cape Canaveral. They
probably can't understand how this problem with the chickens could
happen,'' said Mitsuru Yaku, who frequently offers humorous commentary
on television. ``They probably think: Just give the chickens earplugs
 &MD;  it's a lot cheaper.''
<p>
   Noise is a major complaint of Japanese neighbors because people
live so close together. And since there are no huge expanses of land,
like the American West, where military or space projects can be
tested, to Japanese residents, complaining about loud rocket tests is
like ``complaining about piano noise from next door,'' Yaku said.
<p>
   On May 16, Japan's space agency was conducting preliminary safety
tests involving the new H-IIA rocket. That rocket is to be part of the
Japan's plan to put Selene, an unmanned explorer, on the moon in 2004.
Selene, named after the ancient Greek goddess of the moon, is to orbit
the moon for a year before landing on it, and Japanese scientists hope
it will capture radio signals from Jupiter and Saturn.
<p>
   Endo said there is still plenty of time to find alternative sites
for the rocket safety tests so Selene can be launched on time. But, he
added, ``We don't know how much (moving the tests) will cost, because
we don't know where they will be.''
<p>
   Yoshimasa Sato, a resident of the village of Atsuma, near the May
rocket blasts, said he, like many neighbors, ``sympathized with the
chickens.'' He said he is sorry the business and investment that
accompanied the space agency will leave. And, he said, he understands
that the sparsely populated area on the northern island of Hokkaido is
``one of the last strongholds'' for the space agency. But he said,
``Horses won't have to worry now about being scared off.''
<p>
   Images of forced government land-takings raise uneasy feelings in a
country where unchecked government power led to a disastrous war
earlier this century.
<p>
   There are more than a half-million chickens within a few miles of
the test site. But when the space agency learned 500 in one hen house
had been ``shocked to death,'' as one official said, it organized the
investigating task force.
<p>
   ``We can't be positive about why the hens died, but we can assume
that the tests had an effect,'' Endo said. ``Under these
circumstances, we cannot continue our tests. We had promised people
there would be no problems.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>

<HEADER> - a1457 09-02 0525 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-mir - a1457
 &UR; (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Carol J. Williams </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Russian Space Officials Blame Cosmonauts for Mir Accident (Moscow) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MOSCOW  &MD;  Russian Space Agency officials Tuesday cast blame on two
weary cosmonauts for a disastrous June 25 accident on the Mir space
station and hinted they will be fined for mistakes that cost the
Russian space program millions of dollars.
<p>
   A report by the special inquiry commission set up only two weeks
ago said investigation had determined ``beyond any doubt'' that former
Mir commander Vasily Tsibliyev and flight engineer Alexander Lazutkin
were culpable in the space crash that disabled the station's Spektr
research module and ruined months of experiments.
<p>
   Tsibliyev and Lazutkin remain cloistered at the Star City cosmonaut
base east of Moscow for rehabilitation after six problem-plagued
months in space. But a senior space official confirmed that the two
cosmonauts say they are being made culprits for a deteriorating
manned-space program that reflects this country's general decline.
<p>
   ``From the purely humane point of view, we feel sorry for the crew,
but there is no getting away from the facts,'' Valery Ryumin, deputy
director of the Energia Corp. and coordinator of Mir-NASA programs,
told the quasi-official Tass news agency.
<p>
   Mission Control Center spokesman Vsevolod Latyshev noted there is
precedent for fining cosmonauts deemed responsible for costly errors,
as occurred with Gennady Strekalov after his troubled 1995 flight
aboard Mir. Russian space crews work under contract with the
government and are paid on a per diem basis, with bonuses of as much
as $1,000 for particularly difficult maneuvers like dockings and
spacewalks.
<p>
   While officials declined to specify what punishment might be meted
out to the cosmonauts, one official at Mission Control observed that
``Tsibliyev and Lazutkin may well have made their last trip into
space.''
<p>
   Shortly after the accident, which occurred while Tsibliyev was
practicing a manual docking maneuver with an unmanned cargo craft,
Mission Control sources disclosed suspicions that he had failed to
take into account more than a ton of garbage that had been loaded onto
the cargo drone.
<p>
   Apparently as a result of the weight miscalculation, the supply
capsule approached the docking port with excessive speed and careened
into the hull of the Spektr module. The collision punctured and
depressurized Spektr and knocked out nearly half the space station's
power-generating capacity.
<p>
   Tsibliyev and Lazutkin returned to Earth Aug. 14 and were
immediately confronted with media accusations they had bungled their
mission.
<p>
   ``It's always easy to find a scapegoat,'' Tsibliyev told
journalists at a news conference two days later. ``But tomorrow a
similar problem may occur with another cargo capsule.''
<p>
   Meanwhile, the current crewmen aboard Mir have been practicing for
a spacewalk set for Saturday to find and possibly repair damage to
Spektr. Commander Anatoly Solovev and U.S. astronaut Michael Foale are
expected to don spacesuits and work outside the station for about five
hours.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-alcohol </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0564 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-alcohol
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Rick Weiss </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Experts Say Factors Made Diana Crash Inevitable (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  The blood alcohol concentration present in Princess
Diana's chauffeur increased his risk of being in a fatal, single
vehicle crash 300 to 600 times above normal, according to research on
the effects of alcohol on driving safety.
<p>
   Add to that the risk that comes with driving at high velocity  &MD;  the
vehicle is reported to have been hurtling at 120 mph, or the length of
two football fields every three seconds  &MD;  and of driving in darkness,
and it is almost inevitable that the car and its occupants would meet
a tragic end, experts said.
<p>
   ``A driver at that alcohol level and in those circumstances has
atrocious hand-eye coordination, delayed reaction responses, poor
decision-making, decreased vision and hearing,'' said Matthew Robb,
director of clinical services at Grace Clinic, which administers
Washington, D.C.'s educational program for people who have been
arrested for drinking while intoxicated.
<p>
   ``At 120 miles per hour, you need superhuman driving skills even in
the best of circumstances,'' Robb said. ``And this guy was not in the
best of circumstances.''
<p>
   Driving a car is a far more complex task than many everyday drivers
appreciate, with constantly changing demands that require quick and
often subtle responses. According to experts at the National Institute
on Alcohol Abuse and Alcoholism, those skills can be divided into two
categories: cognitive skills such as information processing, and
psychomotor skills, which involve eye-brain-hand coordination. Both
suffer badly under the influence of alcohol.
<p>
   Princess Diana's chauffeur had undoubtedly lost significant control
over his voluntary eye movements long before his blood alcohol
concentration (BAC) reached the level of about 0.18 grams per
deciliter detected at autopsy, according to NIAAA research. At BAC
levels as low as 0.03, or about one-sixth those found in the
chauffeur, the ability to focus briefly on an object as it passes by  &MD; 
such as a concrete pillar  &MD;  and then refocus attention on the next,
called ``tracking,'' is seriously compromised. The driver's ability to
steer precisely was also certainly undermined; studies have shown
steering errors at blood alcohol levels as low as 0.035.
<p>
   At levels of 0.04, less than one-fourth those found in Diana's
driver, the attentional field begins to narrow. Lacking the bigger
perspective, drivers are more likely to be surprised by an approaching
object, and to overcompensate by pulling the steering wheel too hard.
<p>
   The chauffeur had two things going for him that might have helped
in less extreme circumstances: He was an experienced driver and a man.
A 1989 study by the Arlington, Va.-based Insurance Institute for
Highway Safety found that younger drivers and female drivers have a
higher chance of being involved in fatal single vehicle accidents
compared to more experienced drivers and male drivers with the same
elevated alcohol levels.
<p>
   But at the extremely high alcohol levels found in the chauffeur, no
amount of experience could have helped, Robb said. ``Judgment goes, so
instead of calling off reckless activity you are much more prone to
get swept up in the emotions of the moment,'' he said. ``Years of
training go by the wayside.''
</TEXT>

AP-NY-09-02-97 1808EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-argentina-beef </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0718 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-argentina-beef
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: International, Financial editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Anthony Faiola </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Argentine Beef Returns to U.S. After Long Ban &QL; 
 &UR; (Buenos Aires) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   BUENOS AIRES  &MD;  The cut of beef on the table in front of the
Argentine businessman is big enough to choke a Texan, but other than
size, nothing seems special about this particular piece of red meat.
At that suggestion, however, the incredulous Argentine beef exporter
turns livid.
<p>
   He flashes the universal look that says: Pathetic American fools .
``Americans will discover the difference in taste,'' said Patricio J.
Moche, manager of an Argentine beef exporting firm. ``Unless they are
satisfied eating flavorless beef that must be covered in sauces to
swallow it.''
<p>
   Put that on your plate and eat it. In fact, that is exactly what
Argentines are counting on, as the first new shipments of their fresh
beef arrived in Washington, D.C., last week, marking the return of
this country's legendary national staple after a 70-year U.S. ban.
<p>
   Argentine beef, with a far more pungent taste than its American
cousin, is suitable for export again now that ranchers here have
eradicated bovine hoof and mouth disease from their herds under an
intensive, five-year vaccination plan. Although not proven to cause
sickness in humans, the threat the disease posed to overseas herds was
great enough to lead many nations, including the United States, to
prohibit importation of Argentine beef.
<p>
   The mystique of the local steak has been a source of national pride
since the gauchos  &MD;  South American cowboys  &MD;  began corralling feral
cattle on the vast Argentine pampas. The return of Argentine beef to
the United States is being viewed as a major step toward recapturing
the glory days of the nation's beef industry.
<p>
   In hundreds of local parrillas, or steakhouses, peppering Buenos
Aires, diners are often greeted at entrances by full-size stuffed
cattle as slabs of meat roast slowly atop massive iron grills. Until
the early morning hours, customers consume impossible amounts of beef
piled high on sizzling metal plates, often for about half of what a
similar chunk would cost in the United States.
<p>
   Indeed, historically, one of the problems with Argentine beef
exports has been that the local population eats so much, leaving
little for foreigners. Although domestic consumption has decreased
over the past seven years, the Argentines eat about 70 percent of all
the beef this country can produce each year, according the Argentine
Rural Society.
<p>
   Only 20,000 tons of Argentine beef will be shipped to the United
States this year, equivalent to about half a day's production on U.S.
ranches.
<p>
   Over the next three years, however, Argentina hopes to increase
that to 200,000 tons  &MD;  which would boost their worldwide beef exports
by almost 50 percent. More importantly, Argentine agricultural
officials say, if Argentine beef is accepted again in the land of the
cowboy, it would provide a catalyst for increased international demand
 &MD;  especially in burgeoning markets of Southeast Asia.
<p>
   ``There is no reason why Argentine beef cannot conquer the United
States,'' said Argentine Ambassador Diego Guelar.
<p>
   But, experts say, that will not be easy. Argentine beef has a
distinctly different taste than Americans are used to, because cattle
here have completely different ``lifestyles.'' Argentine cattle are
free-range animals, eating grass instead of grain as is common in the
United States. This gives a stronger, almost gamy taste to the meat.
Also, as a result of grazing conditions, Argentine cattle do not
produce beef with as much internal marbling, the lacing of fat that
makes high-quality American steaks so tender. That means Argentine
steaks tend to be less tender, but also leaner and thus lower in
cholesterol, beef experts say.
<p>
   Argentine cattle also are not bulked up with growth hormones, and
export companies here intend to use their ``more natural'' product as
a selling point with U.S. consumers.
<p>
   The proof will be in the taste, and many experts say the Argentines
will have it tough. ``I don't think steak eaters in the United States
will handle the gamier stuff from Argentina,'' said Marcus Taylor,
general manager and executive chef at Washington's Blackies House of
Beef. &QL; 
</TEXT>

AP-NY-09-02-97 1810EDT &QL;
