<SLUG> bc-federal-diary </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0591 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-federal-diary
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mike Causey </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Defense's Three-Layer Mix (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  When the giant Defense Department gets a cold, most
other agencies quickly get the sniffles. Even when Defense says it is
just thinking about something, other agencies stand at attention.
<p>
   Defense is often the first agency to get approval to try new
things. It led the way in offering a variety of programs to take the
sting out of downsizing. That included the first, massive use of
early-retirement and buyout offers, as well as ``stopper'' lists and
successor programs to hold and find jobs for federal workers displaced
by base closures or budget cuts.
<p>
   The department's leader-of-the-pack status is the reason other
agencies are watching a Defense concept that could lead to a brand-new
personnel system for 790,000 civilian employees of the Army, Navy and
Air Force.
<p>
   Defense officials say yesterday's column made it appear that the
plan is being studied at the highest levels of the Pentagon and might
go to Congress this year. Instead, they say, Friday's meeting is a
``working group'' of various Defense agency personnel directors and
the like. The plan has many hurdles to clear and isn't on a fast
track, officials say.
<p>
   Be that as it may, remember that Defense is very good at doing its
homework and presenting Congress with legislative proposals that fly
more often than they crash and burn.
<p>
   The proposal before Friday's working group is the response to a
request in March for the Quadrennial Defense Review to ``prepare a
legislative package placing the Department's civilian personnel system
under separate legislative authority'' from the regular civil service
system.
<p>
   As part of that proposal, Defense would  &MD;  if the plan is cleared by
the Pentagon and then approved by Congress and the White House  &MD;  set
up a three-tier personnel system.
<p>
   The first tier would be a core group of current civilian employees.
They would stay in their retirement plan (either the Civil Service
Retirement System or the Federal Employees Retirement System). They
would be less likely to be hit by future layoffs.
<p>
   The second tier would be ``non-permanent employees hired for up to
five years, immediately eligible for health, life and thrift savings
plan coverage but with no immediate government contributions.''
Workers in that group, the working group's memo says, ``would be used
to respond to surge, cyclical or otherwise, could be converted to
permanent status noncompetitively, and therefore could be used as a
``recruitment pool' for permanent hires.'' They could also be let go
at the end of their contract period to avoid reductions in force of
the permanent work force. Although unions would be consulted on the
proposal, an official of the National Association of Government
Employees says it ``sounds like a government effort to set up the
equivalent of a giant work force of migrant labors who could be used,
then dropped, when no longer needed.''
<p>
   The third tier would not contain any federal employees. It would be
made up of ``contractor employees performing work determined to be
non-governmental in nature and more cost effective to outsource to the
private sector.''
<p>
   Defense would also get authority, under the plan, to set up its own
pay and personnel system, although it would retain certain things such
as labor-management agreements, veterans preference, affirmative
action and the right of former employees to unemployment benefits.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-genes </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1562 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-genes
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Rick Weiss </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Gene Finding Stirs Anxieties for Jews &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Bert Vogelstein wasn't looking for a ``Jewish'' cancer gene when he
scanned through a database at the Johns Hopkins Medical Center last
year and noticed something coincidental about two blood samples stored
there.
<p>
   In the end, however, that's what he found: A tiny genetic
alteration, present in one of every six Jews of Eastern European
ancestry and virtually absent in non-Jews, which doubles the odds of
getting colon cancer.
<p>
   The discovery, announced last week, promises a newfound ability to
identify those at risk of the nation's third deadliest cancer and to
offer them better preventive care. But the finding also stirred deep
anxieties within the Jewish community, which has watched in growing
discomfort during the past few years as research has focused on
genetic alterations especially common in Jews, including those that
cause breast and ovarian cancer, cystic fibrosis, Tay Sachs, Gaucher's
and Canavan disease.
<p>
   The news led many Jews to ask whether they really have more genetic
diseases than other groups and if so, why. Some said they are worried
that reports of such findings may have a negative impact on the image
of Jews among non-Jews.
<p>
   ``We've been receiving phone calls indicating a certain amount of
fear and confusion,'' said Amy Rutkin, director of American Affairs
for Hadassah, the nation's largest Jewish membership organization.
``People are asking, 'Is too much research focusing on the Jewish
community and are we at risk of stigmatization?'''
<p>
   It has been only a few decades, several Jewish leaders noted, since
millions perished for their purported genetic inferiority. Could new
research lead to a reawakening of those racist views?
<p>
   ``We are still living with some history, a history of
discrimination,'' said Karen Rothenberg, a University of Maryland law
professor who has studied the sociology of Jews and their genes. ``We
all hope that by finding these mutations we can find cures for cancer
that will cure everyone, but so far all we have is a lot of disturbing
stuff about Jewish people and their mutations.''
<p>
   Some experts said they consider it risky to even talk about the
subject of Jews and genes, for fear of reinvigorating antisemitic
ideas. But others in the Jewish community said it's best to discuss
the topic openly, and have faith that an honest discussion will foster
understanding.
<p>
   The essence of that understanding is complicated, both genetically
and spiritually, scientists and Jewish leaders said. But most
geneticists have concluded that any seemingabundance of inherited
mutations among Jews is an artifact born of several unrelated factors,
including the nature of genetic research and the ease with which some
people can be identified as Jewish on the basis of something as simple
as their last name.
<p>
   ``Many Jews might say, `Us again?''' when reading the latest news
about the gene for colon cancer, said Patricia Hartge, a National
Cancer Institute epidemiologist. ``But the truth is, it could have
been the Icelanders, the Finns or anyone else you might be studying.''
<p>
   The problem for Jews, and the great benefit they offer geneticists,
Hartge and others said, is that they constitute a well-defined, easily
identifiable and closely related community  &MD;  exactly the kind of
population that allows geneticists to start identifying
disease-causing genes.
<p>
   Similar links have been found between specific diseases and
similarly isolated and genetically intact populations. The Amish have
a high prevalence of musculoskeletal disease, some Native American
groups are very prone to diabetes, African Americans have sickle cell
anemia, northern Europeans have cystic fibrosis.
<p>
   Francis Collins, chief of the National Human Genome Research
Institute, has repeatedly reminded the public that ``everyone is a
mutant,'' carrying dozens of scrambled genes. If a group were
especially freighted with such errors, he said, members might be
expected to have shorter lifespans as a whole, which Jews do not.
<p>
   But for a variety of reasons  &MD;  including the Jewish community's
early entry into the genetic testing field for prenatal screening of
Tay Sachs disease (a fatal childhood disorder) and a cultural
consensus among many Jews that it is good to contribute to medical
research  &MD;  the number of findings has been notable in that population.
<p>
   No one is more sensitized to the potential for such
misunderstandings than Vogelstein himself, an Ashkenazi geneticist
whose father and father's fathers were all rabbis for 13 generations
back. ``I am aware of the potential for discrimination and
stigmatization,'' he said. ``But I also see a chance for this
information to save lives.''
<p>
   Vogelstein happened onto the colon cancer discovery when a Jewish
acquaintance who had colon cancer asked whether his cancer might be
due to a mutation in a cancer gene known as APC. Tests indicated the
man did not have the classic mutation in that gene, but he did have a
slight genetic ``misspelling'' elsewhere in the gene that Vogelstein
assumed was inconsequential.
<p>
   Later, Vogelstein came upon another case of colon cancer in someone
who had the same alteration. Looking at the name, he realized that
this person, like his friend, was probably Jewish. ``I looked at the
name and said, 'You know, maybe this is not a coincidence,'''
Vogelstein said.
<p>
   The finding of a disease gene specific to Jews should not be
surprising, Vogelstein said. The Ashkenaze population dwindled to very
low numbers about 900 years ago, and the rules of chance suggest that
several of those survivors may have carried unusual genes. Given the
historically low rates of intermarriage between Jews and non-Jews,
those genes would have remained at an unusually high frequency in
descendants today.
<p>
   Many genetically related groups no doubt have similar links to
other diseases, Hartge said, but such links are more difficult to spot
in groups that have splintered and intermarried. Avis Miller,
associate rabbi at Adas Israel congregation in the District, said the
local Jewish population has gained a relativly sophisticated
understanding of genetics, in part because of the educational program
that accompanied a recent study of inherited breast and ovarian cancer
among 5,000 Washington area Jews. ''We understand it's not that we
have so many more diseases than other groups, but that we are a
definable population that can be easily tested,'' Miller said.
<p>
   Still, Miller said, it's not easy for Jews to see stories about
their disease genes popping up in newspapers, magazines, and the
Jewish press (the topic dominates the latest issue of ``Forward,'' a
widely distributed Jewish newspaper). She said she has heard some
people who converted to Judaism say only half-jokingly that their
non-Jewish genes should help the group.
<p>
   Jews' long history of participation in genetic testing has also
contributed to the high number of findings within that group.
Thousands of blood samples are stored at medical centers around the
country, left over from the Tay Sachs tests that most Jews now take
before they have children. These samples have become a convenient
resource for scientists studying various diseases, especially if there
is any hint that the disease may be common in Jews; Vogelstein relied
on hundreds of such specimens  &MD;  some of them decades old  &MD;  to spot the
new colon gene defect. But while the samples are a boon for
scientists, it also means that it is easier to find problems in the
Jewish population than in other populations that do not have such
accessible blood collections. And it's easy for overrepresentation of
a group to become a broader misimpression, experts said.
<p>
   For example, it's still not known whether Jewish women have a
higher rate of inherited breast cancer than other women. But many have
been left with that impression because, of the hundreds of mutations
that have been identified in the so-called breast cancer gene, BRCA1,
the most studied is the one that is common in Jews.
<p>
   Current studies of Icelandic women, many of whom carry a mutation
in another breast cancer gene, BRCA2, may help demonstrate that every
group has its own particular way of getting into the same genetic
trouble.
<p>
   Others said that the particular genetic bottleneck that Jews
squeezed through centuries ago may have left them with a higher
predisposition to inherited cancers while other groups may have higher
predispositions to other diseases. But since cancer is the major focus
of genetic research today, the other groups may get less notice.
<p>
   Rothenberg said she worries as much about the impact of genetic
research on the Jewish ``soul'' as she worries about its impact on
Jewish bodies. ``What will it do to our sense of identity?'' she
asked.
<p>
   There are no simple answers to such questions, but a strong
emphasis on ethics, education and informed consent in genetic research
should help, Rothenberg said. She said current informed consent
documents that must be signed by research study participants spell out
various risks, such as the possibility of losing health insurance or
facing discrimination in the workplace on the basis of genetic
findings. ``But they don't talk about the possibility of group
stigma,'' she said.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-canada-gypsies </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0855 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-canada-gypsies
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Czech Prejudice, TV Fuel Gypsy Migration to Canada &QL; 
 &UR; By Howard Schneider &QC; and Christine Spolar  &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Karolina Bonomova was frightened when she heard that a gang of
Canadian skinheads had gathered outside the Lido Hotel near Toronto,
temporary home to some of the hundreds of Gypsies who have fled the
Czech Republic for Canada in recent months.
<p>
   She said the reminder of her homeland, which she and others left to
avoid beatings and harassment by such neo-Nazi groups, was both eerie
and in conflict with the peaceful image of Canada that has taken root
among the Romany people, as the Gypsies refer to themselves.
<p>
   But what happened next, she said, explains why so many of her
people have left their homes for Toronto. The police arrived within
minutes. They positioned themselves between the Gypsies and the
skinheads and stayed until the possibility of trouble eased. In
Prague, said the former university student, police would have watched
until the skinheads administered their ritual round of kicks and
punches.
<p>
   ``I am very hopeful that the Canadian police don't react the way
the Czech police react,'' Bonomova, 28, said through an interpreter.
The Czech police ``don't react until the skins beat us up. . . . I
hope here that I will find safety, . . . that (people) will understand
our problem so we can start a new life.''
<p>
   Bonomova arrived in Toronto in May with her daughter and other
relatives as part of a surge of Gypsy immigration that has taken
Canadian officials by surprise and embarrassed the Czech Republic's
emerging democracy.
<p>
   About 550 Gypsies have arrived in Canada this year, claiming status
as refugees from racial persecution in the Czech Republic. The numbers
are small, particularly for Canada, which accepts approximately 30,000
refugees among 200,000 annual immigrants.
<p>
   But the Gypsies have filled Toronto homeless shelters to
overflowing. Their arrival has forced Canadian immigration officials
to puzzle through a refugee wave apparently started by word of mouth
but stoked by a Czech television documentary, aired Aug. 7, that
portrayed Canada as a land of spacious housing, plentiful welfare,
jobs for the asking and trips to Niagara Falls.
<p>
   ``All you Gypsies, if you have money, jump on a plane and come
over,'' said a woman identified on the Nova TV show as Margit Bangova,
surrounded by children and speaking Czech. ``There you will be hungry
and pushed aside. And you'll always be judged. . . . Here, you will
live like kings, like the Canadians.''
<p>
   Nova reporter Josef Klima, in an interview in Prague, acknowledged
the documentary erred on the positive side in portraying Canada and
excluded comments from some Gypsies who said their main reason for
emigrating was Canada's reputation for rich social programs.
<p>
   Gypsies have been arriving steadily in Canada since it lifted its
visa requirement for Czech travelers last year. In an interview in
Toronto last week, Slovak-born lawyer George Kubes, who was featured
on the documentary and represents several hundred new arrivals, said
he thinks the migration has just begun.
<p>
   ``The flood has not even started yet,'' Kubes said. ``Whole towns
are eyeing this situation.''
<p>
   Concerned that he might be right, Canada took the unusual step of
sending a delegate from its embassy in Prague to conduct television
interviews and speak with Gypsy groups. The envoy was not spreading
the gospel that Canada heads the 1997 U.N. list of national economic
and social development. The mission, rather, was to remind potential
immigrants that it's hard to find a job if you don't speak English or
French, housing is expensive and hard to acquire and no one greets you
at the airport with a welfare check.
<p>
   ``Nobody is waiting for them with open arms. Nobody is giving them
money. There are no jobs waiting for them. That's the message we're
trying to get across,'' said Terrance Mooney, Canada's acting charge
d'affaires in Prague. ``What we've been attempting to do here is bring
some truth to the matter.''
<p>
   A nomadic people who originated in India, the Gypsies have long
been at the bottom of Europe's social and economic scale. Employment,
even restaurant service, is denied them in some areas, and they are
frequently the target of beatings and violence that some courts are
reluctant to punish.
<p>
   From that perspective, Canada looks pretty good, welfare payments
or not.
<p>
   On the other hand, the Czech Republic has taken steps, and is
pledging more. A citizenship law widely viewed as discriminatory
against Slovak-born Gypsies has been relaxed. And as the country
positions itself for NATO membership in 1999, the government is
preparing to announce a new policy to aid its approximately 300,000
Gypsies.
<p>
   ``Why don't we like it here? They won't serve us coffee in
restaurants. We're afraid to go out at night. . . . But do you know
what's interesting?'' said Helena Sivakova. ``We've been having these
problems for 40 years and nobody's cared. Only when we want to go to
Canada, all of a sudden people pay attention.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>

<STORYID cat=i pri=u sel=tm--a> X3337 </STORYID>

<SLUG> bc-diana-england-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1194 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-england-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign, News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Dan Balz </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Palace Officials Scramble to Deal With Mourning Crowds (London) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LONDON  &MD;  With lines of mourners lengthening and the mounds of
flowers growing larger by the hour, officials at Buckingham Palace
grappled Tuesday with how to handle the massive crowds now expected to
assemble for Saturday's funeral services for Diana, Princess of Wales.
<p>
   The extraordinary outpouring of emotion, which appeared to catch
palace officials and even the public by surprise, seemed to intensify
Tuesday.
<p>
   Police estimated that 100,000 people a day are passing by
Kensington Palace, where Diana resided. The wait to sign the official
Book of Condolence at St. James's Palace was estimated at up to eight
hours, despite the overnight increase to 15 in the number of books
available to sign.
<p>
   Palace officials braced for an even larger gathering of people for
Saturday's funeral services, with estimates of 1 million or more
attempting to pack themselves into the relatively small area of royal
London where the events will take place.
<p>
   Some mourners complained that the processional route from St.
James's Palace, where Diana's coffin lies in private, to the funeral
site at Westminster Abbey will not accommodate all the people hoping
to pay their last respects to a woman whose death has triggered a
worldwide reaction that has been compared to the outpouring that came
after the assassination of President John F. Kennedy in 1963.
<p>
   First Lady Hillary Rodham Clinton will attend the funeral, White
House spokesman Joe Lockhart said Sunday, but will not be representing
the U.S. government. President Clinton's attendance was ruled out,
White House officials said, because it is not a state funeral. The
First Lady's invitation came jointly from Diana's family and the
ruling Windsor family,
<p>
   Diana was killed in an automobile crash in Paris early Sunday that
also took the life of her companion, Dodi Fayed, and the driver of the
car in which they were riding. A bodyguard was badly injured and
remains hospitalized and reportedly unable to speak.
<p>
   Palace officials said that, rather than extending the route of the
procession, which they consider impractical, they will attempt to
narrow the street corridors the cortege will follow to create more
room for the spectators. They also said mourners could pay respects by
lining the route that Diana's hearse will travel after the funeral as
it makes its way to the chapel on the family estate, Althorp, outside
of London, where her remains will be interred.
<p>
   Meanwhile, palace officials announced that the funeral procession
will include 500 representatives of the charities for which Diana had
worked in recent years. Many of those invited to join will be
children, while others may include AIDS patients or land-mine victims,
two causes with which the princess was closely identified in recent
years.
<p>
   At the same time, officials said a memorial fund will be
established in Diana's name to continue the charitable work she was
involved in. The fund was established after charities linked to her
were flooded with inquiries about donations. Called ``The Diana,
Princess of Wales Memorial Fund,'' the foundation will be administered
by Kensington Palace. Officials said contributions could be sent in
the name of the fund to Kensington Palace.
<p>
   Palace officials also announced that the nation will observe a
moment of silence at the end of the funeral services on Saturday, but
no other details were announced. The funeral is scheduled to begin at
11 a.m. (6 a.m. EDT) and will last about 50 minutes, according to
officials. Many businesses here will close for at least part of the
day.
<p>
   The services are being coordinated through Buckingham Palace, in
close consultation with Diana's family. Invitations to the 2,000
people who will be allowed into Westminster Abbey are being made by
telephone, officials said. The 500 charity representatives  &MD;  five from
each of roughly 100 charities  &MD;  will view the services on a large
screen outside the abbey.
<p>
   Already, however, there are complaints that the public may be
shortchanged by those planning the services. There also was criticism
over the decision not to allow Diana's coffin to lie in state. Palace
officials have said that decision was made jointly by the royal family
and Diana's family.
<p>
   Others questioned why members of the royal family have remained
silent since Diana's death, with neither Queen Elizabeth nor Diana's
ex-husband Prince Charles issuing any statement. ``I think the
reaction (of the public) has shocked the royal family,'' said Vanessa
Belton, who was waiting outside St. James's Palace with her mother,
Rose.
<p>
   The royal family remained at Balmoral in Scotland Tuesday, with
reports that Charles and his younger son Prince Harry went for a walk
in the afternoon. It was not clear when they would return to London.
<p>
   Even more disturbing to many of the thousands of mourners who came
out Tuesday was the bare flag pole over Buckingham Palace. Other flags
around the city are at half-staff, but at the palace there is no flag
at all. The absence of a flag is protocol when Queen Elizabeth is not
in residence at Buckingham Palace, but the barren pole was nonetheless
seen by many people as a symbol of insensitivity by the royal family.
<p>
   Throughout central London there was a sense that officials were
scrambling simply to keep up with the masses of people coming to pay
their respects to Diana.
<p>
   Around St. James's Palace, the lines continued to grow, and workers
had to erect new barriers along the sidewalks to allow the snaking
line to remain orderly. As other crews set up stands and platforms
along the procession route for the funeral, mourners continued to
deposit flowers anywhere they deemed appropriate.
<p>
   At Kensington Palace, the flowers covered an area estimated at 50
by 30 feet. There were more flowers as well at Harrods department
store, which is owned by Dodi Fayed's father, billionaire Egyptian
businessman Mohamed Fayed.
<p>
   The mourners represented a remarkable cross section of the
country's population. Some follow the royals' every move, and several
of them said they had attended the marriage of Charles and Diana in
1981. But others have had little interest in the royal family, other
than through Diana.
<p>
   The reports from Paris that the driver of the car in which Diana
was riding at the time of the crash had a blood alcohol level at least
three times the legal limit in France raised new questions about
security arrangements for the princess.
<p>
   Officials here noted that Diana had voluntarily given up her
security guard several years ago after she separated from Charles.
``It was no secret,'' one official said.
<p>
   Her sons continue to have protection through the palace, and
whenever Diana was with them, she was protected as well and would have
had the services of an official driver. But on her own, the officials
said, she chose a scaled-down security entourage that in Paris meant
the use of a driver from the Ritz Hotel, which is also owned by
Mohamed Fayed. &QL; 
</TEXT>

<STORYID cat=i pri=u sel=tm--a> X3338 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-diana-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1184 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Anne Swardson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Paris Investigating Photographers &QL; 
 &UR; For `Involuntary Homicide' (Paris) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   PARIS  &MD;  Six photographers and one of their motorcycle drivers were
placed under investigation by the Paris prosecutor Tuesday for
``involuntary homicide,'' a step toward bringing criminal charges
against them in the deaths of Princess Diana, Dodi Fayed and the
driver of the Mercedes-Benz sedan whose crash killed them.
<p>
   The seven men also will be investigated for failing to aid the
accident's victims, which is obligatory in France. Judicial sources
held out the possibility that other photographers would be named in
the criminal inquiry. Several reportedly fled the scene of the
accident before police could stop them and are being sought by law
enforcement authorities.
<p>
   The photographers and the driver were freed after being placed
under investigation, a step in the French legal system somewhat short
of being a suspect. They had been held since the accident early Sunday
morning. The prosecutor had requested that two continue to be
detained, but instead they were required to post bail and were
forbidden to work as journalists while the case is pending.
<p>
   As they were released and their handcuffs removed, the suspects
slipped out side entrances to the huge courthouse building, avoiding
the waiting banks of television and still cameras. French
photographers said they would not have taken pictures of them anyway,
out of ``solidarity.'' The seven also were said by other journalists
to have avoided the spotlight because they had sold their stories of
detention to tabloid newspapers and wanted to preserve exclusivity.
<p>
   They are: Nicola Arsov of the SIPA agency, Jacques Langevin of
Sygma, Serge Arnal of Stills, freelancer Laslo Veres, Stephane
Darmone, a driver for the Gamma agency, Romuald Rat of Gamma and
Christian Martinez of Angeli. Rat and Martinez had been recommended
for detention and posted bail instead.
<p>
   Involuntary homicide is punishable by a prison sentence of up to 15
years, failing to assist victims by up to five years' incarceration
and a fine of up to $80,600.
<p>
   Although prosecutors did not specify the evidence they had against
the paparazzi, witnesses have described a nightmarish scene of 10 to
15 photographers swarming around the crushed Mercedes, taking pictures
inches from Diana's face as she lay trapped in the back seat,
unconscious. Reports Tuesday also indicated photographers had pushed
policemen and swore at them. Diana died in the hospital less than four
hours after the crash.
<p>
   Also Tuesday, the family of Dodi Fayed and of Henri Paul, the
driver, said they would file civil suits in the case, which under
French law would allow them to participate in the actions by the
French justice system against the suspects.
<p>
   The seven men were placed under investigation as part of a criminal
inquiry that opened Tuesday under the auspices of an investigating
magistrate. The magistrate, Herve Stephan, will continue the
investigation begun by police following the auto accident early Sunday
morning in a highway tunnel on the Right Bank of the Seine River.
<p>
   William Bourdon, lawyer for Arsov, criticized what he called ``show
justice'' and said he thought the seven were being made scapegoats.
<p>
   The men were picked up following a high-speed chase from the Ritz
Hotel, where Diana and Fayed ate their last dinner, that ended when
the Mercedes S-280 rammed into a pillar in a highway tunnel at a speed
that has been reported as high as 125 miles per hour. The speed has
not been confirmed by police.
<p>
   The blood level of the driver, Paul, was more than three times the
legal limit for driving in France. Some press reports Tuesday said
Paul, the assistant director of security at the Ritz, who had been
called in because the regular chauffeur drove a decoy car to lure
photographers away, was well known at the hotel as a drinker. Those
reports could not be confirmed, and family and residents of Paul's
home town of Lorient in western France would not comment. The Ritz had
no comment Tuesday and said it would make a statement Wednesday.
<p>
    &UR; -(optional trim to end)- &QC; 
<p>
   Although police and prosecutors did not specify the results of
their investigations into the actions of the photographers that night,
a widely quoted police report Tuesday said two of them had engaged in
a scuffle with police trying to move them out of their way.
<p>
   The police report said the paparazzi were ``virulent, disgusting,
they were continuing to take pictures and were deliberately preventing
the police officer from aiding the victim,'' according to several
accounts of it. It said one photographer said: ``You are pissing me
off. Let me do my job. In Sarajevo, the cops allow us to work. Go and
get shot at and you'll see what it is like.''
<p>
   Other witnesses also have described photographers ``swarming''
around the car. A doctor who was one of the first to arrive at the
accident scene, however, said that while the photographers were
present, they did not interfere.
<p>
   Rat's lawyer, Philippe Benamou, said his client had reached into
the crashed vehicle and taken Diana's pulse while he was taking
pictures of the wreckage. ``He wanted to see if she was dead or
alive,'' the lawyer said. ``He saw that she was alive, and police were
arriving at the same time. It happened so quickly.''
<p>
   Frederic Mailliez, an emergency room physician, said in a telephone
interview Tuesday that he stopped at the accident scene about a minute
after the crash occurred. Smoke was still rising from the vehicle, he
said, and the horn was sounding from the driver's body leaning on it.
Already, photographers surrounded the car taking pictures. Mailliez
called for an ambulance but was told help was on the way.
<p>
   A volunteer firefighter was working on Trevor Rees-Jones, the
bodyguard in the front passenger seat who was to be the only survivor.
Mailliez confirmed Tuesday that Rees-Jones was the only passenger in
the Mercedes who was wearing a seat belt.
<p>
   Mailliez then turned to the only other living person, Diana. He did
not recognize her, he said, but began to administer first aid
immediately.
<p>
   As he attempted to treat her, for between five and 10 minutes, the
photographers continued taking pictures, but they did not get in the
way, he said. Asked if they assisted, he responded that they told him
the victims spoke English. Should they have done more? ``They wouldn't
have known what to do,'' he said. ``They were like any other
passersby.''
<p>
   For his part, Mailliez, who left as soon as more medical assistance
arrived, said he felt great frustration that he could not put his
skills to better use.
<p>
   ``I could not have done more without equipment,'' he said. ``But
whether it was Lady Di or a drunken bum, my feeling was great
frustration. It would have taken very specialized equipment to revive
her, and I did not have it.''
<p>
   Mailliez said that if photographers had an altercation with
authorities it was after he had left.
<p>
    &QL; 
</TEXT>

<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-scene
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Eugene Robinson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; The Silence of Grief Amid London's Bustle (London) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LONDON  &MD;  The first thing you notice is the silence: The center of
London, with all its monuments and its boulevards and its palaces, is
as quiet as a church.
<p>
   The death of Princess Diana was such a profound shock for Britain
that many people, including many officials, did not know quite how to
react. On Sunday and Monday, crowds of people pouring toward the royal
palaces to express their condolences had to fight their way through
the city's normal choking traffic. The result was gridlock for
motorists and an obstacle course for pedestrians trying to lay
bouquets of flowers.
<p>
   Tuesday, as Britain settled into a routine of mourning, authorities
closed off all the streets around Buckingham and St. James's palaces
to traffic, roughly the area between Hyde Park Corner and Trafalgar
Square. The effect was to create, within this huge metropolis bustling
about its workday, a smaller London that moved to a slow, stately,
elegiac pace.
<p>
   At the circle in front of Buckingham Palace, a place usually filled
with the sound of black taxicabs honking their horns at tourists who
looked left instead of right while stepping off the curb, a policeman
cocked his ear and marveled: ``Listen to that. It's so quiet. I've
never heard it like this. Never.''
<p>
   Britons have surprised even themselves with their great outpouring
of emotion following Diana's death. The national stereotype calls for
accepting tragedy with a stiff upper lip, but Britain is obviously
shocked, and obviously unashamed to show its feelings. Anchormen
become choked up on the air. Callers to talk shows read poetry they
have written in Diana's honor. People talk of little else than her
death and her upcoming funeral.
<p>
   Tuesday, as they have done the previous two days, thousands came to
place flowers at the gates in front of Buckingham Palace, the
residence of Queen Elizabeth II. They came in silence: The perpetual
large crowd in front of the palace, hundreds of people at any given
time, makes no noise. People whisper, or do not speak at all.
<p>
   There are thousands of bouquets stretching the length of the ornate
palace gates, flowers along with teddy bears, handwritten notes,
paintings and sketches of Diana. The messages are full of unabashed
sentiment. ``The one and only Lady Diana. The one jewel in the
crown,'' reads one note, attached to a bunch of irises, slowly wilting
in the uncommonly bright sunshine.
<p>
   Walking down the Mall from Buckingham Palace toward Trafalgar
Square was like being sent back in time. With no traffic noise, and no
shouting or laughing from tourists, the only sound was the crunching
of gravel underfoot. A few mounted policemen rode past, and then a
dozen of the Queen's Guard riding tall in scarlet coats, and then
another mounted group in ceremonial green capes  &MD;  the clip-clop of
horses, sounds that echoed along the Mall 200 years ago.
<p>
   Stretching several blocks was a line of people waiting to sign the
book of condolences at St. James's Palace, where Diana's body is lying
until her funeral Saturday. Polite policemen informed them they would
have to wait several hours, but no one seemed deterred. Some people
tried to explain why they were willing to spend so much time standing
in line in Diana's memory  &MD;  ``It's just her, really, everything she
meant and stood for,'' one man said. Many others declined to try, and
some made clear they blamed the media at least in part for the
princess's death.
<p>
   A couple of miles away, on the other side of Hyde Park, lies
another oasis of quiet amid the cacophonous city  &MD;  Kensington Palace,
where Diana lived. Tuesday, adjacent Kensington Gardens were full of
the usual lunchtime strollers, frisbee throwers and rollerbladers. But
as you walked toward the palace, the moment you crossed an imaginary
line the noise ended.
<p>
   In front of the Kensington Palace gates was a large, silent crowd,
thousands strong  &MD;  a crowd disproportionately full of young people,
black and brown people, women, a crowd that said New Britain rather
than Old. Diana had a special appeal for people who felt left out of
traditional British society, and they have come to say goodbye. ``I
wouldn't do this for any of that other royal lot, not in a hundred
years,'' said a fashionably dressed young black woman who gave her
name as Tandy.
<p>
   Kensington Palace is also the site of the biggest floral display  &MD; 
tens of thousands of bouquets, with more added every minute.
<p>
   The final quiet place in central London on Tuesday was the sidewalk
in front of the Harrods department store  &MD;  the store owned by Mohamed
Fayed, whose son Dodi died with Diana in the automobile crash in Paris
early Sunday. Harrods is in a narrow, bustling, noisy part of west
London. The area is full of people at any time of the day or night,
busy people hurrying about their business.
<p>
   Yet when people passed the tables where the condolence books for
Dodi Fayed were available for signing, they fell silent. They walked
past without speaking. Some stopped and joined the line. &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-balkans </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1611 09-02 1048 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-balkans - a1611
 &UR; (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Tracy Wilkinson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; U.S. Troops Relinquish TV Transmitter to Serbs (Sarajevo) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   SARAJEVO, Bosnia-Herzegovina  &MD;  Surrounded by stick-wielding Bosnian
Serbs, U.S. troops agreed Tuesday to relinquish a television
transmitter they had controlled to forces answering to war crimes
suspect Radovan Karadzic.
<p>
   In exchange, the Serbs, under the direction of Karadzic ally
Momcilo Krajisnik, promised to end inflammatory anti-West rhetoric and
permit opposition voices on the air.
<p>
   Krajisnik, the Bosnian Serb member of the country's three-man
presidency, praised the deal as a ``wise'' step by NATO peacekeepers
to avoid conflict with the Serbs. Others wondered if the Americans had
blinked, having been embarrassed by last week's bungling of a military
operation to take over pro-Karadzic police stations.
<p>
   Adding insult to injury, the newly restored television transmission
was used Tuesday night to cancel Serbian participation in crucial
municipal elections scheduled for Sept. 13-14.
<p>
   The transmitter, on a hilltop in northeastern Bosnia, was seized by
American troops last week as part of a campaign to shore up Karadzic
foe Biljana Plavsic, president of the Bosnian Serb Republic. But the
troops soon became targets of about 200 angry, stone-hurling Serbs.
<p>
   Media and police are the two central tools in the battle to gain
and hold onto power in this part of the world. Last week, U.S.-led
NATO troops attempted to install Plavsic forces in police stations in
three cities and two towns, and to take the transmitter at Udrigovo,
near the city of Bijeljina.
<p>
   In all three cities, the operation failed and police loyal to
Karadzic and his hard-line coterie remained in charge; in one city,
Brcko, angry crowds blocked and attacked American forces in an ugly
melee broadcast the world over. And with Tuesday's agreement, the
transmitter also reverted to Karadzic's allies.
<p>
   From his village stronghold in Pale near Sarajevo, Karadzic,
indicted by an international war crimes court on genocide charges, is
locked in a power struggle with his one-time partner, Plavsic. She
accuses him and his inner circle of corruption and of enriching
themselves while most Bosnian Serbs languish in poverty.
<p>
   For the last several months, the Clinton administration has pursued
a more aggressive course of action in Bosnia, signaling a
determination to isolate Karadzic by promoting Plavsic. But in the
last week, faced with their first military challenge, U.S. efforts
have crashed  &MD;  with negative consequences on military, diplomatic and
political fronts.
<p>
   Plavsic, after making important gains in the northwestern portion
of the Bosnian Serb Republic, has lost momentum and stalled. The
American military has appeared weak to the Serbs, making future
confrontations more likely.
<p>
   And an always precarious unity within the international peacemaking
mission here has been shattered. European and other international
allies said the United States is forcing its agenda through with
little laying of political groundwork and with disregard for legal
backing.
<p>
   The Udrigovo transmitter was crucial because it controlled
television signals to key, disputed cities. With the U.S. takeover of
the tower last Thursday, hateful rhetoric that Bosnian Serb
hard-liners were using to incite violence against Western peacekeepers
could not reach those cities.
<p>
   On Monday, crowds of Bosnian Serbs, bused in and directed by men
with walkie-talkies, began surrounding the heavily armed U.S. troops,
who encircled the transmitter and built barricades. The mobs, which
swelled to around 250 people, hurled stones and insults until the
troops fired tear gas to subdue the onslaught.
<p>
   Still armed with sticks and wooden clubs, and consuming plenty of
plum brandy, the Serbs staged a kind of sit-in around the Americans
overnight and well into Tuesday morning. They finally dispersed later
Tuesday, NATO spokesmen said, after troops permitted four Bosnian Serb
policemen and three technicians from Pale television to enter the
transmitting station.
<p>
   On Tuesday night, the signal from Pale television was again on the
air in cities like Brcko and Bijeljina, which Plavsic and Karadzic
forces continue to dispute.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   International officials said the decision to first take and then
surrender the transmitter came from NATO's Supreme Commander, U.S.
Army Gen. Wesley Clark, who has taken an unusually hands-on approach
to Bosnia since assuming the top NATO military post in July.
<p>
   The compromise was struck between Krajisnik and Clark's
representative in Bosnia, U.S. Army Gen. Eric K. Shinseki, in the wee
hours of Tuesday. Krajisnik promised that the Bosnian Serb television
and radio (SRT) will refrain from inflammatory language against NATO
and international peacekeepers and will give an hour a day in
programming time to opposition politicians.
<p>
   The hard-line Bosnian Serb leadership has signed and then ignored
similar agreements in the past. On Tuesday night's SRT news broadcast,
the station's director, Miroslav Toholj, congratulated the
demonstrators who confronted the American forces at the Bijeljina
transmitter, lauding their ``bravery'' and ``defense of human
rights.''
<p>
   Krajisnik, clearly relieved that pressure seemed to be abating,
said he was glad ``more calm winds'' had prevailed.
<p>
   And on the same newscast, prime minister Gojko Klickovic said
fraudulent voter registration that has allowed Muslims to outnumber
Serbs in some Serb-held cities will prevent Bosnian Serbs from
participating in Sept. 13-14 municipal elections. If this holds, it
would be a major blow to international efforts to conduct elections as
an important step in rebuilding the post-war country.
<p>
   NATO officials contend their actions in recent weeks are not
directed at supporting Plavsic but simply are meant to prevent
violence between rival factions. In a statement released late Monday
attempting to knock down numerous press reports, the NATO leadership
said its operation in Brcko was in support of U.N. police monitors'
inspections of police stations. But U.N. officials denied they had
agreed to go along with NATO's predawn raids.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-clinton </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1614 09-02 0575 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-clinton - a1614
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Elizabeth Shogren </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; First Lady Is Slated to Attend Princess Diana's Funeral (Edgartown) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   EDGARTOWN, Mass.  &MD;  First lady Hillary Rodham Clinton will attend
the funeral of Princess Diana in recognition of the Clintons' personal
relationship with her and their admiration for her humanitarian work,
the White House said Tuesday.
<p>
   ``She will not be representing the government officially, but she
will certainly take the condolences of her husband, the president, and
we fully expect the American people will believe that she will be
passing along the American public's condolences,'' White House
spokesman Joe Lockhart told reporters on Martha's Vineyard, where the
first family is vacationing.
<p>
   The first lady received a personal invitation Tuesday from Diana's
family, the Spencers, as well as from the British royal family,
Lockhart said.
<p>
   President Clinton was not invited to the Saturday funeral because
of the unofficial nature of the service, Lockhart said. Neither the
U.S. government nor other foreign governments will send official
delegations, because it will not be a state funeral, he said.
<p>
   The only other U.S. government official known to be attending the
service is William J. Crowe, the ambassador to Britain and former
chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff.
<p>
   The decision for Hillary Clinton to attend the funeral was made
after consultations involving the White House, the U.S. Embassy, the
royal family and the Spencer family.
<p>
   The first lady plans to arrive in London on Saturday morning and
leave soon after the funeral, which will be held at Westminster Abbey.
The president is expected to remain on Martha's Vineyard to complete
his three-week vacation.
<p>
   Lockhart said the two women's relationship was close because of the
``confluence of interests between the first lady and the late Princess
Diana,'' particularly their mutual interest in helping children and
people suffering from breast cancer and AIDS.
<p>
   In a condolence letter to British Prime Minister Tony Blair,
President Clinton said: ``We fondly recall her last visit to the White
House in June when she met with Hillary. The British people, indeed
all of us, have lost a friend and a strong voice for those less
fortunate.''
<p>
   Other condolence letters  &MD;  which were sent by diplomatic cable to
Queen Elizabeth II, Prince Charles and Diana's family  &MD;  were not made
public.
<p>
   Although she was no longer an official member of the royal family,
Diana had received regal treatment during visits to the White House
both earlier this summer and in September 1996.
<p>
   A year ago, Hillary Clinton hosted an elegant breakfast in the
White House East Room to launch a fund-raising drive to fight breast
cancer. Diana was honorary chairwoman of the effort.
<p>
   During the event, Hillary Clinton praised the princess for her
humanitarian efforts. ``She is a woman who has had a tireless
commitment to the homeless, AIDS, cancer, leprosy and other health
issues, and we are so pleased that she is lending her support to this
continuing fight against breast cancer,'' the first lady said.
<p>
   On her latest trip to Washington, on June 18, Diana met privately
with Hillary Clinton during a visit to the White House. She was in
Washington to endorse an effort to bring about a worldwide ban on land
mines and heighten public awareness of the devastation they cause.
</TEXT>

AP-NY-09-02-97 1915EDT &QL;

<SLUG> bc-balkans-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0912 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-balkans-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Lee Hockstader </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Bosnian Serbs' Standoff With U.S. Troops Ends &QL; 
 &UR; With Pledge to Share Airwaves (Tuzla) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   TUZLA, Bosnia  &MD;  U.S. troops ended a tense standoff and relinquished
control of a key television tower near here Tuesday after Bosnian Serb
hard-liners agreed to halt anti-Western broadcasts and open the
airwaves to one hour daily of differing views.
<p>
   The effect of the move was to return control of the facility  &MD;  and
of the airwaves in eastern part of the Bosnian Serb Republic  &MD;  to
allies of Radovan Karadzic, an indicted war crimes suspect who
Washington says is the main stumbling block to implementing the
U.S.-brokered 1995 peace plan for Bosnia. Government-controlled
television is a critical pillar of political power for Karadzic and
his supporters, who are fiercely opposed to the integrated,
multiethnic Bosnia laid out in the peace plan.
<p>
   The nearly 300 American troops, who seized control of the
transmission relay tower Thursday, withdrew at daybreak after being
harassed by a mob of Serbs for about 20 hours. The withdrawal came
several hours after a U.S. military spokesman said the Americans would
not be dislodged until it was determined which political faction in
the Serb half of Bosnia  &MD;  Karadzic's or that of his rival, Bosnian
Serb President Biljana Plavsic  &MD;  had the rightful claim to the
facility.
<p>
   That determination was never made. Instead, Western officials
acknowledged, they returned the TV tower to Karadzic's camp to defuse
as quickly as possible a potentially dangerous confrontation.
<p>
   The deal they struck overnight, approved Tuesday morning by the
supreme allied commander in Europe, U.S. Lt. Gen. Wesley Clark, was
immediately controversial.
<p>
   Some Western officials and other observers called the U.S.
withdrawal a sign of weakness and a victory for Karadzic. They said
they doubted the Serbs would fulfill the spirit of the accord and
refashion their broadcasts along pluralist lines, and expressed worry
that the deal would undercut Plavsic. Plavsic, who supports the peace
plan, has been gaining power in the western part of the Serb Republic
with help from Washington and its European allies; Karadzic and his
allies have their power base in the republic's eastern half.
<p>
   ``What (the NATO-led force) has actually done is to give back the
means of indoctrination in a very key region'' to Karadzic, said Chris
Bennett, a political analyst with the International Crisis Group, a
private research organization here. ``We've done Plavsic no favors in
this agreement.''
<p>
   Others defended the deal, saying it calmed a touchy situation and
was the best the West could do short of shutting down the tower, a
move that many regarded as akin to censorship.
<p>
   They said the accord could give the West added leverage over Serb
broadcasts, noting that if the Serbs do not live up to it, U.S. troops
in the area are prepared to seize control of the tower again. NATO's
policymaking body, the North Atlantic Council, agreed Saturday to
authorize peacekeepers in Bosnia ``to go ahead and take actions that
are necessary to, as it says, suspend or curtail programming that is
hostile to the spirit of the Dayton accords,'' Pentagon spokesman
Kevin Bacon said in Washington.
<p>
   The Serbs ``agreed on something, and we can use it to our
advantage,'' said Ambassador Gert Wagner, a top deputy at the Office
of the High Representative, which is charged with implementing the
civilian parts of the peace plan brokered in Dayton, Ohio, nearly two
years ago. ``We'll have influence on their programming in a way we've
never had before.''
<p>
   Said a Western official preparing for municipal elections in Bosnia
scheduled for this month: ``The alternative was to do something much
more radical''  &MD;  such as shutting down the tower or jamming
transmissions  &MD;  ``that could have had a very negative effect on the
elections.''
<p>
   The official added that an attempt by NATO to close TV studios and
transmission facilities would likely be opposed by Karadzic's
hard-liners. ``We could have had people killed,'' she said. ``I don't
know this is the time we want body bags going home.''
<p>
   Karadzic's allies  &MD;  based in the village of Pale, the seat of the
Bosnian Serb government  &MD;  threatened to boycott the local elections,
which have already been scheduled and postponed twice.
<p>
   Most Western officials want to see Plavsic in control of all
broadcasting in the Serb Republic; she currently holds sway only in
the western half. But awarding her control of the transmitter seized
by U.S. troops last week near the town of Udrigova was not a realistic
option. Since police in the area remain loyal to Karadzic, she could
not have held it.
<p>
   Tuesday's agreement was negotiated in late-night telephone
bargaining between Jacques Klein, a retired U.S. Air Force general and
a senior Western mediator for Bosnia, and Momcilo Krajisnik, a top
Karadzic aide who is the Serbs' representative on Bosnia's collective
federal presidency.
<p>
   The hard-line Serb media agreed not to air ``inflammatory''
anti-Western reports and to devote one hour daily in prime time to
``other political views.'' Such views were not defined.
<p>
   The Serbs also agreed to grant Carlos Westendorp, the chief Western
mediator for Bosnia, a half-hour of air time in the coming days,
without cuts or commentary, to explain recent clashes between NATO
forces and demonstrators. &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-windsor </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1623 09-02 0321 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-windsor - a1623
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: News editors) &QL;

<BYLINE> By Michele Ingrassia </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Sotheby's Postpones Auction of Duke of Windsor Items &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   It was expected to be the sale of the century  &MD;  Sotheby's auction
next week of the contents of the Paris residence of the duke of
Windsor (for 11 months, he was Edward VIII, king of England) and
Wallis Warfield Simpson (the twice-divorced American for whom he gave
up the throne on Dec. 10, 1936). But Sotheby's announced Tuesday that,
following the weekend deaths of Princess Diana and Dodi Fayed, the
auction is being postponed.
<p>
   The connection: Fayed's father, Mohammed Fayed, owner of Harrods in
London and the Ritz Hotel in Paris, also owns the contents of the
Windsors' Bois de Boulogne residence. It was Mohammed Fayed who had
decided last year to sell the 40,000 items  &MD;  the proceeds were to have
gone to charity  &MD;  and it was Fayed who, along with Sotheby's
officials, decided this was not a moment for commerce.
<p>
   ``I will never be able to reconcile myself to the needless and
cruel deaths of two people who were so vibrant, generous and full of
life: God took their souls to live together in paradise. Now they have
peace,'' Fayed said in a statement issued through Sotheby's. ``As a
mark of respect, I believe there should be an appropriate interval
before the auction takes place.''
<p>
   Fayed has made no other public statement on the death of his oldest
son. He bought the Windsor home from the Pasteur Institute, which had
inherited the mansion from the duchess upon her death in 1986.
<p>
   The collection, which includes 10,000 photos of the royal family
dating back to 1860, is valued at between $5 million and $7 million.
<p>
   Although no date has been set, Sotheby's has a full fall auction
schedule, which means the Windsor sale will likely be delayed until
early 1998.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>

bc-diana-england - a1630

</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By William D. Montalbano </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Mourners Fill Condolence Books With Thoughts of Diana (London) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LONDON  &MD;  Carl Davies brought memories, a message  &MD;  and pain killers
 &MD;  to make his mark on British history. Doreen King came with a single
red rose and a poem. Linda McAdam brought two pre-schoolers and tears
she couldn't staunch.
<p>
   Across Britain, along country lanes and in the palace where she
lies guarded by a single Redcoat in a bearskin hat, an astonishing
national lament swelled Tuesday for Diana, princess of Wales.
<p>
   Even the British themselves seemed surprised to discover the depth
and the scope of their emotions. The character-defining stiff upper
lip seemed suddenly as dated as the empire.
<p>
   At Kensington Palace, where Diana lived, more than 100,000 people
came again Tuesday, adding to an ocean of blossoms that spilled along
nearby pathways.
<p>
   At St. James Palace, the number of condolence books overseen by
ushers in top hats was increased from four to 15. The palace is open
around the clock; no matter.
<p>
   Overnight Monday, Julian McLean joined the line at 1:30 a.m. and
walked into the Long Corridor at the palace to sign his name at 7 a.m.
``It was very cold and very subdued throughout the night. I did it
because I will never get a chance to do this again,'' McLean said.
<p>
   Nonplussed planners now believe that as many as 2 million people
will jam into the restricted area of London through which Diana's
coffin will be carried to Westminster Abbey for her funeral at 11 a.m.
Saturday. After the service, her casket will be driven under escort to
her family's home about 80 miles northwest of London.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Most shops, restaurants and museums will be closed Saturday, and
the country will officially observe a minute of silence when the
service ends around 11:45.
<p>
   Queen Elizabeth and her family, including Diana's sons Prince
William, 15, and Prince Harry, 12, will travel to London for the
funeral from their vacation home at Balmoral Castle in the Scottish
Highlands, where they have remained in seclusion.
<p>
   On Tuesday, the Palace announced that a foundation will be
established to accept gifts in Diana's honor and distribute them among
charities that she supported.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Until the funeral, Diana's body will continue to lie in a
flag-draped coffin before the altar in the Chapel Royal at the
turreted palace that was the London home of Britain's rulers until
they moved to Buckingham Palace.
<p>
   In the low, stone Palace of St. James, built in the 16th century by
Henry VIII, the long wait ends for mourners when they are ushered
through a side door and into a solemn and still room where blank-paged
notebooks sit on on tables covered by black cloths.
<p>
   Mourners may write as much as they like. They stay as long as they
need to in the long room. About 300 people sign every hour, a palace
spokesman said. Eventually, the books will go to the palace, and then
for preservation in national archives.
<p>
   After about two hours in line with as much still to endure, Carl
Davies, 62, leaned heavily on a sturdy wooden cane. His arthritis was
killing him, but it was a familiar devil, and he had brought pills.
<p>
   It was memory that impelled him uncomfortably out into the crowd,
said Davies, a memory from the '80s when Diana was newly married and
he was director of a psychiatric hospital in Epsom.
<p>
   ``I shook her hand and she was charming, but it was the patients
she was most concerned with. That's what struck me,'' Davies
remembered. ``She was wearing green that day, and there was a woman in
a wheelchair who said to her `That's my favorite color,' and Diana
replied, bending down to speak at the level of the woman `I wore it
just for you.' And when a patient admired her bouquet, she plucked a
flower from it and gave it to her. Some of the patients had never
known such joy and comfort.''
<p>
   At last reaching a book of condolence, Davies wrote, ``You will
always be `Her Royal Highness' to me.'' Diana officially lost the
title when she divorced Prince Charles last year.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Biochemist Doreen King carried a single red rose and inscribed in
the palace book a poem she had carefully typed out before leaving home
in Essex:
<p>
    &UR; Diana &LR; 
<p>
   Oh Princess Diana
<p>
   Now we have lost you.
<p>
   You shone like the sun
<p>
   In establishment's power.
<p>
   In our hearts we still see you
<p>
   In the Twentieth century  &MD; 
<p>
   The People's Queen  &MD;  you'll still be.
<p>
   ``She did so much good work. She stood for us, not just in Britain
but in all the world,'' King said.
<p>
   Linda McAdam emerged in tears from the palace pushing a 2 year old
in a stroller and holding the hand of 5-year-old Rikki. ``I'm afraid I
don't remember clearly what I wrote. I feel drained. The wait wasn't
bad; the kids were good. Diana was such a lovely person I felt I had
to come and ask why,'' she said.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-village </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1657 09-02 0874 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-village - a1657
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Michael Slackman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Village Fears Change Once Diana Is Buried There (Great Brington) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   GREAT BRINGTON, England  &MD;  Shortly after Princess Diana buried her
father, Earl Spencer, inside the family chapel, an intruder scaled a
wrought iron fence, broke into the crypt and wrenched off the delicate
marble hands adorning an effigy to one of her ancestors.
<p>
   For 20 generations, members of the Spencer family have been buried
beneath a private chapel tucked in the corner of a 13th-century church
in this small hillside village. On that day five years ago, villagers
recalled Tuesday, they were incensed that someone vandalized the
monument to the very first earl ever buried there.
<p>
   But now many in this small, close-knit community, where the church
is left unlocked and unattended on most days, were wondering what
awaits their village when Princess Diana is buried here Saturday.
<p>
   In fact, many villagers, who initially welcomed ``Diana's
homecoming,'' began Tuesday to dread a transformation of their
picture-postcard village, dotted with thatched-roofed homes and just
two shops  &MD;  a pub and a post office-general store  &MD;  into a world-class
tourist destination.
<p>
   ``Everybody's initial reaction was, `Yes. This is the right place
for her to be,' '' said Christine Whiley, the village postmistress and
a 20-year resident of Great Brington. ``Then you start to think, `What
is this going to do to the village?' ''
<p>
   When Diana was killed in a car wreck in Paris early Sunday, the
royal family agreed to the request of her brother, the current Earl
Spencer, that his sister be buried inside the family chapel.
<p>
   After a public procession through the streets of London Saturday
and a televised funeral in Westminster Abbey, Diana will be brought
here, 60 miles north of London, for a private burial.
<p>
   She will be laid to rest inside the church, St. Mary the Virgin,
with its Norman-style tower and prominent position overlooking acres
of rolling farmland. Mourners Tuesday crowded inside, where there is
room for only 200 worshipers, to leave flowers at the gate of the
Spencer crypt for a second day. And villagers grew increasingly
worried.
<p>
   ``We will all work together,'' the priest's wife, Jean MacPherson,
told parishioners who went to the rectory Tuesday to express concern
about the future of the community.
<p>
   ``Worst-case scenario, we'll lock it up until we can figure out
what to do,'' the vicar, David MacPherson, said.
<p>
   Diana spent most of her childhood, from 6 through 17, living in the
family compound of Althorp, a 600-acre estate that is adjacent to, but
technically not part of, Great Brington. The home she grew up in is
walled and gated, much like the palaces she lived in as an adult.
<p>
   As the daughter of an earl, third highest title in British peerage,
Diana was never, really, part of life in the village.
<p>
   ``One or two people here knew the princess personally, and they
have been devastated,'' the vicar said during the first service after
the death.
<p>
   But the Spencer family has for hundreds of years been an integral
part of life in the region. The church alone is decorated with 400
copies of the Spencer coat of arms, which reads ``Dieu Defend Le
Droit,'' or ``God Defends the Just.'' Before World War II, most of the
villagers worked on farms controlled or run by the Spencer family.
After the war, the Spencers began selling off land and the character
of the population changed.
<p>
   ``We moved from a rural community based on farming to a population
that is largely commuting, largely professional, largely middle
class,'' said Dr. Stephen Mattingly, a retired physician who recently
published a book about the history of the region.
<p>
   Still, the community where Diana will be laid to rest has retained
some of the character and feel of a place lost in time. The population
of 450 includes the residents of another village and a hamlet within
the parish.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   There also is the character of the community: A handwritten sign
posted on the church door Tuesday read: ``There will be no Sunday tea
served on September 7th due to a village trip.''
<p>
   The only public restrooms in the village, situated inside the pub,
are marked ``Farmers'' and ``Milkmaids.''
<p>
   And everyone knows everyone.
<p>
   ``It is a very quiet, lovely community,'' said Deborah Sampson, a
resident of a nearby village, as she strolled across the unmowed
cemetery behind the church Tuesday morning.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Publicly, the villagers are generally cautious not to express too
great an alarm over the future of their community. Mattingly, for
example, said they are accustomed to getting many visitors, as many as
3,000 a year, because George Washington's
great-great-great-grandfather is buried beneath the church.
<p>
   But as cars choked the single street that runs through the middle
of town Tuesday, one man just shook his head. ``These poor people,''
said Adrian Bawl, a resident of Northampton, a nearby city of about
300,000. ``The village will never be the same.''
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-crash </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0601 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-crash
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign, News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Warren Brown </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Seat Belts Might Have Saved Diana &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Seat belts and shoulder harnesses might have saved them.
<p>
   That is the educated guess of U.S. and European auto safety experts
examining available information on the Paris car crash that killed
Princess Diana and two others Sunday.
<p>
   The high-speed slam into a concrete pillar and walls inside a
tunnel in central Paris demolished the Mercedes-Benz S280 sedan in
which Diana was riding in the back seat with her companion, Dodi
Fayed, who died at the scene.
<p>
   Also killed was the car's driver, Henri Paul, who allegedly was
intoxicated. The lone survivor was Diana's bodyguard, Trevor
Reese-Jones.
<p>
   All of the dead reportedly were unbelted. Reese-Jones was belted
and was protected by the S280's front-passenger air bag.
<p>
   Conceding that, for the moment, their comments are based on
hunches, several auto safety experts said Tuesday that Diana and Fayed
might have lived if they had been belted.
<p>
   ``They were sitting in the back seat, farthest from the point of
the initial impact,'' said Brian O'Neill, president of the Insurance
Institute for Highway Safety. ``Usually the back seat is the safest
seat  &MD;  if you are wearing seat belts.''
<p>
   French police would not confirm reports that Diana, Fayed and Paul
were unbelted. But if the reports are true, that failure probably
contributed to their deaths, O'Neill said.
<p>
   ``We know that the impact speed with the post was survivable
because the bodyguard survived,'' British road safety expert Richard
Cuerden told Reuter. ``We expect that people in the rear have as good
a chance, if not better, of making it.''
<p>
   It's a simple matter of physics, said William Boehly, an auto
safety engineer and former administrator of vehicle safety research
and development at the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration.
<p>
   A body at rest tends to remain at rest. A body in motion tends to
remain in motion. Auto crashes are horrific stoppers of motion. And
each crash usually involves a three-in-one collision, Boehly said.
<p>
   There is the initial crash of car into object. There is the
secondary crash of occupant into objects within the car, a collision
that could be fatal if the occupant is unbelted. And there is the
collective third crash of organs banging against one another and
against bone structures within the occupant's body.
<p>
   The inner-body collision, too, is worsened by the severity of the
blows to an unbelted occupant striking structures within the car,
O'Neill and Boehly said.
<p>
   ``I'm presuming that Princess Di went flying into the back of the
front seat so hard it caused massive chest injuries,'' said O'Neill,
whose institute routinely studies such accidents.
<p>
   It's possible to design a car to withstand the reported crash speed
of 100 mph that ended Diana's life, O'Neill said.
<p>
   ``But that kind of crash is exceedingly rare. It would be foolish
to design for that kind of crash because of the trade-offs'' in costs
and safety that would accompany such a design, he said.
<p>
   New cars sold in the United States must meet a 30-mph,
fixed-barrier crash standard set by the National Highway Traffic
Safety Administration. That means unbelted occupants within the car
should be able to survive a 30-mph crash into a wall.
<p>
   That kind of crash is roughly equivalent to a car dropping head
first from a three-story building, with each story being 10 feet high,
Boehly said. By comparison, Diana's crash was ``the equivalent of a
30-story head-first drop,'' he said.
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Annie Groer and Ann Gerhart </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; RELIABLE SOURCE: Notes on Newsmakers (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Sarah Ferguson's new ads for Weight Watchers had all
the makings of a stupendously successful campaign save one: good
timing.
<p>
   Thursday, to kick off the multimillion-dollar effort, Weight
Watchers mailed out tens of thousands of brochures featuring the
Duchess of York saying that losing weight is ``harder than outrunning
the paparazzi.''
<p>
   When the death of Princess Diana instantly made the phrase
offensive, the diet program's execs scrambled to pull television and
print ads. ``It's a tragic irony that the advertising campaign
commenced on the same day as this awful accident,'' said Jeffrey
Schneider, spokesman for the Duchess of York, the former sister-in-law
of Princess Diana. He said Ferguson was ``absolutely devastated'' by
her longtime friend's death. Howard Rubenstein, whose public relations
firm represents both the duchess and Weight Watchers, added that
Fergie is ``very, very concerned about the well-being of Princess Di's
children.''
<p>
   The duchess canceled a luncheon and news conference that were
supposed to be held Wednesday in New York's Rainbow Room and was
flying to London for Saturday's funeral. The campaign will be reworked
and relaunched ``at an appropriate time in the future,'' Schneider
said.
<p>
   Some TV ads did run, and Glamour and Self magazines went to print
with the paparazzi line in the ads, said Rubenstein.
<p>
   It's not the first time a marketing slogan has been swept away by
current events. In October 1995, after O.J. Simpson was acquitted of
murder, Mars Inc. pulled ads for its Starburst candies that declared
``the juice is loose'' and ``turn loose the juice.''
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   ``This was the ultimate Basque celebration,'' said Rep. Dana
Rohrabacher, R-Calif., of his wedding to Rhonda Carmony. ``However, I
refused to allow sheep testicles on the menu.''
<p>
   Carmony, his campaign manager, was covered in tulle for the
noontime Catholic ceremony two weeks ago in a 12th-century stone
church in a small town in the French Pyrenees. Afterward, the
congressman donned a beret.
<p>
   The reception featured traditional Basque fare of sangria, pheasant
and wild boar and ``almost as many toasts to Ronald Reagan as to the
bride and groom,'' said one guest. The wedding cake, similar to the
French
 &UR; croquembouche &LR; , was described by the groom as tasting like ``doughnuts
filled with cream.'' Carmony has Basque relatives in both Spain and
France. Rep. Chris Cox, R-Calif., chairman of the House Republican
Policy Committee, was one of the 60 guests.
<p>
   Carmony, 27, is not totally in the clear on felony counts of
election fraud in a Republican state assembly race. A four-week trial
held earlier this year resulted in a hung jury, and a mistrial was
declared. A new trial is scheduled for Dec. 1.
<p>
   ``She's still got some legal hurdles to jump,'' the 50-year-old
Rohrabacher said Tuesday from California. ``But we're confident that
if it goes to trial again we'll be successful.''
<p>
   For their honeymoon, the newlywed surfers tied their boards to the
top of their rental car and cruised the coasts of Spain and Portugal.
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   White House social secretary Ann Stock, who resigns this month, is
gliding on over to the Kennedy Center for a new job. ``It's an odd
title  &MD;  the vice president of institutional relations,'' she said.
``It means all of their special events, the office of protocol, board
relations and press relations. Oh, and being in charge of all the gift
shops. It takes in all the things that I am interested in, and I'm
excited.''
<p>
   KenCen President Larry Wilker said Stock will be working to raise
the national profile of the performing arts giant, which has programs
and productions in all 50 states. ``We feel very lucky and very
honored to be able to attract her.''
<p>
   Some of Stock's relationships with board members are quite well
developed: She and Chairman Jim Johnson have been close friends since
working together on Walter Mondale's 1984 presidential campaign.
<p>
   The new gig brings more money. ``Oh, boy  &MD;  anything would be a
raise,'' Stock said. ``Remember my salary: The more you work, the less
I make. I make about 89 cents an hour.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>
