<SLUG> bc-diana-security </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1710 09-02 0698 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-security - a1710
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Robert A. Erlandson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Highly Trained Security Would Have Avoided Accident, Expert Says &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Princess Diana would still be alive if she had allowed Scotland
Yard to protect her, an international security consultant said
Tuesday.
<p>
   ``The accident was completely avoidable,'' said Robert L. Oatman,
speaking from London. Oatman, whose main office is in Towson, Md.,
arrived from Paris a day before the fatal high-speed crash in a
Seine-side tunnel that killed Diana; her boyfriend, Dodi Fayed,
<p>
   and chauffeur Henri Paul, who French authorities say was drunk at
the wheel.
<p>
   Although Diana used Scotland Yard's highly trained Royalty
Protection Group when she was with her sons, Princes William, 15, and
Harry, 12, she refused it in her private capacity in her determination
to be independent from the royal family since her divorce from Prince
Charles last year.
<p>
   ``It never would have happened if she had not done that,'' Oatman
said. ``The driver would have been stone sober and he would have been
a skilled professional. The RPG is the best in the world, they're a
model to be around.
<p>
   ``The close-in protection officer would have had her interests
first in mind, he wouldn't have jeopardized her life under any
circumstances. He would have automatically requested help from French
police, who don't tolerate this (paparazzi) nonsense.''
<p>
   In the fatal accident, it appears that the private security
officers around Diana and Fayed broke the basic rules of automobile
security, said Oatman, who has been conferring with French and British
security specialists to promote his new book, ``The Art of Executive
Protection,'' which includes a chapter on automobile security.
<p>
   The area of the tunnel in central Paris where the crash occurred is
heavily policed, said Oatman, 49, who retired eight years ago as chief
of detectives in the Baltimore County Police Department to form his
executive-security agency.
<p>
   Oatman has trained more than 1,000 executive-security specialists
for such companies as Disney, General Motors, Exxon and Federal
Express.
<p>
   Scotland Yard had expressed concern at Diana's decision to abandon
its protection and members of Parliament Tuesday raised questions
about her protection.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Peter Luff, a Conservative member of Parliament, said, ``The
question that needs to be asked is how the mother of a future king was
allowed to be driven by someone over the limit. It is all very well to
protect people from the IRA, but we should be able to protect them
also from being driven by drunkards, a much more mundane but, as it
turned out, fatal threat.''
<p>
   Why didn't Diana have proper protection ``is one of those questions
which we will not get an answer to,'' said Lawrie Quinn, a Labor
member of Parliament.
<p>
   Without official protection, Oatman said, Diana was bound to find
herself eventually in the hands of people unequipped to handle such a
highly charged situation.
<p>
   The reported circumstances of the chase from the Ritz Hotel to the
crash in the tunnel make it appear that Paul was virtually challenging
the pursuing motorcycle-borne paparazzi, Oatman said.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   ``They should never have gone to the speeds they did. The security
officer in the passenger seat should have telephoned to the police
that they were being chased by motorcycles and that he had Princess
Diana in the car. The police would have been able to stop it,'' Oatman
said.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   The only survivor of the crash, Trevor Rees-Jones, another security
man, is still in intensive care in a Paris hospital with head and
internal injuries; he reportedly wore a seat belt. Rees-Jones, a
paratrooper who served two army tours in Northern Ireland, gained
experience working with military police as a close-protection guard
for VIPs who were possible terrorist targets.
<p>
   Investigators are awaiting Rees-Jones' description of events,
particularly of how a drunken man came to be at the wheel and why he
wasn't ordered to stop, or at least, to slow down.
</TEXT>

bc-diana-funeral - a1730
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL;

<BYLINE> By Michael Slackman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Editors: In 2nd graf, St. James's Palace is cq. &QL; 
 &UR; Diana's Funeral Procession Expected to Draw 1 Million (London) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LONDON  &MD;  Buckingham Palace conceded Tuesday that it was caught off
guard by the massive and seemingly endless public display of grief for
Princess Diana.
<p>
   The first acknowledgment came in the early morning Tuesday, when
thousands of people continued to wait in line for up to seven hours to
sign five condolence books set up in the lobby of St. James's Palace.
<p>
   Before the sun rose, the queen's staff increased the number of
condolence books to 15.
<p>
   ``Obviously, we didn't anticipate this,'' said a palace spokesman.
``We would have put more books out in the first place.''
<p>
   By midday, about 300 people an hour were signing the 15 condolence
books.
<p>
   But the backpedaling by the palace continued late into the day as a
controversy erupted over the relatively short one-mile route the
cortege will take on its way to Westminster Abbey for the funeral.
When Diana and Prince Charles were married, more than 1 million people
turned out to view the two-mile procession. With officials saying
Tuesday they expect the funeral to draw a crowd as large, if not
larger, they acknowledged that the likely congestion prompted concerns
for public safety.
<p>
   After defending the length of the route, and insisting it would not
be changed, the palace pointed out that spectators could also view the
casket after the service during its 60-mile-ride to the burial site in
Great Brington.
<p>
   ``When you think about it, the procession to Westminster Abbey is
just the first part,'' said the palace spokesman. ``After that, people
can see the casket in the car.''
<p>
   Asked if the casket would be carried in an open hearse, the
spokesman said: ``That detail hasn't been worked out yet.''
<p>
   The palace also said it was considering allowing people to stand in
the street, to make even more room.
<p>
   In response to growing public pressure for some kind of national
expression of grief, the palace announced late Tuesday that
immediately after the 11 a.m. funeral, expected to last no more than
50 minutes, there will be a formal minute of silence across Britain.
<p>
   The palace also said it plans to invite 500 representatives of the
charitable groups Diana worked with to participate in the procession.
Many are expected to be children and the disabled.
<p>
   Also announced Tuesday: an official charity fund in memory of the
princess, to be distributed to charities associated with her.
Administrators said checks should be made out to: ``The Diana,
Princess of Wales Memorial Fund'' and sent to Kensington Palace,
London, W8 4PU, United Kingdom. Messages of condolence can be sent to
the same address. The law firm representing Diana, Mishcon de Reya,
said donations to the fund could also be sent to: P.O. Box 1, London,
WC1B 5HW, United Kingdom.
<p>
   To further help satisfy the intense public interest in the funeral,
officials said they will allow television cameras in the church, as
well as closed-circuit cameras to broadcast to a large screen that
will be erected outside the Abbey. Speakers also will be set up in the
street outside the service.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   The controversy over details of the funeral, most of which have
been eclipsed by the expressions of grief, began with the decision not
to give Princess Diana a state funeral, which entails a public viewing
and public burial.
<p>
   While Princess Diana was technically no longer royalty, because of
her divorce last year from Prince Charles, public sentiment was strong
for full state obsequies. Instead, the palace crafted what it called a
``unique funeral for a unique person,'' that included elements of the
state funeral.
<p>
   Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-tv-carmody </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0945 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-tv-carmody
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Entertainment editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By John Carmody </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Walters, Downs to Co-Anchor Thursday `20/20' (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  ABC News finally got around to announcing Tuesday that
Barbara Walters and Hugh Downs will co-anchor the new Thursday edition
of ``20/20'' starting Sept. 11.
<p>
   The Friday edition starring Babs and Hugh returns for a new season
the next evening.
<p>
   The first edition of ``20/20'' premiered on a Tuesday in 1978; the
next year the show moved to Thursday and it became a Friday fixture 10
Septembers ago.
<p>
   Downs joined the program as sole anchor  &MD;  saved it, some say  &MD;  on
June 13, 1978, the week after a disastrous premiere anchored by Harold
Hayes and Robert Hughes. They were cashiered almost immediately by an
embarrassed Roone Arledge, then the new president of ABC News.
<p>
   Walters came on board as co-host in September 1984.
<p>
   ``I'm just delighted Hugh and I are both doing it,'' she said
Tuesday, adding that she and Downs ``had a warm hug'' earlier in the
day after Hugh signed, as Barbara had earlier, an extension of their
current contracts. ``I wouldn't want to do the show without him,'' she
added.
<p>
   Walters would not discuss terms of the extensions but said both she
and Downs ``hope to stay for the duration.''
<p>
   There had been some speculation that Walters/Downs might share the
second night anchor chores with others as the broadcast year went on
but that apparently isn't going to happen  &MD;  at least not this first
season as ABC News hopes to make inroads into NBC's Thursday night
audience.
<p>
   Aware that the new ``20/20'' is up against NBC's top-rated ``ER''
in the 10 o'clock timeslot, Walters said, ``We're all predicting a
long climb'' to ratings parity. She expects that ``over time there'll
be an audience for us out there.''
<p>
   She dismissed suggestion that the new lineup of ``PrimeTime Live''
on Wednesdays plus ``20/20'' on each of the next two nights was
borrowing a page from NBC News's successful package of four
``Datelines'' a week.
<p>
   `` `Dateline' was copied from us,'' she pointed out.
<p>
   Walters will co-anchor this Friday's edition of ``20/20'' from
London, where she's flying Wednesday as she and Peter Jennings prepare
to cover the funeral of Princess Diana on Saturday.
<p>
   After checking with executive producer Victor Neufeld, Walters said
next week's debut will be ``a normal show'' with, so far anyway, no
special guests to hype the premiere.
<p>
   She also said Elizabeth Vargas, who is now principally assigned to
``PrimeTime Live,'' will contribute to the Thursday edition, along
with other members of the ``20/20'' correspondents team: Bob Brown,
Arnold Diaz, Deborah Roberts, Tom Jarriel, Lynn Sherr and John
Stossel, plus chief investigative correspondent Brian Ross.
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   Joan Lunden, who has co-hosted ABC's ``Good Morning America'' since
1980, is stepping down after Friday morning's broadcast but she isn't
sure what the farewell broadcast, sure to be stuffed with tributes,
will bring.
<p>
   ``It's somewhat of a secret,'' she said Tuesday. ``I don't really
know. There's no script and it's kind of nice to have it that way.''
<p>
   Co-host Charlie Gibson, who was in London earlier in the week
covering the Princess Diana story, will be in New York to help with
auld lang syne.
<p>
   Lunden, of course, is not saying goodbye to ABC News. She has five
prime-time specials for the network scheduled over the next 18 months
 &MD;  ``certainly a body of work to do''  &MD;  and she says, ``To be honest,
I've been incredibly consumed with planning the next leg of this
journey for me.''
<p>
   The third year of her current three-year contract, which she signed
in February, is ``theoretically'' an option year for both her and the
network and Lunden is frank to admit she's been listening.
<p>
   ``I'm not making any decisions at this time,'' she said, ``but
gratefully I've had a number of inquiries. I don't know what all I'm
going to be doing and, oh, yeah, I'm going to be taking some time
off.''
<p>
   Tacitly responding to tabloid suggestions she's been booted from
the network show, which has slipped far behind NBC's ``Today'' show in
the past 20 months, Lunden said, ``I'm in awe of the fact I've stayed
on this schedule (of 3:30 a.m. wake-up calls) for 20 years, since I
started as a substitute anchor on GMA.
<p>
   ``The schedule is grueling and in the last couple of contract
negotiations I told them I wanted to move on. But they weren't ready.
But they knew this last contract how much I needed a change.
<p>
   ``I need normalcy in my life as a mother of children 17, 14 and 10
years old; they're not going to be children much longer. I want to be
there with them when they wake up in the morning.''
<p>
   ``I love the show; it's a wonderful show, ABC and Charlie and the
staff have afforded me the most extraordinary privilege and it's with
sadness I'm leaving those I've worked with.
<p>
   ``As wonderful as it's been, I'll be frank, I'm excited and
intrigued by whatever the future may bring.''
<p>
   Responding again to the questions she's been repeatedly asked in
recent weeks, Lunden said, ``Have I been offered several sitcoms? Yes.
Have I been offered 20 magazine programs? Yes. Am I continuing those
conversations? Yes. Am I taking acting lessons? Yes. And I'm also
taking an oriental-cooking class. I'm going to go (to other career
choices) prepared. . .
<p>
   ``I'm in a rare position, I'm benefiting from years and years of
hard labor. Perhaps I'll be remaking my career. If I put my toe in the
water it's because I find that it'll be an incredible opportunity. But
whether it's going to lead anywhere I don't know.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-spy </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0902 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-spy
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors)(includes optional trim) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Fern Shen </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Memorial for Cold War Espionage Vets &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   If Vietnam veterans have felt invisible because their war was
unpopular, Cold War espionage veterans have been invisible because
their conflict was, except for the rhetoric, fought in utter secrecy.
<p>
   Consider the aerial reconnaissance specialists who flew over the
Soviet Union and China during the 1950s, '60s and '70s: They couldn't
breathe a word of their missions to wives or loved ones, and when they
disappeared, relatives never quite knew what happened to them, whether
they were dead or alive or if their endeavors had been worth the
terrible price they paid.
<p>
   ``I continued to hope he was alive. ... I have written to every
administration since I first got the telegram, trying to get some
answers,'' said Teresa Durkin, of Levittown, Pa., whose brother,
Master Sgt. George P. Petrochilos was one of 17 crew members of a
C-130A aircraft shot down in 1958 over Soviet Armenia.
<p>
   But Durkin and other relatives of Cold War-era reconnaissance
veterans found a measure of closure and relief as they attended an
emotional ceremony Tuesday near the National Security Agency in
Maryland, dedicating the Aerial Reconnaissance Memorial and National
Vigilance Park.
<p>
   In a public gathering unusual for the highly secretive agency,
about 2,000 people gathered to watch NSA officials recognize ``those
who served in silence.''
<p>
   ``We are taking a major step to publicly recognize and remember the
sacrifices and dedication of aerial reconnaissance servicemen and
women from all branches of the armed forces,'' said Lt. Gen. Kenneth
A. Minihan, director of the NSA.
<p>
   The centerpiece of the memorial is C-130 aircraft refurbished to
resemble the C-130A, which in 1958 strayed inadvertently into Soviet
airspace and was shot down. Surrounding the memorial on Route 32, just
east of the Interstate 295 interchange in Anne Arundel County, Md.,
are 18 trees  &MD;  each symbolizing a type of reconnaissance aircraft lost
during the Cold War.
<p>
   A plaque on the grassy site pays tribute both to ``the 17 airmen
who perished that day'' and to ``their armed forces compatriots who
were killed, injured, taken prisoner or unaccounted for in other
incidents while performing a mission vital to America's security.''
<p>
   The NSA estimates that 152 intelligence specialists, from all
branches of the armed services, died during the Cold War, at least
one-third of them during aerial reconnaissance missions. It is unclear
how many others  &MD;  pilots or others supplying support services  &MD;  lost
their lives in Cold War espionage.
<p>
   Some at the ceremony wept or covered their eyes during the
emotional ceremonies, which included a mournful bagpipe dirge and a
``missing man formation'' flown by the Maryland Air National Guard's
135th Airlift Squadron.
<p>
   ``I lost my best friend, Harry Camps,'' said Lincoln Gilbert, who
was a Serbo-Croatian linguist serving in the same unit as the men who
died in the 1958 incident.
<p>
   After a speaker read the names of the 17 men on board, including
six whose bodies were never found, they were posthumously awarded the
Air Medal. Family members accepted the medals and memorial flags.
<p>
   ``These gallant warriors were engaged in Cold War intelligence
operations absolutely crucial to the security of the United States of
America and the Free World,'' their dedication read, in part.
<p>
   Like most of the other family members present, Durkin was never
quite sure what her brother was doing but guessed it might be
espionage.
<p>
   ``My husband used to tease him all the time, saying if they ever
throw you off the back of the Orient Express, don't expect us to come
get you,'' Durkin said after the ceremony, her eyes still wet. Durkin
kept all the letters and documents she ever received about her
brother's disappearance. The National Cryptologic Museum, which is
adjacent to the park, has a display that includes her nearly
40-year-old letter from President Dwight D. Eisenhower, saying that he
was unable to obtain any information on the 1958 incident from Soviet
leader Nikita Khrushchev.
<p>
    &UR; -(optional trim to end)- &QC; 
<p>
   Some family members seemed resentful that it had taken the
government this long to honor those who had fallen.
<p>
   ``How could they not be bitter?'' said Robert Byrne, who flew in
the same unit as the 17 who were lost. Others complained of the
still-unanswered questions. The Department of Defense has said that
declassified Soviet documents released by Russian President Boris
Yeltsin show that all crew members aboard perished. But even the
program for Tuesday's ceremony noted that ``the Pentagon's Office of
POW/MIA affairs is still investigating this case.''
<p>
   But even if the dedication of the park was largely a symbolic
gesture, some in attendance viewed it as a worthwhile first step.
<p>
   ``It's important symbolism  &MD;  bringing this work out into the open,
recognizing it,'' said Larry Tart, a retired Air Force sergeant and
Russian linguist who flew reconnaissance missions and was the prime
lobbyist for the memorial. Since Tart, of State College, Pa., began
researching these matters, family members have thanked him.
<p>
   ``Growing up, I thought my dad was doing something dirty,'' one
woman told him.``But now I'm proud of him; I know he was doing
something important.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-albert </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0676 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-albert
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Brooke A. Masters </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Albert Defense Seeks Dismissal, Citing &QL; 
 &UR; Juror-Prosecutor Link &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   ARLINGTON, Va.  &MD;  Lawyers for NBC sportscaster Marv Albert Tuesday
asked a judge to dismiss sexual assault charges against Albert because
one of the grand jurors who indicted him managed the 1995 election
campaign of Arlington's chief prosecutor.
<p>
   Albert's lawyers also want the judge to declare Virginia's sodomy
law an unconstitutional invasion of privacy, an attack that could have
broad consequences if it moves up through local and federal courts,
privacy experts said.
<p>
   The constitutional challenge also paves the way for the
sportscaster to argue that he had consensual sexual contact with the
woman from Vienna, Va., who says he bit her and forced her to have
oral sex in an Arlington hotel.
<p>
   Law professors and defense attorneys said Virginia and federal laws
are stacked against the Albert team's effort to get the indictment
thrown out based on an allegation of a possible conflict of interest
involving one of the six grand jurors. But some experts said the
challenge to the sodomy law could go much further because the U.S.
Supreme Court's 1986 ruling on Georgia's similar law is ripe for a
challenge.
<p>
   In motions unsealed Tuesday, Albert's lawyers, Roy Black and Peter
Greenspun, complained about Arlington's procedures for screening
potential grand jurors and about ``the obvious taint and impropriety
of including'' Amy Levine, who managed Commonwealth's Attorney Richard
E. Trodden's campaign. ``Such a close relationship . . . gives an
appearance of bias,'' they wrote.
<p>
   Levine and Margaret Levinson, another member of the grand jury that
indicted Albert on forcible sodomy and assault charges, both declined
to comment. Trodden's office also declined to comment, citing an
informal gag rule.
<p>
   But legal analysts said Virginia and federal laws make it very
difficult to get an indictment dismissed on the grounds that one juror
had a relationship with the prosecutor, in part because grand jury
decisions do not have to be unanimous.
<p>
   ``In Virginia, the grand jury is a giant rubber stamp, and a nick
in that stamp is not going to be a problem,'' defense attorney David
Baugh said.
<p>
   To succeed, Albert's lawyers ``would be required to go the
additional step and show that the relationship (between the juror and
Trodden) was responsible for the indictment,'' defense attorney Blair
Howard said. ``In small towns, there have to be grand jurors who know
prosecutors all the time.''
<p>
   The constitutional challenge to Virginia's sodomy law could be
crucial to Albert's defense if he chooses to argue that the sexual
contact was consensual. All oral sex is illegal in Virginia, and a
jury that votes against forcible sodomy still could find Albert guilty
of consensual sodomy.
<p>
   In 1986, the U.S. Supreme Court upheld the Georgia law, which
applies to heterosexuals and homosexuals, in Bowers vs. Hardwick, but
the written decision referred almost entirely to homosexuals, which
makes its application to heterosexual partners somewhat ambiguous,
experts said.
<p>
   ``An attack on sodomy laws . . . is not frivolous because of the
growing sensitivity to sexual privacy,'' said Rodney Smolla, a law
professor at the College of William and Mary.
<p>
   And George Washington University law professor Jonathan Turley
said, ``If Virginia fought to protect the prohibition on heterosexual
sodomy . . . I believe they would not be able to eke out a 5-4
majority.''
<p>
   Of course, that's a long way off because Albert's case is still in
a local court, the analysts cautioned, but officials at the American
Civil Liberties Union's Virginia chapter said they've been looking for
a case to challenge the state sodomy law.
<p>
   The Albert team also asked Arlington Circuit Court Judge Benjamin
N.A. Kendrick to let them strike more jurors than usual and prevent
the prosecutor from running criminal checks on potential jurors.
Kendrick will hear the motions Thursday. &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-times </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1746 09-02 1180 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-times - a1746
 &UR; (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
 &UR; (Editors: Language in 12th graf may offend some readers.) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By John-Thor Dahlburg </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Court Examines 7 Crash-Scene Photographers for Manslaughter (Paris) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   PARIS  &MD;  Photographers who snapped pictures of the dying Diana were
paraded in front of a French magistrate Tuesday and placed under
formal suspicion of involuntary manslaughter and of failing to come to
the aid of the mortally wounded princess.
<p>
   Two of the photographers, Romuald Rat of the Gamma photo agency and
Christian Martinez of Angeli, were ordered to post 100,000-franc
($16,400) bonds and were temporarily barred from exercising their
profession or leaving France.
<p>
   According to a source close to the case, Rat and Martinez were
among the first photographers to arrive after the high-speed crash of
Diana's limousine in a Paris tunnel, and instead of helping her,
``hindered the actions of rescuers.'' Prosecutors had asked the
magistrate, Investigating Judge Herve Stephan, to jail the two.
<p>
   While in custody, Rat reportedly told police he had taken the pulse
of the injured princess to see if she was dead.
<p>
   The suspects  &MD;  six photographers and an agency motorcycle driver  &MD; 
had been detained at the Palace of Justice in the heart of Paris since
Diana's death early Sunday morning. The offenses for which they are
being investigated  &MD;  ``involuntary homicide and wounding'' and
``non-assistance to a person in danger''  &MD;  both carry
three-to-five-year prison terms and large fines.
<p>
   Tuesday's decision provoked anger among many of the photographers'
colleagues and supporters, who claimed the men were only doing their
jobs by taking photos when the 36-year-old Diana's limousine hit a
concrete post, mortally wounding her and killing her rich suitor, Dodi
Fayed.
<p>
   ``If it's clear they (the suspects) don't want me to take their
pictures, I won't, out of professional solidarity,'' Aurelie Audureau,
a staff photographer for the newspaper Le Parisien, pledged as she
waited outside the Palace of Justice with 150 other journalists for
news of the detainees.
<p>
   William Bourdon, attorney for one of the suspects, Nicolas Arsov of
the Sipa agency, charged that French officials ``want to please public
opinion and the Quai d'Orsay (French Foreign Ministry).'' He said his
client and the others have become ``scapegoats'' for the sake of
``show-biz justice.''
<p>
   All seven men were freed Tuesday night after being quizzed, one by
one, by Stephan. In French legal jargon, the photographers were placed
``under examination.''
<p>
   The judge, after sifting testimony and evidence, will decide
whether to formally charge them and send them to a court for trial. It
is not clear whether the double-barreled charge submitted to the
magistrate by prosecutors applied to all seven, and there were reports
of disagreements over the charges between Stephan and prosecutors.
<p>
   The Mercedes-Benz in which Diana was riding, driven at high speed
by a Ritz Hotel security guard who official sources said Monday was
legally drunk, had been trailed by a number of photographers on
motorcycles after it left the Ritz. On Tuesday evening, the French
television channel TF1 reported that investigators had found no proof
the pursuing pack had interfered with the movements of the Mercedes.
<p>
   However, a police report leaked to the media said ``virulent''
photographers did hinder police trying to give the princess and other
passengers emergency aid after it crashed. ``You're pissing me off,''
one photographer reportedly shouted to one of the first officers to
arrive.
<p>
   The policeman, unable to ``contain the photographers and bring help
to the wounded,'' called for backup, the report said.
<p>
   Unlike the suspects examined Tuesday by Stephan, other
photographers present were able to get away with their film before
police could grab them and their cameras. Pro Sieben, a private German
television channel, said it would broadcast an interview with one of
them, a Frenchman, Wednesday. According to that photographer, who was
not identified, limo driver Henri Paul, 41, who also died in the
accident, exclaimed to photographers at one point before the crash,
``Tonight you won't catch us.''
<p>
   ``The car ran a red light at top speed and swayed several times,''
the photographer said in a summary of the interview. ``We were
following about 200 meters (yards) from the car; nobody was in
front.''
<p>
   ``When we heard the noise of the accident, we first thought of a
terrorist attack, because it sounded like the explosion of a bomb. In
the tunnel, it was a real massacre. In a state of shock, I pushed on
the button of my camera. I did not offer assistance. And then I
left.''
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   The actions of some photographers have sparked intense criticism of
celebrity-chasing paparazzi in the press of France and elsewhere,
along with calls for tougher laws to protect privacy. A Danish
newspaper denounced Diana's pursuers as ``pigs,'' and Le Figaro, a
Paris daily, called them ``rats.''
<p>
   ``There are paparazzi, and there are photographers; one must not
confuse them,'' maintained Jean-Francois Leroy, director of a
photojournalism festival that opened last weekend in Perpignan, in
southern France.
<p>
   But the bosses of some of the suspects Tuesday strongly denied
their employees are money-hungry stalkers of movie stars, royals or
other celebrities. One detained photographer, Jacques Langevin, is a
photojournalist for the Sygma agency accredited to the French
presidential palace and a respected veteran of wars and unrest in
Rwanda, Zaire, and Romania, where he was wounded in 1989. Langevin
also took exclusive photos of the Chinese military crackdown on
students in Beijing's Tiananmen Square.
<p>
   ``He is everything but a paparazzo,'' Sygma editor-in-chief Thierry
Secretan said. After Diana and Fayed left the Ritz, Langevin went home
in his own car, coincidentally taking the same route, Secretan said.
<p>
   ``So he happened to be on the scene just as police arrived. He took
photos because that is his job, but he arrived on the scene several
minutes after the crash,'' the Sygma editor said.
<p>
   At Sipa, President Goksin Sipahioglu said Arsov was a general
assignment photographer who, six months ago, was promoted from his job
as motorcycle driver. He was not part of the mounted pack pursuing
Diana's car, Sipahioglu said, but arrived ``5-10 minutes'' after the
crash.
<p>
   ``Of course he took photos; that is his job,'' the Sipa chief said.
``We will not publish those photos, but if he had come back to the
agency and hadn't taken photos, I would have asked him why, of
course.''
<p>
   A medical source said Tuesday it could be ``many weeks'' before
Trevor Rees-Jones, the British bodyguard who was the sole survivor of
the crash, can help police or the investigating judge determine
exactly why Diana and the others died. A lawyer for Fayed's family
said Rees-Jones is now conscious and out of danger, but that his face
has been ``entirely crushed.''
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Mark Gladstone </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Testing of Kaczynski by Government Psychiatrist Sought (Sacramento) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   SACRAMENTO, Calif.  &MD;  Federal prosecutors contended Tuesday that a
government psychiatrist should be allowed to examine Unabomber suspect
Theodore J. Kaczynski to determine his mental condition.
<p>
   The request is in response to statements by lawyers for the former
University of California, Berkeley, mathematician, who have indicated
that they intend to seek testimony from their own experts about
Kaczynski's mental condition.
<p>
   In arguments before U.S. District Court Judge Garland E. Burrell
Jr., however, defense lawyers opposed the prosecution's request,
describing it as a violation of Kaczynski's Fifth Amendment right
against self-incrimination.
<p>
   Burrell took the arguments under submission. He did not indicate
when he will make a decision.
<p>
   Defense lawyers said they are willing to provide reports on their
client's mental condition by Nov. 3, just nine days before the trial
is scheduled to begin. Kaczynski's lawyers said they are not required
to make the material available any sooner.
<p>
   Kaczynski, 55, is charged with four bombings, including two blasts
in Sacramento  &MD;  one that killed a computer store owner in 1985 and one
that killed a timber industry executive in 1995. He has pleaded not
guilty.
<p>
   Assistant U.S. Attorney J. Douglas Wilson told Burrell that
prosecutors do not want to wait until the eve of the trial to obtain
information about Kaczynski's mental state. He described it as ``too
late a date for us.''
<p>
   ``There is a wide range of mental disorders that could be at issue
here,'' Wilson said. ``We're asking for a simple and short statement
of the kind of disorder they seek to rely on. It doesn't have to be
detailed.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-donate-gore-temple </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1058 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-donate-gore-temple
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Peter Baker </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Gore Staff Saw Signs of Trouble Before &QL; 
 &UR; Temple Appearance (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  The White House mounted a detailed defense Tuesday of
Vice President Al Gore and his appearance at a Buddhist temple
fund-raiser just two days before congressional hearings into campaign
finance improprieties enter a new stage with the spotlight focused
squarely on Gore.
<p>
   Officials released a series of internal memos, electronic messages
and briefing papers intended to prove that Gore was unaware at the
time that the 1996 luncheon at the Hsi Lai Temple in California was a
fund-raising event.
<p>
   But the documents offered ample evidence that, at a minimum, Gore's
staff ignored numerous warning signs that could have averted what has
become one of the major embarrassments of Gore's career. And one
e-mail message from Gore six weeks before the event made clear that he
expected to attend a Los Angeles fund-raiser on that date, although
aides said the temple was not the planned location at that point.
<p>
   The White House is deeply concerned about the possible effect of
this week's hearings on Gore, who is positioning himself as the heir
apparent to President Clinton. When the Senate Governmental Affairs
Committee reconvenes Thursday, the featured witnesses will be two
Buddhist nuns slated to testify that they were used to launder money
to the Democratic National Committee in violation of federal law.
Although there is no evidence tying Gore to the arrangement, the
administration fears he will be linked by association because he
attended the luncheon.
<p>
   ``That's the nuclear issue,'' said one White House official. ``That
could kill his presidential campaign.'' Nationally televised testimony
from innocent-looking nuns exploited by political operatives in an
illegal fund-raising scheme, the official said, could become the
searing image that stays with the voting public.
<p>
   In providing a point-by-point briefing on the vice president's
involvement with the temple Tuesday, the White House intended to send
a warning to Senate Republicans not to try to tar Gore with the
testimony of the nuns. ``We're not going to tolerate any innuendo on
that,'' the official said.
<p>
   Gore is an inviting target for Republicans, not only because
Clinton will never again be on the ballot but because the vice
president's once-squeaky clean image has been tarnished by the temple
episode, as well as by reports about his telephone solicitations from
the White House. Wielding the gavel this week will be Sen. Fred D.
Thompson, a fellow Tennessean and possible Republican presidential
candidate in 2000.
<p>
   In addition to the nuns, the committee plans to call at least one
senior Gore aide to testify this week, either deputy chief of staff
David Strauss or scheduling director Kimberly H. Tilley. No requests
have been made for an appearance by Gore, and aides said Tuesday that
he has no intention of testifying.
<p>
   Gore's visit to the red-pillared, pagoda-style Hsi Lai Temple in
Hacienda Heights, Calif., on April 29, 1996, has emerged as one of the
most memorable moments in the multiple campaign fund-raising
investigations. Although Gore initially characterized the visit as a
``community outreach event,'' about $140,000 was raised at the
luncheon, and temple leaders reimbursed $58,000 to 12 nuns and
devotees who wrote checks to the DNC. Federal law prohibits financing
contributions in someone else's name, and the DNC has acknowledged it
was inappropriate to collect cash in a religious building.
<p>
   The nuanced account the White House provided Tuesday largely
squares with Gore's previous statements, even though aides conceded
the account did not make the vice president or his staff look good.
<p>
   While the documents produced no new evidence proving Gore knew
money would change hands at the temple, they leave little doubt that
the event was inextricably tied to the party's fund-raising campaign.
<p>
   A briefing memo prepared by DNC finance officials and given to Gore
the day of the event noted that those attending were members of an
Asian American donor council organized by DNC fund-raiser John Huang
that council members were required to pay annual membership dues of
$2,500. The memo instructed the vice president that his ``role and
contribution'' was to thank the council members for their past support
``and inspire political and fund-raising efforts among the Asian
Pacific American Community.''
<p>
   Aides contrasted that memo with briefing memos for other Gore
events that were ``formal fund-raisers,'' noting those papers
explicitly stated financial goals. They also produced talking points
prepared for Gore on the day of the temple visit that show overt
references to campaign cash at a separate fund-raiser and no such
mention at Hsi Lai.
<p>
   Others at the White House knew the temple stop was a fund-raiser,
however. On April 11, Huang wrote a memo to Tilley on the event titled
``Fund-raising lunch for Vice President Gore.'' And on April 24, John
B. Emerson, deputy director of intergovernmental affairs at the White
House, sent an electronic message outlining Gore's California schedule
that included the reference, ``DNC funder for lunch.''
<p>
   In addition, officials said one aide traveling with Gore that day,
then-press secretary Peggy Wilhide, believed at the time that the
temple event was a fund-raiser, but they said it was not her job to
brief Gore and that she never raised the matter with him.
<p>
   The confusion arose in part because at one point a full-fledged
fund-raiser was planned for Southern California that day in addition
to one in San Jose. Gore referred to these in a March 15 e-mail reply
to Tilley, who asked whether he wanted to accept another invitation.
<p>
   ``If we have already booked the fundraisers, then we have to
decline,'' Gore wrote.
<p>
   On the same day he sent that message, as it happens, Gore met with
the temple's venerable master, Hsing Yung, at Huang's request in what
aides describe as a 10-minute courtesy call. The cleric invited Gore
to visit the temple, and the vice president replied that he would like
to do that.
<p>
   Officials said Huang later canceled the other Los Angeles-area
fund-raiser for logistical reasons and suggested Gore go to the temple
instead. &QL; 
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By George Ramos </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Two Mexican Mafia Members Sentenced to Life in Prison (Los Angeles) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LOS ANGELES  &MD;  Two members of the Mexican Mafia  &MD;  convicted in May
after a 7-month federal trial that prosecutors hailed as a serious
blow against the secretive prison gang  &MD;  were sentenced Tuesday to
life in prison without the possibility of parole.
<p>
   True to the code of silence in which members deny the
organization's very existence, Randy ``Cowboy'' Therrien, 37, of
Moreno Valley, stoically looked straight ahead and said nothing when
U.S. District Judge Ronald S.W. Lew sentenced him to life imprisonment
after a 2½-hour hearing.
<p>
   Later in the day, David ``Smilon'' Gallardo, 37, of Los Angeles,
also said nothing in his defense before sentencing. But he reiterated
a request that Lew marry him and his girlfriend. ``I ask you once
again (to conduct the marriage),'' Gallardo asked the judge. ``That's
all I have left.''
<p>
   But Lew, who has been a federal judge in Los Angeles since 1986,
rejected the marriage request, explaining he conducted one such
ceremony when he was a Los Angeles Superior Court judge 13 years ago.
``I will never do it again,'' Lew told Gallardo. ``That to me was
enough.''
<p>
   Gallardo continually smiled at his girlfriend, who was seated in
the back of the courtroom, even as he was being sentenced.
<p>
   It will be left up to officials at the federal prison, where
Gallardo is sent, to decide whether to grant his request, officials
said.
<p>
   Under federal guidelines, the severity of their crimes dictated
that Therrien and Gallardo receive life terms without the possibility
of parole although the judge added 300 months to Gallardo's sentence
because of his past criminal record and other issues.
<p>
   Attorneys for both men said they would appeal the convictions.
<p>
   Therrien and Gallardo were first of 12 Mexican Mafia members to be
sentenced after a jury convicted them of racketeering and conspiracy
charges that alleged they committed murder, extorted money from street
gangs and trafficked illegal drugs to expand the prison gang's
influence in Southern California.
<p>
   Twenty-two persons were initially indicted in 1995 in the
government's case against the Mexican Mafia. Seven pleaded guilty to
lesser charges. One person died before he could be brought to trial
and another is awaiting trial.
<p>
   One defendant was acquitted when the jury returned its verdict on
May 30.
<p>
   The others, including four Wednesday, are scheduled to be sentenced
later this week.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Therrien was convicted for his involvement in the murders of two
Mexican Mafia members who were targeted for death by the organization
 &MD;  Manuel ``Rocky'' Luna in 1993 and Charles ``Charlie Brown''
Manriquez in 1992. He was also convicted of conspiring to kill two
others and conspiring to aid and abet in the distribution of illegal
drugs.
<p>
   Gallardo was also convicted for the murders of Luna and Manriquez.
Additionally, he was found guilty for the death of Hazard gang member
Ricardo ``Rascal'' Gonzalez, trying to kill two others and conspiring
to distribute drugs.
<p>
   While Therrien has only one conviction for receiving stolen
property and served 10 days in San Bernardino County jail in 1995,
Gallardo has been in and out of state prison, where the prison gang
gets most of its members, on convictions for robbery, attempted murder
and assault. Gallardo served time at Pelican Bay, California's
toughest prison, in the early 1990s.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-charity </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1757 09-02 0904 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-charity - a1757
 &UR; (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Richard Boudreaux </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Diana's Acts of Charity May Have Been Vastly Understated (London) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LONDON  &MD;  On a raw winter night in 1994, Princess Diana dropped by a
shelter called Off the Streets to comfort 40 prostitutes, drug addicts
and other homeless Londoners.
<p>
   As they waited, a swaggering 23-year-old told Paul George, the
social worker in charge: ``I don't know about these royals. I think
the IRA should shoot them all. She comes in here, we can give her a
good one.''
<p>
   ``Then Diana walks in, and he's the first person she sees in this
big warehouse,'' George remembers. ``She walks over to him, and I'm
thinking, Oh my God, this is going to be trouble!''
<p>
   ``But then Diana says: `It's Ricky, isn't it? Didn't I meet you
when you were sleeping down in the Strand?' And he just melts. `That's
right,' he says, `I'm getting myself together now ... ''
<p>
   That story is one of many surfacing this week that suggest Diana
touched the sick and the down-and-out far more often and perhaps more
deeply than was publicized during her lifetime. ``It's an example of
how she disarms and charms,'' George said, having trouble using the
past tense.
<p>
   The death of Diana, described by the London newspaper The
Independent as ``probably the most successful fund-raiser on the
planet,'' has set off a flood of new offers to her favorite charities.
In response, Buckingham Palace Tuesday set up an official memorial
fund in her name to collect and administer them.
<p>
   While welcoming that move, Britons involved in these causes said
Diana's true legacy is not the millions she raised with celebrity
lunches and auctions of her fashionable clothes but an irreplaceable
compassion she brought directly to people she was helping.
<p>
   The British-based Leprocy Mission and the National Aids Trust
credit Diana with easing the stigma of those diseases by embracing
sufferers on camera, dispelling the myths that either can be spread by
human contact.
<p>
   She also held publicized benefits for the Centrepoint organization
for the homeless, the English National Ballet, the Great Ormond Street
Hospital for children and the Royal Mardsen NHS Trust cancer treatment
center  &MD;  the few charities she focused on after resigning from 100
others in July 1996, just before her divorce from Prince Charles.
<p>
   But Diana kept most of her work from public view so as not to spoil
its intimate nature.
<p>
   ``There was no financial benefit when she made a private visit,''
said George, whose shelter is one of 14 run by Centrepoint. ``Money is
not what it was about. She wanted to know what life was like for
people less fortunate than herself. And she wanted to make a
difference by showing that she cared.''
<p>
   Diana went incognito to the Strand to visit vagrants sleeping on
the pavement, George said, and a few months ago she brought her two
sons to help prepare a meal and meet people staying at his shelter. He
said these visits often involved elaborate security procedures, such
as changing cars, to throw photographers off her trail.
<p>
   While it was never a secret the princess conducted missions of
comfort out of the limelight, the extent of them is just starting to
become known. A wide range of ordinary Britons whose burdens she
shared now feel free to speak about friendships that, until her death,
had to be confidential.
<p>
   Philip Woolcock, a 45-year-old social worker, told the Daily
Telegraph that he and his wife had carried on a friendship with ``the
real Diana'' since 1991, when the princess first learned through her
charity work that their 18-year-old daughter Louise was dying of
cancer.
<p>
   After comforting the girl with calls and bedsides visits until her
death in 1992, Diana dropped in on the parents and confided, in tears,
that her marriage was falling apart, Woolcock said.
<p>
   ``Judy and I simply couldn't believe that here was the future queen
confiding in us,'' he told the newspaper. ``She spoke to us with such
honesty and compassion. ... That visit made us feel that life was
probably still worth living after our terrible loss.''
<p>
   Other Britons aided by Diana said in interviews that they, too,
felt her all the more empathetic because she did not hide the
suffering in her own life. ``She knows that homelessness can happen to
anyone,'' George said. ``In a sense at some point she must have felt
homeless herself.''
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Among the mourners at Kensington Palace this week was Danielle
Stephenson, 8, whom the princess had visited five times after the girl
had heart surgery at London's Royal Brompton Hospital in May. Diana
had also given the child her direct phone number at the palace and
taken her calls.
<p>
   ``She gave our patients what none of us was able to give  &MD;  this
magic,'' said hospital spokeswoman Averil Slade. ``She came here as
often as three times a week. She'd bounce in unannounced, in jeans and
trainers, sit on people's beds and talk for hours.''
<p>
   Another mourner was Vincent Seabrook, 27, who showed up at the
palace with a memorial plaque tracing his bond with Diana in verse.
<p>
   Seabrook was homeless and shivering in a doorway four years ago, he
recounted, when Diana stopped to give him food and the phone number of
Centrepoint. The two kept in touch, the charity gave him shelter and
he later found work as a security guard.
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Gary Silverman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Dow Gets Biggest-Ever Point Gain (New York) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   NEW YORK  &MD;  The bulls are back  &MD;  but what does it mean?
<p>
   In the first day of trading after its August lull, the stock market
soared Tuesday, with the Dow Jones industrial average rising 257.36
points, its biggest gain ever in point terms. The previous record was
186.84 on Oct. 21, 1987, two days after the Black Monday market crash.
<p>
   In relative terms, the Dow rose 3.4 percent, the most since Jan.
17, 1991, when it surged 4.6 percent as the Allies attacked Baghdad,
Iraq, at the start of the Persian Gulf war.
<p>
   The record gain came after a dismal August in which the Dow fell
600 points, or 7.3 percent, marking the worst month since Iraq invaded
Kuwait in August 1990.
<p>
   But unlike many seismic shifts in the Dow, Tuesday's advance
stemmed from no single event, analysts said.
<p>
   Instead, there were many causes. Money managers returning from
vacations saw blue-chip bargains. Some Asian stock markets rebounded
after days of frightening falls. And the latest economic data
suggested U.S. inflation may not reach worrisome levels.
<p>
   But in one aspect, the market remained consistent, at least to
bearish analysts. As in August, stocks remained volatile, with program
traders and momentum players ready to jump on any bandwagon.
<p>
   For pessimists, that was hardly a sign of health.
<p>
   ``I find this volatility a bit unnerving and symptomatic of a
market that is detached from reality,'' said Michael Metz, chief
investment strategist at Oppenheimer &AMP; Co. in New York.
<p>
   ``The market was a bit stretched as far as pessimism was concerned.
It was entitled to a bounce,'' he said. ``It was an excessive one, but
we live in a world of excess  &MD;  socially, artistically and
financially.''
<p>
   The conditions for the rebound developed as traders in New York
were still asleep. In Southeast Asia, share prices steadied, with Hong
Kong's Hang Seng index, which had fallen 18 percent since July,
snapping a five-day losing streak.
<p>
   At the same time, some investors decided the currency turmoil that
caused the Southeast Asian declines had a good side, since weaker
Asian currencies would cut the cost of Asian goods for Americans and
hold down U.S. inflation, said Joseph Battipaglia, chief strategist at
Gruntal &AMP; Co., a brokerage in New York.
<p>
   The positive tone at the market's opening confounded traders who
had sold shares short, Battipaglia said. Short sellers bet on price
declines; they sell borrowed shares in hopes of buying them back later
at a lower price and pocketing the difference.
<p>
   When prices headed up Tuesday instead of declining, the short
sellers scrambled to ``cover'' their positions, buying back the shares
they had sold and thus pushing prices even higher, said Battipaglia.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-thalidomide </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1804 09-02 0618 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-thalidomide - a1804
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: News, Science editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Delthia Ricks </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; FDA Examines New Use for Risky Drug &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   A drug that caused thousands of birth defects in the 1960s could
return in a big way if the U.S. Food and Drug Administration
recommends the approval of thalidomide during special hearings
Thursday and Friday.
<p>
   The FDA's Dermatologic and Ophthalmic Drugs Advisory Committee will
discuss recommending approval of the drug for leprosy and macular
degeneration, a blinding disorder that primarily affects people 60 and
older.
<p>
   Next week, the National Institutes of Health will sponsor a two-day
meeting Tuesday and Wednesday to discuss the benefits and risks of
using the drug for a growing list of medical disorders.
<p>
   Scientists are predicting that the drug may be recommended for a
narrow range of medical conditions and not the growing list of
disorders that includes prostate cancer and other types of tumors.
<p>
   Thalidomide, a pharmaceutical scourge from the past, has emerged in
clinical studies in recent years as a potential effective treatment
for leprosy, macular degeneration, cancer and the mouth ulcers and
physical wasting associated with AIDS.
<p>
   ``Underlying all of these conditions is a common mechanism ...
which involves the inflammatory processes,'' said Dr. Lawrence Fox of
the division of AIDS at the National Institute of Allergy and
Infectious Diseases.
<p>
   In leprosy, also known as Hansen's disease, prescribing thalidomide
ends a virtual go-for-broke war in the body. Proteins known as
antigens are produced by the infecting bacteria. The body mounts an
inflammatory response by its immune-system warrior cells and proteins.
But warfare against these foreign proteins leads to nerve
inflammation, which in turn causes pain.
<p>
   ``Thalidomide doesn't attack the bacteria,'' said Aloys Cabrera,
nurse coordinator of the Hansen's disease program at Staten Island
University Hospital in New York, ``it eases the pain.'' The hospital
has used the drug in a series of clinical trials since 1989, she said.
It is administered mostly to men with leprosy and women who are past
reproductive age. When taken during the early weeks of pregnancy, the
period when limbs bud, thalidomide can retard the normal development
of arms and legs.
<p>
   Three decades ago, thalidomide caused limb defects in about 12,000
children, born primarily in Europe, where the drug was prescribed to
pregnant women as a sedative and treatment for morning sickness. It
was banned worldwide in 1962. Thalidomide was never approved in the
United States.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   But in an evolving list of medical conditions, it is proving to be
a kind of new-found wonder drug. In the wasting disease associated
with AIDS, for example, a broadly active immune-system molecule called
TNF becomes the hyperactive culprit that throws the body's ability to
use food out of balance. Malnutrition and extreme weight loss are
common in these patients.
<p>
   ``By reducing TNF,'' Fox said of administering thalidomide, ``the
wasting factor is reversed.''
<p>
   In macular degeneration and cancer, the inflammatory responses are
even more dramatic and complex, and thalidomide is said to be
effective against them.
<p>
   The drug still carries the threats it did decades ago. The
difference, said Fox, who will conduct sessions next week on
thalidomide's risks and benefits, is that pharmaceutical companies now
know how to tweak thalidomide's chemical chain to eliminate molecules
that cause fetal defects. Studies to make the drug safer, he said, are
now under way.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-donate-gore-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 1605 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-donate-gore-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Washington editors)(includes optional trim) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Bob Woodward </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Gore Donors' Funds Used as `Hard Money' (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  More than $120,000 in campaign contributions
personally solicited in 1995-96 by Vice President Gore for a ``soft
money'' account not covered by federal election regulations instead
went into a ``hard money'' account subject to federal limits.
<p>
   The money came from at least eight of 46 donors the vice president
telephoned from his White House office to ask for contributions to the
Democratic National Committee, according to records released by Gore's
office.
<p>
   The distinction is significant because Attorney General Janet Reno
has cited the absence of evidence that high-level government officials
sought hard money donations as a key reason not to recommend
appointment of an independent counsel to investigate fund-raising
activities for last year's elections.
<p>
   Soft money is defined as contributions not intended to support
individual candidates but to promote ``party-building'' and other
general campaign activities such as television advertising, and is
largely unregulated. Hard money contributions are highly valued by
campaigns because they can be used directly to benefit individual
candidates. But federal law places specific restrictions on the
solicitation, amount and use of such contributions.
<p>
   Among those restrictions, the law says that such regulated
contributions cannot be solicited on federal property. Thus, Reno has
maintained that because she has seen no evidence regulated
contributions were solicited from the White House  &MD;  or, in the words
she used in an April 14 letter, any White House area ``occupied in the
discharge of official duties''  &MD;  no independent counsel is warranted.
<p>
   A senior White House official said Tuesday that Gore did not ask
for any hard money contributions, and was unaware that money he
solicited was directed into the DNC hard money account.
<p>
   DNC spokeswoman Amy Weiss Tobe said Tuesday night that ``the Vice
President was not aware that money was being designated for the
federal (hard money) account.'' She said that it was ``routine
procedure'' to assign the first $20,000 of a large donation  &MD;  the
legal limit for a hard money contribution  &MD;  to that account and to
deposit the rest into the soft money account, and suggested that had
inadvertently happened with the contributions solicited by Gore.
<p>
   Officials said Tuesday night this practice may bring scrutiny by
the Federal Election Commission because it does not necessarily
reflect the wishes of the donors, who are supposed to designate the
use of the money. Records made available by Gore's office show that
his telephone solicitations resulted in at least $105,000 being
deposit into the DNC's hard money account within days or months after
he solicited the donors.
<p>
   The practice poses a more immediate question for Reno, however, who
has been sharply criticized by senior Republicans for declining to ask
for appointment of an independent counsel.
<p>
   Burt Brandenburg, the Justice Department's chief spokesman, said
Tuesday night that Reno would have no comment on the matter. Reno has
previously said that prosecutors assigned to a Justice Department task
force examining fund-raising activities in last year's elections would
look at any new allegations presented to them.
<p>
   The controversy about Gore's telephone solications arose ealier
this year when he was questioned about calls he made from the White
House seeking contributions for last year's reelection effort.
Although Gore originally acknowledged making a few calls from the
White House for soft-money contributions, the White House last week
acknowledged that he had made 86 calls and reached 46 potential
donors.
<p>
   A separate issue concerning the phone solicitations also arose
again last week when Gore's office said the DNC would reimburse the
U.S. Treasury on June 27 for $24.20 for calls presumably made from
official government phones. Though the sum is small, it establishes
that Gore's attorneys believed that at least 20 calls to potential
donors could not be attributed to credit cards controlled by the
Clinton-Gore campaign or the DNC and that the government therefore had
to be reimburse for the cost of the calls.
<p>
   In March, when the question of Gore's personal telephone
solicitations first became an issue, Gore said the long distance calls
had been made on a DNC credit card. Later, he said the calls were made
on a Clinton-Gore credit card. A review of the phone calls by Gore's
attorneys shows that $58.21 of the calls were billed on a Clinton-Gore
card, none to any DNC credit card and that calls totaling $24.20 were
presumably made on government phones.
<p>
   Throughout the solicitation controveersy, even while defending
themselves against specific allegations, White House officials have
said that laws relating to the use of government properties and
telephones do not apply to the President and Vice President.
<p>
   Records compiled by Gore's attorneys show that Gore was seeking
``soft money'' contributions because he was calling potential donors
for contributions of at least $25,000, which is $5,000 more than the
$20,000 hard-money maximum allowed under federal election law.
<p>
   At the time, the party was almost desperate for hard money because
it could be pumped directly into the Clinton-Gore campaign and used
for advertising and other campaign-specific activities.
<p>
   It is more difficult to raise large amounts of hard money because
of the legal limit. In addition to the $20,000 cap, corporations and
labor unions are barred from giving hard money, which must come from
individuals.
<p>
   It is not clear how the DNC decided to allocate the money raised by
Gore, but his role in soliciting the funds is clear, in some cases,
from the records. For example, on Feb. 1, 1996, the DNC prepared a
sheet for Gore recommending that he call Robert L. Johnson, the head
of Black Enterainment Television, and suggest that the Vice President
``ask him'' for $30,000 for the DNC media campaign.
<p>
   Four days later, on Feb. 5, Gore spoke with Johnson, according to a
letter Gore sent on that date. ``Thank you for your commitment to make
an additional contribution of $30,000,'' the letter said.
<p>
   Then, 11 days later on Feb. 14, the DNC deposited the $30,000 from
Johnson, splitting it into $20,000 for the federally regulated
hard-money account and $10,000 for unregulated soft-money account.
<p>
   Gore's letter raises another possible problem. He wrote that
``President Clinton and I thank you. ... We appreciate your dedication
to our Administation.'' An FEC regulation says that if a party
soliciation includes a ``reference to a federal candidate or a federal
election,'' the ``soliciation shall be subject to'' federal election
law.
<p>
    &UR; -(optional trim to end)- &QC; 
<p>
   Other Gore thank-you letters contain the same language. In another
call sheet prepared for Gore on Feb. 1, 1996, he was aked to call
William B. Dockser, head of CRI Inc., a real estate development firm
based in Rockville, Md. ``Ask him to send in $25,000 to fund the media
campaign,'' a notation on the call sheet said. The thank-you letter
was dated four days later, and it included a reference to President
Clinton, then running for reelection. The $25,000 contributed by
Dockser was split a week later, with $20,000 going to the DNC's hard
money account and $5,000 to the soft money account.
<p>
   Dockser said in an interview Tuesday that the full $25,000 was
supposed to go to the DNC's soft money account. Dockser said the Vice
President called him and that they spoke for about 10 minutes. ``He
talked about the Democratic Party and its needs to counter the
Republican party,'' Dockser said, adding that the discussion ``was
clearly focused on the reelection'' campaign of Clinton and Gore.
<p>
   Another Feb. 1, 1996, call sheet requested that Gore call George
Marcus, president of a California real estate firm, and ask him to
give $50,000. Eleven days later, the DNC split Marcus's contribution,
with the $20,000 maximum going to the hard-money account and $30,000
to the soft-money account.
<p>
   On Feb. 5, 1996, Gore placed a call to E. Blake Byrne, a former
executive at Argyle Television, according to a thank-you note Gore
sent to Byrne later that day. Fifteen days later, a contribution of
$20,000 was recorded in Byrne's name to the DNC's hard-money account
and another, for $5,000, to the soft-money account. In all, Byrne
contributed more than $74,000 to the DNC.
<p>
   In another case, Gore was asked on Nov. 22, 1995, to call Sanford
Robertson, chairman of Robertson, Stephens and Co. in San Francisco,
and request $100,000 soft-money donation. On Jan. 23, 1996,
Robertson's $100,000 was split, with $20,000 going to the hard money
account and $80,000 to the soft.
<p>
   On Apr. 22, 1996, Gore was asked to solicit Peter Angelos, the
owner of the Baltimore Orioles. A month later, on May 24, Angelos's
$100,000 was split, with $20,000 going to the hard money account and
$80,000 to the soft.
<p>
   The DNC was sufficiently concerned about the issue of Gore raising
hard money that Joseph E. Sandler, the DNC general counsel, made an
elaborate spread sheet listing nearly 50 people that Gore called or
had been requested to call. All hard money contributions were then
listed for these people, and the Sandler found that the total exceeded
$600,000. Based on the call sheets, the thank-you letters and phone
records, more than $120,000 could be directly traced after Gore's
calls, documents show.
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Jill Dutt </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Stock Average Surges Record 257 Points (New York) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   NEW YORK  &MD;  A burst of stock buying pushed the Dow Jones industrial
average up a record 257.36 points Tuesday, erasing in one day nearly
half the decline suffered by the Dow last month.
<p>
   Though the Dow's gain was a record in point terms, the percentage
gain of 3.38 percent was well below the record post-World War II gain
of 10.1 percent posted Oct. 21, 1987.
<p>
   ``There was heavy money flowing into the market today,'' said
Richard Cripps, director of equity marketing at Legg Mason Inc., a
Baltimore brokerage firm.
<p>
   Tuesday's market surge confounded the fears of bearish investors
who worried that the big, multinational companies that make up the Dow
average  &MD;  which has doubled in the past three years  &MD;  were finally due
for a significant fall. The average had lost 7.71 percent of its value
since its peak Aug. 6 at 8259.31.
<p>
   But analysts said it was too soon to say if Tuesday's action
signaled the beginning of a new leg up for the bull market. Rather,
several analysts expect big-company stocks to hover in a narrow
trading range, at least until economists get a better fix on whether
Federal Reserve Board policymakers will decide to raise short-term
interest rates when they meet Sept. 30.
<p>
   One important indicator investors will be watching to gauge the
economy's strength will be the August employment report, which will be
released Friday. Bruce Steinberg, Merrill Lynch's chief economist,
said in a fax to clients today, ``If the August employment report,
released on Friday, comes out as we expect, the recent market
correction could come to an end.''
<p>
   Still, few analysts expect the large-company stocks that make up
the Dow to resume their surge of last spring and early summer. The Dow
has risen 22.2 percent since the beginning of the year. Between now
and year-end, some analysts believe smaller stocks will record bigger
gains than larger capitalization brethren.
<p>
   This surge in big-company stocks was sparked by a wave of buying
from European investors, traders said. Despite earlier signals that
the German central bank was poised to raise interest rates  &MD;  signals
that had driven down the value of the U.S. dollar versus the German
mark for the past few weeks  &MD;  the Bundesbank announced no change in
rates Tuesday. The dollar gained two pfennings against the mark,
making dollar-denominated stocks more valuable, and Europeans
stampeded into the U.S. market.
<p>
   ``Europeans had been very skittish about the U.S. market, but after
today's Bundesbank statement, at least in the short run, they see our
market as a buying opportunity,'' said John Mueller, principal and
chief economist at Lehrman Bell Mueller Cannon Inc., a market
forecasting firm in Arlington, Va.
<p>
   Jeffrey S. Tabak, a managing partner at Miller Tabak Hirsch + Co.,
a New York institutional trading firm, agreed European buying was a
big factor Tuesday. He also noted that traders were relieved when a
survey of U.S. manufacturing strength came in weaker than expected,
easing fears that the nation's economic expansion might be
accelerating so fast that the Fed would have to raise interest rates.
<p>
   The National Association of Purchasing Managers reported that its
index of manufacturing orders, production, employment, deliveries,
inventories and prices paid for goods slipped to 56.8 in August, after
rising in July to 58.6. Any number over 50 on the index denotes growth
in manufacturing.
<p>
   ``The market has outlined the border of a trading range,'' said
Elaine Yager, chief technical analyst at Herzog, Heine, Geduld Inc.,
an institutional brokerage firm in New York. ``While we're in this
range, there will be a lot of yelling, hollering and screaming as
volatility increases.''
<p>
   Yager said she does not foresee another big move on the upside for
stocks until interest rates drop substantially, the U.S. dollar
retraces its earlier highs against European and Japanese currencies,
and prices on stock market futures contracts overtake earlier levels.
<p>
   But Jack Bouroudjian, vice president for equity futures at Credit
Agricole Futures, a trading firm in Chicago, said he believes the
overall market has plenty of strength to go higher.
<p>
   ``In hindsight, it was good in August to get the froth out of the
market,'' he said. ``But the things that made it go up before August
are still there. All the smart money I talk to used this weakness as a
buying opportunity.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-fishkill </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0765 </HEADER>

bc-fishkill
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Todd Shields </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Microbe May Be Specific to Pocomoke River (Pocomoke, Md.) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   POCOMOKE, Md.  &MD;  The Pocomoke River's singular ecology may make it
particularly vulnerable to a fish-killing microorganism that can
sicken people, a senior Maryland scientist said Tuesday, indicating
that the microbe might be less likely to strike elsewhere than
previously feared.
<p>
   Robert Magnien, who leads Maryland's efforts to monitor the health
of the Chesapeake Bay and its tributaries, presented his hypothesis to
a panel of scientists investigating problems on the Pocomoke, where
12,000 or more fish died in two fish kills in August.
<p>
   Magnien hypothesizes that as the Pocomoke changes from a deep,
narrow waterway to one that is shallow and broad, it creates an
environment that attracts fish and nurtures Pfiesteria piscicida . The
toxin-emitting microbe has blossomed in dangerous levels on the
Eastern Shore river but not, apparently, on other Chesapeake Bay
tributaries.
<p>
   Although untested, Magnien's idea drew favorable initial reactions
from a task force of 16 scientists who work for federal and state
governments, universities and the nonprofit Chesapeake Bay Foundation.
The task force advises state officials grappling with the Pfiesteria
problem.
<p>
   ``The set of circumstances he's describing fits really well with
our understanding of Pfiesteria 's biology and ecology,'' said JoAnn
Burkholder, a North Carolina State University researcher who first
identified the microorganism.
<p>
   Maryland officials said Magnien's hypothesis indicates that
Pfiesteria outbreaks elsewhere are not inevitable, and they expressed
relief.
<p>
   ``I think this is a very good sign that there might be several
factors isolating it to the Pocomoke,'' said Verna Harrison,
Maryland's assistant secretary of natural resources for Chesapeake Bay
and watershed programs. But, Harrison added, ``we definitely are going
to remain vigilant.''
<p>
   At a briefing on the Pocomoke River for local officials on the
Eastern Shore, scientists sought to reassure the public that the
problem doesn't extend beyond the Pocomoke.
<p>
   ``All evidence so far is these conditions remain pretty
localized,'' said Stephen Jordan, a fish pathologist and director of
the joint state-federal laboratory in Oxford, Md.
<p>
   The Pocomoke, scene of a fish kill over at least three days last
week, remained closed for a fourth straight day Tuesday, although it
appeared that no fish were dying, officials said.
<p>
   Gov. Parris N. Glendening, Democrat, ordered the closing late
Friday after doctors found brain damage and other ailments in a group
of 13 people who complained of memory loss, breathing difficulty and
skin sores after having come in contact with the river.
<p>
   Glendening said it appeared that Pfiesteria , which emits a toxin,
had harmed the 13, who included watermen, a water-skier and state
employees sent to sample the river during the first fish kill in early
August.
<p>
   Tuesday, Magnien, director of Tidewater Ecosystem Assessment for
the Maryland Department of Natural Resources, addressed the committee
of scientists.
<p>
   Few, if any, other major rivers around the bay share the
characteristics of the Pocomoke, said Donald Boesch, president of the
University of Maryland's Center for Environmental and Estuarine
Studies. Magnien, speaking of pieces of a puzzle falling into place,
said the deep and dark waters of the upper Pocomoke are relatively low
in dissolved oxygen and are inhospitable to algae and fish.
<p>
   Algae normally consume nutrients such as nitrogen and phosphorus,
which can be washed into the river from farm fields. But with few
algae present, nutrients in the Pocomoke pass relatively intact into
the broad, shallow mouth of the river and fuel algae growth. There,
Pfiesteria and fish both prey on the algae, and the microbe transmutes
into toxic forms, which feed on the fish.
<p>
   In its search for the cause of the fish kills, the state
Agriculture Department is interviewing more than 200 farmers in the
Pocomoke River watershed. Specialists have speculated that nutrient
runoff, from poultry farms in particular, may have triggered the
release of the deadly toxin.
<p>
   Assistant Agriculture Secretary Royden Powell III said that not all
farmers in the watershed have agreed to cooperate with the survey but
that most have been receptive.
<p>
   ``Clearly,'' Powell added, ``there are issues that go beyond the
Pocomoke. There may be other watersheds in the state that could be
affected in the same way.''
</TEXT>
