<SLUG> bc-diana-charles </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0876 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-charles
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Christine Spolar and Dan Balz </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Londoners Fault Charles as Catalyst for Tragedy &QL; 
 &UR; (London) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LONDON  &MD;  Not even tragedy has raised Prince Charles in the esteem
of his countrymen.
<p>
   The cool, remote Prince of Wales might be weeping over the death of
his former wife, Princess Diana, if tabloid tales are true, but the
British public appears to be oddly unmoved by his sorrow.
<p>
   Charles, whose dour demeanor, eccentric enthusiasms and affair with
a married woman have long made him the loser to Diana in the
competition for public affection, has said nothing publicly and issued
no statement since Diana was killed in an auto accident with companion
Dodi Fayed and a driver early Sunday. But, according to media
accounts, he is suffering deeply.
<p>
   He has been roaming the moors of Scotland, contemplating his sorrow
over the loss of his ex-wife, said the Daily Mail. Red-eyed and
sleepless, he is totally distraught, said the Sun.
<p>
   But many media commentators urged the heir to the throne to heed
his former wife's example, loosen up and reach out to his subjects in
the same open-hearted way she did  &MD;  or risk his future and that of the
House of Windsor.
<p>
   Among the throngs of mourners in London, few people are shedding
tears for the prince. Diana touched them in a special way, they say,
but talk of Charles turns the conversation cold. As cold, some noted,
as Charles appears to be.
<p>
   ``He's full of guilt  &MD;  and he should be,'' said Vanessa Belton, who
stood in line with thousands of others to sign a book of condolence at
St. James's Palace in memory of Diana. ``If he'd loved her  &MD;  and all
she wanted was her love returned  &MD;  this would never have happened.
She'd never have had to go elsewhere.''
<p>
   ``He's in a sad state, is he?'' harrumphed 85-year-old Morris
Regan, part of another vast assembly of mourners outside Kensington
Palace, Diana's official residence. ``Let me tell you this: I'm a
royalist. I like the royals. She was a lovely lady. A credit to
Britain. His tears? Guilt. He was a two-timer, wasn't he?''
<p>
   ``Look at all the people here,'' said Regan. ``What does the
country think of him? Well, he didn't think it would end this way. And
he never counted on this.''
<p>
   Royal watchers are debating whether Diana, even in death, will
control Charles's destiny. Britons still don't much like the woman
with whom Charles cheated on Diana, Camilla Parker-Bowles, and
analysts say Diana's death makes it less likely that Charles will be
able to formalize his relationship with her.
<p>
   ``Camilla should leave the country,'' said one woman, who echoed
the sentiments of countless mourners here Tuesday. ``Without the
mistress, none of this would've happened.''
<p>
   Many fault the prince for failing to maintain his marriage. Diana
traveled the world to fulfill royal duties, but many mourners pointed
out she would not have been in Paris that fateful night  &MD;  in the
company of boyfriend Fayed  &MD;  if her marriage had remained intact.
Others asserted that it was Charles and the Royal Family who pushed
for the divorce that ended the union last year.
<p>
   ``I think (Charles) is to blame for all of this,'' said Jean Lewis,
who waited in line at St. James's Palace to record a tribute to Diana
in the book of condolence. ``She would have been quite happy at home
with her two boys. Will he take those two boys to McDonald's (as she
did)? I don't think so.''
<p>
   Lewis, like others interviewed Tuesday, said Charles has forfeited
the right to become king and declared the next monarch should be his
and Diana's older son, Prince William, 15. ``I don't know if people
will forgive him for this,'' Lewis said. ``I know I won't. I don't
think he deserves to be king.''
<p>
   Even if the outpouring of sympathy for Diana diminishes with time,
royal watchers pointed out that Charles has a long-term public
relations problem: Diana will now remain forever young and forever
tragic and forever wronged. Can Prince Charles ever hope to compete?
<p>
   ``Perhaps the mood of the royal fans will be forgiving,'' wrote
columnist Polly Toynbee. ``Perhaps they will take pity on him in all
his contorted awkwardness, so ill at ease with everything in his life.
Perhaps they will forgive Camilla, whom Diana fingered so firmly as
the destroyer of her marriage.
<p>
   ``But,'' she added ominously, ``perhaps not.''
<p>
   ``Can Charles take the strain'' and change as so many of his
countrymen would like him to, asked the Daily Mail. ``Further erosion
and implosion of the monarchy is more likely,'' the paper opined.
<p>
   Charles will come under even greater public scrutiny in coming
weeks, if that is possible. Those more sympathetic to his plight are
calling his task ``agonizing'' and his prospective struggle to find
serenity for himself and his sons ``a sad story.'' He's undertaken his
initial duties in the wake of Diana's death admirably, supporters
note; his journey to Paris to bring her body home earned him respect
among those who mold opinion about the royals.
<p>
   Toynbee wrote that no one should view the prince now as an
``out-and-out'' villain. ``He wanted to be the right man in that
marriage and in life,'' she said. ``He just couldn't do it.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>

 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: News editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Fred Bruning </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Diana Had Firm Hold on America's Imagination (New York) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   NEW YORK  &MD;  Clutching a strand of rosary beads and a small bouquet,
Jean Eckhoff stood weeping in the marble and terrazzo lobby of the
British consulate in Manhattan Tuesday  &MD;  one of many Americans who
responded to the death of Diana, princess of Wales, as though it were
a family tragedy, not a far-off news event.
<p>
   ``Princess Di meant a lot to me as a child,'' said Eckhoff, 27, a
Florida native who lives and works in Manhattan. ``I used to sleep
with her picture. I wrote her letters. I put her in short stories.''
Eckhoff even named the family cat ``Lady Di'' to honor the beautiful
British bride who, she said, often appeared in her girlish dreams.
<p>
   As Eckhoff struggled with her grief, other mourners who had lined
up on Third Avenue filed into the consulate to write condolences or to
add floral arrangements to the more than 2,000 received since Diana's
death Sunday, or to gaze in reverie at photographs of the young woman
they so admired.
<p>
   Cheeks flushed and eyes bright with tears, Megan Wands, 21, of
Chatham, N.J., said the news that Diana had perished in a Paris auto
wreck was a harsh introduction to the realities of adulthood. ``She
made people believe in fairy tales,'' Wands said. ``I'm never going to
believe in fairy tales again.''
<p>
   Such adoration might have been expected in Britain where Diana was
beloved despite marital woes and well-publicized spats with the royal
family, but her hold on the American imagination is proving at least
as tenacious in death as in life.
<p>
   The reasons are varied, according to experts in the fields of
psychology and pop culture, who note that Americans long have been
fascinated with the British crown  &MD;  and with celebrity everywhere.
<p>
   ``We've always been hung up on the Brits,'' said Jack Nachbar,
professor emeritus in the department of popular culture at Bowling
Green State University in Ohio. ``Though we fancy ourselves democratic
 &MD;  and fought a revolution  &MD;  we feel culturally inferior. And, in the
20th century, we are hung up on celebrities. Those two things met
perfectly in Princess Diana.''
<p>
   Beyond the American fascination with fame, fortune and royalty,
there seemed a particularly keen interest in Diana  &MD;  the fairy
princess with a common touch, the regal lady who left the impression
that, if it were possible, she would have everyone over for a couple
of brews in Buckingham's back yard.
<p>
   ``Diana exemplified the ordinary person and the princess and
carried off that dual role with grace,'' said Donald Weatherley, a
psychologist at the University of Colorado. ``We were fascinated with
a person pulled out of the masses who becomes an acceptable kind of
royalty.''
<p>
   Weatherley's words were echoed at the British Embassy in
Washington, D.C., by Camille Wahl, 28, of Rockville, Md., who, with
her sister, Stephanie, 21, had come to pay Diana last respects. ``She
gave to people and gave of herself; people like to see that. She was
royal, she was a celebrity and a real person, too.''
<p>
   It was Diana's reputation for decency that seemed to resonate
especially in the United States.
<p>
   ``Not only would she present herself as a fairy princess but she
was a good princess, too  &MD;  the good mother, the beneficent person who
worked for causes and visited hospitals,'' said Fredrick Koenig, a
social psychologist at Tulane University in New Orleans. ``Her
appearance in the living room through television provided a glow for
the day.''
<p>
   If members of British royalty often seem to Americans distant,
disdainful and intellectually arrogant, Diana seemed OK.
<p>
   ``She's never said anything important, nothing profound,'' said
Marshall Fishwick, a specialist in popular culture at Virginia
Polytechnic and State University. ``She had no real philosophy and was
almost embarrassingly open, frank and vulnerable.'' But those were
precisely the qualities that made Diana irresistible to many on these
shores. ``Americans like someone who comes in and tells it the way it
is,'' Fishwick said.
<p>
   When it came to Diana, Americans could never get enough.
<p>
   ``We've done 43 covers and three special issues on Diana, and
people were always clamoring for more,'' said Susan Toepfer, executive
editor of People magazine. ``I think it was her human quality that
endeared her to the American public  &MD;  especially women.''
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Meanwhile, Diana's followers lament her loss.
<p>
   In June, Diana met Mother Teresa at a Bronx mission house operated
by the Sisters of Mercy. Tuesday, Jeffrey Ling, the British consul
general in New York, brought a van load of bouquets from the consulate
to the mission for distribution throughout the community in Diana's
name.
<p>
   One nun, Sister John Janet, said she had been talking to
neighborhood residents who remembered Diana fondly.
<p>
   ``They told me, `She was not prejudiced. She was not afraid to come
to us,' '' said Sister John Janet. And, Sister John Janet said,
another sentiment expressed by people who remembered the princess: ``I
hope you are praying for her.''
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-donate-gore-ndy </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1819 09-02 0885 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-donate-gore-ndy - a1819
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Ken Fireman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Gore Aides Detail Fund-Raiser at Buddhist Temple (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Seeking to pre-empt Senate hearings that resume
Thursday, aides to Vice President Al Gore Wednesday offered their most
detailed account yet of how Gore came to participate in a
controversial fund-raiser at a California Buddhist temple and what he
knew about it beforehand.
<p>
   Aides to the vice president acknowledged that several members of
his staff  &MD;  including one unnamed member of his traveling party  &MD;  knew
in advance that the 1996 luncheon was designed to raise money for the
Democrats.
<p>
   They also conceded that the vice president's files are rife with
internal documents describing the event as a fund-raiser, and that
some of those documents should have alerted senior staffers about the
true nature of the luncheon and its potential for sparking
controversy.
<p>
   But the aides, who spoke to journalists on condition of anonymity,
insisted that Gore himself had no idea money would be raised at the
event and offered internal documents to support that contention. They
also asserted that Gore was completely unaware of an alleged scheme by
two organizers of the luncheon, John Huang and Maria Hsia, to jack up
the yield afterward by illegally laundering contributions through
nuns, monks and devotees of the temple.
<p>
   Some of those nuns are expected to testify under a grant of
immunity from prosecution Thursday when the Senate Governmental
Affairs Committee resumes its hearings on campaign finance abuses
after a monthlong break.
<p>
   After delving into the temple fund-raiser, the committee plans to
shift its focus to an exploration of the degree to which the Clinton
White House controlled the Democratic National Committee during last
year's campaign. Former DNC Chairman Donald Fowler is scheduled to
testify next week, with the combative former White House deputy chief
of staff, Harold Ickes, likely to take the stand later in the month.
<p>
   At least one member of the vice president's staff is expected to
testify about Gore's role in the temple event after the nuns tell
their stories. But the committee has not asked Gore himself to appear,
and aides say he has no plans to request such an appearance.
<p>
   ``There is no reason for him to go up there,'' said one Gore aide.
``It would just turn into a political circus. He has nothing new to
offer.''
<p>
   Nonetheless, no single event has caused Gore more embarrassment
than his appearance at the Hsi Lai Temple in Hacienda Heights, a Los
Angeles suburb, on April 29, 1996. And given the strong likelihood
that Gore will run for president in three years, the affair is of far
more than historical significance.
<p>
   Gore has offered varying accounts of the luncheon and his
understanding of it. After first calling it a ``community outreach
event,'' he acknowledged that it was ``finance-related'' and that
holding it at a place of worship was therefore inappropriate. But Gore
continued to assert that he did not know in advance that contributions
would be solicited or collected at the lunch.
<p>
   Gore's aides Tuesday reiterated that account and said it would be
borne out by a detailed examination of the record. They acknowledged
that six separate documents from the vice president's files apparently
refer to the event as a fund-raiser, including one that contains a
potential range for ticket prices  &MD;  $1,000 to $5,000 per head  &MD;  and
another by Huang that was written after he had supposedly assured
Gore's chief of staff that no money would be raised at the temple.
<p>
   The aides attempted to resolve the apparent contradiction by
explaining that two events, rather than one, were originally planned
for Gore's visit to the Los Angeles area on that day. One, organized
by Huang, was to be a traditional fund-raiser at a restaurant in
Monterey Park. The second, arranged by Hsia, was the stop at Hsi Lai  &MD; 
whose leader, Hsing Yun, had met with Gore in Washington the previous
month and invited him to visit the temple.
<p>
   But the Monterey Park event was canceled, in part because it did
not appear to be generating much in the way of money commitments, the
aides said. The two events were then collapsed into one, they said,
with some Gore staffers aware of the fact that money would now be
raised at the temple, and others  &MD;  as well as Gore himself  &MD;  left in
the dark.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   ``The staff perception of the event (as a fund-raiser) was not
passed up the line to the vice president,'' said one aide.
<p>
   In support of this assertion, Gore's aides released the scheduling
documents and ``talking points'' for Gore's appearance at the temple
and two other events: a fund-raiser at a private home in Northern
California later on the same day at the temple luncheon, and a
fund-raising dinner in Washington in November 1995.
<p>
   The schedules for the latter two events contain specific references
to the amounts expected to be raised, and the ``talking points''  &MD;  an
outline of the speaker's planned remarks  &MD;  allude to the fact that
those present have paid to attend. The documents for the temple event
contain no such references.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-books </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1821 09-02 0704 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-books - a1821
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: News editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Paul D. Colford </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Di's Death Renews Interest in Books About Her (New York) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   NEW YORK  &MD;  On Sunday and Monday, many customers entering Barnes &AMP;
Noble stores around the United States were asking for books about
Princess Diana, said Bob Wietrack, the chain's senior merchandise
manager. By late morning Tuesday, Beryl Needham, director of
merchandise for the 940 Waldenbooks stores, had heard from several
publishers wanting to gauge the chain's interest in new books and
reissues about the late princess.
<p>
   Soon afterward, publishers were nailing down plans for the first
new crop of titles.
<p>
   Kitty Kelley's ``The Royals,'' an unauthorized chronicle of the
House of Windsor, already had loomed as one of the bigger books this
fall. It will go on sale as scheduled Sept. 23. ``We are staying the
course,'' said Lynn Goldberg, a publicist for the Warner book,
responding to a suggestion in the industry that ``The Royals'' would
be delayed because its explosive revelations might clash with the
mourning for Diana.
<p>
   Newsweek reports this week that Kelley, who has written sharp-edged
biographies of Frank Sinatra and Nancy Reagan, is expected to allege
that the Queen Mother is illegitimate, that Queen Elizabeth II and
Princess Margaret were conceived through artificial insemination and
that Sarah Ferguson went through detox for cocaine abuse. Warner has
announced a first printing of 1 million copies.
<p>
   Simon &AMP; Schuster anticipates strong new sales for the Diana books
in its catalog. At the end of the week, the company's Pocket Books
division plans to ship two of the more successful books about Diana,
Andrew Morton's ``Diana: Her True Story'' (1992) and ``Diana: Her New
Life'' (1994), in paperback reprintings of 500,000 and 250,000 copies
respectively.
<p>
   Morton, a British journalist whose books benefited from his access
to Diana and her inner circle of friends, also is writing a new
20-page introduction to a commemorative hardcover edition of ``Diana:
Her True Story'' from S&AMP;S that will include a 16-page color photo
section; the book's release date has not been determined. Publishing
executives said they expected any commemorative keepsake on Diana's
life to sell especially well.
<p>
   In addition, Pocket is going back to press for 100,000 copies of
Penny Thornton's ``With Love From Diana: The Princess of Wales'
Personal Astrologer Shares Her First Hand Account of Diana's Turbulent
Years'' (1995) and for 100,000 copies of Donald Spoto's ``Decline and
Fall of the House of Windsor'' (1996). Randi Reisfeld's recent
biography of the future monarch, ``Prince William: The Boy Who Would
Be King,'' also will receive a fresh printing of 50,000 copies.
<p>
   ``When something this untimely happens, no one is prepared,'' said
Wietrack of Barnes &AMP; Noble. ``But from a customer demand, publishers
were quick to react.''
<p>
   As for so-called instant books about Diana and her tragic end, a
master of the species, Pinnacle Books Executive Editor Paul Dinas,
said Tuesday that he expected none would be rushed out to compete with
existing titles. Indeed, Amazon.com, the online bookseller, Tuesday
listed 75 titles on Diana alone, of which about 15 were widely
available in bookstores before Sunday's fatal crash. ``I can't believe
anyone would be foolish to compete from scratch with all that
product,'' Dinas said.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   On newsstands, the issue of People magazine that goes on sale
Friday will be the weekly's 44th to feature Diana on the cover  &MD;  she
already holds that magazine's record for cover appearances. A
spokeswoman said Tuesday that the magazine also may publish a special
stand-alone issue on the princess like those it produced after the
deaths of Audrey Hepburn, Jacqueline Kennedy Onassis, Jerry Garcia and
Selena.
<p>
   A decision to do so may be hastened by consumer response to this
week's Diana cover on Time, People's sister publication, which said
Tuesday that it was distributing 250,000 additional copies to meet
reader demand. Time's average weekly newsstand sale is 184,000.
<p>
   Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-reactor </SLUG>

bc-reactor - a1823
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL;

<BYLINE> By Lauren Terrazzano </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Politicians Seek Shutdown of Nuclear Reactor (Mineola, N.Y.) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MINEOLA, N.Y.  &MD;  In a move that scientists from across the United
States said could jeopardize research in the nation, two lawmakers
Tuesday called for the permanent shutdown of Brookhaven National
Laboratory's nuclear reactor, blaming a ``culture of arrogance'' they
charge has fueled a public health hazard and undermined the
laboratory's other work.
<p>
   ``This is the only prudent, sensible course of action if we are
going to protect the public's health,'' said Sen. Alfonse D'Amato,
R-N.Y., speaking Tuesday at a morning news conference in Mineola with
Rep. Michael Forbes, R-N.Y., on their plan to introduce legislation to
stop the reactivation of the high-flux beam reactor at the Long Island
laboratory. The reactor has been closed since December 1996 after a
pool used to store its used fuel had been found to be leaking
radioactive tritium into groundwater. The lab has maintained that
drinking water has been unaffected and that the leak has posed no
public health threat.
<p>
   ``There is little patience for any more of the revelations we've
seen almost weekly at this facility,'' said Forbes, who along with
D'Amato said the laboratory's ``public be damned'' attitude dated as
far back as 1986, when elevated levels of tritium contamination were
first discovered.
<p>
   However, Peter Bond, the laboratory's interim director, called the
proposal disheartening and the charges inaccurate, saying he had hoped
that Forbes and D'Amato would have waited until the Department of
Energy decides whether to reopen the reactor, a decision that is
expected to be made in January after extensive community input. ``I'm
obviously disappointed they've chosen to short-circuit the process,''
said Bond.
<p>
   Nearly 20 reactor employees sat silently at the conference Tuesday,
at times shaking their heads at the politicians and saying that the
proposed permanent shutdown was more about election-year politics and
would squelch immense scientific progress that has resulted from the
reactor's use.
<p>
   ``It's a national asset,'' said Terri Kneitel, an engineer at the
reactor since 1991 who also said she fears losing her job. ``I'm
shocked, appalled.''
<p>
   Forbes said he originally envisioned reopening the reactor after
assurances that health, safety, and environmental concerns would be
addressed. ``But what made me decide conclusively is related to a
series of recent events, stemming from late July when a reluctant
laboratory officials verified leaking tritium from the sewage
treatment plant into the Peconic River system.''
<p>
   Environmentalists and residents who live in the surrounding area
said they were thrilled with the announcement. ``Today, I'm breathing
a sigh of relief,'' said Sarah Nuccio, 41, of Manorville, who lives
just a few miles from the laboratory and who has been vocal about what
she terms as its unwelcome presence in the community.
<p>
   In a statement released Tuesday, the Department of Energy's
director of the Office of Energy Research said the department has not
made any decision about whether to restart the reactor.
<p>
   Since it was constructed 32 years ago, the reactor has been
involved in a range of scientific discoveries, including the
development of radioactive isotopes used to diagnose heart disease in
10 million patients each year to research on enzymes that dissolve
blood clots.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   News of the move to permanently shut down the reactor shocked the
nation's scientific community Tuesday.
<p>
   ``It's going to put us at a significant disadvantage with our
research,'' said Robert Birgeneau, dean of science at the
Massachusetts Institute of Technology, who has used the reactor a
number of times to study high-temperature superconductors, and
magnetic materials. ``It's the premier research reactor.''
<p>
   Closing the reactor would mean that only two comparable reactors in
the country would remain for scientists to conduct their work: a
Department of Energy-run reactor at the Oak Ridge National Laboratory
in Tennessee and the National Institute of Standards and Technology in
Maryland.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-apple-times </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1833 09-02 0689 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-apple-times - a1833
 &UR; (ATTN: Financial editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Greg Miller </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Apple Pares Mac-Clone Making to the Core (San Francisco) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   SAN FRANCISCO  &MD;  Apple Computer Inc. signaled an end to its stormy
relationship with makers of Macintosh clone computers Tuesday by
buying the business of the largest clone maker and indicating that
licensing agreements with others will not be renewed.
<p>
   Increasingly convinced that clone makers' success has come at
Apple's expense, the struggling computer manufacturer agreed to buy
the core Macintosh-based business of Power Computing Corp. for $100
million in stock.
<p>
   Austin, Texas-based Power Computing is among a handful of companies
that have been licensed to sell computers that use Apple's Macintosh
operating system. Since the licensing program began in 1994, Power
Computing's sales have surged to nearly $400 million, accounting for
12 percent of the Macintosh market.
<p>
   But Apple, which has lost $1.5 billion over the last 18 months and
has seen its market share decline, is now backpedaling from those
licensing agreements. Apple founder and de facto leader Steve Jobs
complained Tuesday that the terms of the licensing deals  &MD;  in which
Apple collects a royalty of about $50 per machine  &MD;  have sapped the
Cupertino company's profitability.
<p>
   ``We cannot continue to subsidize the clones,'' said Jobs,
explaining that licensing revenues don't compensate for costs borne
solely by Apple, including development of Macintosh software,
marketing and maintenance of relationships with developers of
Macintosh-based programs.
<p>
   Shares of Apple closed at $22.38, up 63 cents on Nasdaq.
<p>
   Apple will honor its existing contracts with clone manufacturers,
Jobs and other executives said, but the company does not expect to
expand or extend them.
<p>
   ``I don't want to say that's not possible,'' said Fred Anderson,
Apple's chief financial officer. ``We're always open for a licensee to
agree to economic terms that would benefit Apple. ''
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Analysts said the moves will strengthen Apple's financial position
by eliminating competitors that squeeze profit margins and now account
for about 21 percent of the Macintosh market.
<p>
   But some warned that Apple could face a backlash from customers and
software developers who have come to expect choices and aren't
confident of the company's ability to expand the Macintosh market
single-handedly.
<p>
   ``It's good for Apple because it will allow them to return to
profitability quicker,'' said James Staten, an analyst at Dataquest in
San Jose. ``But is it good for the overall Macintosh market? No, it
can't be.''
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Ironically, many industry experts trace Apple's declining fortunes
back to its decisions in the 1980s not to allow others to license its
Macintosh software, then considered vastly superior to any other
operating system.
<p>
   Apple relented in 1994, but by then Microsoft Corp.'s Windows
operating system had caught up technologically, and was licensed to
computer makers that accounted for about 90 percent of the market.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Tuesday's news appeared to be a blow to a number of companies,
including Motorola Corp. and Umax Computer Corp., that have spent
millions of dollars creating businesses around selling Macintosh
clones. Anderson and Jobs said Apple does not plan to purchase the
Macintosh business units of any other clone makers.
<p>
   Umax, a subsidiary of Umax Data Systems of Taiwan, derives
two-thirds of its sales from models priced below $2,000.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   The crux of Tuesday's deal with Power Computing is that it
<p>
   has agreed to exit the Macintosh market and turn over to Apple
exclusive rights to the clone maker's list of more than 200,000
Macintosh customers.
<p>
   Power Computing keeps its brand name, its employees and its
manufacturing operations, presumably to be used in the company's
efforts to move into the market for computers that use Intel Corp.
microprocessors and Microsoft's Windows operating system.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-09-02-97 2134EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<DOCID> latwp970902.0154 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=f pri=r sel=tm--a> X3380 </STORYID>

<SLUG> bc-apple-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 09-02 0701 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-apple-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Financial editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Rajiv Chandrasekaran </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Apple Buys Biggest Maker of Its `Clones' (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Apple Computer Inc. Tuesday moved to crimp the business of selling
``clones'' of its Macintosh computers, purchasing the largest maker of
the machines and saying it would deny new technology to other clone
manufacturers.
<p>
   The decisions effectively reverse Apple's three-year-old strategy
of encouraging other firms to make Macintosh knockoffs in an effort to
expand the Macintosh market as a whole. Although those companies have
increased the number of computers running Macintosh software, the
competition has backfired for Apple, severely cutting into its own
sales.
<p>
   ``Mac clones are dead,'' said Chris Le Tocq, an analyst with
Dataquest Inc., a market research firm in San Jose. ``Who wants to buy
a Mac that doesn't come with the latest software?''
<p>
   Analysts saw the hand of Apple co-founder Steve Jobs in the
strategy shift. He has largely run Apple since chief executive Gilbert
Amelio was forced to resign in July and is known to oppose the terms
of the cloning deals.
<p>
   Apple said early Tuesday that it would spend $100 million to
acquire the core assets of Power Computing Corp., the largest clone
maker. Later in the day, Fred Anderson, who is officially the
company's acting chief executive, said in a conference call with
reporters and analysts that the company would honor existing cloning
agreements but would deny other cloners rights to ship machines with
the latest Macintosh operating system software.
<p>
   ``We don't have any current plans to give them new Apple designs,''
Anderson said. He said Apple does not want to put cloners out of
business but the company has been unable to renegotiate what it now
considers to be unfair licensing agreements.
<p>
   Guerrino De Luca, Apple's vice president for marketing, said the
terms of the current licensing agreements were ``a de facto subsidy to
the tune of several hundred dollars'' per machine for clone makers.
<p>
   Other companies that make Macintosh clones are Motorola Inc. and
UMax Inc. International Business Machines Corp. has a license to make
clones but has not yet produced any.
<p>
   Apple, the company that popularized the personal computer in the
late 1970s, refused for years to license its technology to other
computer makers. IBM, Apple's early rival in the PC market, took an
opposite approach with its system hardware, which became an industry
standard running Microsoft Corp.'s Windows operating software.
<p>
   Faced with plummeting market share, Apple decided in 1994 to allow
clones as a way to rejuvenate the Macintosh. The hope was that clones
would pump up the overall market for Macintosh systems, meaning
greater sales for everyone.
<p>
   But by early this year, that strategy appeared to be hurting Apple.
The cloners pushed down prices  &MD;  and Apple's profit margin.
<p>
   Anderson said clone makers also were not expanding the base of
Macintosh users fast enough. ``The unfortunate reality was that the
clone vendors did not sell very many systems to new customers,'' said
Anderson, who estimated that 99 percent of the clone makers' sales
went to previous Mac owners.
<p>
   In the first half of 1996, Apple had 94 percent of the market for
desktop Macintoshes, while Power Computing had 4 percent, according to
market research firm International Data Corp. In the same period this
year, Apple's share fell to 79 percent and Power's grew to 9 percent,
IDC said. Other clone makers, including Motorola and UMax, had the
remaining 12 percent.
<p>
   In addition to shoring up market share, the purchase of Power
Computing will set up Apple to sell computers directly to the public,
as do PC giants such as Dell Computer Corp. and Gateway 2000 Inc.
While the new sales approach could anger some retailers, it is likely
to boost Apple's profit margins, analysts said.
<p>
   Putting an end to cloning means Apple could charge higher prices
for its machines, said Eric Lewis, an IDC analyst. ``It's a very
dangerous thing,'' he said. ``We could be going back to the old days,
when Apples were significantly more expensive than comparably equipped
(Windows) PCs.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-bounty </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1838 09-02 0869 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-bounty - a1838
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Louis Sahagun </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Double Slaying Unmasks Bounty Hunters' Lethal Trade (Denver) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   DENVER  &MD;  In the wake of the deaths of a young Phoenix couple slain
during a bungled raid, law enforcement authorities are dusting off old
lawbooks and discovering that bounty hunting is the most unregulated
arm of the American justice system.
<p>
   Operating under broad powers granted by the Supreme Court more than
a century ago, the nation's estimated 2,000 bounty hunters routinely
strap on bullet-proof vests and long-barreled handguns to enter homes
and automobiles without search warrants. They are held to looser
standards than conventional law enforcement officers. And they enjoy
almost carte blanche privileges to use deadly force in self-defense
when tracking down and apprehending their prey: wanted criminals who
skipped bail.
<p>
   Never mind that there is no formal licensing process for these
pseudo-cops  &MD;  glamorized in movies such as ``The Hunter,'' starring
Steve McQueen  &MD;  in any state. With prisons overflowing, and more
people being released on bail, the bounty hunting industry is booming.
<p>
   Trouble is, their mistakes can be tragic.
<p>
   At 4 a.m. Sunday, five men calling themselves fugitive recovery
agents  &MD;  all wearing black clothes, ski masks and body armor  &MD;  broke
down the front door of the wrong house in Phoenix in search of a
California bail jumper.
<p>
   Authorities said their ostensible target  &MD;  an unidentified man who
had skipped out on a $25,000 bail  &MD;  originally had been charged with
possession of drug paraphernalia.
<p>
   Two of the masked men tied up a woman who lived in the house and
held her three children at gunpoint, authorities said. The others
kicked down a locked bedroom door, and then walked into a fierce gun
battle.
<p>
   Killed were Christopher Foote, 23, and his girlfriend, Spring
Wright, 21. There were 29 bullet holes in the wall behind the couple's
bed and an adjacent hallway.
<p>
   Foote, who was armed with a 9 mm handgun, apparently emptied his
clip in self-defense and wounded two of the bounty hunters in their
arms and legs.
<p>
   Police arrested Matthew Brackney, 20, and Michael Martin Sanders,
40  &MD;  both of Phoenix  &MD;  and charged them with two counts of
second-degree murder. Brackney's father, David, 45, who was injured in
the gun battle, was expected to face similar charges upon release from
the hospital.
<p>
   An intense manhunt continued on Tuesday for two accomplices still
at large. They are regarded by police to be armed and dangerous.
<p>
   In response to the incident, law enforcement authorities here and
across the nation were calling for legislative inquiries into the
industry  &MD;  and curbs on the powers of individual bounty hunters.
<p>
   ``There's been little change in the law since the territorial days
when bounty hunters were used by sheriffs to look for people who had
robbed stage coaches,'' said Arizona state Sen. John Kaites, a former
Maricopa County attorney.
<p>
   ``That was when law enforcement authorities were few and far
between and they accepted even crude and unsophisticated help,'' he
said. ``In 1997, we need other means to keep innocent people from
getting hurt.''
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Kaites is drafting legislation that would require prospective
bounty hunters to undergo extensive background checks and a formal
permitting process. It would also require that they notify police
before entering an occupied structure.
<p>
   A similar bill that would have limited use of deadly physical force
by bounty hunters was defeated a decade ago by Arizona's Senate
Judiciary Committee. That proposal was in response to a 1987 incident
in which a 19-year-old California bounty hunter shot and killed an
unarmed fugitive in Phoenix.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   As it stands, these guns-for-hire remain all but unregulated,
although organizations such as the 1,500-member National Association
of Bail Enforcement Agents offer their own classes, training manuals
and equipment.
<p>
   But for those wanting to bypass classwork and get right to work
nabbing so-called ``skips,'' there is a ``hunter starter kit'' on the
market that sells for about $205. It contains a black tote bag
embossed with the word ``AGENT'' in gold letters on the sides, as well
as a black jacket, black cap, black T-shirt, handcuffs, pepper spray
and leg irons.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Phoenix bounty hunter Linda Ownbey said the industry has not done a
commendable job of policing itself.
<p>
   ``We have to change the rules regarding these free-lance cowboys in
the field,'' she said. ``Right now, there are bounty hunter classes in
which people can pay $500 for three days of instruction. Then they are
turned loose wearing ninja clothes and guns.''
<p>
   She counts suspects Matthew Brackney and his father among the
problem fringe element.
<p>
   ``They came into my office twice last year looking for work; I ran
them out both times,'' she recalled. ``They were wearing black pants,
black T-shirts and combat boots; and they had a `we're big and bad'
attitude.'' ``I took one look at them and thought: No way. These guys
are trouble.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-kendrew-obit </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1849 09-02 0396 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-kendrew-obit - a1849
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Myrna Oliver </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Nobel Prize-Winning Chemist John Cowdery Kendrew, 80, Dies &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   John Cowdery Kendrew, British molecular chemist who shared a 1962
Nobel Prize in chemistry for detailing the intricate structure of a
major protein, has died. He was 80.
<p>
   Kendrew, who was knighted and given the Order of the British Empire
in 1963, died Aug. 23 in Cambridge, England.
<p>
   He and colleague Max Ferdinand Perutz earned the Nobel for their
work at Cambridge in cataloging elements in human blood and providing
a major insight on how proteins do their crucial work.
<p>
   Cited for their studies on globular proteins, they perfected
methods using X-rays to map the structure of the vital bodybuilding
large protein molecules. Laying bare the structure of the blood
proteins, Kendrew concentrated on myoglobin while Perutz worked on
hemoglobin.
<p>
   Among those who inspired Kendrew to conduct his work were
crystallographer J.D. Bernal, whom he encountered during his war work
in Asia, and by chemist Linus Pauling, whom he met during a trip to
California.
<p>
   Born in Oxford, England, Kendrew studied at Oxford and Cambridge
and worked in radar technology with the Ministry of Aircraft
Production during World War II.
<p>
   The department that Kendrew and Perutz created at Cambridge is now
known as the Laboratory for Molecular Biology. Kendrew served as the
department chairman until 1974.
<p>
   Kendrew then established the European Molecular Biology Laboratory
at Heidelberg, Germany, and served as director until 1982.
<p>
   He also helped set up a committee of scientists to keep tabs on
worldwide gene experiments that could be considered both beneficial
and hazardous, under the aegis of the International Council of
Scientific Unions.
<p>
   ``What we want to do is accumulate a kind of pool of wise thinking
to make sure that the use that is made of it is of a positive kind,''
Kendrew said in 1976 as secretary general of the council that
represents 18 international scientific unions and 65 academies and
research groups.
<p>
   Kendrew founded the Journal of Molecular Biology and edited it
until his retirement in 1987 as president of St. John's College at
Oxford.
<p>
   His extensive writings include the book ``The Thread of Life: An
Introduction to Molecular Biology.''
<p>
   Kendrew was not married.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-diana-paparazzi </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1855 09-02 0882 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-diana-paparazzi - a1855
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: News editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Rita Ciolli </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Editors: In 5th graf, Eliane Laffont is cq. &QL; 
 &UR; Photographer Held by French Is Known for Award-Winning Shots &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Among the photojournalists in trouble Tuesday after the death of
Princess Diana is a highly regarded photographer who spent most of his
career in war zones and the world's trouble spots.
<p>
   The decision by French authorities to press forward with their
investigation of him and six others came as the behavior of the
paparazzi continued to roil the media world and a leading American
photojournalist condemned their behavior.
<p>
   Jacques Langevin of the Sygma Agency was put under formal
investigation for suspected manslaughter by a French magistrate
Tuesday.
<p>
   Langevin received international recognition in 1989 when he
outmaneuvered Chinese authorites and made pictures of the government's
crackdown on the student uprising in Tiananmen Square. A year later,
working outside of the established press pools and military censors,
Langevin documented the human toll of the Persian Gulf war. His
horrific photographs of annihilated Iraqi troops were published in a
1991 book.
<p>
   Eliane Laffont, president of Sygma, denied that Langevin, 42, was
involved with the paparazzi chase. In a statement Tuesday, Laffont
explained that Langevin, as the shift photographer on call, had taken
photos of Diana and Dodi Fayed at the Ritz Hotel. He then got into his
car to return to dinner with friends, his route taking him past the
crash scene. ``As a professional photojournalist, Langevin took photos
of the rescue team and the wreckage of the car.''
<p>
   In an interview for Wednesday's edition of the daily Liberation,
Langevin told the publication he arrived about 10 minutes after the
crash. ``The car was surrounded by police, firemen and photographers.
Things were happening normally,'' he said.
<p>
   He said emergency services had taken out the body of Fayed and were
trying to revive him and were seeking to help the other victims. He
said he learned later that photographers had alerted the emergency
services by mobile phone.
<p>
   ``Personally, I have four or five photographs, taken about 15
meters away, under the eyes of the police who had set up a sort of
light barrier,'' he said.
<p>
   Langevin said he tried to leave but that he and other photographers
still at the scene were abruptly rounded up. ``I never thought for a
second that we would be held for questioning, far less placed under
investigation.''
<p>
   For his photos at Tiananmen Square, Langevin was given a
prestigious award in 1990 by the International Center of Photography
in New York City. ``While Chinese authorities were detaining
journalists at the Beijing Hotel, at that moment Jacques was actually
there, already in the streets,'' his citation read.
<p>
   Paradoxically, it was Cornell Capa, the founder of ICP, who Tuesday
condemned the action of the paparazzi.
<p>
   ``Photography, since its invention, serves as a mirror of life and
consciousness. Princess Di's death is beyond the threshold of
brutalization of our lives, the mirror and the medium. I feel
desperate,'' said Capa, considered the dean of photojournalists and
the brother of the legendary war photographer Robert Capa.
<p>
   Meanwhile, the sense that the media increasingly are becoming part
of the story continued to trouble the journalism establishment
Tuesday.
<p>
   ``Just one more picture of Lady Di going out to dinner is
meaningless, and stupid, and has no place in the media. But that is
where the money is, unfortunately,'' said John Morris, former photo
editor at Life magazine, The New York Times and other news
organizations. ``The problem goes back to the public, which demands
this kind of paparazzi coverage,'' said Morris, author of a
forthcoming book on photojournalism.
<p>
   ``Whether or not the paparazzi go to jail for the crash, we were,
as the press, there at the creation of Diana and at her destruction,''
said Tom Rosenstiel, director of the Project for Excellence in
Journalism funded by the Pew Charitable Trusts.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   This debate over the role of the paparazzi comes when dozens of
leading journalists were already prepared to launch an inquiry to
clarify the core purposes and principles of their craft. This new
group, the Committee of Concerned Journalists, calls upon its
colleagues to sign a statement calling for a ``period of national
reflection.'' It is being led by Bill Kovach, director of the Nieman
Foundation at Harvard University, and Rosenstiel.
<p>
   Rosenstiel said Tuesday that the public's readiness to ``jump to
the conclusion that somehow the media killed Princess Diana'' is only
the most recent and most dramatic sign of the troubles in journalism.
<p>
   ``The public no longer makes that much of a distinction between
so-called paparazzi and the rest of the more serious media,''
Rosenstiel said. And perhaps the traditional press is itself
responsible for blurring the lines. ``There is a distinction between
the network news and the European paparazzi, but the network news
probably has more in common with the paparazzi than it did 15 years
ago,'' Rosenstiel said.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>

bc-netscape
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Financial editors) &QL;

<BYLINE> By Rajiv Chandrasekaran </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Netscape to Offer Service Aimed at Business Users &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Netscape Communications Corp. plans to announce Wednesday a World
Wide Web service that will include business-related news feeds and
discussion groups as well as an electronic store for corporate
software, according to company executives.
<p>
   Buffeted by competition from Microsoft Corp., Netscape is hoping to
take advantage of what's effectively a huge captive audience. Its
popular Navigator software automatically takes users to Netscape's
corporate site when they sign onto the Web, unless they change the
settings, which many never do. The corporate site now registers about
4 million visits a day.
<p>
   The new service, which would be offered at that site for free, aims
to create a virtual community for Internet users interested in
business and professional issues. The company hopes to make money
through advertising and partnership agreements with other firms.
<p>
   ``Think of it as an (America Online) community targeted at business
users,'' said Mike Homer, Netscape's senior vice president for
marketing.
<p>
   Netscape said the service will have about 1 million members at its
outset, largely because the company will transfer memberships from a
program it already runs to send personalized news reports to users'
electronic mailboxes.
<p>
   Making money off Web sites is increasingly important to Netscape.
Sales of Web ads, content agreements and business consulting services
made up almost 30 percent of the company's revenue last year, Homer
said.
<p>
   The new service, to be called Netcenter, will include a feature
called Industry Watch that will offer business news from a variety of
media organizations. The service also will have business-related chat
rooms and bulletin-board-like discussion groups as well as help in
setting up Web pages and chat groups that can be reached only by
designated users.
<p>
   The software store will feature products from Netscape, Borland
International Inc., Sun Microsystems Corp. and International Business
Machines Corp., among others.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-donate-gore-temple </SLUG>
<HEADER> 1stld-writethru   09-02 1242 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-donate-gore-temple 1stld-writethru
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Washington editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Peter Baker </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Gore Staff Saw Signs of Trouble &QL; 
 &UR; Before Temple Appearance (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  The White House Tuesday mounted a detailed defense of
Vice President Gore and his appearance at a Buddhist temple
fund-raiser just two days before congressional hearings into campaign
finance improprieties enter a new stage with the spotlight focused
squarely on Gore.
<p>
   Officials released a series of internal memos, electronic messages
and briefing papers intended to prove that Gore was unaware at the
time that the 1996 luncheon at the Hsi Lai Temple in California was a
fund-raising event.
<p>
   But the documents offered ample evidence that, at a minimum, Gore's
staff ignored numerous warning signs that could have averted what has
become one of the major embarrassments of Gore's career. And one
e-mail message from Gore himself six weeks before the event made clear
that he expected to attend a Los Angeles fund-raiser on that date,
although aides said the temple was not the planned location at that
point.
<p>
   The White House is deeply concerned about the possible effect of
this week's hearings on Gore, who is positioning himself as the heir
apparent to President Clinton. When the Senate Governmental Affairs
Committee reconvenes Thursday, the featured witnesses will be two
Buddhist nuns slated to testify that they were used to launder money
to the Democratic National Committee in violation of federal law.
Although there is no evidence tying Gore to the arrangement, the
administration fears he will be linked by association simply because
he attended the luncheon.
<p>
   ``That's the nuclear issue,'' said one White House official. ``That
could kill his presidential campaign.'' Nationally televised testimony
from innocent-looking nuns exploited by political operatives in an
illegal fund-raising scheme, the official said, could become the
searing image that stays with the voting public.
<p>
   In providing a point-by-point briefing on the vice president's
involvement with the temple Tuesday, the White House intended to send
a warning to Senate Republicans not to try to tar Gore with the
testimony of the nuns. ``This is a shot across the bow,'' the official
said. ``Anyone who suggests the slightest knowledge by Al Gore of the
illegal reimbursement schemewe will not permit to go unchallenged.''
<p>
   Gore is an inviting target for Republicans, not only because
Clinton will never again be on the ballot but because the vice
president's once-squeaky clean image has been tarnished by the temple
episode, as well as by reports about his telephone solicitations from
the White House. Wielding the gavel this week will be Sen. Fred D.
Thompson, a fellow Tennessean and possible Republican presidential
candidate in 2000.
<p>
   In addition to the nuns, the committee has told two senior Gore
aides, deputy chief of staff David Strauss and scheduling director
Kimberly H. Tilley, to be prepared to testify this week. No requests
have been made for an appearance by Gore, and aides said Tuesday that
he has no intention of testifying.
<p>
   Gore's visit to the red-pillared, pagoda-style Hsi Lai Temple in
Hacienda Heights, Calif., near Los Angeles, on April 29, 1996, has
emerged as one of the most memorable moments in the multiple campaign
fund-raising investigations. Although Gore initially characterized the
visit as a ``community outreach event,'' about $140,000 was raised at
the luncheon, and temple leaders ultimately reimbursed $58,000 to 12
nuns and devotees who wrote checks to the DNC. Federal law prohibits
financing contributions in someone else's name, and the DNC has
acknowledged that it was inappropriate to collect cash in a religious
building.
<p>
   The White House Tuesday provided a nuanced account of Gore's
attendance at the temple event that appeared aimed largely at shifting
the blame from the vice president to his own assistants. ``This is a
story of mid-level Gore staff people not communicating what they had
learned,'' one official said after the briefing.
<p>
   While Gore has disclaimed knowledge, documents make clear that his
office knew the temple stop was a fund-raiser. On April 11, DNC
fund-raiser John Huang sent Tilley a memo about the event titled
``Fund-raising lunch for Vice President Gore.'' On April 24, John B.
Emerson, deputy director of intergovernmental affairs at the White
House, sent an electronic message outlining Gore's California schedule
that included the reference, ``DNC funder for lunch.'' And an undated,
unsigned draft schedule prepared by a Gore aide cited ``DNC Luncheon
in LA/Hacienda Heights: 1000-5000 head,'' an apparent reference to how
much money each guest would give.
<p>
   In addition, officials said one aide traveling with Gore that
day-later identified as then-press secretary Peggy Wilhide-believed at
the time that the temple event was a fund-raiser. But they said it was
not her job to brief Gore and she never raised the matter with him.
<p>
   According to the documents, Gore was informed for months that he
would attend a fund-raiser in the Los Angeles area on that day. What
remains unclear from the papers is whether he knew the original plan
to have it at a restaurant had been rearranged to combine it with his
temple visit, and not simply canceled as aides say he believed.
<p>
   In a March 15 e-mail reply to Tilley, Gore said he could not attend
a New York function because he would be flying to San Jose and Los
Angeles for fund-raisers on April 29. ``If we have already booked the
fund-raisers, then we have to decline,'' Gore wrote.
<p>
   As it happens, on the same day he sent that message, Gore met in
Washington with the temple's venerable master, Hsing Yung, at Huang's
request. At the end of that 10-minute session, the cleric invited Gore
to visit the temple sometime, and the vice president replied that he
would like to do that. Officials said the timing was coincidental, but
Huang later used this as a reason to add the temple to the April
schedule.
<p>
   Another memo sent to Gore by Harold Ickes, then deputy White House
chief of staff, shows that as late as April 10 the vice president was
told he would attend a fund-raiser in Los Angeles with ``projected
revenue'' of $250,000. That memo was not among those released by the
White House Tuesday, although it has been provided to investigators.
At that time, officials said, the fund-raiser was still planned for
the restaurant, but Huang's memo to Tilley just a day later listed the
temple as the location.
<p>
   On the day of the event, Gore was given a briefing memo prepared by
DNC finance officials noting that those attending the temple luncheon
were members of a Huang-organized Asian American donor council that
required annual membership dues of $2,500. The memo instructed the
vice president that ``your role and contribution'' was to thank the
council members for their past support ``and inspire political and
fund-raising efforts among the Asian Pacific American Community.''
<p>
   Aides Tuesday contrasted that memo with briefing memos for other
Gore events that were ``formal fund-raisers,'' noting that those
papers explicitly stated financial goals, unlike the one for the Hsi
Lai luncheon. They also produced talking points prepared for Gore on
the day of the temple visit that show overt references to campaign
cash at a separate fund-raiser and no such mention at Hsi Lai.
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Jill Dutt </BYLINE>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Stock Average Surges Record 257 Points (New York) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   NEW YORK  &MD;  A burst of stock buying pushed the Dow Jones industrial
average up a record 257.36 points, erasing in just one day nearly half
the decline suffered by the Dow last month.
<p>
   The Dow closed at 7879.78 in heavy trading. Though the Dow's gain
was a record in point terms, the percentage gain of 3.38 percent was
well below the record post-World War II gain of 10.1 percent posted
Oct. 21, 1987.

<SLUG> bc-market-beat </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a2045 09-02 1035 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-market-beat - a2045
 &UR; (ATTN: Financial editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Tom Petruno </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; MARKET BEAT: Putting Their Money Where Their Mouth Is &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Any mutual fund manager can load up a portfolio with hundreds of
stocks and say he's bullish. If the market goes up, he'll be lifted
with it. If the market goes down, he'll sink with it  &MD;  but he (or she)
can always hide behind the boilerplate that ``not even a diversified
portfolio can escape a bear market.''
<p>
   Uh-huh.
<p>
   And how much conviction do such managers have about the individual
stocks they own? Usually not a heck of a lot.
<p>
   If you really want to get a sense of whether there are reasonably
priced stocks left to buy in a market that is near record highs  &MD;  and
increasingly volatile, as Tuesday's 257.36-point Dow Jones surge
demonstrated  &MD;  a good place to start is with managers who run very
concentrated portfolios, often owning 50 stocks or fewer.
<p>
   When every stock has to matter, a manager is likely to be much more
thorough in choosing. That's no guarantee of success, of course, but
at some point in this bull market  &MD;  maybe now, in fact  &MD;  conviction
should begin to count for more than mere herd-following.
<p>
   Herewith, four fund managers who pass the conviction test give a
sense of how they're applying that discipline today:
<p>
    &MD; Chuck Bath, Nationwide Fund, Columbus, Ohio (phone:
(800)-848-0920): Many Wall Streeters worry that blue-chip drug stocks
are overvalued after their spectacular run since 1994. Chuck Bath's
retort: ``Compared to what?''
<p>
   His highly focused fund of about 50 stocks, up nearly 30 percent
year-to-date, is very heavily weighted with two drug stocks:
Warner-Lambert and Schering Plough, which together make up about 14
percent of the $1.35 billion portfolio.
<p>
   Isn't it time to sell, with Warner-Lambert priced at 30 times
estimated 1998 earnings per share, and Schering at 23 times, versus a
price-to-earnings ratio of about 20 for the typical blue-chip stock in
the Standard &AMP; Poor's 500 index?
<p>
   ``Not when I have so much confidence in their earnings growth,''
Bath says of his drug stocks, which he has held for many years. As
long as the market overall stays high, he believes that Warner-Lambert
and Schering deserve their current prices, and more.
<p>
   Less pricey among his major holdings is Mellon Bank, the
Pittsburgh-based banking firm, which sells for a P/E of about 15 based
on estimated 1998 earnings. Bath loves the Mellon franchise and says
of it and other high-quality bank stocks: ``People are still afraid of
the problems the banks had in the 1970s and 1980s (with bad loans),
but that's history. I think these stocks deserve to at least sell for
the market's (P/E).''
<p>
    &MD; Richard Pender, Sentinel Common Stock fund, Montpelier, Vt.
((800)-282-3863): Pender used to feel about Coca-Cola and Gillette the
way Chuck Bath feels about Warner-Lambert. But in early summer, Pender
jettisoned those stocks from his $1.6 billion fund. ``Egregiously
overvalued,'' he says of them.
<p>
   To fill their place in the fund, which is up about 20 percent this
year and which limits itself to between 50 and 60 issues, Pender has
picked up some stocks that aren't household names, but which fit his
definition of undervalued, often under-followed issues that should
post strong returns over a three- to five-year period.
<p>
   One is Canadian Pacific, which is the trans-Canada railway and a
lot more, Pender says. Little-known to many U.S. investors, the
company also is the biggest transatlantic shipping firm, owner of
major resorts, and an energy company  &MD;  all for 11 times estimated 1998
earnings.
<p>
   Pender also likes B.F. Goodrich, which long ago got out of the tire
business and has instead built a big business supplying parts to the
booming aerospace industry. ``They've got tremendous growth potential
and significantly undervalued assets,'' Pender asserts. And at a 1998
estimated P/E of 15, Goodrich is a much cheaper way to play the
aerospace boom than Boeing, Pender notes.
<p>
    &MD; Richard Meagley, Safeco Equity fund, Seattle ((800)-426-6730):
Like Pender, Meagley worries about stock price valuations in a market
that is clearly pushing the edge of the envelope by historical
standards.
<p>
   So he has tried to skew his $1.4-billion fund toward high-quality
stocks that are less likely to be dragged down should the market
suddenly develop a bad case of acrophobia, he says.
<p>
   Anheuser-Busch fills that bill, Meagley says. At 17 times estimated
1998 earnings and sporting an above-average 2.4 percent dividend
yield, he thinks Anheuser is a good way be ``defensive'' yet also play
for decent earnings growth. Ditto for supermarket chain owner American
Stores, which also is a ``cost-cutting'' story that could yield better
earnings growth than the market now expects, Meagley says.
<p>
   The 48-stock fund, up about 20 percent year-to-date, also continues
to hold such blue-chip favorites as Fannie Mae, the mortgage giant.
``I've been adding to things like that &MD; companies you really want to
own looking out five years,'' Meagley says.
<p>
    &MD; Richard Peterson, SteinRoe Special Venture fund, Chicago
((800)-338-2550): Peterson's focus is on smaller stocks, which only in
the last few months have begun to capture more investors' attention,
after lagging blue chips since 1994.
<p>
   ``I think the undervaluation (in smaller stocks) has been
extreme,'' Peterson says. If the number of investors who feel the same
continues to grow, the small-stock rally is likely to persist for some
time to come, he says. ``These kinds of (trends) don't turn off all at
once,'' Peterson says.
<p>
   His fund, up about 14 percent year-to-date, favors such names as
Zytec, a supplier of converters used in computer networking. ``All
they need is for the networking business to grow'' &MD; it doesn't matter
much which of the big networking companies leads the charge, Peterson
says.
<p>
   He also favors a few real estate-related companies, including
property-services firm CB Commercial Real Estate Services, and Spieker
Properties, a big owner of commercial and industrial properties in
California. ``I figure it's your turn out there to grow again,''
Peterson says of the California real estate market.
</TEXT>
